👣 Two-Pointer Warm-ups

The two-pointer technique exploits a sorted or symmetric structure to walk two indices toward each other (or in tandem), eliminating large swaths of the search space in a single linear pass instead of checking all pairs. It is the foundational warm-up family for Google L6 loops: kSum, palindrome/partition scans, and the classic Trapping Rain Water all reduce to "advance the pointer that can't yet help" — cheap to code, high-signal on whether you can reason about invariants.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 5 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • Array is sorted (or you can afford to sort) and you're searching for a pair/triple/quad meeting a sum condition.
  • "Find k numbers summing to a target" → fix k-2 elements, two-pointer the innermost pair.
  • Symmetric structure you can squeeze from both ends: palindromes, sorted-squares, container/water between walls.
  • In-place partition / compaction with a slow "write" pointer and a fast "scan" pointer (Move Zeroes, Dutch National Flag, dedup).
  • You want O(1) extra space and the brute force is O(n²) over pairs.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two shapes dominate. (1) Opposite-end pointers on a sorted array: compare the current sum to the target and move the pointer that brings you closer — too small advances left, too big retreats right. (2) kSum generalization: sort once, fix the outer k-2 indices with duplicate-skipping, and two-pointer the last pair. The recurring crux is skipping duplicates at every fixed position and after every match so result sets stay unique.

// Opposite-end two pointers on a sorted array (the core idiom)
int left = 0, right = n - 1;
while (left < right) {
    int sum = a[left] + a[right];
    if (sum == target) { /* record */ left++; right--; }
    else if (sum < target) left++;          // need a bigger value
    else right--;                            // need a smaller value
}

// kSum: fix (k-2) anchors with dup-skips, two-pointer the inner pair
Arrays.sort(nums);
for (int a = 0; a < n; a++) {
    if (a > 0 && nums[a] == nums[a - 1]) continue;   // skip duplicate anchor
    // ... fix more anchors for larger k ...
    int l = a + 1, r = n - 1;
    while (l < r) {
        long s = (long) nums[a] + nums[l] + nums[r]; // long guards overflow
        if (s == target) { /* add */ l++; r--;
            while (l < r && nums[l] == nums[l - 1]) l++;
            while (l < r && nums[r] == nums[r + 1]) r--;
        } else if (s < target) l++; else r--;
    }
}

📘 Deep dive

1 Two Sum II — Input Array Is Sorted LC 167 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a sorted array and a target, return the 1-indexed positions of the two numbers that sum to the target (exactly one solution exists).

💡 Key insight. A sorted array plus "find a pair" calls for opposite-end pointers: the sum is monotonic in each pointer's movement. If the sum is too small, only moving left up can increase it; if too big, only moving right down can decrease it — so each step provably discards a value that can never be part of the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Set left = 0, right = n - 1.
  2. While left < right: compute sum. If it equals the target, return {left+1, right+1} (1-indexed).
  3. If sum < target, do left++; otherwise right--.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each element is visited at most once 💾 Space O(1) — two index variables

⚠️ Gotchas. Return positions are 1-indexed, not 0-indexed — a classic off-by-one. This works only because the array is sorted; on an unsorted array you'd use a hash map (the original Two Sum). The "exactly one solution" guarantee means you don't handle the no-answer case in the happy path.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because the array is sorted, I start with the widest pair. If their sum is too small, the smallest element can't pair with anything to reach the target, so I drop it by moving left up; if too big, I drop the largest by moving right down. Each move eliminates one candidate, so it's a single O(n) pass with O(1) space."
📄 TwoSumII.java

2 3Sum LC 15 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return all unique triples in the array that sum to zero.

💡 Key insight. Sort, then fix each nums[i] as an anchor and run Two-Sum-II's opposite-end two pointers on the suffix, searching for a pair summing to -nums[i]. The crux is duplicate-skipping in three places — the anchor i, and left/right after each match — so the result set stays unique without a hash set.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort the array.
  2. For each anchor i: if nums[i] > 0 break (three sorted positives can't reach 0); if nums[i] == nums[i-1] skip the duplicate anchor.
  3. Two-pointer left = i+1, right = n-1 for sum == 0. On a hit, record the triple, advance both pointers, and skip equal neighbors on each side.
  4. Otherwise move left up if the sum is negative, right down if positive.

⏱️ Time O(n²) — outer anchor loop × inner linear two-pointer scan 💾 Space O(1) extra (ignoring the sort and the output)

⚠️ Gotchas. Three separate dup-skips are required; forgetting any one yields duplicate triples. The nums[i] > 0 early break is a real pruning win on sorted input. Skip the anchor duplicate before the two-pointer scan (using i > 0 && nums[i] == nums[i-1]), and skip left/right duplicates only after recording a match.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force is O(n³). I sort so I can fix one number and reduce the rest to Two Sum on a sorted array — that inner part is the O(n) two-pointer scan, giving O(n²) overall. The only subtlety is avoiding duplicate triples, which I handle by skipping equal values at the anchor and after each successful pair, so I never need a set."
📄 ThreeSum.java

3 4Sum LC 18 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return all unique quadruples summing to a given target.

💡 Key insight. 4Sum is 3Sum with one more nested layer: fix nums[a], fix nums[b], then two-pointer the remaining pair. Skip duplicates at all four positions (a, b, left, right). Use long for the running sum, since four values near 10⁹ overflow a 32-bit int.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort. Loop the first anchor a (skip duplicate a), then the second anchor b from a+1 (skip duplicate b).
  2. Two-pointer left = b+1, right = n-1; compute sum as a long.
  3. On a match, record the quadruple, advance both pointers, and skip equal neighbors on each side; otherwise move left/right by comparison to the target.
  4. (Optional) prune with min/max-window checks to cut real runtime, as the practice set does.

⏱️ Time O(n³) — two nested anchors × linear two-pointer scan 💾 Space O(1) extra (ignoring the sort and the output)

⚠️ Gotchas. Overflow is the headline trap: (long) nums[a] + nums[b] + nums[left] + nums[right] — cast the first operand so the whole expression promotes. Now there are four dup-skip sites. The duplicate-anchor guards use a > 0 and b > a + 1 respectively so you don't skip a legitimately repeated value that belongs in a quad (e.g. [2,2,2,2]).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "This generalizes 3Sum: I fix two anchors and two-pointer the inner pair, so it's O(n³). The two things I always call out are overflow — I sum in long because four billion-scale ints exceed int range — and the four duplicate-skip positions needed to keep quads unique. kSum is the same recipe with k-2 fixed anchors."
📄 FourSum.java

4 Trapping Rain Water LC 42 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given bar heights of width 1, compute the total units of water trapped between them after rain.

💡 Key insight. Water above bar i is min(maxLeft, maxRight) - height[i]. The two-pointer trick avoids the O(n) prefix-max arrays: walk inward from both ends and always advance the shorter side. When height[left] < height[right], some bar on the right is already taller than left, so leftMax alone bounds the water at left — you never need the exact right wall.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Two pointers left = 0, right = n-1; track leftMax, rightMax (start at 0).
  2. While left < right: if height[left] < height[right], the right side is guaranteed taller — update leftMax, add leftMax - height[left], and advance left.
  3. Otherwise mirror: update rightMax, add rightMax - height[right], retreat right.
  4. Return the accumulated total.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each index visited exactly once 💾 Space O(1) — a handful of scalars (no prefix-max arrays)

⚠️ Gotchas. The invariant is the whole proof: you may process the shorter side because the taller side guarantees a bounding wall exists beyond it. Fewer than 3 bars trap nothing — guard empty/short inputs. Adding leftMax - height[left] is naturally 0 when the current bar is a new max, so no special case for "ascending" runs. The DP/stack solutions also work but cost O(n) space; the two-pointer is the expected optimal.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The water on a bar is bounded by the shorter of the tallest walls to its left and right. Computing both maxes is O(n) space; instead I converge two pointers and always move the shorter side, because whichever side is shorter, I already know a taller wall exists on the other side to bound it. So I only need the running max on the side I'm processing — one O(n) pass, O(1) space."
📄 TrappingRainWater.java

5 Two-Pointer Practice (bonus drill set) Medium ⚪ Practice set

Problem. A bonus drill of ~11 two-pointer micro-problems collected in one file, spanning Easy → Hard, to make the pattern reflexive across its many disguises.

💡 Key insight. Every entry is a variation on "use a sorted or symmetric structure to move two indices and eliminate work in linear passes." The set drills all three sub-shapes: opposite-end convergence, slow/fast in-place compaction, and the three-way Dutch National Flag partition — plus the kSum generalization (fix k-2, two-pointer the rest).
🛠️ Approach. The collection covers:
  1. Easy: Valid Palindrome (opposite ends, skip non-alphanumeric), Squares of a Sorted Array (both ends, fill from the back), Move Zeroes (slow/fast swap in place), Remove Duplicates from Sorted Array (slow writes uniques).
  2. Medium: 3Sum Closest (fix one + two pointers, track the closest sum), Sort Colors / Dutch National Flag (low/mid/high in one pass), Two Sum II (opposite-end pair), Boats to Save People (sort + greedy lightest/heaviest), Partition Labels (last-index + expanding window).
  3. Hard: Trapping Rain Water (resolve the shorter side), 4Sum (fix two anchors + two pointers, long sum, sorted-order pruning).

⏱️ Time varies — O(n) for the linear scans, up to O(n³) for 4Sum 💾 Space O(1) for most; O(n) for Sorted Squares' output

⚠️ Gotchas. Each disguise has its own trap: in Dutch National Flag, do not advance mid after swapping in from high (the swapped-in value is unchecked); Move Zeroes/dedup need a slow "write" cursor distinct from the fast scanner; Boats always boards the heaviest and conditionally the lightest. The file ships a built-in harness (22/22 tests) — use it to confirm a from-scratch rewrite before moving on.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "This is my reflex-builder for the two-pointer family. I can recognize, on sight, whether a problem wants opposite-end convergence, a slow/fast in-place rewrite, or a three-way partition — and I know kSum is just fixing k-2 anchors and two-pointering the last pair. The point is that once the structure is sorted or symmetric, two pointers turn an O(n²) scan into O(n)."
📄 TwoPointerPractice.java

🔁 Practice plan

Go strictly in order: Two Sum II (167) to internalize opposite-end convergence, then 3Sum (15) and 4Sum (18) to make the kSum recipe (fix k-2, two-pointer, dup-skip everywhere, long for overflow) automatic. Then earn Trapping Rain Water (42) by re-deriving the shorter-side invariant from a blank file — it's the Google favorite here and the proof is the interview. Finish with the Two-Pointer Practice set and run its built-in harness to confirm each micro-problem from scratch. Google follow-ups to rehearse: Container With Most Water (11), 3Sum Smaller (259), and "kSum for arbitrary k" as a recursive generalization.

  • ✅ I can explain why opposite-end pointers are correct on a sorted array (each move discards a dead candidate).
  • ✅ I can write 3Sum/4Sum with all duplicate-skips and the long overflow guard from memory.
  • ✅ I can state and prove the Trapping Rain Water shorter-side invariant.
  • ✅ I can do Dutch National Flag in one pass and explain why mid doesn't advance on a high-swap.
  • ✅ I can generalize the whole family to kSum: fix k-2 anchors, two-pointer the rest.

🧮 Arrays & Hashing

The bedrock pattern family: scan a sequence once while a hash structure or a couple of indices remember just enough to answer in linear time. At Google L6 these are warm-up rounds you are simply expected to nail cold — the bar is not "can you solve it" but "do you instantly reach for the O(1)-lookup or in-place trick and narrate the complexity without hesitation."

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 18 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Has it been seen before?" / "find a pair / complement / count of something" → hash map or set for O(1) membership.
  • Array is already sorted, or asks to dedupe / merge / partition in place → two pointers (slow-write + fast-read, or converging ends).
  • "Best/largest/longest contiguous subarray" → Kadane / running extreme in one pass.
  • "Number of subarrays summing to k" or any range-sum question → prefix sums + hash map.
  • Constant extra space demanded on an array of values in [1, n] → use the array itself as a hash (index = value-1, or XOR).
  • Exactly one element differs / is missing among pairs → XOR cancellation.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two reusable idioms cover most of this chapter. First, the seen-map / complement pass: walk left to right, and before recording the current element, ask whether the thing that would complete the answer is already in the map. Second, the two-pointer in-place rewrite: a slow "write" index marks the boundary of the finished region while a fast "read" index scans ahead, so you compact or partition without extra memory.

// Idiom A — one-pass hash lookup (Two Sum, Subarray Sum = K, Longest Consecutive)
Map<Integer, Integer> seen = new HashMap<>();
for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
    int need = target - nums[i];          // what would complete the answer?
    if (seen.containsKey(need)) return new int[]{ seen.get(need), i };
    seen.put(nums[i], i);                 // record AFTER the check to avoid self-match
}

// Idiom B — slow-write / fast-read in place (Move Zeroes, Remove Dups, partitioning)
int write = 0;
for (int read = 0; read < nums.length; read++) {
    if (keep(nums[read])) {               // condition defines what survives
        nums[write++] = nums[read];       // (or swap for stable reordering)
    }
}
// nums[0..write) is the answer; `write` is its length

📘 Deep dive

1 Two Sum LC 1 ↗ Easy ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return the indices of the two numbers that add up to target (exactly one solution, can't reuse an element).

💡 Key insight. For each number, its partner is fixed: target - num. If you've already recorded every earlier number in a map, you can recognise the partner the instant it appears — no nested loop.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a map value → index of numbers seen so far.
  2. For each nums[i], compute complement = target - nums[i].
  3. If the complement is in the map, return { map.get(complement), i } (earlier index first).
  4. Otherwise store nums[i] → i and continue.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one scan, O(1) lookups 💾 Space O(n) — up to n map entries

⚠️ Gotchas. Insert after the lookup, never before, or a number could match itself. Duplicate values (e.g. [3,3]) work precisely because you return the moment the second one's complement is found. Don't sort — that destroys the original indices the problem asks for.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force checks every pair in O(n²). But the partner I need is determined by the current value, so instead of searching for it I store each number in a hash map and ask, in O(1), whether its complement already passed by. One pass, O(n) time, O(n) space for the map."
📄 TwoSum.java

2 Contains Duplicate LC 217 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Return true if any value appears at least twice in the array.

💡 Key insight. A hash set answers "have I seen this before?" in O(1). The first failed insertion is the duplicate, so you can short-circuit immediately.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Iterate the array, adding each value to a HashSet.
  2. set.add(x) returns false when x is already present — return true right there.
  3. If the loop finishes cleanly, there were no duplicates: return false.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass, early exit 💾 Space O(n) — set of distinct values

⚠️ Gotchas. Using set.add's boolean return is cleaner than a separate contains+add. If asked for the O(1)-space variant, sorting first (O(n log n) time) and scanning adjacent pairs trades time for space — mention it but lead with the set.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I stream the values into a set; if any insert fails the element is a repeat and I bail early. O(n) time, O(n) space. The alternative is sort-then-scan for O(1) extra space at the cost of O(n log n) time."
📄 ContainsDuplicate.java

3 Best Time to Buy and Sell Stock LC 121 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. One buy and one later sell; maximise profit, or return 0 if no profitable trade exists.

💡 Key insight. The best sell on day i can only pair with the cheapest price seen on or before day i. Track that running minimum and the answer is the largest price - minSoFar gap.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Initialise minSoFar = prices[0] and best = 0.
  2. For each later price: update best = max(best, price - minSoFar) (sell today against cheapest prior buy).
  3. Then update minSoFar = min(minSoFar, price).

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass 💾 Space O(1) — two scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. Buy must precede sell — updating best before minSoFar guarantees the min is from a strictly earlier day. Monotonically decreasing prices must yield 0, not a negative number, so best starts at 0. This is just Kadane in disguise over the daily price deltas.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I sweep once, tracking the cheapest price so far. At each day I imagine selling there, which is only constrained by the minimum to its left, so the best answer is the max gap. O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 BestTimeToBuyAndSellStock.java

4 Single Number LC 136 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Every element appears twice except one; find that lone element in O(n) time and O(1) space.

💡 Key insight. XOR is associative and commutative with x ^ x = 0 and x ^ 0 = x. XOR-ing the whole array cancels every pair and leaves only the unique value.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Start an accumulator acc = 0.
  2. XOR every element into acc.
  3. Return acc — all paired bits have cancelled.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass 💾 Space O(1) — single accumulator

⚠️ Gotchas. Works for negatives too (XOR is bit-level, sign-agnostic). The O(1) space constraint is the whole point — a hash-set count would pass tests but misses the intended trick. Order of elements is irrelevant since XOR commutes.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because duplicates come in pairs and x ^ x is 0, XOR-ing everything annihilates the pairs and the survivor is the unique number. One pass, constant space — no hashing needed."
📄 SingleNumber.java

5 Majority Element LC 169 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. An element appears more than n/2 times; return it in O(1) space.

💡 Key insight. Boyer-Moore voting: pair off each occurrence of the majority with a different element and they cancel. Because the majority strictly outnumbers everything else combined, it is the last candidate standing.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a candidate and a count.
  2. When count == 0, adopt the current element as the new candidate.
  3. Add +1 to count if the element equals the candidate, else −1.
  4. The final candidate is the majority element.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass 💾 Space O(1) — candidate + counter

⚠️ Gotchas. Only valid because a strict majority is guaranteed; without that guarantee you'd need a second pass to verify the candidate actually exceeds n/2. Don't confuse with the "more than n/3" variant, which needs two candidates.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I treat it as a voting war: each non-matching element cancels one vote for the current leader. Since the majority element occurs more than n/2 times it can never be fully cancelled, so whoever holds the lead at the end is the answer. O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 MajorityElement.java

6 Missing Number LC 268 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. An array holds n distinct numbers drawn from 0..n; return the one value that's missing.

💡 Key insight. XOR every index 0..n against every value present. Each value cancels its matching index; the index with no value left over is the missing number.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed acc = n (folds in the top index n, which has no array slot).
  2. For each i, do acc ^= i ^ nums[i] — cancel index i against the value there.
  3. Return acc; only the unmatched index survives.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass 💾 Space O(1)

⚠️ Gotchas. The seed n is easy to forget — without it you only XOR indices 0..n-1 and miss the top of the range. The Gauss-sum approach n(n+1)/2 - sum also works but can overflow for large n; XOR never overflows.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I XOR all indices 0 through n with all the values. Every number cancels its own index, so the leftover bit pattern is the index that never received a value — the missing number. O(n) time, O(1) space, and unlike the sum trick it can't overflow."
📄 MissingNumber.java

7 Move Zeroes LC 283 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Move all zeroes to the end in place while keeping the relative order of the non-zero elements.

💡 Key insight. A write pointer insert marks where the next non-zero belongs. Swapping each non-zero down to insert preserves order and lets the zeroes bubble to the tail in a single pass.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. insert = 0 marks the next slot for a non-zero.
  2. Scan with a read pointer; on each non-zero, swap(nums, insert++, read).
  3. insert only ever trails or equals read, so the swap never disturbs unprocessed data.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass 💾 Space O(1) — in place

⚠️ Gotchas. Swapping (rather than overwrite-then-pad-with-zeroes) keeps it a single clean pass and avoids a separate fill loop. Guard swap against a == b to skip no-op writes. Relative order of non-zeroes must be preserved — don't sort or two-end swap.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep a write index for the next non-zero's home. As I scan, every non-zero swaps down to that index, which advances; zeroes naturally accumulate at the back. Stable, in place, O(n) time and O(1) space."
📄 MoveZeroes.java

8 Remove Duplicates from Sorted Array LC 26 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Remove duplicates from a sorted array in place; return the new length, with the unique values at the front.

💡 Key insight. Sorted means duplicates are adjacent. A slow write pointer marks the last unique value; the fast pointer only writes when it finds something different.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. write = 0; nums[0..write] is the unique prefix.
  2. Scan read from index 1. When nums[read] != nums[write], do nums[++write] = nums[read].
  3. Return write + 1 as the length of the deduped prefix.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass 💾 Space O(1) — in place

⚠️ Gotchas. Compare against nums[write] (the last kept value), not nums[read-1] — they coincide here but the former generalises to "keep at most k copies". Handle the empty array up front. The trailing slots past write are left as garbage; that's allowed.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Since it's sorted, equal values cluster. I hold a write index at the last unique element and only copy forward when the reader sees a new value, compacting the uniques into the front. O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 RemoveDuplicatesFromSortedArray.java

9 Merge Sorted Array LC 88 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Merge sorted nums2 (length n) into sorted nums1 (which has m real elements plus n free slots) in place.

💡 Key insight. Merge from the back. The free tail of nums1 is exactly where the largest remaining element goes, so writing downward never clobbers a value you still need to read.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Pointers i = m-1, j = n-1, write index write = m+n-1.
  2. While j >= 0: place the larger of nums1[i] / nums2[j] at nums1[write--] and advance that pointer.
  3. When nums2 drains, the nums1 prefix is already correctly placed — stop.

⏱️ Time O(m+n) — each element placed once 💾 Space O(1) — in place into nums1

⚠️ Gotchas. Looping on j >= 0 (not i >= 0) is the clean termination: leftover nums1 values are already home. Forward merging would overwrite unread nums1 elements — backward is what makes O(1) space possible. Guard i >= 0 inside the comparison.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Merging front-to-back would overwrite nums1's own data, so I fill from the back where the spare slots are. I compare the two tails and drop the bigger into the highest free index. Once nums2 is exhausted, nums1's remaining prefix is already sorted. O(m+n) time, O(1) space."
📄 MergeSortedArray.java

10 Maximum Subarray LC 53 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the contiguous subarray with the largest sum and return that sum.

💡 Key insight. Kadane's: the best subarray ending at i either extends the best ending at i-1 or restarts at nums[i]. A negative running sum can only drag down what follows, so drop it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Init best = current = nums[0].
  2. For each later element: current = max(nums[i], current + nums[i]) (extend or restart).
  3. Track best = max(best, current).

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass 💾 Space O(1) — two scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. Initialise from nums[0], never 0 — an all-negative array (e.g. [-3,-1,-2]) must return the least-negative element, not 0. The "restart vs extend" comparison is what handles negatives correctly. If asked for the actual indices, track the start when you restart.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "At each position I decide whether the running sum still helps: if it went negative, carrying it forward only hurts, so I restart at the current element. I keep the best sum seen. One pass, O(n) time, O(1) space — a divide-and-conquer O(n log n) exists but Kadane is strictly better."
📄 MaximumSubarray.java

11 Maximum Product Subarray LC 152 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the contiguous subarray with the largest product and return that product.

💡 Key insight. Unlike sums, a negative factor swaps the roles of largest and smallest, so today's running minimum can become tomorrow's maximum. Carry both the max and min product ending at i.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Init best = curMax = curMin = nums[0].
  2. If nums[i] < 0, swap curMax and curMin first (the sign flip exchanges the extremes).
  3. curMax = max(nums[i], curMax * nums[i]); curMin = min(nums[i], curMin * nums[i]).
  4. Update best = max(best, curMax).

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass 💾 Space O(1) — running max/min scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. You must swap before the extend step on a negative, or the extremes get multiplied wrong. Zeroes reset both running products (the max(nums[i], …) restart handles this). A single negative element like [-2] must return -2, so seed from nums[0].
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Sum-Kadane breaks because a negative can turn a tiny product into a huge one. So I track both the max and the min ending here; on a negative I swap them because multiplying flips which is biggest. Zeroes naturally restart the window. O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 MaximumProductSubarray.java

12 Container With Most Water LC 11 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given wall heights, pick two lines forming a container that holds the most water: maximise width × min(left, right).

💡 Key insight. The shorter wall caps the area. Start at the widest pair and always move the shorter wall inward — moving the taller one only loses width without ever lifting the cap.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Pointers at both ends; track best.
  2. Compute area = (right - left) * min(height[left], height[right]).
  3. Advance whichever pointer is at the shorter wall (its best is already realised here).
  4. Stop when the pointers meet.

⏱️ Time O(n) — pointers meet after n steps 💾 Space O(1)

⚠️ Gotchas. The correctness argument is the crux interviewers probe: moving the taller wall can never beat the current area because the shorter wall still caps it while width shrinks. On ties either pointer may move. Don't confuse with Trapping Rain Water — here it's a single container, not summed traps.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force tries all pairs in O(n²). Instead I start at the widest gap; the area is limited by the shorter wall, so I discard it — keeping it can only do worse as width falls. Moving the shorter wall is the only way to possibly find a taller limiter. Linear time, constant space."
📄 ContainerWithMostWater.java

13 Product of Array Except Self LC 238 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return an array where answer[i] is the product of every element except nums[i] — without using division.

💡 Key insight. answer[i] = (product of everything left of i) × (product of everything right of i). Two sweeps — prefix products left-to-right, then suffix products right-to-left — build it with no division and zeros handled naturally.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. First pass: answer[i] = answer[i-1] * nums[i-1], so each slot holds the prefix product of everything to its left (answer[0] = 1).
  2. Second pass: walk right-to-left with a scalar suffix = 1; do answer[i] *= suffix then suffix *= nums[i].
  3. Return answer.

⏱️ Time O(n) — two linear passes 💾 Space O(1) extra — output aside, only a suffix scalar

⚠️ Gotchas. "No division" is the explicit constraint — the divide-then-multiply shortcut also dies on any zero. The output array doesn't count against the space bound, but a second prefix array would; reusing answer plus one scalar is what earns true O(1) extra space. One zero zeroes all but its own slot; two zeroes zero everything.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each answer is the product of the left part times the right part. I fill the array with running prefix products, then sweep backward multiplying in a running suffix product. No division, so zeros just work, and apart from the output I use a single scalar. O(n) time, O(1) extra space."
📄 ProductOfArrayExceptSelf.java

14 Longest Consecutive Sequence LC 128 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given an unsorted array, return the length of the longest run of consecutive integers — in O(n) time.

💡 Key insight. Put everything in a set for O(1) membership, then only start counting at a value that is a sequence start (i.e. num-1 is absent). That guard means each element is walked at most once overall, so it's O(n) despite the inner loop.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Add all values to a HashSet.
  2. For each value, skip it if set.contains(num-1) — it's mid-sequence, not a start.
  3. From a start, walk upward (num+1, num+2, …) while present, counting the length.
  4. Track the maximum length seen.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each value is an inner-loop subject once 💾 Space O(n) — the set

⚠️ Gotchas. The num-1 "is this a start?" check is what prevents O(n²); without it you'd re-walk the same run from every member. Sorting gives an easy O(n log n) solution — mention it, but the set version hits the required O(n). Duplicates are absorbed by the set. Empty input returns 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I drop everything into a hash set. To avoid recounting, I only begin a walk at numbers that have no predecessor in the set — the true starts — and extend upward. Each value is visited once as part of exactly one run, so it's O(n) time and O(n) space."
📄 LongestConsecutiveSequence.java

15 Subarray Sum Equals K LC 560 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Count the number of contiguous subarrays whose elements sum to exactly k (values may be negative).

💡 Key insight. A subarray (j, i] sums to k iff prefix[i] - prefix[j] == k, i.e. prefix[j] == prefix[i] - k. Counting how often each prefix sum has occurred turns the search for valid starts into an O(1) map lookup.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Map of prefixSum → count, seeded with {0 : 1} (the empty prefix).
  2. Maintain a running prefix. At each element, add map.getOrDefault(prefix - k, 0) to the answer.
  3. Then increment the count for the current prefix.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass, O(1) map ops 💾 Space O(n) — distinct prefix sums

⚠️ Gotchas. The {0:1} seed is essential — it lets a prefix that itself equals k count (subarray starting at index 0). Because values can be negative, the sliding-window trick used for positive-only arrays does not apply; you must use prefix counts. Add to the answer before recording the current prefix to avoid counting a zero-length subarray.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep running prefix sums. A subarray ending here sums to k exactly when some earlier prefix equals my current prefix minus k, so I store the frequency of every prefix and look that up in O(1). Negatives rule out a sliding window, which is why the hash-map-of-prefixes is the right tool. O(n) time and space."
📄 SubarraySumEqualsK.java

16 Sort Colors LC 75 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Sort an array of 0s, 1s, and 2s in place in a single pass (no counting-sort two-pass).

💡 Key insight. Dutch National Flag: maintain three regions with three pointers — [0,low) all 0s, [low,mid) all 1s, (high,end] all 2s — and shrink the unknown middle [mid,high] to nothing.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. low = mid = 0, high = n-1. Loop while mid <= high.
  2. nums[mid] == 0: swap(low++, mid++) — grow the 0 region.
  3. nums[mid] == 2: swap(mid, high--) and do not advance mid (the swapped-in value is unexamined).
  4. nums[mid] == 1: already in place, just mid++.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass, mid and high converge 💾 Space O(1) — sorted in place

⚠️ Gotchas. The one detail that trips people: after swapping a 2 to the back, don't increment mid — you haven't looked at what came from high yet. After swapping a 0 forward you can advance both, because what comes from low is a known 1. The two-pass counting sort is the easy fallback to mention.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep three zones — settled 0s on the left, 1s in the middle, 2s on the right — with a cursor scanning the unknown gap. A 0 goes to the left boundary, a 2 to the right; on a 2 I hold the cursor because the value I pulled from the right is still unchecked. One pass, in place, O(n)/O(1)."
📄 SortColors.java

17 Next Permutation LC 31 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Rearrange the numbers into the next lexicographically greater permutation in place; if none exists, wrap to the smallest (ascending).

💡 Key insight. The next permutation is the smallest possible bump. Scan from the right for the first nums[i] < nums[i+1] (the pivot); everything after it is already the largest descending arrangement. Swap the pivot with the smallest suffix element greater than it, then reverse the suffix to ascending.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. From the right, find the first index i with nums[i] < nums[i+1] (the pivot).
  2. If found, scan from the right for the first nums[j] > nums[i] and swap i with j.
  3. Reverse the suffix i+1 .. n-1 to put it in ascending (smallest) order.
  4. If no pivot exists (fully descending), reversing the whole array gives the first permutation.

⏱️ Time O(n) — two right-to-left scans + a reverse 💾 Space O(1) — done in place

⚠️ Gotchas. The descending suffix is already maximal, so it needs reversing, not sorting (reverse is O(n) vs O(n log n)). Use strict nums[j] > nums[i] when picking the swap target so equal values don't break lexicographic minimality. The no-pivot case ([3,2,1]) is the wrap-around and is just a full reverse with i = -1.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I find the rightmost spot where the order rises — that's the digit to increase minimally. I swap it with the smallest value to its right that still beats it, then reverse the now-descending tail so the suffix is as small as possible. If the whole array descends, there's no next, so I reverse it to the smallest permutation. O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 NextPermutation.java

18 First Missing Positive LC 41 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Find the smallest positive integer absent from the array, in O(n) time and O(1) extra space.

💡 Key insight. With n elements the answer must lie in [1, n+1]. Use the array itself as a hash table: send each value v in [1, n] to its home slot v-1 via cyclic swaps. Then the first index whose value isn't i+1 reveals the missing positive.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each i, while nums[i] is in [1, n] and not already home (nums[nums[i]-1] != nums[i]), swap it into slot nums[i]-1.
  2. This parks every in-range value at its index; out-of-range and duplicate values are left where they fall.
  3. Scan again: the first i with nums[i] != i+1 means i+1 is missing.
  4. If all slots match, the answer is n+1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — amortized, each swap parks one value home 💾 Space O(1) — in place

⚠️ Gotchas. The placement loop is a while, not an if — one swap may bring in yet another out-of-place value. The nums[nums[i]-1] != nums[i] guard prevents an infinite swap loop on duplicates. It's amortized O(n) because each successful swap permanently seats one element. Ignore non-positives and values > n; they can never be the first gap below n+1.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The answer is between 1 and n+1, so I only care about values in that range. I rearrange in place so value v sits at index v-1 — the array becomes its own hash. Then the first index missing its expected value is the answer, or n+1 if all are present. The cyclic swaps are amortized linear because each one fixes an element for good. O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 FirstMissingPositive.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work top-to-bottom: the nine Easy problems drill the two muscle-memory idioms (hash lookup, two-pointer compaction) plus the bit/voting tricks; the Mediums layer on Kadane variants and prefix-sum counting; finish with First Missing Positive, the one Hard that fuses "array-as-hash" with amortized analysis. From a blank file, re-derive these four without peeking — they are the highest-leverage idioms: Two Sum (complement map), Maximum Subarray (Kadane), Product of Array Except Self (prefix × suffix), and First Missing Positive (index-as-hash). For each, narrate the brute force first, then the insight that kills a factor of n, then the exact complexity.

  • ✅ I can explain why Two Sum inserts into the map after the complement check.
  • ✅ I can state Kadane's invariant ("best subarray ending at i") and seed it from nums[0] for all-negative inputs.
  • ✅ I can justify why moving the shorter wall in Container With Most Water never loses the optimum.
  • ✅ I can derive Product Except Self with no division and only O(1) extra space beyond the output.
  • ✅ I can argue Longest Consecutive Sequence is O(n) because each value is walked once from a true start.
  • ✅ I can explain the {0:1} seed in Subarray Sum = K and why negatives forbid a sliding window.
  • ✅ I can explain why Sort Colors holds mid after swapping in a 2 but advances it after a 0.
  • ✅ I can argue First Missing Positive is amortized O(n) and why the placement step is a while, not an if.
  • ✅ Rehearse Google-favourite follow-ups: Two Sum → "return all unique pairs / 3Sum"; Best Time to Buy/Sell → "with unlimited / k transactions"; Product Except Self → "now you're allowed division — handle the zeros."

📊 2D Prefix Sum & Range Queries

Range-sum-over-a-grid is a deceptively deep design family: the immutable case is a one-time prefix build plus O(1) inclusion-exclusion, while the mutable case forces you to reach for a 2D Fenwick (BIT) tree. Google L6 loves these because they probe whether you can pick the right data structure for the read/write mix and reason cleanly about per-operation complexity — exactly the trade-off conversation that separates a Staff candidate from a strong mid-level one.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Sum of an axis-aligned sub-rectangle" asked many times over a static grid → precompute a 2D prefix grid.
  • Cells can be updated between queries → a frozen prefix grid is invalidated by every write; switch to a 2D BIT.
  • Query and update counts are comparable and large (e.g. each up to 10⁴) → you need both operations poly-logarithmic, not one O(1) and one O(mn).
  • The phrase "inclusion-exclusion" or four-corner subtraction should fire the moment you see a rectangle defined by (row1,col1,row2,col2).
  • Off-by-one pain around region boundaries → pad with a zero row/column (1-indexed prefix space).

🧠 Core idea & template

Both problems compute a rectangle sum as four corner lookups. Define pre(r,c) = sum of everything from the origin up to (but framed in 1-indexed space). Then the region [r1,c1]..[r2,c2] = big rectangle − strip above − strip left + the top-left corner added back (it was subtracted twice). The immutable version stores pre directly so each lookup is O(1). The mutable version cannot freeze pre — instead each pre(r,c) is itself a 2D Fenwick prefix query that walks down both index dimensions in O(log m · log n).

// Inclusion-exclusion is identical in both worlds; only pre() differs.
int sumRegion(int r1, int c1, int r2, int c2) {
    return pre(r2 + 1, c2 + 1)   // whole rectangle to bottom-right corner
         - pre(r1,     c2 + 1)   // minus rows strictly above the region
         - pre(r2 + 1, c1)       // minus cols strictly left of the region
         + pre(r1,     c1);      // add back the top-left block removed twice
}

// IMMUTABLE: pre is a precomputed grid (1-indexed, zero border) — O(1) read.
pre[i][j] = matrix[i-1][j-1] + pre[i-1][j] + pre[i][j-1] - pre[i-1][j-1];

// MUTABLE: 2D Fenwick tree. Update walks UP both dims; prefix walks DOWN both.
void update(int row, int col, int delta) {        // delta = newVal - oldVal
    for (int i = row + 1; i <= m; i += i & -i)
        for (int j = col + 1; j <= n; j += j & -j)
            tree[i][j] += delta;
}
int pre(int r, int c) {                            // sum of [0..r-1][0..c-1]
    int s = 0;
    for (int i = r; i > 0; i -= i & -i)
        for (int j = c; j > 0; j -= j & -j)
            s += tree[i][j];
    return s;
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Range Sum Query 2D - Immutable LC 304 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill 2D prefix grid + inclusion-exclusion build O(mn) / query O(1)O(mn)
2Range Sum Query 2D - Mutable LC 308 ↗ Hard ◻️ Gap-fill 2D Fenwick / BIT update O(log m·log n) / query O(log m·log n)O(mn)
3Number of Submatrices That Sum to Target LC 1074 ↗ Hard 📌 Reported L6 Row-band collapse + prefix-sum hashmap O(rows²·cols)O(cols)

📘 Deep dive

1 Range Sum Query 2D - Immutable LC 304 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given a fixed matrix, answer many queries each asking for the sum of the sub-rectangle bounded by (row1,col1) top-left and (row2,col2) bottom-right.

💡 Key insight. Precompute pre[i][j] = sum of the submatrix from (0,0) to (i−1,j−1) inclusive — a one-indexed prefix grid with a zero border. Any region sum is then exactly four lookups via inclusion-exclusion. The padding row/column is the trick that erases every boundary special-case: indices i-1 and j-1 never go negative.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate pre[m+1][n+1] (extra zero row and column).
  2. Fill it: pre[i][j] = matrix[i-1][j-1] + pre[i-1][j] + pre[i][j-1] - pre[i-1][j-1] — current cell + strip above + strip left − the doubly-counted upper-left block.
  3. For a query, shift bounds by +1 into prefix space and apply the four-corner formula: pre[r2+1][c2+1] - pre[r1][c2+1] - pre[r2+1][c1] + pre[r1][c1].

⏱️ Time — constructor O(m·n) to build the grid; each sumRegion O(1), exactly four array reads 💾 Space O(m·n) — the prefix grid

⚠️ Gotchas. The most common bug is mixing prefix-space (1-indexed) with matrix-space (0-indexed): the rows-above term is pre[r1] not pre[r1-1], because r1 already points at the row above the region in prefix space. Forgetting to add back pre[r1][c1] double-subtracts the corner. Also handle a single-cell region and a 1×1 matrix — the zero border makes both fall out for free.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force re-sums the rectangle per query at O(mn) each, which dies under many queries. Instead I pay O(mn) once to build a prefix grid where pre[i][j] is the sum of everything up-and-left of that cell. Then any rectangle is the big block minus the two strips outside it, plus the corner I subtracted twice — four reads, O(1) per query. I one-index the grid with a zero border so I never special-case the top or left edge."
📄 RangeSumQuery2DImmutable.java

2 Range Sum Query 2D - Mutable LC 308 ↗ Hard ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Same rectangle-sum queries, but now cells can be updated to new values between queries — both operations must stay fast.

💡 Key insight. A 2D Binary Indexed (Fenwick) tree generalizes the 1D BIT: node (i,j) owns a rectangle whose height is the low bit of i and whose width is the low bit of j. A point update walks UP both dimensions (i += i&-i, j += j&-j), and a prefix query down to the origin walks DOWN both (i -= i&-i). The region sum is the same inclusion-exclusion as the immutable case — only now each corner prefix costs O(log m · log n).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a 1-indexed tree[m+1][n+1] and a copy of current cell values vals[m][n] so updates can compute a delta.
  2. Build by calling update(i,j,matrix[i][j]) for every cell (each insertion is a delta from zero).
  3. update(row,col,val): compute delta = val - vals[row][col], store the new value, then add delta to every node covering this cell by walking up both dimensions.
  4. sumRegion: same four-corner formula, each corner evaluated by the Fenwick prefix walk.

⏱️ Time — update O(log m · log n); sumRegion O(log m · log n) = four prefix walks; construction O(m·n·log m·log n) 💾 Space O(m·n) — the BIT plus the vals copy used for delta computation

⚠️ Gotchas. You must store deltas, not absolute values — a Fenwick tree only knows how to add; setting a cell means adding (new - old), which is why the vals mirror exists. Off-by-one: the tree is 1-indexed, so update loops start at row+1/col+1. Building via O(mn) updates is the simple route (good enough at interview scale); mention that a smarter O(mn) construction exists if pushed. Don't forget the inner loop must reset j each outer iteration in both walks.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "With updates, a frozen prefix grid is wrong the instant a cell changes, and rebuilding is O(mn) per write. So I use a 2D Fenwick tree: each node aggregates a power-of-two-sized rectangle keyed off the low bits of its indices. An update touches O(log m · log n) nodes, and a prefix sum reads O(log m · log n) nodes; the rectangle query is the same inclusion-exclusion over four prefixes. To update a cell I add the delta from its old value, so I keep a mirror of the raw values. Both operations are poly-logarithmic, which is what we want when reads and writes are both frequent."
📄 RangeSumQuery2DMutable.java

3 Number of Submatrices That Sum to Target LC 1074 ↗ Hard 📌 Reported L6

Problem. Count the non-empty submatrices (contiguous blocks of rows and columns) whose element sum equals a given target.

💡 Key insight. A submatrix is pinned by its top/bottom rows and left/right columns. Fix the vertical band (top, bottom) and collapse each column to its sum over those rows: the 2-D shape becomes a 1-D array colSum[], and any submatrix in this band is just a contiguous subarray of it. Counting subarrays with sum == target is the classic 1-D "subarray sum equals k" — walk a running prefix and, at each position, add the count of earlier prefixes equal to prefix − target, since each marks a left boundary closing a window summing to target. Summing over all O(rows²) bands covers every submatrix exactly once.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each top row, start a fresh colSum[cols] of zeros.
  2. Extend bottom downward, folding matrix[bottom] into colSum in place (no rebuild from scratch).
  3. For the current band, count subarrays of colSum summing to target via a prefix-sum HashMap seeded with {0:1}; add to the running total.
  4. Return the accumulated count.

⏱️ Time O(rows²·cols) — O(rows²) bands, each scanned across cols once 💾 Space O(cols) — the colSum array plus the prefix-count map (≤ cols+1 entries)

⚠️ Gotchas. Seed the prefix map with {0:1} so windows starting at index 0 are counted. Extending the band in place (colSum[c] += matrix[bottom][c]) rather than re-summing the rows is what keeps each band O(cols), not O(rows·cols). Negative values and duplicate sums are fine — the HashMap counts multiplicities of each prefix, so use merge(prefix, 1, +), not a set.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I collapse one dimension: fixing a top and bottom row turns the band into a 1-D array of column sums, and then I'm just counting contiguous subarrays that hit the target — the prefix-sum-with-hashmap trick from 'subarray sum equals k'. Iterating all row pairs is O(rows²), and I grow the band incrementally so each pair costs O(cols), giving O(rows²·cols). If columns were the smaller dimension I'd fix column pairs instead to make it O(cols²·rows)."
📄 NumberOfSubmatricesThatSumToTarget.java

🔁 Practice plan

Do 304 first from a blank file until the four-corner formula and the zero-border 1-indexing are muscle memory — that single idiom unlocks the whole family. Then build 308's 2D BIT from scratch; the test is whether you can write the i += i&-i / i -= i&-i walks in both dimensions without peeking, and explain why updates store a delta. Rehearse the read-vs-write trade-off aloud: when is the O(mn)-build/O(1)-query immutable grid the right call, and when does the workload force the Fenwick tree?

  • ✅ I can derive the inclusion-exclusion four-corner formula from a drawing, and justify each ± term.
  • ✅ I can explain why the zero-border 1-indexing removes all boundary special-cases.
  • ✅ I can write a 2D Fenwick update and prefix query from memory and state both as O(log m · log n).
  • ✅ I can argue when to prefer the immutable prefix grid vs the mutable BIT based on the query/update mix.
  • ✅ Follow-ups to rehearse: 303 (1D immutable), 307 (1D mutable / 1D Fenwick), and extending 308 to range updates + range queries via a difference-style BIT.

➕ Difference Array (Range Updates)

A difference array is the dual of a prefix sum: where prefix sums turn point values into range queries, difference arrays turn range updates into point stamps. Mark +delta at a range start and -delta just past its end, then a single prefix-sum pass materializes the final array — every range update is O(1) instead of O(range). This is high-ROI for Google L6 because it's the canonical "you don't have to touch every element" insight, and it recurs disguised as flights, carpools, calendars, and meeting-room load.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 2 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • Many operations each add the same value to every element of a contiguous range → stamp two endpoints, sweep once.
  • You only ever need the array after all updates (offline / batched) — no interleaved reads → perfect fit, no Fenwick needed.
  • "Does the running total ever exceed a capacity?" over interval load (passengers, attendees, bandwidth) → difference array + prefix-sum feasibility check.
  • Intervals are half-open [from, to) or inclusive [first, last] — the only subtlety is where the -delta lands.
  • Coordinate range is small/bounded (e.g. stops 0..1000) → a fixed-size array beats sorting events.

🧠 Core idea & template

Keep an array diff initialized to zero. A range-add of delta over [lo, hi] (inclusive) is recorded as diff[lo] += delta and diff[hi+1] -= delta. After all updates, the prefix sum of diff is the final array: at index lo the +delta switches the contribution on, and at hi+1 the -delta switches it back off, so every index in between carries exactly the deltas of the ranges that cover it. The whole pattern is two writes per update + one linear sweep.

// Range-add delta over inclusive [lo, hi], then materialize.
int[] diff = new int[n + 1];        // +1 guard so hi+1 is always in bounds
for (int[] u : updates) {
    int lo = u[0], hi = u[1], delta = u[2];
    diff[lo]     += delta;          // turn the contribution ON at lo
    diff[hi + 1] -= delta;          // turn it OFF just past hi
}
int[] result = new int[n];
int running = 0;
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
    running += diff[i];             // prefix sum reconstructs the value at i
    result[i] = running;
}

// Feasibility variant (Car Pooling): half-open [from, to), check a cap on the fly.
int[] diff = new int[MAX + 1];
for (int[] t : trips) { diff[from] += load; diff[to] -= load; } // to, not to+1
int onboard = 0;
for (int d : diff) { onboard += d; if (onboard > capacity) return false; }
return true;

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Corporate Flight Bookings LC 1109 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Difference array (inclusive ranges) O(n + m)O(n)
2Car Pooling LC 1094 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Difference array over bounded coordinate + capacity check O(n + R)O(R)

📘 Deep dive

1 Corporate Flight Bookings LC 1109 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given n flights (1-indexed) and bookings [first, last, seats] that each add seats to every flight in [first, last], return the total seats booked per flight.

💡 Key insight. A booking adds the same seats across the whole inclusive range, so don't loop the range per booking. Stamp a difference array: +seats at the start, -seats just past the end, then one prefix-sum pass turns the boundary marks back into per-flight totals. Flights are 1-indexed, so shift to 0-indexed and let the -seats land at last (which is (last-1)+1 in 0-indexed terms).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate res[n] that doubles as the difference array and, later, the answer.
  2. For each booking: res[first-1] += seats; if last < n then res[last] -= seats (the guard avoids writing past the array when the booking reaches the final flight).
  3. Prefix-sum in place: res[i] += res[i-1] for i = 1..n-1.

⏱️ Time O(n + m) — m bookings stamp two endpoints each, then an O(n) sweep over flights 💾 Space O(n) — the difference array, reused as the answer (O(1) beyond the output)

⚠️ Gotchas. The 1-to-0 index shift is the whole game: first-1 for the start, and the decrement at last (not last-1) because we want it after the inclusive end. The if (last < n) guard is what lets you reuse a size-n array instead of n+1 — when last == n the range runs to the end and there's nothing to turn off. Doing the brute-force double loop is O(n·m) and TLEs at scale.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Naively I'd add seats to every flight in each booking's range — O(n·m), too slow. The key realization is that a range-add only changes things at its two boundaries: I add seats where the range starts and subtract them right after it ends. After all bookings, a single prefix sum reconstructs each flight's total, because the running sum carries exactly the bookings whose start I've passed but whose end I haven't. That's O(n+m) with O(1) extra space."
📄 CorporateFlightBookings.java

2 Car Pooling LC 1094 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. A car with fixed capacity takes trips [passengers, from, to]; return whether it can serve them all without ever exceeding capacity (passengers occupy [from, to)).

💡 Key insight. Each trip adds load on the half-open interval [from, to) — passengers board at from and are gone before stop to "departs". Record only the two boundaries in a difference array (+passengers at from, -passengers at to), then a prefix-sum sweep reconstructs live occupancy at each stop; if it ever exceeds capacity the ride is infeasible. Because stops are bounded 0..1000, a fixed-size array beats sorting events.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate diff[1001] indexed by stop location.
  2. For each trip: diff[from] += passengers and diff[to] -= passengers — note the decrement is at to (not to+1) because the interval is half-open.
  3. Sweep the array accumulating onboard += diff[i]; the moment onboard > capacity, return false. Survive the sweep → true.

⏱️ Time O(n + R) — n trips mark boundaries, R = 1001 stops to sweep 💾 Space O(R) — the difference array over the bounded stop range (effectively O(1))

⚠️ Gotchas. The half-open interval is the trap versus 1109: here -passengers goes at to, not to+1, so that someone alighting at a stop frees the seat exactly when another boards there (the "leaves precisely as the next group boards" test case). Check capacity during the prefix sweep, not just at the end — the constraint is the running max, not the final value. Relying on the 0..1000 bound is fine here; if stops were unbounded you'd switch to sorting boundary events (a sweep line).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I treat each trip as a load that switches on at from and off at to — a half-open interval — so I stamp +passengers and −passengers at those two stops in a difference array. Prefix-summing it gives me the number of people in the car at each stop, and I just check it never exceeds capacity as I sweep. Stops are capped at 1000, so a flat array is O(1) space and the whole thing is linear; if the range were unbounded I'd sort the events into a sweep line instead."
📄 CarPooling.java

🔁 Practice plan

Solve 1109 first — it's the cleanest statement of the idiom and forces you to nail the inclusive-range index shift. Then do 1094 from a blank file and pay attention to how the half-open interval moves the -delta from hi+1 to to; being able to articulate that single difference proves you understand the boundary semantics rather than memorizing. Re-derive the "why prefix sum reconstructs it" argument aloud every time.

  • ✅ I can explain why a range-add only needs two writes plus one prefix-sum sweep.
  • ✅ I can place the -delta correctly for inclusive [lo,hi] (at hi+1) vs half-open [from,to) (at to).
  • ✅ I can state both solutions as O(n + range) time and O(range) — often O(1) — extra space.
  • ✅ I can check a capacity/feasibility constraint during the sweep, not only at the end.
  • ✅ Follow-ups to rehearse: 370 Range Addition (the bare idiom), 1854 Maximum Population Year, and 731/732 My Calendar II/III (when bounds are large → sort events / sweep line instead of a flat diff array).

🪟 Two Pointers & Sliding Window

Sliding window is the technique for "find the best/longest/shortest contiguous run satisfying some constraint" — you maintain a window [left, right] and amortize the work so each element enters and leaves at most once, turning an O(n²) scan into O(n). It is high-ROI for Google L6 because the same expand-right / shrink-left skeleton, parameterized by a constraint check and a maintained aggregate (a frequency array, a count of satisfied characters, or a window sum), solves a whole cluster of string and array problems — and interviewers prize a candidate who can state the loop invariant cleanly.

Source: Prep Tracker tab · 5 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • Asks for the longest / shortest / max / min contiguous subarray or substring meeting a condition.
  • Constraint is monotonic: if a window is valid, any sub-window is too (or vice-versa) — so you can grow/shrink greedily.
  • "At most k …", "exactly k distinct …", "with at most k replacements / operations" — the budget k tells you when to shrink.
  • You can maintain a cheap incremental summary of the window (frequency counts, a running sum, a formed satisfied-count) updated in O(1) per pointer move.
  • Sorting first turns a "make elements equal / close" array problem into a window problem (the rightmost element becomes the target).

🧠 Core idea & template

Keep two pointers bounding a window. Advance right to acquire a new element and update your incremental summary; while the window violates the constraint, advance left to release elements until it's valid again. Because each pointer only moves forward across the whole array, the total work is O(n) even though the window resizes constantly. Record the best window either right after acquiring (for "longest valid") or while the window is valid and you're shrinking (for "shortest valid").

// Canonical variable-size sliding window.
int left = 0, best = 0;          // or best = Integer.MAX_VALUE for "shortest"
// maintain some summary of the window: int[] freq, long sum, int formed, ...
for (int right = 0; right < n; right++) {
    acquire(arr[right]);          // update summary to include the new right element

    while (windowIsInvalid()) {   // constraint broken -> shrink from the left
        release(arr[left]);
        left++;
    }
    best = Math.max(best, right - left + 1);   // longest-valid: record after restoring validity
}
// For "shortest valid": instead, shrink while VALID and record inside that while-loop.
return best;

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Longest Repeating Character Replacement LC 424 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core Window + freq, (len − maxFreq) ≤ kO(n)O(1)
2Longest Substring Without Repeating Characters LC 3 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav Window + last-seen index jumpO(n)O(min(n, σ))
3Frequency of the Most Frequent Element LC 1838 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged Sort + window, lift all to nums[right]O(n log n)O(1)
4Minimum Window Substring LC 76 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav Window + need/window maps + formed counterO(|s| + |t|)O(|s| + |t|)
5Find All Anagrams in a String LC 438 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav Fixed window + freq match counterO(n)O(1)

📘 Deep dive

1 Longest Repeating Character Replacement LC 424 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the longest substring you can turn into all-identical characters by replacing at most k of them.

💡 Key insight. A window is "fixable" with k replacements exactly when (windowLen − countOfMostFrequentChar) <= k — the minority characters are the ones you'd rewrite. Grow right every step; when the window stops being fixable, slide left by one so the window length never decreases. Crucially, maxFreq is never decreased: a stale-but-too-large value only makes the window over-confident by exactly the steps left moved, so the best answer is still captured.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a 26-slot frequency array and track maxFreq = the highest single-char count seen.
  2. On each right: increment that char's frequency and update maxFreq.
  3. If (right − left + 1) − maxFreq > k, decrement the left char and advance left by one (no loop).
  4. Record best = max(best, right − left + 1).

⏱️ Time O(n) — both pointers move forward ≤ n times; freq update is O(1) 💾 Space O(1) — fixed 26-slot array

⚠️ Gotchas. Don't recompute maxFreq by scanning the array each step (that's O(26n) and obscures the trick) and don't bother decreasing it — the window only ever slides, never shrinks, so an over-large maxFreq is self-correcting. Using a single if (not a while) to slide is deliberate: the window size is monotonic non-decreasing.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "For any window, the cheapest way to make it uniform is to keep its most frequent char and replace the rest, costing len − maxFreq. So I expand right, and whenever that cost exceeds k I nudge left by one. I never shrink the window or lower maxFreq, which keeps it O(n) and still finds the longest fixable window."
📄 LongestRepeatingCharacterReplacement.java

2 Longest Substring Without Repeating Characters LC 3 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return the length of the longest substring containing no repeated character.

💡 Key insight. Slide a window keeping a map of char → last index seen. When the current char was already seen inside the window, jump left straight past its previous occurrence rather than crawling one step at a time — that single jump is what keeps the whole scan linear.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each right, read char c.
  2. If c is in the map and its last index is >= left (i.e. inside the window), set left = lastSeen[c] + 1.
  3. Update lastSeen[c] = right.
  4. Record best = max(best, right − left + 1).

⏱️ Time O(n) — right advances once per char; left only ever moves forward 💾 Space O(min(n, σ)) — at most one entry per distinct char (σ = alphabet size)

⚠️ Gotchas. The lastSeen[c] >= left guard is essential — a stale index from before the current window must never pull left backwards. Take the max with left rather than blindly jumping. The empty string returns 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep a window with no duplicates and a map of each char's most recent index. When I hit a repeat that's inside the window, I don't shrink one char at a time — I leap left to just past the prior occurrence. Each pointer only moves forward, so it's O(n) time and O(min(n, alphabet)) space."
📄 LongestSubstringWithoutRepeating.java

3 Frequency of the Most Frequent Element LC 1838 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. You may add 1 to elements at most k times total; maximize the count of any single value you can make appear.

💡 Key insight. Sort, then for each right try to raise every element in window [left, right] up to nums[right] (the window's max). The cost is nums[right] * windowLen − windowSum; while that exceeds the budget k, shrink from the left. The largest valid window size is the answer — and sorting is precisely what makes "raise everyone to the rightmost value" the cheapest possible leveling.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort nums ascending.
  2. Maintain a running windowSum. On each right, add nums[right].
  3. While nums[right] * (right − left + 1) − windowSum > k, subtract nums[left] and advance left.
  4. Record best = max(best, right − left + 1).

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort dominates; the two-pointer sweep is O(n) 💾 Space O(1) extra — just the running sum (ignoring sort)

⚠️ Gotchas. Use long for windowSum and the cost expression — nums[right] * windowLen overflows int easily. The target is always the window's max (nums[right] after sorting), because raising small values up is cheaper than lowering large ones (and you can only add). Start best = 1 since a single element is always achievable.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "After sorting, the cheapest way to make a window all equal is to lift everyone to its largest element. The cost is that max times the window length minus the window's sum. I expand right and shrink left whenever the cost exceeds k, tracking the widest affordable window. Sort is O(n log n), the sweep O(n)."
📄 FrequencyOfMostFrequentElement.java

4 Minimum Window Substring LC 76 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Find the smallest substring of s that contains every character of t (with multiplicity), or "" if none exists.

💡 Key insight. Expand right to acquire characters, tracking how many distinct required chars are fully satisfied with a single formed counter. The moment formed == required, the window is valid, so shrink from the left as far as it stays valid, recording the smallest window each time. The formed == required test is O(1), so validity is never re-scanned.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build need = char→count from t; set required = need.size().
  2. Expand right, incrementing window[c]; when window[c] hits need[c] exactly, formed++.
  3. While formed == required: update the best window if smaller; then drop s[left] — if that pushes window[leftChar] below need[leftChar], formed--; advance left.
  4. Return the best window, or "" if none was found.

⏱️ Time O(|s| + |t|) — each pointer traverses s once; building need is O(|t|) 💾 Space O(|s| + |t|) — two frequency maps bounded by distinct chars

⚠️ Gotchas. Increment formed only when a count hits its target exactly (use ==, not >=) so duplicates beyond the requirement don't over-count. Symmetrically, decrement formed only when removing a char drops it strictly below need. Track (bestStart, bestLen) rather than building substrings inside the loop. Guard the trivial cases: t empty or longer than s returns "". Use .intValue() when comparing boxed Integer counts to avoid identity pitfalls.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I count what t needs, then expand right until every required char is satisfied — tracked by a single formed counter so checking validity is O(1). Once valid I contract from the left to find the tightest window, decrementing formed only when a removal actually breaks a requirement. Each character is visited a constant number of times, so it's linear in |s| + |t|."
📄 MinimumWindowSubstring.java

5 Find All Anagrams in a String LC 438 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return every start index in s where a window of length |p| is a permutation (anagram) of p.

💡 Key insight. The window is fixed-size (|p|), so each slide is exactly one character entering and one leaving. Rather than re-comparing all 26 frequency buckets every step, maintain matches = the number of letters whose window count exactly equals the need. Each single-bucket update only ever crosses or lands on equality, so you adjust matches by ±1; matches == 26 means the window is an anagram.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build need[26] from p; pre-seed matches by counting letters absent from p (count 0 == need 0).
  2. For each i, add s[i]: if window[in] == need[in] before the bump, matches--; increment; if now equal, matches++.
  3. If i - m >= 0, remove the char that fell out at index i-m with the symmetric ±1 update.
  4. When matches == 26, record start index i - m + 1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — n window steps, each O(1) work (no 26-bucket rescan) 💾 Space O(1) — two fixed 26-element frequency arrays

⚠️ Gotchas. Compare against need with == both before and after each count change so the ±1 bookkeeping is exact — adjusting only one side drifts matches. Pre-seeding the letters absent from p (where 0 already equals 0) is essential, or matches can never reach 26. If |p| > |s| there are no windows — return empty early.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because the window length is fixed at |p|, every step swaps one char in and one out. I keep a single counter of how many of the 26 letters currently match their required frequency, nudging it by ±1 as each affected bucket crosses equality, instead of re-scanning all 26. When all 26 match, the window is an anagram. That's O(1) per slide, O(n) overall."
📄 FindAllAnagramsInAString.java

🔁 Practice plan

Internalize the variable-window skeleton first, then notice how each problem only swaps the maintained summary and the shrink condition. Order: re-derive Longest Substring Without Repeating blind (the cleanest "longest valid" template with the last-seen-index jump), then Longest Repeating Character Replacement (the subtle never-shrink-maxFreq argument), then Frequency of the Most Frequent Element (sort-to-unlock-a-window plus the long overflow), and finally the boss, Minimum Window Substring (the need/window/formed machinery and "shrink while valid" recording). Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Longest Substring with At Most K Distinct Characters (LC 340), Permutation in String / Find All Anagrams (fixed-size window variant, LC 567 / 438), and Minimum Size Subarray Sum (LC 209) as the shortest-valid counterpart.

  • ✅ I can write the expand-right / shrink-left skeleton from scratch and say its O(n) amortization argument.
  • ✅ I can articulate why maxFreq never needs to decrease in LC 424 without hand-waving.
  • ✅ I can explain why sorting turns LC 1838 into a window problem and where the int overflow hides.
  • ✅ I can implement the formed == required validity counter in Minimum Window Substring and justify the == vs >= choice.
  • ✅ I know when to record the best window — after acquiring (longest-valid) vs. while shrinking (shortest-valid).

🔤 Strings

String problems are the connective tissue of a Google interview: they hide hash-map counting, stack parsing, two-pointer expansion, greedy packing, and clever simulation behind innocent-looking text. The signature L6 twist is rarely the algorithm itself but the framing — index bookkeeping against the original string, carrying state across repeated calls, or memoizing a row shape — where a clean, edge-case-proof implementation separates a hire from a no-hire.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 22 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Are these anagrams / group by anagram / permutation of each other" → count letters into a fixed 26-slot vector and compare or key on it.
  • Nested brackets, balanced delimiters, or "expand k[...]" → push context onto a stack and reconcile on the closer.
  • "Longest palindromic / symmetric substring" → expand outward from each of the 2n-1 centers.
  • "Rearrange so no two adjacent are equal" / "most frequent first" → greedy with a max-heap by frequency; feasibility bound (n+1)/2.
  • Replacements or matches that reference the original string and are non-overlapping → sort by source index, stitch left-to-right with one cursor.
  • "Count how many words are subsequences of s" → invert the loop; scan s once and bucket words by the char they wait for.
  • "Fit text into rows/columns" or "justify a line" → greedy packing, then distribute leftover space (last/short line is special).
  • A provided chunked API (read4) called repeatedly → keep an internal carry buffer for the unconsumed tail.

🧠 Core idea & template

Most string interview tasks collapse to one of a few reusable idioms. The two highest-frequency are the fixed-alphabet count vector (anagram fingerprinting) and the stack-of-context (parsing nested or balanced structure). Internalize these two skeletons and a large fraction of the chapter writes itself; the harder problems then layer simulation or greedy distribution on top.

// Idiom 1 — count vector as a canonical anagram key (O(word) per word)
int[] count = new int[26];
for (char c : word.toCharArray()) count[c - 'a']++;
StringBuilder key = new StringBuilder();
for (int c : count) key.append(c).append('#');   // '#' delimits so 1,11 != 11,1
// groups.computeIfAbsent(key.toString(), k -> new ArrayList<>()).add(word);

// Idiom 2 — stack of context for nested / balanced parsing
Deque<Character> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
    if (isOpener(c)) stack.push(expectedCloser(c)); // push what you must see next
    else if (stack.isEmpty() || stack.pop() != c) return false; // LIFO match
}
return stack.isEmpty();

// Idiom 3 — expand around center (palindromes)
int len = Math.max(expand(s, i, i), expand(s, i, i + 1)); // odd + even centers
