📘 Deep dive

1 Valid Anagram LC 242 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given two strings, decide whether t is a rearrangement of s (identical letter multisets).

💡 Key insight. Anagrams have identical character multisets. Tally s with +1 and t with -1 in a single 26-slot array; if every slot returns to zero, the multisets matched.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Length mismatch is an instant false.
  2. In one pass over both strings, do count[s[i]-'a']++ and count[t[i]-'a']--.
  3. If any slot is non-zero at the end, the letters didn't cancel — return false; otherwise true.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass over each string 💾 Space O(1) — fixed 26-element counter (lowercase only)

⚠️ Gotchas. Empty vs empty is a valid anagram. The 26-slot trick assumes lowercase ASCII — for full Unicode use a HashMap<Character,Integer> instead. Forgetting the length check lets unequal lengths slip through if you only test "every slot non-negative".
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force would sort both strings and compare in O(n log n). But the alphabet is fixed, so I can just count: increment for s, decrement for t in one shared array. If every count nets to zero the strings are anagrams — O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 ValidAnagram.java

2 Valid Parentheses LC 20 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a string of ()[]{}, decide whether every bracket is closed by the correct type in the correct order.

💡 Key insight. On every opener, push the closer we expect to see next; on every closer, it must match the most recent expectation (LIFO). Pushing the expected closer (rather than the opener) makes the match a single equality check.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For (/[/{, push )/]/} respectively.
  2. For a closer, if the stack is empty or its top ≠ this char, return false.
  3. After the scan, the stack must be empty — leftover openers mean it's unbalanced.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each char pushed/popped at most once 💾 Space O(n) — stack holds up to n/2 pending closers

⚠️ Gotchas. A closer with an empty stack ("nothing to close") is the easy-to-miss case, e.g. ")(". The end-of-string emptiness check catches the opposite, unclosed openers like "(". Use ArrayDeque, not the legacy synchronized Stack.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brackets nest, so the last opener must close first — that's a stack. I push the closer I expect, and each closing char just has to equal the top. Empty stack on a closer, or a non-empty stack at the end, both mean invalid. Linear time, linear space."
📄 ValidParentheses.java

3 Longest Common Prefix LC 14 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Find the longest string that is a prefix of every string in the array (empty if none).

💡 Key insight. Vertical scanning: compare character column 0 across all strings, then column 1, and so on. The moment a string is too short or a character disagrees, the prefix is exactly what we've matched so far. The first string bounds the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Guard the null/empty array → return "".
  2. Walk col over strs[0]; for each column, check every other string at that index.
  3. If a string ends at col or its char differs, return strs[0].substring(0, col).
  4. If we exhaust the first string, it is itself the common prefix.

⏱️ Time O(S) — S = total characters, worst case we touch every one 💾 Space O(1) — only indices; prefix sliced from the first string

⚠️ Gotchas. The bounds check col == strs[row].length() must come before charAt(col) or you'll throw on a shorter string. A single-element array returns that element. Disjoint first chars (e.g. "dog","car") correctly yield "" at column 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I scan column by column — the answer can't be longer than the shortest string, and the first string is a fine reference. As soon as any string disagrees or runs out at a column, I stop and slice. That's O(total characters) and constant extra space."
📄 LongestCommonPrefix.java

4 Roman to Integer LC 13 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Convert a Roman numeral string to its integer value, honoring subtractive forms like IV, IX, CM.

💡 Key insight. Add each numeral's value, EXCEPT when a smaller numeral immediately precedes a larger one — that pairing means subtraction. Scanning right-to-left, each numeral simply compares to its already-processed right neighbour: smaller ⇒ subtract, otherwise add.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Map each char to its value via a switch.
  2. Iterate from the last char to the first, tracking prev = the value just to the right.
  3. If val < prev, subtract it; else add it. Update prev = val.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass over the string 💾 Space O(1) — fixed lookup, a couple of scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. Right-to-left avoids look-ahead; a left-to-right version must peek at i+1. Only six subtractive pairs are legal, but the comparison rule handles them uniformly without enumerating them. Equal-adjacent (e.g. III) correctly adds, since val is not strictly less than prev.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Roman numerals are additive except for the subtractive pairs. If I walk from the right, each symbol knows what's to its right, so I just check: is this symbol smaller than the one I just saw? If so it subtracts, otherwise it adds. One pass, constant space."
📄 RomanToInteger.java

5 Reverse Integer LC 7 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Reverse the digits of a signed 32-bit integer; return 0 if the reversed value overflows the int range.

💡 Key insight. Pop the last digit with x % 10 and push it onto the result via result*10 + digit. The trap is overflow: check BEFORE multiplying whether result has already passed Integer.MAX_VALUE/10 (or the boundary-digit case), so you never actually overflow a 32-bit int.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Loop while x != 0: take digit = x % 10 (sign-preserving in Java), then x /= 10.
  2. Before appending, test the positive overflow boundary (> MAX/10, or == MAX/10 and digit > 7) → return 0.
  3. Symmetric test for the negative boundary (< MIN/10, or == MIN/10 and digit < -8) → return 0.
  4. Otherwise result = result*10 + digit.

⏱️ Time O(log|x|) — one iteration per decimal digit 💾 Space O(1) — a single accumulator

⚠️ Gotchas. Java's % keeps the operand's sign (-123 % 10 == -3), so negatives need no special casing. The boundary digits 7 and -8 come from the last digit of 2147483647 / -2147483648. Don't reverse into a long and cast — the problem assumes a strict 32-bit environment.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I peel digits off the right and rebuild left-to-right. The only subtlety is 32-bit overflow: I check the bound before I multiply, comparing against MAX/10 and the special last-digit case, so the int never actually wraps. Digit-count time, constant space."
📄 ReverseInteger.java

6 Zigzag Conversion LC 6 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Write the string in a zigzag across numRows rows, then read it off row by row.

💡 Key insight. Simulate writing the string row by row. A direction toggles: move down the rows until you hit the bottom, then bounce up to the top, and repeat. Concatenating the per-row buffers top-to-bottom yields the zigzag reading order — no index/geometry formula needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Early return s when numRows == 1 or numRows ≥ s.length() (no zigzag).
  2. Keep a row index and step (+1 down, -1 up); append each char to rows[row].
  3. Flip step to +1 at the top row, -1 at the bottom row, then row += step.
  4. Concatenate all row buffers into the result.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each char placed once, then concatenated once 💾 Space O(n) — the per-row buffers hold every character

⚠️ Gotchas. The numRows == 1 guard prevents a divide/bounce degenerate case where you'd never toggle. Flip direction after appending, checking edges before the move. The formula-based solution is faster to write incorrectly — the simulation is the safe interview answer.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rather than derive the index arithmetic, I literally simulate laying letters into rows, bouncing direction at the top and bottom edges. Then I read the rows out in order. It's linear time and space, and far less error-prone than the closed-form indexing."
📄 ZigzagConversion.java

7 Group Anagrams LC 49 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Group the input words so that all anagrams of each other land in the same list.

💡 Key insight. Words are anagrams iff they share the same letter-count signature, so build a canonical key from each word's 26 counts and bucket by it. Using the count vector (not a sorted string) keys in O(word) instead of O(word log word) — optimal when the alphabet is fixed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each word, count its 26 letters.
  2. Encode counts into a stable string key, appending '#' after each count to avoid collisions like "1,11" vs "11,1".
  3. computeIfAbsent(key, …).add(word) to grow the bucket.
  4. Return the map's values.

⏱️ Time O(n·k) — n words, k = max length, to build each key 💾 Space O(n·k) — keys plus the grouped words

⚠️ Gotchas. The '#' delimiter is essential — without it, counts run together and collide. Sorting each word (O(k log k) key) also works and is shorter to write, but the count-vector key is asymptotically better and worth stating. [""] groups to [[""]], not empty output.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Anagrams share a letter histogram, so I fingerprint each word by its 26-count vector and use that as a hash-map key. I could sort each word instead, but counting is O(k) versus O(k log k). The delimiter in the key stops multi-digit counts from colliding."
📄 GroupAnagrams.java

8 Encode and Decode Strings LC 271 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Serialize a list of arbitrary strings to one string and deserialize it back exactly, with no assumptions about the content.

💡 Key insight. Length-prefix framing: emit each string as "<len>#<payload>". The decoder reads digits up to the '#', learns exactly how many chars follow, and slices that many verbatim — so any delimiter inside the payload (even '#' itself, or an empty string) is irrelevant.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Encode: for each string append length + "#" + string.
  2. Decode: from cursor i, find the next '#'; parse the length header before it.
  3. Take exactly len chars starting after the '#' (content-agnostic), add to the result.
  4. Advance the cursor past that slice and repeat.

⏱️ Time O(N) — encode and decode each touch every character once 💾 Space O(N) — the encoded string / decoded list

⚠️ Gotchas. A naive single-char separator (e.g. comma) breaks the moment a payload contains it — the length prefix is precisely what makes it robust. Empty strings encode as "0#" and round-trip cleanly. The header delimiter must be the first '#' at or after the cursor, found via indexOf('#', i).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The hard part is content that contains your delimiter. I solve that with length prefixing: each string becomes its length, a marker, then the bytes. On decode I read the count, then blindly copy that many characters, so the payload can be anything. Linear in total length."
📄 EncodeAndDecodeStrings.java

9 Longest Palindromic Substring LC 5 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return a longest contiguous substring of s that reads the same forwards and backwards.

💡 Key insight. Every palindrome has a center, and there are 2n-1 of them (n single chars for odd lengths, n-1 gaps for even). Expand outward from each center while the two ends match, keeping the longest span. No DP table needed — O(1) space.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each index i, expand an odd center (i, i) and an even center (i, i+1).
  2. expand grows left--/right++ while in-bounds and chars match, returning right - left - 1.
  3. If the larger of the two beats the best, recover bounds: start = i - (len-1)/2, end = i + len/2.
  4. Slice s[start..end].

⏱️ Time O(n²) — 2n-1 centers, each expansion O(n) worst case 💾 Space O(1) — only index bookkeeping

⚠️ Gotchas. Must try both odd and even centers — even-length palindromes ("bb") have no single-char center. The bound recovery (len-1)/2 vs len/2 is what makes one formula serve both parities. Manacher's algorithm gets O(n) but is overkill for an interview unless explicitly asked.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A palindrome is symmetric about a center, so I try all 2n-1 centers — single chars and gaps — and expand outward while the ends match. I track the longest. That's O(n²) time but O(1) space, and I'd mention Manacher's O(n) as the optimal if pressed."
📄 LongestPalindromicSubstring.java

10 Decode String LC 394 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Decode strings like 3[a2[c]] where k[...] means the bracketed substring repeated k times, supporting nesting.

💡 Key insight. Two stacks track the nested k[...] context. On '[' push the repeat count and the string built so far, then reset the builder; on ']' pop both, repeat the just-finished inner string k times, and splice it back onto the saved prefix.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Digits accumulate into count via count*10 + (c-'0') (multi-digit safe).
  2. On '[': push count and the current builder, then reset both.
  3. On ']': pop k and the saved prefix, append current.repeat(k) onto the prefix, make that the new current.
  4. Letters extend current. Return current at the end.

⏱️ Time O(output) — each decoded char emitted once 💾 Space O(output) — stacks plus the result buffer

⚠️ Gotchas. Multi-digit counts (10[a]) require accumulating the number, not reading a single digit. You need two stacks — one for counts, one for the prefix string — kept in lockstep. Resetting count and current on '[' is what isolates the inner segment.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Nesting screams stack. When I hit an opening bracket I stash the multiplier and whatever I've built so far, then start fresh inside. On the closing bracket I expand the inner string by its multiplier and glue it back onto the stashed prefix. Output-linear time."
📄 DecodeString.java

11 Reorganize String LC 767 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Rearrange s so no two adjacent characters are equal, or return "" if impossible.

💡 Key insight. Greedy with a max-heap by frequency: repeatedly pull the two most frequent remaining characters and append both — they differ, so no two equal chars land adjacent. It's feasible iff no character exceeds (n+1)/2; otherwise some copy is forced to touch itself.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Count frequencies; if any exceeds (n+1)/2, return "" immediately.
  2. Push all positive counts into a max-heap keyed by frequency.
  3. While ≥ 2 entries remain, poll the top two, append both chars, decrement, and re-offer any still positive.
  4. If one entry remains (guaranteed count 1 by feasibility), append it.

⏱️ Time O(n log k) — n placements, heap ops over k ≤ 26 distinct chars 💾 Space O(k) — counts plus the heap

⚠️ Gotchas. Polling two-at-a-time is what guarantees adjacency is broken; placing one and "holding the previous" is the equivalent alternative. The feasibility bound is (n+1)/2, not n/2 — odd lengths allow one extra. Decrement and re-offer only if the count stays positive, or you'll push zeros.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I always want to place the most frequent leftover char, but not twice in a row — so I pop the top two from a frequency max-heap and place both, since they're distinct. If the heaviest char exceeds (n+1)/2 it's impossible. That's O(n log 26), effectively O(n)."
📄 ReorganizeString.java

12 Find And Replace in String LC 833 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Apply a batch of replacements — each "at index i, if source matches, swap to target" — where all indices refer to the original string and matches are non-overlapping.

💡 Key insight. All replacement indices refer to the ORIGINAL string and matches are non-overlapping, so sort operations by their source index and stitch the answer left-to-right: copy the untouched gap up to the next index, then if the source actually matches emit the target and jump past it, else emit the original. One forward cursor walks both string and op list.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build an index permutation and sort it by indices[idx] ascending.
  2. Keep a cursor = next untouched position in s.
  3. For each op: append the gap s[cursor..at]; if s.startsWith(src, at), append the target and set cursor = at + src.length(); otherwise leave cursor = at.
  4. Append the trailing remainder after the last op.

⏱️ Time O(m log m + n + Σtarget) — m = #ops, n = |s| 💾 Space O(m) — index permutation plus the output builder

⚠️ Gotchas. Why sort? Applying ops in input order would shift later indices; anchoring to the original string and processing left-to-right avoids index drift entirely. startsWith(src, at) matches against the original s, never the partial output. On a non-match, set cursor = at (not at+1) so the next gap/tail copies s[at].
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The catch is that all indices point at the original string, so I must not let earlier replacements shift later ones. I sort the ops by index and rebuild the string with a single cursor: copy the gap, test the source against the original, then either swap-and-skip or fall through. Sorting dominates at O(m log m)."
📄 FindAndReplaceInString.java

13 Number of Matching Subsequences LC 792 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given s and a list of words, count how many words are subsequences of s.

💡 Key insight. Instead of scanning s once per word (O(|words|·|s|)), scan s ONCE and let its characters pull every word forward together. Bucket each word by the char it currently waits for; as you read c from s, every word parked in bucket[c] has its next char satisfied — advance it and re-file under its new waiting char, or count it matched if it ran out.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Create 26 buckets of (wordIndex, matchedSoFar); park each word in the bucket of its first char.
  2. For each char of s, snapshot bucket[c]'s size and drain exactly that many (only words waiting before this char).
  3. For each drained word, advance its pointer; if it reaches the word's length, increment matched, else re-file under the next char's bucket.
  4. Return matched.

⏱️ Time O(|s| + Σ|word|) — each char of each word causes at most one re-bucket 💾 Space O(Σ|word|) — pointers held across the 26 buckets

⚠️ Gotchas. Snapshot the bucket size before the inner loop — otherwise a re-filed word could be re-processed within the same character of s (an infinite/over-count bug on repeated letters). The naive per-word scan is the brute force to contrast against. Duplicate words are counted independently, which is correct.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force checks each word against s independently — quadratic. I invert it: I park every word on the character it needs next, then sweep s once. Each character wakes the words waiting on it and pushes them forward. Total work is |s| plus the total length of all words."
📄 NumberOfMatchingSubsequences.java

14 Sentence Screen Fitting LC 418 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a sentence (array of words) and a rows × cols screen, count how many times the whole sentence fits, words kept intact and separated by single spaces.

💡 Key insight. Lay the sentence out as one cyclic stream "word1 word2 … wordk " (note the trailing space). For a row of width cols, fitting is just: advance a cursor by cols, then if the landing char is a space skip it, else back up to the previous space. Sentences shown = cursor / streamLength. Memoize per starting word so repeated row shapes are O(1).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Precompute, for each possible starting word i: how many full sentences a single row completes and which word the next row starts on.
  2. Greedily pack words while used + len(word) ≤ cols, adding len + 1 per placed word; wrap past the last word increments the sentence count.
  3. Store nextStart[i] and sentencesAdded[i].
  4. Walk rows rows, summing sentencesAdded[word] and jumping via nextStart[word] — O(1) per row.

⏱️ Time O(rows + k·L) — each start word built once, k = #words, L = max word length 💾 Space O(k) — two memo arrays keyed by starting word index

⚠️ Gotchas. Any word longer than cols means zero fits — the packing loop naturally completes nothing. The trailing space in the conceptual stream is what makes the word-end accounting clean. Without the per-start memo, the row simulation is O(rows · cols), which TLEs on large screens — the cache caps distinct starts at k.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A row is fully determined by which word it starts on, so there are at most k distinct row shapes. I precompute, for each start word, how many sentences that row finishes and where the next row begins, then just hop row to row. That turns an O(rows·cols) simulation into O(rows + k·L)."
📄 SentenceScreenFitting.java

15 Read N Characters Given Read4 II LC 158 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Implement read(buf, n) using only a read4 primitive (reads up to 4 chars), where read may be called multiple times on the same stream.

💡 Key insight. read4 only hands back chars in chunks of 4, so a single read(n) may grab more than it needs and must REMEMBER the unconsumed tail for the next call — that internal carry buffer is the whole trick of the "call multiple times" variant.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep instance state: a 4-char buf4, its valid size, and a read position.
  2. In read, loop until n chars are written or the source is drained.
  3. If buf4 is empty, refill via read4; a result of 0 means EOF → break.
  4. Copy from buf4 into buf only as many as still needed; leftover chars stay parked for the next read.

⏱️ Time O(n) — amortized; each source char is moved into buf once 💾 Space O(1) — a fixed 4-char carry buffer regardless of n

⚠️ Gotchas. The distinguishing feature vs LC 157 (single call) is persisting buf4 state between calls — a local buffer fails the multi-call tests. A read4 returning fewer than 4 signals EOF; don't loop forever expecting a full chunk. The two inner conditions (written < n AND buf4Pos < buf4Size) jointly stop you from over-copying or over-reading.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because read4 only comes in groups of four, a read of, say, one character pulls four and must keep the other three. So I hold a 4-char buffer as instance state across calls: each read first drains leftovers, then fetches fresh chunks until satisfied or EOF. Amortized linear, constant extra space."
📄 ReadNCharactersGivenRead4II.java

16 Text Justification LC 68 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Format words into lines of exactly maxWidth, fully justified (extra spaces spread evenly, leftmost gaps favored); the last line and any single-word line are left-justified.

💡 Key insight. Greedy packing: fit as many words on a line as possible (each gap needs ≥ 1 space). Once a line is fixed, distribute the surplus spaces as evenly as possible — when they don't divide evenly, the leftmost gaps get one extra. Single-word lines and the last line are left-justified instead.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Greedily extend a run [i, j) while lettersLen + words[j].length() + (j-i) ≤ maxWidth (the j-i term reserves one space per gap).
  2. If it's the last line or a single word, join with single spaces and right-pad to maxWidth.
  3. Else compute base = totalSpaces / gaps and extra = totalSpaces % gaps; give the first extra gaps one more space.
  4. Emit the line, set i = j, repeat.

⏱️ Time O(n) — every character is emitted once across all lines 💾 Space O(n) — the output strings (O(maxWidth) scratch aside)

⚠️ Gotchas. The packing test lettersLen + len + (j-i) ≤ maxWidth accounts for the minimum one-space gaps; off-by-one here is the classic bug. Two special cases break the even-distribution rule: the final line and any line with a single word (zero gaps) are left-justified and right-padded. The extra leftover spaces go to the leftmost gaps, not the rightmost.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I greedily pack each line, reserving a minimum space per gap. For a full line I split the leftover spaces across the gaps, handing the remainder to the leftmost ones. The last line and any single-word line are left-justified and padded right. It's a careful simulation — linear time, the difficulty is purely in the edge cases."
📄 TextJustification.java

17 Ransom Note LC 383 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Decide whether ransomNote can be built from the letters of magazine, each magazine letter usable at most once.

💡 Key insight. Order is irrelevant — only counts matter. Tally the magazine's 26 lowercase letters once, then "spend" them down as you read the note; the instant any letter's remaining supply dips below zero, the note out-demands the magazine and you can stop early.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Count every letter of magazine into a 26-slot array.
  2. Walk ransomNote, doing --freq[c-'a'] for each char.
  3. If any decrement drops below 0, return false immediately.
  4. Survive the whole note ⇒ return true.

⏱️ Time O(m+n) — one pass to count the magazine, one to spend on the note 💾 Space O(1) — fixed 26-slot frequency array

⚠️ Gotchas. The supply is finite — two 'a's in the note need two in the magazine, so you must decrement, not just test membership. An empty note is trivially buildable (needs nothing). The 26-slot trick assumes lowercase only; full Unicode would need a HashMap.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "This is just a multiset containment check: the magazine must supply at least as many of each letter as the note demands. I count the magazine once, then debit each note letter from its bucket — the first time a bucket goes negative I know it's impossible. Linear time, constant space."
📄 RansomNote.java

18 Longest Palindrome LC 409 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a string, return the length of the longest palindrome that can be built using its letters (case-sensitive).

💡 Key insight. This BUILDS a length from character counts — it does not search for a substring like Longest Palindromic Substring (LC 5). A palindrome mirrors around its center, so each letter must contribute in even pairs: claim (count/2)*2 of every letter for the two halves, then if any letter had a leftover odd one, a single odd char may sit in the very middle for a +1.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Count every character into a 128-slot ASCII table (case-sensitive: 'A' ≠ 'a').
  2. For each count, add (c/2)*2 to the running length (all complete pairs).
  3. If any count is odd, set a hasOdd flag.
  4. Return length + 1 if hasOdd, else length.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass to count, fixed-size sweep to total 💾 Space O(1) — 128-slot ASCII frequency table

⚠️ Gotchas. Only one odd-count letter can occupy the center — adding +1 per odd letter is the classic bug; it's a single +1 total. Case sensitivity matters: "Aa" yields 1, not 2 (no pair). Don't confuse with LC 5 — here you never build or check an actual palindrome string, just total a length.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A palindrome is symmetric, so every letter except the center must appear an even number of times. I count letters, take all the pairs, and if at least one letter has a spare odd copy I add one for the middle slot. That's O(n) counting — and it's a length-building problem, distinct from finding a palindromic substring."
📄 LongestPalindrome.java

19 Long Pressed Name LC 925 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Decide whether typed could be name with some keys long-pressed — i.e. some characters accidentally repeated.

💡 Key insight. A long press only ever DUPLICATES a character that was genuinely typed, so every "extra" char in typed must equal the char immediately before it. Walk both strings: on a match consume both; on a mismatch the only legal explanation is that typed[j] repeats the previous typed char (a held key) — skip j alone — otherwise it's a real typo.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Two pointers: i over name, j over typed.
  2. If name[i] == typed[j], advance both (a real keystroke matched).
  3. Else if j > 0 and typed[j] == typed[j-1], advance j only (a long-press repeat).
  4. Else return false. At the end, accept iff all of name was consumed (i == name.length()).

⏱️ Time O(|typed|) — j advances every step and never rewinds 💾 Space O(1) — two indices only

⚠️ Gotchas. The final check is i == name.length(), not j == typed.length() — trailing long-press repeats in typed are fine, but every letter of name must be covered (e.g. "saeed"/"ssaaedd" fails because the second 'e' is never matched). The j > 0 guard avoids reading typed[-1]. A repeat is only legal if it matches the previous char, not any earlier one.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I sync two pointers. When the characters agree I consume from both names. When they differ, the only way typed can be legitimate is if this char is a repeat of the one just before it — a stuck key — so I skip it in typed alone; anything else is a typo. Finally I confirm I actually consumed all of name. Linear, constant space."
📄 LongPressedName.java

20 String to Integer (atoi) LC 8 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Parse a leading optional-signed integer from a string (skipping leading spaces, stopping at the first non-digit), clamped to the signed 32-bit range.

💡 Key insight. The parse itself is trivial — skip spaces, read one optional sign, fold digits as result*10 + d. The real problem is never letting that fold overflow a 32-bit int. Before each multiply-add, test whether result has already reached Integer.MAX_VALUE/10: if strictly larger, or equal with the incoming digit past the boundary (7 positive, 8 negative), the next step WOULD overflow — so clamp immediately and stay purely in int.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Skip leading spaces; read at most one '+'/'-' sign.
  2. While the next char is a digit, compute digit.
  3. Pre-emptively test overflow: result > MAX/10, or result == MAX/10 && digit > (sign==1 ? 7 : 8) → return the clamped limit.
  4. Else result = result*10 + digit; finally return sign * result.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single left-to-right scan, stopping at the first non-digit 💾 Space O(1) — a sign flag and one int accumulator

⚠️ Gotchas. Check overflow before the multiply-add, not after — relying on a long or post-hoc parseInt trick defeats the point of the strict-32-bit framing. The boundary digits 7 and 8 come from the last digit of 2147483647 / 2147483648. Only the first sign counts; whitespace is leading-only — a space after digits stops the scan. "words and 987" returns 0 because the first non-space is non-digit.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Stripping spaces, the sign, and folding digits is the easy part; the whole problem is overflow. I check the bound before each multiply — if result is already at MAX/10 and the next digit pushes past the limit's last digit, I clamp to MIN or MAX right then. That keeps me in pure 32-bit int with no long-casting. Linear time, constant space."
📄 StringToInteger.java

21 Expressive Words LC 809 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given a target s and a list of words, count how many words become s by stretching some groups of equal letters to length ≥ 3.

💡 Key insight. Stretching only ever lengthens a run of equal letters, and only to size ≥ 3. So compare s and each word run-by-run: for matching letters, a word group of length w can produce an s group of length sLen ONLY when w == sLen (untouched) or sLen ≥ 3 && sLen ≥ w (extended past the size-3 threshold). A group with sLen < 3 can't have been stretched, so it must match w exactly.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each word, run two pointers over s and the word in lockstep.
  2. At each position the current letters must agree, else reject.
  3. Measure both run lengths sLen and wLen for that letter; accept the group if sLen == wLen, or sLen ≥ 3 && sLen ≥ wLen; else reject.
  4. Both strings must end together — leftover groups on either side mean a mismatch.

⏱️ Time O(|s|·|words|) — each word is scanned once alongside s 💾 Space O(1) — only run-length pointers, no extra structures

⚠️ Gotchas. You can't shrink: a target run shorter than the word's run is always a reject ("aaa" from "aaaa" fails). A target run that stays under 3 must equal the word's run exactly — equal letters with mismatched short runs (single l vs ll) fail. Forgetting the "both fully consumed" end check accepts strings that differ in a trailing group.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I walk s and the word group by group. The letters of each group must match, and then I compare run lengths: either they're already equal, or the target run was stretched to at least 3 and is no shorter than the word's. A target run below 3 has to match exactly, and I can never shrink. Both must finish together. It's O(s) per word."
📄 ExpressiveWords.java

22 Minimum Window Subsequence LC 727 ↗ Hard 📌 Reported L6

Problem. Find the shortest substring of s that contains t as a SUBSEQUENCE (order-preserving, gaps allowed); ties go to the earliest start.

💡 Key insight. t must appear as a SUBSEQUENCE — order-preserving and non-contiguous — which is fundamentally different from the substring/anagram window of Minimum Window Substring (LC 76). The trick is two-phase per candidate: scan FORWARD through s matching t's chars in order; the moment t finishes you have a window ending at i but with a loose front, so immediately walk BACKWARD re-matching t in reverse to snap the start to the latest possible position — the tightest window ending at i.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Forward scan: advance i over s, advancing j over t on matches; when j == m, the window ends at i.
  2. Shrink-back: from that end, walk i leftward re-matching t from its last char to its first; where j falls below 0, start is the rightmost valid start.
  3. Record [start, end] if strictly shorter (earliest start wins ties).
  4. Resume the forward scan from start + 1 to find the next window; stop when s is exhausted before t completes.

⏱️ Time O(|s|·|t|) — each successful match triggers an O(|t|) back-walk 💾 Space O(1) — a handful of indices only

⚠️ Gotchas. Contrast sharply with LC 76: this is a subsequence (gaps OK, order fixed), so a frequency-count sliding window does NOT apply. Restart the forward scan from start + 1 (not end + 1), or you'll skip overlapping windows that begin inside the current one. Use strict < on length so ties keep the earliest start. No window found ⇒ return "".
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because t is a subsequence, not a contiguous substring, the sliding-window-with-counts approach for LC 76 doesn't work. I scan forward until I've matched all of t, which fixes the window's end; then I walk backward re-matching t in reverse to pull the start as far right as possible — the tightest window for that end. I keep the shortest and restart just past each start. That's O(|s|·|t|), constant space."
📄 MinimumWindowSubsequence.java

Save the 🟦 signature quartet (833, 792, 418, 158) and the two hard favorites (767, 68) for focused sessions — each has one non-obvious trick (cursor-against-original, invert-the-loop, per-start memo, carry buffer, frequency heap, space distribution) that interviewers probe.   • ✅ I can state when a stack is the right tool (balanced brackets, nested k[...]) and push the expected closer for a one-line match.

🧬 String Matching — KMP & Rabin-Karp

This family turns naïve O(n·m) substring search and "find a repeat" problems into linear or near-linear algorithms using two ideas: KMP's failure (prefix) function, which reuses already-matched prefixes to never re-compare a character, and Rabin-Karp's rolling hash, which scores a sliding window in O(1) per step. It is high-ROI for Google L6 because the rolling-hash + binary-search combo and the KMP "prefix == suffix" trick unlock a whole cluster of otherwise-intimidating Hard string problems.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Find a duplicated / repeated substring" — fixed length (fixed-width rolling hash) or longest (binary search + Rabin-Karp).
  • "Longest prefix that is also a suffix", "shortest characters to prepend/append to make a palindrome", or any palindrome-by-extension question → KMP failure function on a cleverly concatenated string.
  • A small, fixed alphabet (e.g. 4 DNA bases) lets you pack a window into an integer for a collision-free hash.
  • You need substring equality checks fast and many times → precompute prefix hashes, compare windows in O(1).
  • Monotonic feasibility ("if length L repeats, so does L−1") screams binary-search-on-the-answer wrapping a linear check.

🧠 Core idea & template

KMP failure function (prefix function). For string t, lps[i] is the length of the longest proper prefix of t[0..i] that is also a suffix of it. On a mismatch you "fall back" to lps[len−1] instead of restarting, so every character is examined a constant number of times — O(n). The killer trick: to find the longest prefix of s that is also a suffix of some other string r, build s + '#' + r (a separator that can't be matched across) and read the last lps value.

Rabin-Karp rolling hash. Treat a window as a base-B number mod a large prime. Sliding right is O(1): multiply, add the new char, subtract the outgoing char's contribution (or, with prefix hashes, h[to] − h[from]·B^(to−from)). Hash collisions are possible, so verify a candidate by comparing the actual characters (or use a 2⁶¹−1 Mersenne modulus / double hashing to make collisions negligible). Combine with binary search when the answer length is monotonic.

// ---- KMP failure (prefix) function: O(n) ----
int[] prefixFunction(String t) {
    int[] lps = new int[t.length()];
    int len = 0;                                  // length of current matched prefix
    for (int i = 1; i < t.length(); i++) {
        while (len > 0 && t.charAt(i) != t.charAt(len)) len = lps[len - 1]; // fall back
        if (t.charAt(i) == t.charAt(len)) len++;
        lps[i] = len;
    }
    return lps;
}
// longest prefix of s that is also a suffix of r:  build s + '#' + r, take lps[last]

// ---- Rabin-Karp with prefix hashes + O(1) window hash ----
long MOD = (1L << 61) - 1, BASE = 131;
long[] h = new long[n + 1], pow = new long[n + 1];   // h[i] hashes s[0..i)
pow[0] = 1;
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
    h[i + 1] = (mulmod(h[i], BASE) + s.charAt(i)) % MOD;
    pow[i + 1] = mulmod(pow[i], BASE);
}
long windowHash(int from, int to) {                  // hash of s[from..to)
    long res = (h[to] - mulmod(h[from], pow[to - from])) % MOD;
    return res < 0 ? res + MOD : res;
}
// On a hash hit, ALWAYS verify with s.regionMatches(...) before trusting it.

🗺️ Problems at a glance

📘 Deep dive

1 Repeated DNA Sequences LC 187 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return every 10-letter-long substring of a DNA string (alphabet A/C/G/T) that occurs more than once.

💡 Key insight. With only four bases, each fits in 2 bits, so a 10-letter window packs into a 20-bit integer — a perfect, collision-free rolling hash. Sliding one base is pure arithmetic: shift the code left by 2, OR in the new base, and mask to 20 bits (that mask drops the base that fell off the left). No string hashing, no verification needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Map A→0, C→1, G→2, T→3 via a small lookup table; set mask = (1 << 20) − 1.
  2. Slide an index i across the string, updating hash = ((hash << 2) | code[s[i]]) & mask — append new base, evict the oldest.
  3. Once i ≥ 9 a full 10-base window ends at i. Keep a seen set; the first time a code reappears, also add it to an added set and emit s.substring(i−9, i+1) — so each repeated sequence is reported exactly once.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one constant-work step per starting index. 💾 Space O(n) — the seen and added sets hold up to O(n) distinct 20-bit codes.

⚠️ Gotchas. Return early when n < 10 (no window fits). You need two sets (or a count): with one, the same repeat gets emitted on every later occurrence, producing duplicates in the output. The mask is essential — without it the integer grows unbounded and old bases never leave the window.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The alphabet is just four bases, so I encode each in 2 bits and a 10-letter window becomes a 20-bit integer — a rolling hash with no collisions. I slide the window in O(1) by shifting and masking, track seen codes in a hash set, and the first time a code repeats I record it once. Linear time and space, and because the hash is exact I never need to verify characters."
📄 RepeatedDNASequences.java

2 Shortest Palindrome LC 214 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. By prepending characters only, convert s into a palindrome using the fewest additions; return that shortest palindrome.

💡 Key insight. Since you may only prepend, the answer is fixed once you know the longest prefix of s that is already a palindrome — everything after it must be mirrored and added in front. Finding that prefix reduces to KMP: build t = s + '#' + reverse(s) and run the failure function; the separator blocks overlap across the join, so lps[last] equals the length of the longest prefix of s that is also a suffix of reverse(s) — which is exactly the longest palindromic prefix of s.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Compute rev = reverse(s) and form combined = s + "#" + rev. The # must be a character absent from s so no match spans the boundary.
  2. Run the KMP prefix function on combined; let palLen = lps[combined.length() − 1] — the longest palindromic prefix length.
  3. Take the tail s[palLen..n) (the non-palindromic part), reverse it, and prepend it to s.

⏱️ Time O(n) — building t and its prefix-function table are both linear. 💾 Space O(n) — the concatenated string and its lps array.

⚠️ Gotchas. Forgetting the separator lets a match run from s straight into reverse(s) and overstates palLen (e.g. "aaaa"-style inputs). Handle the empty string (return ""). Only the prefix being a palindrome matters — a naïve "is s a palindrome, else add one char" loop is O(n²) and is the trap this KMP framing avoids.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I can only add characters in front, so the shortest palindrome is determined by the longest palindromic prefix of s: the rest gets mirrored to the front. To find that prefix in linear time I concatenate s, a separator, and reverse(s), then take the last value of the KMP failure function — that's the longest prefix of s matching a suffix of its reverse, i.e. the longest palindromic prefix. Prepend the reversed remainder. O(n) time and space."
📄 ShortestPalindrome.java

3 Longest Duplicate Substring LC 1044 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Find a longest substring that appears at least twice in s (occurrences may overlap); return it, or "" if none.

💡 Key insight. Feasibility is monotonic — if some length L duplicates, every shorter length does too — so binary-search L over [1, n−1]. The check at a fixed L is a Rabin-Karp rolling-hash scan: slide a width-L window computing each hash in O(1) from prefix hashes, recording the first start index per hash value; a recurring hash is a candidate, confirmed by comparing the actual substrings to rule out a modular collision. The longest L that passes yields the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Precompute prefix hashes h[] and powers pow[] with a large modulus (here the Mersenne prime 2⁶¹−1, multiplied via 32-bit splitting to dodge overflow).
  2. Binary search mid = candidate length. For each, scan all width-mid windows, derive each hash in O(1) via windowHash, and look it up in a map.
  3. On a hash hit, verify with s.regionMatches(prev, s, i, len) before declaring a duplicate (guards the rare collision); return the start index, else −1.
  4. If a length is feasible, record (start, len) and search longer; otherwise search shorter. Slice out s[bestStart, bestStart+bestLen).

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — log n binary-search steps, each an O(n) rolling-hash scan with amortized verification. 💾 Space O(n) — the prefix-hash/power arrays and the hash-to-start map at the probed length.

⚠️ Gotchas. Products of two ~61-bit values overflow a long — split into 32-bit halves (the mulmod helper) or you get silent corruption. Always verify the characters on a hash hit; skipping that is correct "usually" but fails adversarial inputs. A single weak modulus invites collisions — use 2⁶¹−1 plus verification, or a double hash. Watch the binary-search bounds: max usable length is n−1.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Whether a duplicate of length L exists is monotonic, so I binary-search L. For each candidate length I do a Rabin-Karp pass: prefix hashes let me hash every window in O(1), I store first-seen start per hash, and on a collision-suspect I verify the real characters before accepting. That's O(n) per length and log n lengths, so O(n log n). I use a 2⁶¹−1 modulus and explicit verification so false positives are effectively impossible."
📄 LongestDuplicateSubstring.java

🔁 Practice plan

Order: 187 to get comfortable with a rolling hash on a tiny alphabet, then 214 for the KMP failure-function "prefix == suffix" trick, then 1044 which fuses binary-search-on-answer with a verified Rabin-Karp scan. From a blank file, re-derive the prefix function (including the fall-back loop), the s + '#' + rev construction, and an overflow-safe mulmod with O(1) window hashing. Be able to explain why you verify on a hash hit.

  • ✅ I can write the KMP failure function from scratch and explain the fall-back step.
  • ✅ I can derive a window hash in O(1) from prefix hashes and justify the modulus choice.
  • ✅ I can explain why Rabin-Karp needs character verification on a hash match.
  • ✅ Follow-ups to rehearse: Implement strStr() (LC 28, KMP search), Find All Anagrams in a String (LC 438, sliding hash), and Distinct Echo Substrings (LC 1316, rolling hash).

🥞 Stack & Parsing

Stacks turn "I need to remember earlier context until a later event resolves it" into linear-time passes — that's the engine behind monotonic-stack range queries and recursive-descent expression evaluation. For Google L6 these problems are high-ROI because they reward clean state management and precise reasoning about operator precedence, parenthesis scoping, and amortized O(n) bounds under pressure.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 7 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Next greater / next warmer / next smaller element" or "span until a larger value" → monotonic stack of indices.
  • "Largest rectangle / maximal area bounded by the limiting height" → monotonic increasing stack; width is known at pop time.
  • Expression strings with + - * / and/or parentheses to evaluate → stack-based or recursive-descent parsing.
  • Nested structure with multipliers that compound outward (chemical formulas, encoded strings like 3[a2[c]]) → stack of partial results / count-maps, one frame per nesting level.
  • Precedence matters (*// bind tighter than +/-) → defer addition by pushing terms; apply *// against the stack top immediately.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two idioms dominate. (A) Monotonic stack: keep indices in sorted (increasing or decreasing) height/temperature order; when the incoming element breaks the order, popped elements have both boundaries known — the incoming index on one side and the new stack top on the other — so their answer is computed exactly once. (B) Expression parsing: maintain a current number and a pending operator; resolve *// immediately against the top of an operand stack (so precedence is baked in) and sum the stack at the end; handle parentheses by pushing context (iterative) or recursing on a shared cursor. Here is the precedence-aware evaluator skeleton:

// Operand stack with precedence: defer +/- by pushing, apply */ to the top now.
Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
int num = 0;
char op = '+';                      // operator pending in front of `num`
for (each char c, plus a virtual terminator at the end) {
    if (isDigit(c)) num = num * 10 + (c - '0');
    // on an operator OR ')' OR end-of-string, flush the pending op:
    if (isOperatorOrBoundary(c)) {
        switch (op) {
            case '+' -> stack.push(num);
            case '-' -> stack.push(-num);
            case '*' -> stack.push(stack.pop() * num);   // binds tighter than +/-
            case '/' -> stack.push(stack.pop() / num);
        }
        op = c; num = 0;
    }
}
int total = 0;
for (int v : stack) total += v;     // all */ resolved → just sum additive terms

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace

📘 Deep dive

1 Daily Temperatures LC 739 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. For each day, how many days until a strictly warmer temperature? Return 0 where none exists.

💡 Key insight. Keep a stack of indices whose temperatures strictly decrease from bottom to top. Today's temperature resolves every colder day still waiting on top of the stack — each such day's "next warmer day" is exactly today, so pop and record the gap. Each index is pushed and popped at most once → linear, not O(n²).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Init answer[] to 0 (the default "no warmer day").
  2. Scan left to right. While the stack is non-empty and temps[i] > temps[stack.peek()], pop prev and set answer[prev] = i − prev.
  3. Push i.

⏱️ Time O(n) — every index pushed and popped once 💾 Space O(n) — stack holds a strictly decreasing run in the worst case

⚠️ Gotchas. Store indices, not temperatures — you need the index to compute the day gap and to look up the temperature. The comparison is strict (>): equal temperatures do not count as warmer, so they stay on the stack. Days that never get popped keep their default 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force compares each day against all later days, O(n²). But I notice that when a warm day arrives it answers every colder day still pending, so I keep a decreasing stack of indices: each incoming day pops all the cooler days it resolves and records the gap. Every index is pushed and popped exactly once, so it's O(n) time and O(n) space."
📄 DailyTemperatures.java

2 Basic Calculator LC 224 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Evaluate a string expression of non-negative integers with +, -, parentheses, and spaces (no * or /).

💡 Key insight. A single left-to-right scan with a running result and a current sign (±1) handles +/-. Parentheses need no recursion: on ( push the current result and the sign applying to the whole group, then reset to a fresh sub-expression; on ) fold the group back in by multiplying by the pushed sign and adding the pushed result.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Accumulate multi-digit numbers with number = number*10 + (c−'0').
  2. On +/-: commit result += sign*number, reset number, set the new sign.
  3. On (: push result then sign; reset result = 0, sign = 1.
  4. On ): commit the last inner number, then result = result*stack.pop() + stack.pop() (sign first, then outer result).
  5. Return result + sign*number to fold in a trailing number.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each character processed once 💾 Space O(d) — stack depth equals parenthesis nesting depth

⚠️ Gotchas. Order of the two pops matters: you pushed result then sign, so on ) you pop sign first (multiply) and result second (add). Don't forget the trailing result + sign*number after the loop — the last number isn't committed by any operator. A leading unary minus like -(3-(2+1)) works because sign starts able to flip and applies to the group.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Since there's no multiplication, the only state I need is a running total and the sign in front of the next number. Parentheses just save and restore that context: when I hit an open paren I push the result-so-far and the sign that multiplies the whole group, evaluate the inside from zero, and on the close paren I scale the inner value by that saved sign and add back the saved result. Every character is touched once, O(n), and the stack only grows with nesting depth."
📄 BasicCalculator.java

3 Largest Rectangle in Histogram LC 84 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given bar heights, find the area of the largest rectangle that fits entirely within the histogram.

💡 Key insight. For each bar as the limiting height, its widest rectangle stretches from the first shorter bar on its left to the first shorter bar on its right. A stack of indices with strictly increasing heights finds both boundaries in one pass: when a shorter bar arrives, every taller bar popped is "closed off" on the right by i and on the left by the new stack top, so its maximal width — and thus area — is known exactly at pop time.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Iterate i from 0 to n inclusive; treat h = 0 at i == n as a sentinel that flushes the stack.
  2. While the stack's top bar is ≥ h, pop its height; left boundary is the new top (-1 if empty), so width = i − leftExclusive − 1; update best.
  3. Push i.

⏱️ Time O(n) — every index pushed and popped at most once 💾 Space O(n) — the monotonic stack

⚠️ Gotchas. The trailing zero-height sentinel (loop to i == n) is what guarantees every remaining bar gets popped and measured — without it, an increasing histogram never flushes. Width is i − stack.peek() − 1 after popping (the boundary is exclusive), and -1 when the stack empties means the rectangle extends to the far left. Use >= on the pop condition so equal-height bars merge correctly.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The largest rectangle is bounded by some bar's height, extended left and right until it hits something shorter. I keep an increasing stack of indices; when a shorter bar comes in, each taller bar I pop now knows both its right boundary, the current index, and its left boundary, the element below it on the stack, so I can compute its exact width and area in O(1). A virtual zero bar at the end flushes everything. Each bar is pushed and popped once, so O(n)."
📄 LargestRectangleInHistogram.java

4 Number of Atoms LC 726 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a chemical formula (elements, optional counts, nested parentheses with multipliers like K4(ON(SO3)2)2), return the atom counts as a string sorted by element with counts > 1 appended.

💡 Key insight. Parentheses define nested scopes whose multipliers compound outward, so keep a stack of count-maps — one per open group. An element adds its (optionally suffixed) count to the top map; ( pushes a fresh scope; ) pops the scope, reads the trailing multiplier, scales every count in the popped map, and merges it into the now-current scope. A TreeMap keeps names sorted for free.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Push an initial empty TreeMap as the outermost scope.
  2. On an element: read an uppercase letter + following lowercase letters as the name, then an optional count (default 1); merge into the top map.
  3. On (: push a new TreeMap.
  4. On ): read the multiplier, pop the group, multiply each entry by it, and merge into the parent.
  5. Build the result from the final top map, appending counts only when > 1.

⏱️ Time O(n log k) — n = formula length, k = distinct elements (TreeMap merges) 💾 Space O(n) — the scope stack plus accumulated counts

⚠️ Gotchas. An absent count defaults to 1, both for an element and after a ) — handle "no digits" cleanly in the number reader. Element names are uppercase-then-lowercase (Mg, not MG); read greedily. Multipliers apply to the entire popped group, so scale before merging upward. Counts can exceed what a naive parse expects (nested multipliers multiply), but they stay within int for the constraints. Append the count only when it's > 1.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each parenthesis level has its own multiplier that scales everything inside, and those multipliers nest, so I use a stack of maps — one per open group. I add atoms to the current top map; an open paren starts a fresh map; a close paren takes the multiplier after it, scales the whole popped map, and folds it into the map below. I store names in a TreeMap so the output is already sorted, and I append a count only when it's greater than one. It's linear in the formula length up to the log factor from the sorted-map merges."
📄 NumberOfAtoms.java

5 Basic Calculator III LC 772 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Evaluate an expression with + - * /, parentheses, and non-negative integers, respecting operator precedence and integer (truncating) division.

💡 Key insight. Handle precedence by deferring addition: push numbers onto a stack, but apply *// immediately against the stack top, so by the time you sum the stack the high-precedence operators are already resolved. The pending operator (the one before the current number) decides the action. Parentheses are handled by recursion on a shared cursor: ( spawns a sub-evaluation that returns at the matching ), and its scalar result becomes an ordinary operand.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a shared pos index so recursion resumes where the sub-expression stopped.
  2. In eval: build num; on ( set num = eval(s) (recurse).
  3. On an operator, ), or end-of-string, apply the pending op: +/- push (±num), *// rewrite the top in place; then store the new op and break on ).
  4. Return the sum of the stack — all *// already folded in.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each character consumed once across all recursion levels 💾 Space O(n) — operand stack plus recursion depth bounded by nesting

⚠️ Gotchas. The shared cursor (pos as a field) is essential — a local index per call would re-scan the inner expression. The "apply pending op" trigger must also fire at end-of-string (atEnd), or the final number is dropped. Operate on the operator before the current number, not after. Integer division truncates toward zero in Java for non-negative operands, matching the spec. Skip spaces without resetting the pending op.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I combine two ideas. For precedence, I push additive terms onto a stack but resolve times and divide immediately against the top, so the final answer is just the sum of the stack. For parentheses, I recurse using a shared cursor — an open paren kicks off a sub-evaluation that returns the value at the matching close paren, which I then treat as a plain number. The key is that the operator I act on is the one in front of the current operand, and I must also flush at the very end of the string. Every character is read exactly once, so O(n)."
📄 BasicCalculatorIII.java

6 Implement Queue using Stacks LC 232 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Build a FIFO queue (push/pop/peek/empty) using only two LIFO stacks.

💡 Key insight. Use an in-stack for pushes and an out-stack for pops. Reversing one LIFO into another yields FIFO order, but you only need to pour in → out when out is empty. Because each element is moved across at most once over its lifetime, that transfer cost is charged to a single element → amortized O(1) per operation even though one individual pop can be O(n).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. push(x): always push onto in.
  2. pop()/peek(): if out is empty, drain all of in into out (reversing order), then read from out.
  3. empty(): true only when both stacks are empty.

⏱️ Time O(1) amortized — each element moved in→out at most once 💾 Space O(n) — the two stacks hold all queued elements

⚠️ Gotchas. Refill out only when it is empty — pouring eagerly on every op breaks FIFO order and the amortized bound. empty() must check both stacks, since pending elements may sit in either. A single pop right after many pushes is O(n), but the amortized cost stays O(1).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A stack is LIFO, so I reverse it once to get FIFO: pushes go to an in-stack, and pops read from an out-stack. I only refill the out-stack from the in-stack when it's empty, so each element is transferred at most once. That makes every operation amortized O(1), even though a single pop after a burst of pushes is O(n)."
📄 ImplementQueueUsingStacks.java

7 Evaluate Reverse Polish Notation LC 150 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Evaluate an arithmetic expression in postfix (Reverse Polish) notation with operators + - * /, where division truncates toward zero.

💡 Key insight. Postfix needs no precedence rules or parentheses: by the time an operator is read, its two operands are guaranteed to be the most recently produced values, sitting on top of the stack. The only subtlety is operand order — the second value popped is the left-hand operand, which matters for non-commutative − and ÷.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Scan tokens left to right. A number token is pushed onto an operand stack.
  2. On an operator, pop right first then left (the deeper value), apply left op right, and push the result.
  3. After consuming all tokens, the single remaining stack value is the answer.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each token is pushed/popped a constant number of times 💾 Space O(n) — the stack holds up to O(n) operands for an all-numbers prefix

⚠️ Gotchas. Pop order is the classic trap: the first pop is the right operand, the second is the left — reversing them flips a − b and a ÷ b. Integer division truncates toward zero in Java (-7 / 2 == -3, not -4), which matches the spec. Negative-number tokens like "-11" are operands, not the − operator — parse the whole token.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Postfix is exactly what a stack wants: I push operands, and when an operator appears its two operands are already on top, so I pop them, apply, and push the result. The one gotcha is order — the first value I pop is the right operand and the second is the left, which matters for subtraction and division. It's a single linear pass, O(n) time and O(n) space."
📄 EvaluateReversePolishNotation.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the two tracks separately, then together. Monotonic-stack track: derive Daily Temperatures (739) cold, then Largest Rectangle (84) — the sentinel and the exclusive-boundary width are the bits people forget. Parsing track: build Basic Calculator (224) first (sign + context stack, no precedence), then layer precedence in Basic Calculator III (772), then generalize the nesting idea to Number of Atoms (726). From a blank file, re-derive: the decreasing-vs-increasing stack invariant and why each index is touched twice; the "defer +/- , apply */ now, sum at end" precedence trick; and the recursive-cursor pattern for parentheses. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Basic Calculator II (227) (precedence without parens — the warm-up for 772), Decode String (394) (the exact stack-of-frames nesting pattern as Number of Atoms), Trapping Rain Water (42) and Sum of Subarray Minimums (907) as monotonic-stack cousins of 84, and Maximal Rectangle (85) which literally calls 84 per row.

  • ✅ I can explain why a monotonic stack gives O(n): each index is pushed and popped at most once.
  • ✅ I can state, at pop time, what the left and right boundaries of a popped bar are and why the width is i − newTop − 1.
  • ✅ I can justify the zero-height sentinel in Largest Rectangle and why the loop runs to i == n.
  • ✅ I can show how pushing result then sign on ( and popping in the right order restores context on ).
  • ✅ I can explain how deferring addition while applying *// immediately encodes operator precedence with no second pass.
  • ✅ I can describe why a shared cursor (not a local index) is required for the recursive parenthesis evaluation.
  • ✅ I can explain why a stack of count-maps captures compounding nested multipliers in Number of Atoms.

🔃 Sorting Algorithms

Sorting is the bedrock primitive that almost every other technique leans on — two pointers, greedy, intervals, binary search — so an L6 is expected to implement the classic O(n log n) sorts from a blank file, articulate the stability/space trade-offs, and know when an O(n) non-comparison sort is legal. The high-ROI gap here is the long tail: custom comparators, in-place selection with a weird operation budget (pancake flips), and bucket/pigeonhole tricks that beat the n log n lower bound.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 5 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Implement sort X" / "don't call the library sort" → reproduce merge, quick, or heap and defend the choice on stability and space.
  • "Order to maximise/minimise some concatenation or score" → the real answer is a custom comparator, not a numeric sort.
  • A restricted mutation primitive ("you may only reverse a prefix") → in-place selection sort expressed in that operation.
  • "Linear time" or "the input is integers in a known range" → escape the n log n lower bound with counting / radix / bucket.
  • "Maximum gap between sorted neighbours" / "k-th something" without fully sorting → pigeonhole buckets or quickselect.
  • "Rearrange so no two adjacent are equal / strictly alternate" → median + 3-way partition with virtual indexing.

🧠 Core idea & template

Comparison-sort cheat sheet (the n log n lower bound applies). Every comparison sort needs Ω(n log n) comparisons in the worst case. Pick by the constraint you're handed: need a stable sort or sorting a linked list → merge sort; need in-place with the best constants on random data → quicksort (but guard the pivot against the already-sorted O(n²) trap); need guaranteed O(n log n) and O(1) space → heap sort.

// Merge — STABLE, O(n log n) time, O(n) space. '<=' on ties keeps left run first.
void merge(int[] a, int[] buf, int lo, int mid, int hi) {
    int i = lo, j = mid + 1, k = lo;
    while (i <= mid && j <= hi) buf[k++] = (a[i] <= a[j]) ? a[i++] : a[j++];
    while (i <= mid) buf[k++] = a[i++];
    while (j <= hi)  buf[k++] = a[j++];
    System.arraycopy(buf, lo, a, lo, hi - lo + 1);
}

// Quick — IN-PLACE, avg O(n log n), O(log n) stack. Median-of-three pivot dodges
// the sorted-input O(n^2) trap; recurse small side, loop large side -> bounded depth. NOT stable.
int partition(int[] a, int lo, int hi) {
    int mid = lo + (hi - lo) / 2;
    swap(a, medianOfThree(a, lo, mid, hi), hi);   // pivot parked at end
    int pivot = a[hi], store = lo;
    for (int i = lo; i < hi; i++) if (a[i] < pivot) swap(a, i, store++);
    swap(a, store, hi);
    return store;                                  // pivot's final sorted index
}

// Heap — IN-PLACE, O(n log n) worst, O(1) space. Build max-heap, then pop max to the back. NOT stable.
int[] heapSort(int[] a) {
    int n = a.length;
    for (int i = n / 2 - 1; i >= 0; i--) siftDown(a, i, n);   // O(n) heapify
    for (int end = n - 1; end > 0; end--) { swap(a, 0, end); siftDown(a, 0, end); }
    return a;
}

Non-comparison linear sorts (only when the keys cooperate). These sidestep the n log n bound by reading the keys directly instead of comparing pairs:

  • Counting sort — keys are small integers in [0, K]. Tally counts, take a prefix sum, place each key. O(n + K) time, O(n + K) space. Stable if you walk the input right-to-left when placing. Falls apart once K ≫ n.
  • Radix sort (LSD) — fixed-width integers/strings; stable-counting-sort one digit at a time, least-significant first. O(d·(n + b)) for d digits in base b. Stable (and relies on its inner pass being stable). Great when values are huge but digit count d is small.
  • Bucket / pigeonhole sort — keys roughly uniform over a range. Scatter into n buckets by value, sort each bucket, concatenate. O(n) expected on uniform data, O(n²) if everything clumps into one bucket. Stability follows the per-bucket sort. This is the engine behind Maximum Gap.

One-line rule of thumb: comparison sorts are universal but bounded at n log n; non-comparison sorts are linear but only legal when the key space is small or structured.

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace

2Largest Number LC 179 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged Custom comparator (a+b vs b+a)O(n log n · k)O(n)
