📘 Deep dive

1 Sort an Array LC 912 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Sort an integer array ascending without calling the library sort — implement an O(n log n) algorithm yourself.

💡 Key insight. This is the "can you actually write a sort" gate. Have all three classic O(n log n) sorts in muscle memory so you can pick the one that matches a follow-up constraint: merge when stability or external/linked data matters, quick for the best in-place constants, heap when you need a worst-case guarantee with O(1) space.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Merge (stable, top-down): halve, sort each half, merge by repeatedly taking the smaller front element; take the left run on ties (a[i] <= a[j]) to preserve relative order. A scratch buffer holds the merged run.
  2. Quick (in-place): choose a median-of-three pivot deterministically (first/mid/last — no Math.random), Lomuto-partition so smaller keys precede the pivot, then recurse into the smaller side and loop on the larger to bound stack depth at O(log n).
  3. Heap (in-place): sift down from the last internal node to build a max-heap in O(n), then repeatedly swap the root to the end of the heap region and sift the new root down; the sorted suffix grows from the back.
  4. Each method clones the input first so the caller's array is untouched.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — all three; quick degrades to O(n²) only on adversarial pivots, which median-of-three avoids on sorted input 💾 Space O(n) merge buffer · O(log n) quick stack · O(1) heap

⚠️ Gotchas. Quicksort with a naive first/last pivot is O(n²) on already-sorted input — the single most common interview blunder; median-of-three (or a random pivot) fixes it. In merge, using < instead of <= silently breaks stability. Heapify must run from n/2 - 1 down to 0, not the other way. Watch the recursion bound: only recursing the smaller half keeps quicksort's stack logarithmic even in the worst case.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Library sort is off the table, so I'll implement one of the n log n classics. If they want stability I reach for merge sort — split, recurse, merge taking the left element on ties. If they want in-place I use quicksort with a median-of-three pivot so sorted input doesn't blow up to quadratic, and I recurse the small side and iterate the big side to keep the stack at O(log n). If they want a hard worst-case guarantee with constant extra space, heap sort: build a max-heap in linear time, then pop the max to the back n times, each pop O(log n)."
📄 SortAnArray.java

2 Largest Number LC 179 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given non-negative integers, arrange them so their concatenation forms the largest possible number; return it as a string.

💡 Key insight. Sort by a pairwise concatenation rule, not by numeric value: a should precede b iff the string a+b > b+a. That comparator is a valid total order (transitive on the digit strings), so greedily placing the "more valuable in front" element first yields the global maximum.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Convert each number to its decimal string.
  2. Sort descending with the comparator (a, b) -> (b + a).compareTo(a + b) — whichever concatenation order makes the bigger number wins.
  3. All-zeros guard: if the largest piece is "0", the whole result is just "0" (otherwise [0,0] would render as "00").
  4. Concatenate the sorted strings into the answer.

⏱️ Time O(n log n · k) — a comparison sort where each compare concatenates two k-digit strings 💾 Space O(n) — the boxed string array

⚠️ Gotchas. Comparing the numbers numerically (or by length, or lexicographically) is wrong: 3 vs 30 must compare via "303" vs "303"… actually "330" vs "303" → 3 first. The all-zeros case is the classic trap — forget it and you return "00" or "000". Build the result with a StringBuilder, not repeated String +=, to avoid O(n²) string churn.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I can't sort by value — [3, 30] should give 330, not 303. The right key is the concatenation itself: a beats b when a+b as a string exceeds b+a. That relation is a consistent total order, so a normal comparison sort with this comparator produces the optimal arrangement. The only edge case is all zeros, where I collapse the answer to a single '0'."
📄 LargestNumber.java

3 Pancake Sorting LC 969 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Sort the array using only "pancake flips" — reverse a prefix of length k — and return the sequence of flip sizes (each k is 1-indexed).

💡 Key insight. It's selection sort expressed entirely in prefix reversals. To park the largest unsorted value at the end of its window, flip it to the front, then flip the whole prefix so it slides to the back. Two flips fix one element ⇒ at most 2n flips overall, well within the limit.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Iterate the window arr[0..i] from i = n-1 down to 1.
  2. Find the index of the max within that prefix.
  3. If it's already at i, skip (no flips). Otherwise: if it isn't at index 0, flip the prefix ending at it (record maxIdx + 1) to bring it to the front.
  4. Flip the prefix of length i + 1 (record i + 1) to send the front element to position i.
  5. Shrink the window and repeat; the suffix stays sorted.

⏱️ Time O(n²) — n iterations, each scans and flips a prefix of length up to n 💾 Space O(1) — beyond the output list of flip sizes

⚠️ Gotchas. The flip size k is 1-indexed (number of elements reversed), but the in-place reversal works on 0-indexed bounds — off-by-one here is the usual bug (record maxIdx + 1 and i + 1, reverse [0 .. k] internally). Skip the no-op flip when the max is already at i, and skip the front-flip when the max is already at index 0, or you emit useless flips. Any sequence that ends sorted is accepted — there's no unique answer.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I only have prefix reversal, so I run selection sort with that single primitive. For each position from the back, I find the largest remaining element, flip it to the front, then flip the prefix up to the target so it drops into place. That's at most two flips per element, so at most 2n flips total — comfortably under the bound — at O(n²) time because each scan-and-flip is linear."
📄 PancakeSorting.java

4 Maximum Gap LC 164 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Return the maximum difference between successive elements once the array is sorted — but achieve it in linear time and space.

💡 Key insight. Pigeonhole. With n numbers the smallest the maximum gap can possibly be is ceil((max-min)/(n-1)). Size each bucket to that span, so any two numbers in the same bucket differ by less than the answer. The maximum gap must therefore cross a bucket boundary — so compare each non-empty bucket's min against the previous non-empty bucket's max. You never need a bucket's contents, only its running min and max.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Edge cases: fewer than 2 elements → 0; all equal → 0.
  2. Compute global min and max in one pass.
  3. Set bucketSize = max(1, (max-min)/(n-1)) and bucketCount = (max-min)/bucketSize + 1.
  4. One pass to place each number into bucket (x-min)/bucketSize, updating that bucket's min/max and a used flag.
  5. Sweep buckets left to right tracking prevMax (starting at the global min); the answer is the largest bucketMin - prevMax over non-empty buckets.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass to fill buckets, one pass to sweep them 💾 Space O(n) — n+1 buckets each holding just a min and max

⚠️ Gotchas. bucketSize must be floored to at least 1 or integer division gives a zero-width bucket and a divide-by-zero. Skip empty buckets in the sweep — comparing across them is exactly where the gap hides. The whole point is that within-bucket pairs are provably smaller than the answer, so you can ignore bucket internals; candidates who try to fully sort each bucket miss the O(n) win. LSD radix sort then one adjacent-difference scan is the accepted alternative if buckets feel fiddly.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Sorting then scanning is O(n log n); to hit linear I use the pigeonhole principle. If I make every bucket exactly as wide as the minimum-possible max-gap, then two numbers in one bucket can't be the answer — the answer has to jump a gap between buckets. So I record only each bucket's min and max, then walk the buckets comparing this bucket's min to the previous bucket's max. Two linear passes, O(n) time and space."
📄 MaximumGap.java

5 Wiggle Sort II LC 324 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Reorder so that nums[0] < nums[1] > nums[2] < nums[3] … (strict), ideally in O(n) time and O(1) extra space.

💡 Key insight. Put the larger half on odd indices and the smaller half on even indices and every neighbour pair straddles the median, so the wiggle holds. The hard part is keeping equal medians from touching: walk a virtual index (2k+1) % (n|1) that fills odd slots first (1,3,5,…) then wraps to even slots (0,2,4,…), and 3-way-partition around the median so its two copies land maximally far apart.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Find the median (the n/2-th smallest) with quickselect in O(n) average using a median-of-three pivot.
  2. Run a Dutch-national-flag 3-way partition, but address the array through the virtual map mapIndex(i) = (2*i+1) % (n|1).
  3. Values > median swap toward the low virtual slots (the odd, "peak" positions); values < median swap toward the high virtual slots (the even, "valley" positions); equals stay in the middle.
  4. Standard Dutch-flag pointer discipline: on a "greater" swap advance both, on a "less" swap don't advance the scan (the swapped-in value is unexamined), on equal just advance.

⏱️ Time O(n) average — quickselect O(n) plus a single Dutch-flag pass 💾 Space O(1) — partitions in place over the virtual mapping

⚠️ Gotchas. The (n|1) modulus (force odd) is what makes the virtual map a permutation for both even and odd n — using plain n breaks odd-length inputs. Without virtual indexing, equal medians end up adjacent and the strict inequality fails on inputs like [1,3,2,2,3,1] — this is precisely why Wiggle Sort II is harder than I. Don't advance the scan pointer after a "less than median" swap. Quickselect's pivot must be deterministic (median-of-three) to avoid adversarial O(n²).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "If I split at the median and interleave the larger half onto odd positions and the smaller half onto even positions, neighbours alternate around the median automatically. The subtlety is duplicate medians colliding, so I use virtual indexing: I map my partition cursor through (2k+1) % (n|1), which lays the big values onto peaks first and the small values onto valleys, pushing the two median copies as far apart as possible. Quickselect finds the median in linear average time, and the partition is one pass — so O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 WiggleSortII.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work in increasing order of how far each strays from a vanilla sort. Day 1: re-derive merge, quick, and heap in Sort an Array from a blank file until the pivot guard and the stable-merge tie-break are automatic — this is the foundation everything else reuses. Day 2: the comparator twist (Largest Number) and the operation-budget twist (Pancake Sorting). Day 3: the two linear/selection tricks (Maximum Gap bucket reasoning, Wiggle Sort II quickselect + virtual index). Re-explain the n log n lower bound and exactly which problems legally escape it.

  • ✅ I can write merge, quick, and heap sort from scratch and state each one's stability and space.
  • ✅ I can explain why median-of-three (or a random pivot) rescues quicksort from O(n²) on sorted input.
  • ✅ I can name when counting / radix / bucket are legal and which of them are stable.
  • ✅ I can justify the bucket width in Maximum Gap via the pigeonhole principle.
  • ✅ I can explain why virtual indexing keeps equal medians apart in Wiggle Sort II.
  • ✅ Follow-ups rehearsed: Kth Largest Element (215, quickselect), Sort Colors (75, Dutch flag), Merge Intervals (56, sort-then-sweep).

🔎 Binary Search

Binary search is two patterns wearing one coat. The classic form halves a sorted (or locally-ordered) array to find an index in O(log n); the deeper form — binary search on the answer — searches a numeric answer range guarded by a monotone yes/no predicate. The second is the Google L6 differentiator: once you see "minimize the maximum" or "smallest feasible X", you can turn an O(n!) optimization into O(n log range), and the interview is half won.

Source: Prep Tracker & curated Staff gaps · 16 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • Sorted (or rotated-sorted) input + "find / does it exist". The default reflex on any sorted array — O(log n) beats a linear scan for free.
  • A monotone predicate. "If capacity C works, every capacity > C works too." Whenever feasibility flips exactly once from false→true (or true→false) along a number line, binary search the flip point.
  • "Minimize the maximum" / "maximize the minimum" / "smallest k such that…". The signature phrasing of binary-search-on-the-answer (Koko, Capacity, Split Array).
  • "First / last occurrence", "smallest index ≥ x", "insertion point". Lower/upper-bound templates, not the vanilla equality search.
  • Answer is a contiguous window or a single pivot/peak. A discrete structure you can converge onto by comparing a candidate to its neighbour (peak, rotation min, k-closest window).
  • O(log) demanded on data that isn't a flat sorted array. 2-D matrix, value range [1,n], two arrays' median — flatten, search the value axis, or partition.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two families, one invariant. Always state the invariant out loud — "the answer is in [lo, hi]" — and make every branch preserve it. The bugs are never the idea; they're the boundary (< vs <=), the mid update (mid vs mid±1), and integer overflow.

Family 1 — classic lower/upper bound (search an index). Prefer the half-open [lo, hi) lower-bound template; it generalises to first/last occurrence, insertion point, and rotated variants. Use lo + (hi - lo) / 2 to dodge overflow.

// Smallest index i in [0, n] with pred(i) true, given pred is false...false,true...true.
int lowerBound(int[] a, int key) {
    int lo = 0, hi = a.length;          // hi EXCLUSIVE: search [lo, hi)
    while (lo < hi) {                   // stop when the window is empty
        int mid = lo + (hi - lo) / 2;   // overflow-safe midpoint
        if (a[mid] < key) lo = mid + 1; // mid fails: first valid is strictly right
        else              hi = mid;     // mid qualifies: keep it as a candidate
    }
    return lo;                          // lo == hi == the boundary
}
// first occurrence  = lowerBound(a, key)        (verify a[i] == key)
// last  occurrence  = lowerBound(a, key + 1) - 1
// "converge to one element" peak/min variant: while (lo < hi) { hi = mid | lo = mid+1; } return lo;

Family 2 — binary search on the answer (monotone predicate). When the answer is a number and feasible(x) is monotone, search the value range itself. The greedy/counting feasible check is the real work; binary search just finds where it flips. The on-answer family in this chapter is Koko Eating Bananas, Capacity To Ship Packages, and Split Array Largest Sum — all three are the identical skeleton with a different feasible.

// Smallest x in [lo, hi] with feasible(x) true (feasible is monotone false→true).
int searchAnswer(int[] data) {
    int lo = lowestPossibleAnswer;      // e.g. max(element) — a day must hold its heaviest item
    int hi = highestPossibleAnswer;     // e.g. sum(elements) — everything in one piece always works
    while (lo < hi) {
        int mid = lo + (hi - lo) / 2;
        if (feasible(data, mid)) hi = mid;     // mid works → try tighter
        else                     lo = mid + 1; // mid too small → need more
    }
    return lo;                          // smallest feasible answer
}
// feasible = a greedy O(n) sweep returning days/pieces/hours needed at budget mid,
// compared against the allowed limit. Pick lo/hi as the tightest provable bounds.

📘 Deep dive

1 Sqrt(x) LC 69 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return floor(sqrt(x)) for a non-negative integer x, using only integer arithmetic.

💡 Key insight. floor(sqrt(x)) is the largest k with k*k <= x, and k*k is monotonically increasing, so the valid k form a prefix [0 .. answer]. This is binary-search-on-the-answer in miniature: search the last k that satisfies the predicate.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Set lo = 0, hi = x (since sqrt(x) <= x for all x >= 0), and keep a running ans.
  2. For each mid, test (long) mid * mid <= x. If true, mid is a valid floor — record it in ans and move lo = mid + 1 to reach for a larger root.
  3. If false, mid overshoots — move hi = mid - 1. Return ans.

⏱️ Time O(log x) — the candidate range halves each step 💾 Space O(1) — a handful of scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. mid * mid overflows int near x = 2³¹−1 (root ≈ 46340) — the cast to long before multiplying is mandatory. x = 0 must return 0; the inclusive lo <= hi loop with ans = 0 handles it. Don't return lo or hi reflexively — track ans so the floor is unambiguous.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The brute force scans k upward until k² exceeds x — O(√x). But k² is monotone, so the answer is the boundary of a true/false prefix, and I binary search it in O(log x). The one trap is overflow, so I compute mid*mid as a long before comparing."
📄 SqrtX.java

2 Find Peak Element LC 162 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return the index of any peak (an element strictly greater than both neighbours) in an array where adjacent elements differ, treating out-of-bounds as −∞.

💡 Key insight. The slope at mid points toward a peak. If nums[mid] < nums[mid+1] the array is still rising, so a peak must exist to the right; otherwise we're at or past a peak and one exists at mid or to its left. Following the ascent is guaranteed to land on a peak because the boundaries are −∞.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Use the converge-to-one-index template: lo = 0, hi = n - 1, loop while lo < hi.
  2. mid + 1 is always in bounds because lo < hi. If nums[mid] < nums[mid + 1], ascend right: lo = mid + 1.
  3. Else keep the left side including mid as a candidate: hi = mid. Return lo when the window collapses.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — halve the range each step 💾 Space O(1) — two indices

⚠️ Gotchas. This is binary search on unsorted data — the trick is the local monotone signal, not global order. Use hi = mid (not mid - 1) since mid may itself be the peak; pairing that with lo < hi avoids an infinite loop. Single-element and strictly-decreasing arrays both correctly return index 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Even though it's unsorted, there's a monotone signal: if I'm on an upslope a peak lies to the right, and the −∞ boundaries guarantee a peak always exists in whichever half I keep. So I walk uphill via binary search in O(log n) instead of scanning O(n)."
📄 FindPeakElement.java

3 Find First and Last Position LC 34 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. In a sorted array, return the first and last indices of target, or [-1, -1] if absent — in O(log n).

💡 Key insight. A target occupies a contiguous block, so its two ends are two lower-bound searches. lowerBound(target) gives the first index with value >= target; lowerBound(target + 1) gives the first index strictly past the block, so its predecessor is the last occurrence.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Compute first = lowerBound(nums, target). If first == n or nums[first] != target, the value never appears — return [-1, -1].
  2. Compute last = lowerBound(nums, target + 1) - 1.
  3. lowerBound uses the half-open [lo, hi) template: hi = n, shrink with lo = mid + 1 when nums[mid] < key else hi = mid.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — two binary searches 💾 Space O(1) — index arithmetic only

⚠️ Gotchas. The membership check after the first search is essential — lowerBound returns an insertion point even when the target is absent, so you must verify nums[first] == target and guard first == n before indexing. The target + 1 trick avoids writing a separate upper-bound routine. Empty array: lowerBound returns 0 == n, handled cleanly.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I reduce 'first and last' to two lower-bound queries. First-index-≥-target gives the start; first-index-≥-(target+1) minus one gives the end. Both are O(log n), and a single equality check after the first search distinguishes 'present' from 'would-insert-here'."
📄 FindFirstAndLastPosition.java

4 Search in Rotated Sorted Array LC 33 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. A sorted array of distinct values is rotated at an unknown pivot; find target's index (or −1) in O(log n).

💡 Key insight. At any mid, at least one half is fully sorted. If nums[lo] <= nums[mid] the left half [lo..mid] is sorted; otherwise the right half [mid..hi] is. A clean range check tells us whether target lies inside the sorted half, and we discard the other half.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Standard inclusive loop lo <= hi; return mid on a hit.
  2. If nums[lo] <= nums[mid] (left sorted): if nums[lo] <= target < nums[mid], go left (hi = mid - 1); else go right.
  3. Else (right sorted): if nums[mid] < target <= nums[hi], go right (lo = mid + 1); else go left. Return −1 if the loop exits.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — halve the search space every iteration 💾 Space O(1) — iterative, a few indices

⚠️ Gotchas. Use nums[lo] <= nums[mid] (with <=) so a two-element window where lo == mid still classifies the left half as sorted. Get the range bounds' strictness right: the sorted-half check is half-open on the mid side (target < nums[mid] / nums[mid] < target) because mid was already tested. Distinct values are assumed — duplicates are LC 81 below.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rotation breaks global order but never both halves at once — one side of mid is always sorted. I identify the sorted half by comparing nums[lo] to nums[mid], range-check the target against that half, and recurse into whichever half can contain it. That keeps it O(log n)."
📄 SearchInRotatedSortedArray.java

5 Search in Rotated Sorted Array II LC 81 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. The rotated-array search of LC 33, but now duplicates are allowed — return whether target exists (boolean).

💡 Key insight. The sorted-half logic is unchanged except for one ambiguous case: when nums[lo] == nums[mid] == nums[hi], you cannot tell which half is sorted. The fix is to conservatively shrink both ends by one (lo++; hi--) and retry — correct, but it degrades the worst case to O(n).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Inclusive loop; return true on a hit.
  2. First test the ambiguity: if nums[lo] == nums[mid] && nums[mid] == nums[hi], do lo++; hi-- and continue — this branch must come before the sorted-half checks.
  3. Otherwise apply the identical LC 33 logic: classify the sorted half via nums[lo] <= nums[mid] and range-check target. Return false if the loop exits.

⏱️ Time O(log n) average, O(n) worst — duplicates can force linear shrinking 💾 Space O(1) — iterative, a few indices

⚠️ Gotchas. The all-equal input [1,1,1,…] with an absent target is the O(n) worst case — interviewers love to ask for it explicitly. Order matters: the lo == mid == hi trim must be checked before the nums[lo] <= nums[mid] branch, otherwise you'd misclassify the sorted half. Note the return type changed from index to boolean because duplicates make "the index" ambiguous.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's LC 33 with one wrinkle: duplicates can make nums[lo], nums[mid], nums[hi] all equal, so I can't tell which half is sorted. In that single case I trim both ends by one and retry — everything else is identical. That trim is why the worst case is O(n) on an all-equal array, though it stays O(log n) on average."
📄 SearchInRotatedSortedArrayII.java

6 Find Minimum in Rotated Sorted Array LC 153 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the minimum value of a rotated sorted array of distinct elements in O(log n).

💡 Key insight. The minimum is the single "drop" point of the rotation. Comparing mid to the right end localizes it: if nums[mid] > nums[hi] the dip is strictly right of mid, so the min is in (mid, hi]; otherwise mid itself could be the min and the answer is in [lo, mid]. Comparing to hi (not lo) sidesteps the fully-sorted ambiguity.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Converge-to-one template: lo = 0, hi = n - 1, loop while lo < hi.
  2. If nums[mid] > nums[hi], the drop is right of mid: lo = mid + 1.
  3. Else mid may be the min: hi = mid. Return nums[lo].

⏱️ Time O(log n) — halve the range each step 💾 Space O(1) — two indices

⚠️ Gotchas. Comparing mid to lo instead of hi breaks on a non-rotated array (e.g. [1,2,3,4]) because nums[mid] >= nums[lo] is always true and you'd wrongly discard the left. Use hi = mid (not mid - 1): mid is still a min candidate. A non-rotated input correctly returns nums[0]. (With duplicates this becomes LC 154, where you'd add an nums[mid] == nums[hi] → hi-- branch.)
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The min is the rotation's pivot — the one place the sequence drops. I compare mid to the right endpoint: if mid is bigger, the drop is to its right; otherwise the min is at mid or left. Anchoring on the right end rather than the left is what keeps the non-rotated case correct, and it's O(log n)."
📄 FindMinimumInRotatedSortedArray.java

7 Search a 2D Matrix LC 74 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. In an m×n matrix where each row is sorted and every row's first value exceeds the previous row's last, decide if target exists in O(log(mn)).

💡 Key insight. Those two ordering guarantees mean reading the matrix row-by-row yields one fully sorted sequence of m*n values. Binary search that virtual flattened array and map a flat index back to a cell via (index / cols, index % cols) — never materializing it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Set lo = 0, hi = rows*cols - 1 over the imaginary 1-D array.
  2. Unflatten each mid: val = matrix[mid / cols][mid % cols].
  3. Compare and shrink as in plain binary search (lo = mid + 1 / hi = mid - 1); return true on equality, false if the loop exits.

⏱️ Time O(log(mn)) — halve the flattened space every iteration 💾 Space O(1) — index arithmetic only

⚠️ Gotchas. Divide and mod by the column count (cols), not rows — swapping them silently transposes the index. This O(log mn) trick relies on the "next row starts above this row's max" guarantee; if only rows and columns are independently sorted (no cross-row guarantee), that's LC 240 and you need the staircase below. Guard the empty-matrix case before reading matrix[0].length.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because each row's first element beats the previous row's last, the whole matrix is one sorted run if you read it in row-major order. So I binary search indices 0..mn−1 and convert each index to (row, col) with divide and mod — O(log mn), no flattening, O(1) space."
📄 SearchA2DMatrix.java

8 Search a 2D Matrix II LC 240 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Rows and columns are each sorted ascending (but no cross-row guarantee); decide if target exists.

💡 Key insight. Start at the top-right corner: it's the maximum of its row and the minimum of its column — a perfect pivot. If it exceeds target, the entire column is too big (move left); if it's smaller, the entire row is too small (move down). Each comparison eliminates a whole row or column, so no per-cell binary search is needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Begin at row = 0, col = cols - 1; loop while row < m and col >= 0.
  2. If val == target return true; if val > target the column's smallest is still too big, so col--.
  3. Else the row's largest is still too small, so row++. Return false when you walk off the grid.

⏱️ Time O(m + n) — at most m down-moves plus n left-moves 💾 Space O(1) — two indices

⚠️ Gotchas. The corner choice is the whole trick — top-right (or symmetrically bottom-left) works because it's simultaneously a row-max and column-min; the top-left and bottom-right corners are not decisive (both neighbours move the same way), so starting there fails. Don't reach for O(m log n) per-row binary search — the staircase is strictly better at O(m + n) and far simpler. This is a perennial Google favourite precisely because the naive instinct (binary search each row) is suboptimal.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Here rows and columns are sorted but not globally, so the flatten trick fails. I start at the top-right corner, which is a row-max and column-min at once: too big means kill the column and step left, too small means kill the row and step down. Each step drops a full row or column, giving O(m+n) — better than binary searching every row."
📄 SearchA2DMatrixII.java

9 Find the Duplicate Number LC 287 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. An array of n+1 integers each in [1, n] has exactly one repeated value; find it without mutating the array and in O(1) extra space.

💡 Key insight. Binary search the value range, not indices. For a candidate mid, count how many entries are <= mid. With no duplicates that count would be exactly mid; if it exceeds mid, some value in [1, mid] repeats, so the duplicate sits in the low half. This reads the array read-only — no mutation, no extra space.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Search candidate values lo = 1, hi = n (= nums.length - 1), loop while lo < hi.
  2. Count count = #{v : v <= mid} in one O(n) pass.
  3. If count > mid the duplicate is in [lo, mid]: hi = mid; else lo = mid + 1. Return lo.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — log n value steps, each a full O(n) count pass 💾 Space O(1) — running counters only

⚠️ Gotchas. Don't binary search indices — the array isn't sorted; the monotone quantity is the count of values ≤ mid, which is what makes the predicate work. The constraints forbid the easy outs (a hash set is O(n) space; sorting mutates), so this counting search is the intended O(1)-space answer. Floyd's tortoise-and-hare (value-as-next-index cycle detection) is the O(n) alternative — worth naming as a follow-up, but the binary search is far easier to reason about under pressure.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I can't sort or use extra space, so I binary search on the value 1..n. The count of elements ≤ mid is monotone: it equals mid when there's no early duplicate and exceeds mid once a value in [1,mid] repeats. I converge on the smallest value where the count overshoots — O(n log n) time, O(1) space. The O(n) Floyd cycle version exists but is trickier to derive live."
📄 FindTheDuplicateNumber.java

10 Find K Closest Elements LC 658 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given a sorted array, return the k elements closest to x, sorted ascending; ties prefer the smaller element.

💡 Key insight. The answer is always a contiguous length-k window, so binary search its left edge over [0, n-k]. For a candidate left mid, compare the two outer rivals: the left gap x - arr[mid] versus the right gap arr[mid + k] - x. If the left gap is strictly larger, sliding right (dropping arr[mid]) is at least as good.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Search the left edge: lo = 0, hi = n - k, loop while lo < hi.
  2. If x - arr[mid] > arr[mid + k] - x, the element leaving on the right is closer than the one on the left — slide right: lo = mid + 1.
  3. Else keep the current edge: hi = mid. Then copy arr[lo .. lo+k-1] into the result.

⏱️ Time O(log(n−k) + k) — search the edge, then copy k elements 💾 Space O(k) — the output list

⚠️ Gotchas. Compare the two window-boundary candidates arr[mid] and arr[mid + k] — not arr[mid] vs x. The tie rule ("favour smaller") falls out of using strict >: on a tie you take the hi = mid branch, keeping the smaller-indexed (smaller-valued) element. The high bound is n - k (inclusive of that left edge), and mid + k stays in bounds because mid <= n - k - 1 in the loop body. Avoid the O(n) two-pointer-shrink or O(n log n) sort-by-distance — this is the clean log-time edge search.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The result is a contiguous window, so I only need its left edge. I binary search the edge in [0, n−k], comparing the element about to drop on the left against the one about to be picked up on the right; whichever is farther tells me which way to slide. Strict inequality makes ties resolve to the smaller value. Then I copy out k elements — O(log(n−k) + k)."
📄 FindKClosestElements.java

11 Koko Eating Bananas LC 875 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given banana piles and h hours, find the minimum integer eating speed k (bananas/hour, one pile at a time) that finishes all piles within h hours.

💡 Key insight. Hours-needed is monotone in speed k: a faster Koko never needs more hours, so feasible speeds form a suffix [answer .. maxPile]. This is the canonical binary-search-on-the-answer problem — search the smallest k whose total hours <= h. A pile of size p at speed k costs ceil(p / k) = (p + k - 1) / k hours.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Bounds: lo = 1, hi = max(piles) (no point eating faster than the biggest pile).
  2. For each candidate mid, compute total hours via hoursNeeded summing (p + mid - 1) / mid.
  3. If hoursNeeded <= h the speed works — hi = mid (search smaller); else lo = mid + 1. Return lo.

⏱️ Time O(n log maxPile) — feasibility scans n piles per search step 💾 Space O(1) — running counters only

⚠️ Gotchas. Accumulate hours in a long — up to ~10⁴ piles of 10⁹ at speed 1 overflows int. Compute the ceiling with integer math (p + k - 1) / k, never floating point (precision + speed). Lower bound is 1, not 0 (speed 0 eats nothing). The feasibility predicate is the substance; the binary search around it is mechanical.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Total hours decreases monotonically as speed rises, so I binary search the speed range [1, maxPile] for the smallest speed that finishes within h. Feasibility is an O(n) sum of ceil(pile/speed). That turns a guess-and-check over a billion-wide range into O(n log maxPile). The only traps are using long for the hour sum and an integer ceiling."
📄 KokoEatingBananas.java

12 Capacity To Ship Packages Within D Days LC 1011 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Packages must ship in order within D days; find the minimum ship capacity (per-day weight limit) that achieves it.

💡 Key insight. Days-needed is monotone in capacity: a bigger ship never needs more days, so feasible capacities form a suffix [answer .. sum]. Binary search the smallest capacity whose greedy packing fits within D days. Same on-answer skeleton as Koko, with a different bounds choice: lower = max(weights) (a day must hold its heaviest package), upper = sum(weights) (one giant day).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. In one pass set lo = max(weights) and hi = sum(weights).
  2. daysNeeded(capacity): greedily load packages in order; when the next would overflow capacity, increment days and reset the load.
  3. If daysNeeded(mid) <= D shrink (hi = mid); else grow (lo = mid + 1). Return lo.

⏱️ Time O(n log(sum − max)) — feasibility scans n packages per search step 💾 Space O(1) — running counters only

⚠️ Gotchas. The lower bound must be max(weights), not 1 — a capacity below the heaviest package can never ship it, so smaller candidates aren't just slow, they're invalid. Packages ship in fixed order (it's not bin-packing / not reorderable), which is exactly why the greedy day-count is optimal and easy. Start daysNeeded with days = 1 (the first day is already open). Identical structure to Koko/Split Array — recognising the family is the point.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "This is binary-search-on-the-answer again. Days needed falls monotonically as capacity rises, so I search capacities from max(weight) to sum(weight) for the smallest that fits in D days. Feasibility is a greedy in-order packing scan, O(n). Choosing the lower bound as the heaviest package is the subtle part — anything smaller can't even carry that one item."
📄 CapacityToShipPackagesWithinDDays.java

13 Split Array Largest Sum LC 410 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Split an array into at most k contiguous subarrays so the largest subarray sum is minimized; return that minimized largest sum.

💡 Key insight. "Minimize the maximum" → binary search the answer. Minimizing the largest sum is monotone in the cap we allow: a bigger cap never needs more pieces, so search the smallest cap achievable with <= k subarrays. Feasibility is a greedy left-to-right sweep counting pieces; the range is [max element, total sum].
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Bounds: lo = max(nums) (a single element must fit a piece), hi = sum(nums) (whole array in one piece). Hold hi and the search range as long.
  2. piecesNeeded(cap): keep extending the current piece; when adding the next element exceeds cap, start a new piece (pieces++, reset running to that element).
  3. If piecesNeeded(cap) <= k the cap suffices — right = cap; else left = cap + 1. Return (int) left.

⏱️ Time O(n log(sum)) — greedy check scans n elements per search step 💾 Space O(1) — running counters only

⚠️ Gotchas. This is the same on-answer family as Koko and Capacity — Capacity-to-Ship is literally this problem with k = D. There's an O(n²k) interval-DP alternative; the binary-search-on-answer is the expected O(n log sum) solution and the one that impresses. Use long for hi/cap since the sum can exceed int. Lower bound is max(nums), never 0 — the largest single element is an unavoidable floor on any piece's sum.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The phrase 'minimize the largest sum' is my cue for binary-search-on-the-answer. For a candidate cap, a greedy sweep tells me the minimum pieces whose sums each stay ≤ cap; that piece count is monotone in cap. So I search caps from max-element to total-sum for the smallest cap needing ≤ k pieces — O(n log sum), versus the heavier interval DP. It's the identical engine to Koko and Capacity-to-Ship."
📄 SplitArrayLargestSum.java

14 Median of Two Sorted Arrays LC 4 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the median of two sorted arrays in O(log(min(m, n))) — no full merge allowed.

💡 Key insight. The median splits the merged array into equal-size left and right halves where every left element <= every right element. You only choose how many of the left-half slots come from array A; the rest come from B. Binary search that count on the smaller array: a partition is valid when maxLeftA <= minRightB and maxLeftB <= minRightA.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Recurse to ensure a is the shorter array. Let half = (m + n + 1) / 2 = size of the combined left half.
  2. Binary search i (elements taken from a) over [0, m]; set j = half - i. Use ±∞ sentinels for partitions at an edge (i==0, i==m, j==0, j==n).
  3. If maxLeftA <= minRightB && maxLeftB <= minRightA: odd total → return max(maxLeftA, maxLeftB); even → average that with min(minRightA, minRightB). If maxLeftA > minRightB took too much from a (hi = i - 1); else lo = i + 1.

⏱️ Time O(log(min(m, n))) — binary search the partition on the shorter array 💾 Space O(1) — constant scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. Always binary search the shorter array (the recursive swap) so j = half - i never goes out of range. The (m + n + 1) / 2 form puts the extra element on the left for odd totals, which lets odd/even share one partition test. The Integer.MIN/MAX_VALUE sentinels are what make edge partitions (one array fully on a side) just work — forgetting them is the classic bug. This searches a partition index, not a value — a subtler binary search than the rest of the chapter, which is why it's a Hard.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of merging, I look for the partition that cuts both arrays into a combined left and right half of equal size with left ≤ right across the boundary. I only decide how many come from the smaller array and binary search that count; the cross-checks maxLeftA ≤ minRightB and maxLeftB ≤ minRightA tell me which way to move. Infinity sentinels handle edge partitions. That's O(log min(m,n))."
📄 MedianOfTwoSortedArrays.java

15 Binary Search LC 704 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the index of target in a sorted array of distinct integers, or −1 if absent.

💡 Key insight. The reference template the whole chapter builds on. Hold the inclusive invariant "if target exists it is always inside [lo, hi]", and each step compares nums[mid] to discard the half that provably cannot contain it. The midpoint lo + (hi - lo) / 2 avoids the lo + hi overflow.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Closed interval lo = 0, hi = n - 1; loop while lo <= hi (empty exactly when lo > hi).
  2. Compute mid = lo + (hi - lo) / 2; return mid on equality.
  3. If nums[mid] < target the target is strictly right (lo = mid + 1); else strictly left (hi = mid - 1). Return −1 if the window collapses.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — the window halves every iteration 💾 Space O(1) — only two indices

⚠️ Gotchas. Pair the closed interval lo <= hi with mid ± 1 updates — mixing it with hi = mid loops forever. Always write the overflow-safe midpoint, not (lo + hi) / 2, since both indices can be large. The distinct-value assumption means any hit is unambiguous; duplicates push you to the lower-bound template instead.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "This is the canonical sorted-array search and the template the rest of the chapter specializes. I keep the answer inside a closed window and halve it each step by comparing the middle to the target. Two things I always get right: the overflow-safe midpoint, and pairing lo <= hi with mid ± 1 moves. O(log n) time, O(1) space."
📄 BinarySearch.java

16 First Bad Version LC 278 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Versions read good…good,bad…bad; given an isBadVersion(v) API, find the first bad version with the fewest calls.

💡 Key insight. Binary-search the boundary of a monotone false→true predicate, not a stored value. The predicate is a step function, so search for where it flips: when mid is bad the first bad is at mid or left (hi = mid), when good it is strictly right (lo = mid + 1). The window shrinks to the single boundary index.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Converge-to-one template: lo = 1, hi = n, loop while lo < hi.
  2. mid = lo + (hi - lo) / 2. If isBadVersion(mid), keep mid as a candidate: hi = mid.
  3. Else the boundary is to its right: lo = mid + 1. Return lo when the range collapses.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — the candidate range halves each step 💾 Space O(1) — two indices only

⚠️ Gotchas. The overflow-safe midpoint is not optional here: n can be near Integer.MAX_VALUE, so (lo + hi) / 2 overflows and breaks. Use hi = mid (not mid - 1) because mid may itself be the first bad version; pairing that with lo < hi avoids an infinite loop. This finds the FIRST true — a lower-bound on a predicate, not an equality search.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of searching for a value, I search the boundary of a monotone predicate: everything from the first bad version onward is bad. When the midpoint is bad I keep it and look left; when it's good I move right. The window converges to that flip point. The one real trap is overflow — n is near 2³¹, so I compute mid as lo + (hi − lo)/2."
📄 FirstBadVersion.java

🔁 Practice plan

Drill in two tracks. Track A — index search: from a blank file re-derive the half-open lowerBound until it's muscle memory, then layer rotation onto it (33 → 81 → 153) and the matrix variants (74 → 240). Track B — search on the answer: solve Koko (875) first, then prove to yourself that Capacity (1011) and Split Array (410) are the same code with a swapped feasible — being able to say "this is the Koko family" out loud is the L6 signal. Save Median (4) for last; re-derive the partition invariant on paper before touching the keyboard. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Search a 2D Matrix II (staircase vs. per-row search), Find the Duplicate Number (count-on-value vs. Floyd's cycle), and Find K Closest Elements (edge search vs. two-pointer shrink).

  • ✅ I can write the half-open [lo, hi) lower-bound template — and the converge-to-one while (lo < hi) peak/min variant — from memory, and explain the < vs <= and mid vs mid±1 choices.
  • ✅ Given "minimize the maximum" or "smallest k such that…", I instantly reach for binary-search-on-the-answer and can state the monotone predicate plus the tightest lo/hi bounds.
  • ✅ I can explain why rotated search keeps one sorted half, and why duplicates (LC 81) force the lo++; hi-- trim and an O(n) worst case.
  • ✅ I can justify the top-right corner in LC 240 and why it beats per-row binary search (O(m+n) vs O(m log n)).
  • ✅ I can solve Find-the-Duplicate by binary-searching the value range with a count-≤-mid predicate, and name Floyd's cycle detection as the O(n) alternative.
  • ✅ I can derive the Median-of-Two-Sorted-Arrays partition, explain the ±∞ sentinels, and say why I search the shorter array.
  • ✅ I always guard integer overflow: lo + (hi - lo) / 2 for mid, long for sums/products (Sqrt, Koko, Split Array), and an integer ceiling (p + k - 1) / k.

🔗 Linked Lists

Linked-list problems are pure pointer surgery: you re-wire next (and sometimes prev/random) links in place instead of copying data. A small toolkit — dummy heads, fast/slow two-pointers, in-place reversal, and the heap/hash-map pairings — solves nearly all of them, which makes this family extremely high-ROI: Google L6 interviewers lean on it to test whether you can mutate a structure cleanly under pressure without losing a node or leaking a cycle.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 11 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The input is a singly/doubly linked list and you're asked to mutate structure (reverse, reorder, splice, merge) rather than just read values.
  • "In O(1) extra space" or "without using an array" — screams in-place pointer rewiring, not a copy-to-list-and-back.
  • "Nth from the end", "middle of the list", or "is there a cycle" — the fast/slow two-pointer idiom.
  • You need the node before a target to splice it out, or removal of the head is a possible edge case → reach for a dummy/sentinel head.
  • "k sorted lists" or "always take the smallest front" → a min-heap over the current heads.
  • O(1) get/put with eviction by recency → hash map + doubly linked list (LRU).

🧠 Core idea & template

Three idioms cover this whole chapter. (1) A dummy head gives every node a predecessor, so appending and deleting need no special case for the first node. (2) Fast/slow pointers find the middle or a cycle in one pass: advance one by 1 and one by 2. (3) In-place reversal walks the list flipping each next backward, with prev accumulating the reversed prefix. Master these and most problems become a composition of two or three of them.

// (1) Dummy head — build/splice with no first-node special case
ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
ListNode tail = dummy;
// ... tail.next = node; tail = tail.next; ...
return dummy.next;

// (2) Fast/slow — middle (and cycle) detection in one pass
ListNode slow = head, fast = head;
while (fast != null && fast.next != null) {
    slow = slow.next;
    fast = fast.next.next;
    // if (slow == fast) -> cycle
}
// slow is now at/near the middle

// (3) In-place reversal — flip each next backward
ListNode prev = null, cur = head;
while (cur != null) {
    ListNode next = cur.next; // stash before overwriting
    cur.next = prev;
    prev = cur;
    cur = next;
}
return prev; // new head

📘 Deep dive

1 Reverse Linked List LC 206 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Reverse a singly linked list and return the new head.

💡 Key insight. Walk once, flipping each node's next to point back at the node you just left. prev accumulates the reversed prefix and is the new head when cur runs off the end. The only trap is overwriting cur.next before you've saved it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Hold prev = null, cur = head.
  2. Each step: stash next = cur.next, set cur.next = prev, then slide prev = cur, cur = next.
  3. Return prev. (Recursive variant: recurse to the tail as the new head, then head.next.next = head; head.next = null on the way up.)

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass over the list 💾 Space O(1) iterative — three pointers (O(n) for the recursive stack)

⚠️ Gotchas. Save cur.next before rewriting it or you lose the rest of the list. Handle empty/single-node lists (loop simply doesn't run). In recursion, forgetting head.next = null leaves a 2-node cycle at the old head.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep a prev pointer and march through the list flipping each link backward, stashing the next node first so I don't lose it. prev ends up at the old tail, which is the new head. One pass, O(1) space. I can also do it recursively, but that costs O(n) stack."
📄 ReverseLinkedList.java

2 Merge Two Sorted Lists LC 21 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Merge two sorted linked lists into one sorted list by splicing nodes.

💡 Key insight. A classic two-finger merge: at each step append the smaller front node. A dummy head removes the "is this the first node?" special case, and when one list empties you splice the entire remaining tail in a single link.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Create dummy; let tail = dummy.
  2. While both lists are non-empty, attach the smaller head (l1.val <= l2.val keeps it stable) and advance that list and tail.
  3. After the loop, tail.next = (l1 != null) ? l1 : l2 to attach the leftover.
  4. Return dummy.next.

⏱️ Time O(m+n) — each node is linked exactly once 💾 Space O(1) — re-links existing nodes, no copies

⚠️ Gotchas. Use <= not < to keep the merge stable and avoid dropping equal values. Don't forget the leftover-tail splice — without it you truncate the longer list. Both-empty inputs must return an empty list (dummy handles it).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I use a dummy head and a two-finger walk: compare the two front nodes, splice the smaller one on, advance. When one list runs out I attach the other in one move. It's O(m+n) time and O(1) extra space because I reuse the existing nodes."
📄 MergeTwoSortedLists.java

3 Linked List Cycle LC 141 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Return whether the linked list contains a cycle.

💡 Key insight. Floyd's tortoise and hare: move one pointer 1 step and another 2 steps per iteration. Inside a cycle the fast pointer gains one node per step on the slow one, so it must eventually lap and coincide; with no cycle, fast simply walks off the end.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Start slow = fast = head.
  2. While fast != null && fast.next != null: advance slow by 1 and fast by 2.
  3. If slow == fast, return true; if the loop exits, return false.

⏱️ Time O(n) — fast traverses at most ~2n nodes before meeting or exiting 💾 Space O(1) — two pointers, no auxiliary set

⚠️ Gotchas. Guard both fast and fast.next before the double hop or you NPE on the last node. A hash-set of visited nodes also works but costs O(n) space — interviewers want the O(1) Floyd version. Follow-up LC 142 asks for the cycle's entry node (reset one pointer to head, advance both by 1).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I run two pointers, one twice as fast as the other. If there's a loop the fast one closes the gap by one node each step and they collide; if there's no loop the fast one falls off the end. Constant space, linear time — strictly better than tracking visited nodes in a set."
📄 DetectCycle.java

4 Palindrome Linked List LC 234 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return whether a singly linked list reads the same forward and backward, ideally in O(1) space.

💡 Key insight. A list is a palindrome iff its reversed second half equals its first half. Find the middle with slow/fast, reverse the second half in place, then walk inward from both ends comparing values — no array copy, so O(1) space.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Slow/fast to the middle; for even length slow lands on the first node of the second half.
  2. Reverse from slow to the end, giving prev as the reversed-half head.
  3. Walk left = head and right = prev in lockstep, bounded by the shorter reversed half; any mismatch returns false.

⏱️ Time O(n) — find-mid, reverse, and compare are each linear 💾 Space O(1) — in-place reversal, only pointers

⚠️ Gotchas. Loop on the reversed (second) half since it's the shorter side for odd lengths — the unmatched middle node is harmless. Empty/single-node lists are palindromes. Purists restore the list afterward (re-reverse the half); mention it even if you don't code it.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The simple version copies values into an array and checks two pointers — O(n) space. To hit O(1), I find the midpoint with fast/slow, reverse the back half in place, then compare it node-by-node against the front half. Three linear passes, constant space. If asked, I'd re-reverse to leave the list intact."
📄 PalindromeLinkedList.java

5 Remove Nth Node From End of List LC 19 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Remove the n-th node counting from the end and return the list head, in one pass.

💡 Key insight. Open a gap of exactly n nodes between a lead and a trail pointer, then advance both until the lead hits the last node — the trail now sits on the node just before the target. Anchoring trail at a dummy head makes removing the real head need no special case.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Make dummy.next = head; set lead = trail = dummy.
  2. Advance lead by n steps to open the gap.
  3. While lead.next != null, advance both — trail stops on the predecessor.
  4. Splice: trail.next = trail.next.next; return dummy.next.

⏱️ Time O(L) — one pass; the lead pointer crosses the list once 💾 Space O(1) — two pointers

⚠️ Gotchas. The dummy head is what lets you delete the first node uniformly — without it, removing head is a special case. Loop on lead.next != null (stop on the last node), not lead != null, so trail lands on the predecessor. Assumes n is valid (1 ≤ n ≤ length).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I put a dummy in front, then space two pointers n apart. When the lead reaches the end, the trailing pointer is exactly one before the node to delete, so I splice it out. The dummy means deleting the head is no different from deleting anything else. Single pass, O(1) space."
📄 RemoveNthNodeFromEnd.java

6 Add Two Numbers LC 2 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Two numbers are given as linked lists of digits in reverse order; return their sum as a linked list.

💡 Key insight. Because the least-significant digit is at the head, you add column by column straight from the heads, propagating a carry forward — exactly grade-school addition. Treating a missing node as 0 lets unequal lengths and a trailing carry fall out of the same loop.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Dummy head, carry = 0.
  2. Loop while l1 != null || l2 != null || carry != 0: sum = carry + (l1?.val) + (l2?.val).
  3. Append new ListNode(sum % 10); update carry = sum / 10; advance whichever lists exist.
  4. Return dummy.next.

⏱️ Time O(max(m,n)) — one column per iteration 💾 Space O(max(m,n)) — the result list (+ O(1) working state)

⚠️ Gotchas. The carry != 0 term in the loop condition is what handles a final carry (e.g. 5+5 → an extra node). Don't forget to advance only the lists that still have nodes. The trickiest variant is LC 445 (digits in forward order) — there you reverse both lists first or use stacks.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Since the lists store digits least-significant-first, the heads line up like columns in long addition. I walk both at once, sum each column plus the carry, emit sum % 10, carry sum / 10. Keeping the carry in the loop condition handles different lengths and a leftover carry in one shot."
📄 AddTwoNumbers.java

7 Reorder List LC 143 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Reorder L0→L1→…→Ln into L0→Ln→L1→Ln-1→… in place.

💡 Key insight. The target order interleaves the list with its own reverse. So split at the middle, reverse the second half in place, then zip the two halves together one node at a time — no copying, no extra storage. It's a composition of all three core idioms.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Find the middle (here fast.next/fast.next.next so slow ends at the first half's tail).
  2. Sever at slow, then reverse the second half into prev.
  3. Zip: alternately splice nodes from first and the reversed second, saving each next before relinking.

⏱️ Time O(n) — find-mid, reverse, and merge are each linear passes 💾 Space O(1) — pointer surgery only

⚠️ Gotchas. Sever the first half (slow.next = null) before reversing, or you create a cycle. The split must leave first no longer than second so the zip loop terminates cleanly on second. Cache both fNext and sNext each step. Handle length ≤ 2 up front.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The result is the list interleaved with its reverse. So I find the midpoint, cut the list, reverse the back half, then weave the two halves together node by node. It's just find-middle plus reverse plus merge — all three of the standard linked-list moves — done in O(n) time and O(1) space."
📄 ReorderList.java

8 Copy List with Random Pointer LC 138 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Deep-copy a linked list where each node has an extra random pointer to any node (or null).

💡 Key insight. Avoid the O(n) hash map by weaving each clone directly after its original (A→A'→B→B'→…). Then a clone's random is just its original's-random's next, resolvable in place. A final pass unzips the two interleaved lists, restoring the original and extracting the copy.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Pass 1 — interleave: for each cur, insert clone between cur and cur.next.
  2. Pass 2 — wire randoms: cur.next.random = cur.random.next (guarding null random).
  3. Pass 3 — unzip: restore each cur.next and chain the clones onto a dummy copy list.

⏱️ Time O(n) — three linear passes 💾 Space O(1) — no map; only the cloned nodes themselves (output excluded)

⚠️ Gotchas. Step into cur.next.next when iterating the interleaved list, not cur.next. Null-check cur.random before dereferencing in pass 2. The unzip must restore the original links — leaving the lists interleaved is a common silent bug. The simpler-to-derive O(n)-space version maps original→clone in one pass, then fills next/random in a second; lead with that, then offer the O(1) weave.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The naive approach maps each original node to its clone in a hash map, then a second pass wires next and random — O(n) time and space. The slick trick is to interleave each clone right after its original so I can read a clone's random as its original-random's next, no map needed. Three passes, O(1) extra space, and I unzip at the end to restore the input."
📄 CopyListWithRandomPointer.java

9 Merge k Sorted Lists LC 23 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Merge k sorted linked lists into one sorted list.

💡 Key insight. The global minimum across all lists is always one of the k current heads. A min-heap keyed on node value surfaces that minimum in O(log k): pop it, append it, push its successor. The heap never holds more than k nodes.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed a min-heap (PriorityQueue comparing val) with every non-empty head.
  2. Pop the smallest, append to the result tail, and if it has a successor, push that.
  3. Repeat until the heap empties; return dummy.next.

⏱️ Time O(N log k) — N total nodes, each pushed/popped once at O(log k) 💾 Space O(k) — heap holds at most one node per list

⚠️ Gotchas. Only seed non-empty heads or you'll NPE. Empty input array and arrays of all-null lists must return an empty list. The alternative — divide-and-conquer pairwise merge — is also O(N log k) time but O(1) extra space; mention it as the trade-off. Naively merging one-by-one is O(Nk).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The next node in the output is always the smallest among the k list fronts, so I keep those fronts in a min-heap. Each pop is the global minimum; I append it and push its successor. That's O(N log k) since every node enters and leaves the heap once. Alternatively I can pairwise-merge the lists in a tournament for the same time bound with O(1) space."
📄 MergeKSortedLists.java

10 LRU Cache LC 146 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Design a fixed-capacity cache with O(1) get and put that evicts the least-recently-used key on overflow.

💡 Key insight. A hash map gives O(1) lookup by key; a doubly linked list gives O(1) reordering by recency. Pair them — the map value is the list node — so both get and put touch only a constant number of links. Sentinel head/tail nodes erase every null-edge case.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Doubly linked list with sentinel head (MRU side) and tail (LRU side); Map<key, Node>.
  2. get: miss → -1; hit → moveToFront(node) and return its value.
  3. put: existing key → update value + move to front; new key → if at capacity, unlink tail.prev and remove it from the map, then add the fresh node at the front.

⏱️ Time O(1) — each op is a map lookup plus a fixed number of relinks 💾 Space O(capacity) — at most capacity entries across map and list

⚠️ Gotchas. Store the key inside the node so eviction can map.remove(lru.key) — a list-only node can't find its map entry. Sentinels mean addToFront/unlink never touch null. Update an existing key's value and bump recency. LinkedHashMap with removeEldestEntry is the library shortcut — know the hand-rolled version cold, since interviewers usually ban it.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I need O(1) lookup and O(1) recency updates, so I combine a hash map keyed by the cache key with a doubly linked list ordered by recency, where the map points straight at the list node. On access I unlink the node and move it to the front; on insert past capacity I drop the tail and evict its key. Sentinel head and tail nodes keep the relinking branch-free."
📄 LRUCache.java

11 Middle of the Linked List LC 876 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the middle node of a singly linked list in one pass; for an even length, return the second of the two middles.

💡 Key insight. A two-speed walk: slow advances one node while fast advances two, so slow has always covered exactly half of what fast has. When fast runs off the end, slow sits at the middle. This is the reusable tortoise-and-hare primitive behind cycle detection and Reorder/Palindrome.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Start slow = fast = head.
  2. While fast != null && fast.next != null, advance slow by 1 and fast by 2.
  3. Return slow — it lands on the (second) middle when the loop exits.

⏱️ Time O(n) — fast traverses the whole list once 💾 Space O(1) — two pointers, no extra structures

⚠️ Gotchas. The fast != null && fast.next != null condition is precisely what yields the second middle on even lengths — fast lands on null after the last step, leaving slow one past center. To get the first middle instead, loop on fast.next != null && fast.next.next != null (the variant Reorder/Palindrome need to keep the first half no longer than the second). Single-node lists return the node itself.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I run two pointers, one at double speed. By the time the fast one falls off the end, the slow one has covered exactly half the list, so it's sitting on the middle — one pass, constant space. The loop condition I pick decides whether I get the first or second middle on even lengths, which matters when this is the setup step for reorder or palindrome checks."
📄 MiddleOfTheLinkedList.java

🔁 Practice plan

Drill in the table's order — the three Easies (206, 21, 141) install the dummy-head, two-finger-merge, and fast/slow idioms you'll reuse everywhere. Then 234/19/143 layer them together; 138, 23, and 146 are the design-flavored payoffs. From a blank file, re-derive the iterative reversal and the fast/slow middle without looking — they're the backbone of half this chapter. Rehearse these Google-favorite follow-ups out loud: LC 142 (cycle entry node), LC 445 (add two numbers, forward order), and LRU → LFU (LC 460).

  • ✅ I can explain why a dummy head removes the head-deletion special case.
  • ✅ I can prove the fast/slow pointers must meet inside a cycle, and find the cycle's entry.
  • ✅ I can reverse a list both iteratively (O(1)) and recursively (O(n) stack) and state the trade-off.
  • ✅ I can compose find-mid + reverse + merge for Reorder List and Palindrome without losing a node.
  • ✅ I can copy the random-pointer list both with a hash map and with the O(1) interleave-and-unzip trick.
  • ✅ I can build LRU from a hash map + doubly linked list and justify O(1) per operation, including why the node stores its key.

🌳 Trees

Binary-tree problems are about choosing the right traversal and then carrying exactly the right state across it: a BFS queue when you reason by level, a post-order DFS when a node's answer depends on its children, and bounds threaded top-down when validity is a global property. This family is high-ROI for Google L6 because it rewards a clean recursive contract ("what does this call return, and what does it leave behind?") over memorized code — the exact judgment interviewers probe.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 18 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Per level", "right side", "top view", "zigzag", "depth" → BFS with a size snapshot per level.
  • A node's answer is built from its children's answers (height, max path, LCA, flatten) → post-order DFS returning a value up the stack.
  • "Is this a valid BST / does ordering hold globally?" → thread a (low, high) bound interval downward, not a local parent comparison.
  • "Distance / directions / common ancestor of two nodes" → root-to-target paths or a split-point post-order search.
  • "Reconstruct / encode the tree" → pre-order with explicit null markers makes the shape self-delimiting.
  • Repeatedly stripping leaves / grouping by height → one post-order pass bucketing by height-from-bottom beats O(n²) re-pruning.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two skeletons cover almost everything here. (1) Level-order BFS: a queue holds exactly the current frontier, so freezing queue.size() before draining gives that level's node count without storing depth per node. (2) Post-order DFS with a return contract: each call returns a summary of its subtree (a height, a best downward path, a "did I find a target?" flag), and the parent combines the two child summaries. Pin down what the recursion returns versus what it records globally and most tree problems collapse to a few lines.

// (1) Level-order BFS: size snapshot groups nodes by depth
Queue<TreeNode> q = new LinkedList<>();
if (root != null) q.offer(root);
while (!q.isEmpty()) {
    int size = q.size();                 // freeze the frontier count
    for (int i = 0; i < size; i++) {
        TreeNode node = q.poll();
        // ... use node for this level ...
        if (node.left  != null) q.offer(node.left);
        if (node.right != null) q.offer(node.right);
    }
}

// (2) Post-order DFS: return a subtree summary, fold a global answer
int dfs(TreeNode node) {
    if (node == null) return BASE;       // e.g. 0 or -1
    int left  = dfs(node.left);
    int right = dfs(node.right);
    global = combine(global, node, left, right);   // answer that "turns" at node
    return contribution(node, left, right);        // what a parent may extend
}
