📘 Deep dive

1 Binary Tree Level Order Traversal LC 102 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the node values grouped level by level, top to bottom, left to right.

💡 Key insight. At any moment a BFS queue holds exactly the nodes of one frontier. Snapshotting queue.size() before you start draining tells you how many nodes belong to the current level, so you can group output without ever storing a depth on each node.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Push the root (if non-null) and loop while the queue is non-empty.
  2. Capture size = queue.size() and start a fresh level list of that capacity.
  3. Poll exactly size nodes; append each value to the level list and enqueue its non-null children.
  4. Add the completed level list to the result and repeat.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each node is enqueued and dequeued exactly once 💾 Space O(n) — the queue holds up to one full level (~n/2 leaves at peak)

⚠️ Gotchas. Guard the empty tree (return an empty list, not a list containing an empty list). You must read size into a variable before the inner loop — calling queue.size() in the loop condition is wrong because the queue grows as you enqueue children of the current level.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "BFS naturally visits in level order, but a plain queue loses the level boundaries. The trick is that the queue, right after finishing a level, contains precisely the next level — so I freeze its size, drain exactly that many nodes into one group, and enqueue their children for the following round. That's O(n) time and O(n) space for the widest level."
📄 BinaryTreeLevelOrderTraversal.java

2 Binary Tree Right Side View LC 199 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Standing to the right of the tree, return the values you can see from top to bottom — the rightmost node at each depth.

💡 Key insight. Do a DFS that recurses right before left. Then the first time the recursion arrives at any new depth, that node is exactly the rightmost one at that depth. Tracking "is this a new depth?" is just depth == result.size().
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Recurse from the root carrying a depth counter and the shared result list.
  2. If depth == view.size(), this is the first node reaching this depth — append its value.
  3. Recurse into node.right first (so the right side wins each depth), then node.left.
  4. Left-side nodes only fill in depths the right side never reached (deeper-left subtrees).

⏱️ Time O(n) — every node is visited once 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The visible node at the deepest level can come from the left subtree when the right subtree is shorter — the right-first ordering handles this automatically, but it trips people who assume "rightmost child always". A BFS-last-of-level solution also works and is O(n)/O(n); the right-first DFS is more elegant at O(h) space.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I want the first node seen at each depth from the right. If I DFS visiting the right child first, the very first arrival at a depth is guaranteed rightmost. I record a value only when the current depth equals the result size, which means I haven't filled that depth yet. Left subtrees then back-fill any depths the right side couldn't reach. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 BinaryTreeRightSideView.java

3 Validate Binary Search Tree LC 98 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Decide whether a binary tree satisfies the BST property: every node's value is greater than all values in its left subtree and less than all in its right subtree.

💡 Key insight. BST validity is global, not local. A node must be greater than every ancestor it sits to the right of and less than every ancestor it sits to the left of. Thread an open interval (low, high) down the recursion: going left tightens high to the parent's value, going right tightens low.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Call validate(root, null, null) — null bounds mean unbounded, which also dodges Integer overflow at the extremes.
  2. At each node, fail if node.val <= low or node.val >= high (open interval, so equal values are rejected).
  3. Recurse left with (low, node.val) and right with (node.val, high).
  4. A null node is vacuously valid.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one comparison per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The classic bug is checking only node.left.val < node.val < node.right.val locally — a deep right-descendant smaller than the root passes that check but violates the BST. Use Integer (nullable) for bounds rather than Integer.MIN_VALUE/MAX_VALUE sentinels so a node holding exactly Integer.MIN_VALUE is handled. An in-order traversal that checks strict increase is an equally valid alternative.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The naive parent-child check is insufficient because the constraint is inherited from the entire root path, not just the immediate parent. So I carry a valid open range down: every left turn lowers the ceiling, every right turn raises the floor, and each node must fall strictly inside its inherited window. Null bounds avoid overflow. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 ValidateBinarySearchTree.java

4 Lowest Common Ancestor of a Binary Tree LC 236 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given two nodes p and q in a binary tree (not a BST), return their lowest common ancestor — the deepest node that has both as descendants.

💡 Key insight. The LCA is the deepest node from which p and q descend into different subtrees, or a node that is itself one target with the other below it. A post-order search returns "I found p, q, or the LCA in my subtree"; the node where both the left and right searches come back non-null is exactly that split point.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Base case: if the node is null or equals p or q, return it (you've hit a target or bottomed out).
  2. Recurse into the left and right subtrees.
  3. If both sides return non-null, p and q split here — this node is the LCA.
  4. Otherwise return whichever side is non-null (both targets, or neither, lie on one side).

⏱️ Time O(n) — each node visited once 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. A node can be its own ancestor: if p is an ancestor of q, the early return at root == p correctly yields p. This assumes both nodes are guaranteed present; if existence isn't guaranteed you'd need an extra found-count check before trusting the result. The comparison is by node identity here, not by value.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I post-order DFS and have each call report whether it saw either target below it. If a node hears 'yes' from both its left and right, the two targets first meet at that node, so it's the LCA. If only one side reports a hit, the answer is somewhere on that side, so I bubble it up. The early return when a node is a target handles the ancestor-of-itself case. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 LowestCommonAncestor.java

5 Flatten Binary Tree to Linked List LC 114 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Flatten the tree in place into a "linked list" that follows pre-order: every left child becomes null and the right pointer chains the pre-order sequence.

💡 Key insight. In pre-order a node is immediately followed by its entire left subtree, then by whatever used to follow it (its right subtree). So for each node with a left child, find that left subtree's rightmost node (its in-order tail), graft the current right subtree onto it, then slide the left subtree over to the right. This Morris-style rewiring needs only O(1) extra space.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Walk a curr pointer down the tree.
  2. If curr.left exists, descend the left subtree's right spine to its rightmost node.
  3. Attach curr.right to that rightmost node, move curr.left onto curr.right, and null out curr.left.
  4. Advance curr = curr.right down the now-linear chain and repeat.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each edge is traversed a constant number of times 💾 Space O(1) — pure pointer rewiring, no recursion or explicit stack

⚠️ Gotchas. You must reattach the old right subtree to the rightmost node of the left subtree, not just the left child, or you'll drop nodes. The simpler recursive / reverse-pre-order (visit right, left, prepend) solution is correct too but uses O(h) stack — the interviewer's follow-up is usually "can you do it in O(1) space?", which is this Morris approach.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Pre-order says left subtree comes right after the node, then the right subtree. For each node with a left child I splice the right subtree onto the tail of the left subtree — its rightmost node — then promote the left subtree to be the new right and clear the left pointer. Marching down the resulting right spine flattens everything in O(n) time and O(1) space, no stack needed."
📄 FlattenBinaryTreeToLinkedList.java

6 Find Leaves of Binary Tree LC 366 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Repeatedly collect and remove all current leaves until the tree is empty; return the groups in removal order.

💡 Key insight. The round in which a node gets stripped equals its height from the bottom: leaves are height 0, and any node leaves on round h = 1 + max(child heights). Compute that height bottom-up in one post-order pass and bucket each node by it directly — no repeated re-pruning.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Post-order recurse; a null child returns height -1 so a true leaf computes height 0.
  2. Compute h = 1 + max(height(left), height(right)).
  3. If h == result.size(), this is the first node seen at that height — append a new bucket.
  4. Add the node's value to result.get(h) and return h upward.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single post-order visit per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack (output excluded), h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The naive "find leaves, delete, repeat" is O(n²) on a degenerate tree — the height-bucketing reframing is the whole point and the reason this is a signature question. Returning -1 for null (not 0) is what makes leaf height come out as 0; mixing this up shifts every bucket. The result index is the height, so buckets are naturally ordered.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of literally peeling leaves layer by layer, I notice a node's removal round is just its height above the deepest leaf. One post-order pass gives every node its height, and I drop each node into the bucket indexed by that height — creating the bucket the first time I reach a new height. That turns an O(n²) simulation into a single O(n) traversal."
📄 FindLeavesOfBinaryTree.java

7 Step-By-Step Directions From a Binary Tree Node to Another LC 2096 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given a tree with unique values, return the shortest path of moves ('L', 'R', 'U') from the node valued startValue to the node valued destValue.

💡 Key insight. The shortest route climbs up to the lowest common ancestor, then descends. Build both root-to-target direction strings; their shared prefix is precisely the path down to the LCA. Strip that common prefix: the leftover of the start-path becomes all 'U' (climb), and the leftover of the dest-path is appended verbatim (descend) — no explicit LCA node needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. DFS once to record the root→start path and once for root→dest, appending 'L'/'R' and backtracking on dead ends.
  2. Compute the length of the longest common prefix of the two strings.
  3. Emit 'U' repeated (startLen - common) times to walk up from start to the LCA.
  4. Append the dest-path's suffix from common onward to descend to the target.

⏱️ Time O(n) — two root-to-target DFS passes 💾 Space O(h) — path buffers and recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The path-builder reuses one StringBuilder slot: it sets 'L', and on left-failure overwrites that same character with 'R' before trying right, then deletes on full backtrack — get the slot bookkeeping wrong and paths corrupt. All start-side steps become 'U' regardless of whether they were 'L' or 'R' originally, since climbing is direction-agnostic. Values are unique, which is what lets a value-targeted DFS work.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Any node-to-node path in a tree goes up to the LCA then down. I find the root paths to both endpoints as strings of L/R. Their common prefix is the descent into the LCA, so I discard it. Whatever remains of the start path I replace with that many 'U' moves to climb to the LCA, then I tack on the remaining dest path to go down. Two DFS passes, O(n) total."
📄 StepByStepDirections.java

8 Binary Tree Maximum Path Sum LC 124 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the maximum sum over all non-empty paths, where a path is any sequence of connected nodes and need not pass through the root.

💡 Key insight. Every maximal path has a single highest node where it "turns". At that apex the path sum is node.val + bestLeftDownward + bestRightDownward. A post-order pass returns the best straight downward path a parent can extend (one side only), while separately folding the turning sum into a global max. Clamping each child's contribution at 0 means "skip a branch that only hurts".
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Define gain(node) = the largest sum of a downward path starting at node.
  2. Compute left = max(gain(node.left), 0) and right = max(gain(node.right), 0) (drop negatives).
  3. Update the global best with node.val + left + right — the path that turns at this node.
  4. Return node.val + max(left, right), since a parent can only extend through one child.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one visit per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The value you return (single-branch extension) differs from the value you record (two-branch turn) — conflating them is the canonical mistake. Initialize the global best to Integer.MIN_VALUE, not 0, so all-negative trees (e.g. a single -3) return the least-negative node rather than 0. Clamping at 0 is what lets the algorithm ignore harmful subtrees.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I think of each path as turning at one topmost node. At that node the best path is its value plus the best downward gain from each side, where I clamp negative gains to zero to skip them. That turning value updates a global maximum. But to my parent I can only hand back a straight path through one child, so I return value plus the larger side. Single O(n) post-order pass, O(h) stack, and I seed the answer with negative infinity for all-negative inputs."
📄 BinaryTreeMaximumPathSum.java

9 Serialize and Deserialize Binary Tree LC 297 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Design serialize and deserialize so any binary tree can be encoded to a string and rebuilt identically.

💡 Key insight. A pre-order walk that emits an explicit marker (#) for every null pins down the shape uniquely. The first token is always the root, and each subtree is a self-delimiting prefix — so deserialize can rebuild by consuming tokens in the same root→left→right order, with no separate in-order traversal required.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Serialize: pre-order recurse; for null append "#,", otherwise append the value plus separator, then recurse left and right.
  2. Deserialize: split on the separator into a token iterator.
  3. Read one token: if it's # return null; else create the node and recurse to build left then right from the next tokens.
  4. The shared pre-order ordering makes serialize and deserialize mirror images.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one append / one token consumed per node and null slot 💾 Space O(n) — the output string (or token list) plus recursion stack

⚠️ Gotchas. Without null markers the shape is ambiguous (pre-order alone can't reconstruct a general binary tree). Negative values and multi-digit numbers work because tokens are split on the separator, not by character — don't try to read fixed-width digits. An empty tree must round-trip (serialize to just "#," and back to null). Level-order (BFS) encoding is an equally accepted alternative.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I serialize with a pre-order traversal, writing a sentinel for every null so the structure is unambiguous. Because pre-order lists the root first and each subtree forms a contiguous self-delimiting block, deserialize just walks the tokens in the same order: a sentinel means an empty child, otherwise build the node and recursively fill its left then right from the remaining tokens. Both directions are O(n)."
📄 SerializeAndDeserializeBinaryTree.java

10 Maximum Depth of Binary Tree LC 104 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the number of nodes along the longest path from the root down to the farthest leaf.

💡 Key insight. Depth is a pure post-order fold with no cross-subtree interaction: a node's depth is 1 + max(depth(left), depth(right)), and an empty subtree contributes 0. That single recurrence fully defines the answer at the root.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If the node is null, return 0 (an empty subtree has no depth).
  2. Recurse into both children to get their depths.
  3. Return 1 + Math.max(leftDepth, rightDepth) — this node plus the deeper side.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one visit per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The base case returning 0 (not 1) is what makes a single-node tree come out as depth 1. On a degenerate, fully-skewed tree the recursion stack is O(n); an iterative BFS counting levels is the O(n)-space alternative if stack depth is a concern.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Depth obeys one clean recurrence: a node is one level deeper than its deepest child, and an empty subtree is depth zero. So I post-order recurse, take the max of the two child depths, add one, and return it. No global state is needed because nothing crosses between subtrees. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 MaximumDepthOfBinaryTree.java

11 Invert Binary Tree LC 226 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Mirror the tree in place — swap every node's left and right children — and return the new root.

💡 Key insight. Inverting is mirroring, and mirroring is structurally recursive: swap a node's two children, then mirror each child. Crucially the swap commutes with the recursion (invert-then-swap equals swap-then-invert), so processing order is irrelevant — the subtree is correctly mirrored once both children are.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If the node is null, return null.
  2. Recursively invert the left and right subtrees, capturing the two results.
  3. Assign the inverted right result to root.left and the inverted left result to root.right.
  4. Return the (now mirrored) node.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one swap per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. Because the operation commutes with the recursion you can swap before or after recursing — but if you swap first and then reuse the old root.left/root.right references, capture them into temporaries to avoid reading already-overwritten pointers. An iterative BFS/DFS that swaps children as it dequeues is an equally valid O(n) approach.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Mirroring a tree just means swapping every node's two children. That's recursive: I mirror each subtree, then swap the two mirrored results at the current node. Since the swap commutes with the recursion, order doesn't matter — the whole thing is correct once both sides are done. One pass, O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 InvertBinaryTree.java

12 Balanced Binary Tree LC 110 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Decide whether the tree is height-balanced: at every node the heights of the two subtrees differ by at most 1.

💡 Key insight. Checking balance and measuring height are the same post-order pass. Fuse them by letting the height function return a -1 sentinel meaning "already unbalanced" — that -1 then poisons every ancestor and short-circuits the rest of the walk, avoiding the O(n²) of recomputing height at every node.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Define height(node): a null node returns 0.
  2. Recurse left; if it returns -1, propagate -1 immediately. Same for right.
  3. If abs(leftHeight - rightHeight) > 1, this node fails — return -1.
  4. Otherwise return 1 + max(leftHeight, rightHeight); the tree is balanced iff height(root) != -1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each node's height computed exactly once 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The naive solution calls a separate height() at every node while checking balance, which is O(n²) on a skewed tree — the -1 sentinel fusing the two passes is the whole point. You must short-circuit on the left subtree's -1 before recursing right, or you lose the early exit. Returning -1 is safe because a real height is always ≥ 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Balance and height come from the same bottom-up pass, so I compute height once and overload its return value: a normal height when the subtree is balanced, or -1 the moment any node violates the ≤ 1 rule. That -1 propagates straight to the root and short-circuits everything, turning the naive O(n²) into a single O(n) traversal."
📄 BalancedBinaryTree.java

13 Diameter of Binary Tree LC 543 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return the length (in edges) of the longest path between any two nodes, which need not pass through the root.

💡 Key insight. The longest path "turns" at a single highest node, where it spans the full depth on both sides — exactly leftHeight + rightHeight edges. So one post-order pass computes each node's height and, in passing, folds that turning width into a global max; the diameter is the largest such width over all nodes.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Define height(node) returning 0 for null.
  2. Recurse to get left and right child heights.
  3. Update best = max(best, left + right) — the path that turns at this node, counted in edges.
  4. Return 1 + max(left, right) upward; the answer is the accumulated best.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one visit per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The diameter is measured in edges, so left + right is correct and a single-node tree gives 0 — off-by-one bugs creep in if you count nodes instead. Like max-path-sum, the value you return (single-side height) differs from what you record (both sides joined); conflating them is the classic mistake. The longest path often doesn't pass through the root, which is why a global accumulator is needed.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Every candidate longest path bends at one topmost node, and there its length is just the heights of the two subtrees added together. So I run a normal height computation, and at each node I also update a global best with left-height plus right-height. I return only one side up to my parent, but I record both joined here. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 DiameterOfBinaryTree.java

14 Range Sum of BST LC 938 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given the root of a BST and an inclusive range [low, high], return the sum of all node values that fall within it.

💡 Key insight. The BST ordering lets you prune whole subtrees instead of visiting them. If a node's value is below low, its entire left subtree is also below low and cannot contribute, so you recurse right only — and symmetrically above high. Only when the node lies inside the range must both children be explored.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Null node contributes 0.
  2. If node.val < low, skip the left subtree entirely — recurse right only.
  3. If node.val > high, skip the right subtree — recurse left only.
  4. Otherwise add node.val and recurse into both children.

⏱️ Time O(n) worst case, but only boundary-path and in-range nodes are touched 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The pruning is the entire point — a naive full traversal that filters by range is correct but wastes work the BST structure rules out. The range is inclusive, so the boundary comparisons must be strict (< low / > high) to keep values exactly equal to low or high in the sum. Worst case (whole tree in range) is still O(n), but typical inputs touch far fewer nodes.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I exploit the BST order to avoid visiting subtrees that can't contribute. If the current value is below the low bound, everything to its left is even smaller, so I only go right; symmetrically for the high bound. When the node is inside the range I count it and explore both sides. That prunes large chunks while staying O(n) in the worst case, O(h) stack."
📄 RangeSumOfBST.java

15 Longest Univalue Path LC 687 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return the length (in edges) of the longest path where every node on the path has the same value; the path need not pass through the root.

💡 Key insight. Like the diameter, the longest same-value path turns at one apex — but the twist is what each child returns: the longest same-value "arrow" that can be extended through the parent, which is non-zero only when the child holds the same value as the parent (otherwise the chain breaks and the child contributes 0). At each node you join the two qualifying arrows for the turning length, but pass only one up.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Define arrow(node) returning 0 for null.
  2. Recurse to get left and right arrow lengths from the children.
  3. Set leftArrow = left + 1 only if the left child exists and equals node.val (else 0); same for rightArrow.
  4. Update best = max(best, leftArrow + rightArrow), and return max(leftArrow, rightArrow) upward.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one visit per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The value check must compare the child to the current node, gating whether that child's arrow can extend — a child's returned length is only usable if the values match. The arrow that turns at the apex (leftArrow + rightArrow) is recorded globally, but only one side (max) can be handed to the parent, since a path continues down a single branch. Counting edges (not nodes) is why a lone node returns 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's the diameter pattern with an equality gate. Each call returns the longest equal-valued chain reaching down from that node, but a child's chain only counts if the child's value matches the parent — otherwise it's cut to zero. At each node I join the left and right qualifying chains for a candidate answer, update a global max, and pass only the longer single side upward. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 LongestUnivaluePath.java

16 Kth Smallest Element in a BST LC 230 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return the k-th smallest value (1-indexed) among all nodes of a BST.

💡 Key insight. An in-order traversal of a BST visits values in ascending order, so the k-th node popped in an in-order walk is exactly the k-th smallest. Doing it iteratively with an explicit stack lets you stop the instant you pop the k-th node, touching only O(h + k) nodes rather than materializing the whole sorted sequence.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Maintain a stack and a cur pointer starting at the root.
  2. Dive left, pushing every node, until cur is null (reaching the smallest unvisited).
  3. Pop a node — the next value in ascending order; decrement k, and if it hits 0 return that value.
  4. Otherwise move cur to the popped node's right child and repeat.

⏱️ Time O(h + k) — descend to the smallest, then pop k nodes 💾 Space O(h) — the stack holds at most one root-to-leaf spine

⚠️ Gotchas. The early stop at --k == 0 is the whole efficiency win — a full in-order pass into a list is O(n) and discards the BST's advantage. k is 1-indexed, so decrement-then-test (not test-then-decrement) lands on the right node. If the interviewer says the tree is modified often and queries are frequent, the follow-up is to augment nodes with subtree counts for O(h) lookups.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "In-order traversal of a BST yields sorted order, so the k-th node I visit in-order is my answer. I do it iteratively with a stack: dive to the leftmost, pop the smallest, and each pop is the next value up. I count down k and return the moment I reach zero, so I only ever touch O(h + k) nodes instead of the whole tree. O(h) stack space."
📄 KthSmallestElementInABST.java

17 Lowest Common Ancestor of a BST LC 235 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given two nodes p and q in a BST, return their lowest common ancestor.

💡 Key insight. In a general tree (LC 236 above) the LCA needs a full post-order split search because membership can only be confirmed by visiting subtrees. A BST hands you that information for free: the ordering says which side each target lives on without looking. Walk straight down — both targets smaller means go left, both larger means go right; the first node where they fall on opposite sides (or one equals the node) is the split point. That's O(h) time and O(1) space instead of 236's O(n)/O(h) post-order.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Start at the root with an iterative pointer.
  2. If both p.val and q.val are less than the current value, move left.
  3. If both are greater, move right.
  4. Otherwise they split here (or one equals the node) — this is the LCA; return it.

⏱️ Time O(h) — a single root-to-split descent 💾 Space O(1) — iterative, no stack

⚠️ Gotchas. The contrast with the general-tree LCA (236) is the point: don't fall back to a post-order search when the BST property lets you decide direction by value comparison alone. The "split" includes the case where the current node is p or q (one value equals node.val), which correctly returns that node as an ancestor of itself. Comparison is by value here, valid because BST values are distinct.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "For a general tree I'd post-order search for the split point in O(n). But a BST tells me which subtree each node lives in just from its value, so I walk down from the root: if both targets are smaller I go left, if both larger I go right, and the first time they diverge — or one equals the current node — that node is the LCA. No recursion, O(h) time, O(1) space."
📄 LowestCommonAncestorOfABST.java

18 Construct Binary Tree from Preorder and Inorder LC 105 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Reconstruct a binary tree from its preorder and inorder traversal arrays (values are unique).

💡 Key insight. Preorder lists roots in the exact order we want to create them (root before either subtree), so a single left-to-right pointer over preorder always names the next root. That root's position in inorder splits the remaining inorder window into the left subtree (left of it) and right subtree (right of it). A value→inorder-index map makes each split O(1); recursing left-first keeps the preorder pointer synced with the roots it is about to consume.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build a value → inorder index map for O(1) splits, and keep a global preIdx starting at 0.
  2. Recurse over an inorder window [inLo, inHi]; if inLo > inHi the window is empty, return null.
  3. Take preorder[preIdx++] as the root and look up its inorder position mid.
  4. Build the left subtree from [inLo, mid-1] first (so preorder stays aligned), then the right from [mid+1, inHi].

⏱️ Time O(n) — each node created once, splits are O(1) via the map 💾 Space O(n) — the index map plus O(h) recursion

⚠️ Gotchas. You must recurse left before right, because the shared preIdx advances as you consume roots and the left subtree's roots come first in preorder — swapping the order corrupts the tree. The inorder index map is what turns an O(n²) linear-scan-for-root into O(n); without it every split rescans. Uniqueness of values is what lets the map work.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Preorder gives me roots top-down, so I sweep a single pointer through it to name each next root. For each root I find its spot in inorder, which partitions the inorder window into the left and right subtrees. I recurse left first so the preorder pointer stays aligned with the roots it's about to build. A precomputed value-to-index map makes each split O(1), so the whole build is O(n)."
📄 ConstructBinaryTreeFromPreorderAndInorder.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the list in the order above — it climbs from the two foundational traversals (BFS level-order, DFS-by-depth) to the post-order "return-a-summary" family, then the two Hards. From a blank file, re-derive: the BFS size-snapshot loop (102), the (low, high) bound recursion (98), the post-order split-point for LCA (236), and the two-value return-vs-record split in max-path-sum (124) — that last one is the single best discriminator of recursion fluency. Rehearse these Google-favourite follow-ups out loud: "flatten in O(1) space" (114, Morris), "serialize with level-order instead" (297), and "what changes if the tree is a BST?" for LCA (236, you can walk down by value comparison in O(h)).

  • ✅ I can explain why a queue's size at the top of the loop equals the current level's node count.
  • ✅ I can explain why local parent-child checks fail for BST validity and how bounds fix it.
  • ✅ I can explain why the LCA is the post-order node whose left and right searches both return non-null.
  • ✅ I can articulate the difference between what max-path-sum returns versus what it records globally.
  • ✅ I can re-derive Find Leaves as height-from-bottom bucketing and say why it beats O(n²).
  • ✅ I can reconstruct a tree from a pre-order-with-null-markers string and explain why the markers are essential.

🎄 Tree DP / Rerooting

Tree dynamic programming solves a problem on a rooted tree by combining answers from each node's children during a single post-order pass; rerooting extends this so you get the answer "as if rooted here" for every node in O(n) via a second pre-order pass. It is high-ROI for Google L6 because returning a small tuple of states per node — and reasoning about how that tuple recombines — is the unifying technique behind a cluster of Medium/Hard tree problems that look unrelated on the surface.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • A constraint couples a node to its children/parent ("can't pick adjacent nodes", "cover this node"), so each node's optimum depends on its children's states.
  • You can describe the answer at a node as a small fixed tuple — e.g. {take, skip} or three coverage states — that merges from the children's tuples.
  • "For every node, compute X over the whole tree" (sum of distances, max path through each node) → two-pass rerooting, not n independent DFS runs.
  • A greedy bottom-up decision that "pushes work up to the parent" (cameras on leaves' parents) — classic tree-DP-as-greedy.
  • Recursion that returns a value AND mutates a shared accumulator (counting cameras, best path) as it unwinds.

🧠 Core idea & template

Post-order subtree DP. Recurse to both children first, then compute this node's answer from theirs. Return whatever the parent needs — usually a tuple of states, not a single number — so the parent can choose. House Robber III returns {rob, skip}; Binary Tree Cameras returns one of three coverage states. The shape is always: leaf/null base case → recurse children → combine → return.

Two-pass rerooting. When you need the answer rooted at every node, n separate DFS runs are O(n²). Instead: Pass 1 (post-order from an arbitrary root) computes subtree aggregates (sizes, partial sums) and the answer at the root. Pass 2 (pre-order) derives each child's answer from its parent's already-known answer in O(1), using how the metric changes when the root moves one edge. Total O(n).

// ---- Post-order subtree DP: return a tuple of states per node ----
int[] dfs(TreeNode node) {           // e.g. {robThis, skipThis}
    if (node == null) return new int[]{0, 0};
    int[] l = dfs(node.left), r = dfs(node.right);
    int take = node.val + l[1] + r[1];                       // children must be skipped
    int skip = Math.max(l[0], l[1]) + Math.max(r[0], r[1]);  // children free to choose
    return new int[]{take, skip};
}

// ---- Two-pass rerooting (answer for every node) ----
void post(int u, int p) {            // subtree sizes + ans at the chosen root
    cnt[u] = 1;
    for (int v : adj.get(u)) if (v != p) {
        post(v, u);
        cnt[u] += cnt[v];
        ans[u] += ans[v] + cnt[v];   // every node in v's subtree is one edge farther
    }
}
void pre(int u, int p, int n) {      // shift the root from u to each child v
    for (int v : adj.get(u)) if (v != p) {
        ans[v] = ans[u] - cnt[v] + (n - cnt[v]); // cnt[v] closer, n-cnt[v] farther
        pre(v, u, n);
    }
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1House Robber III LC 337 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav Post-order {rob, skip} DPO(n)O(h)
2Binary Tree Cameras LC 968 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature Greedy post-order, 3 statesO(n)O(h)
3Sum of Distances in Tree LC 834 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature Two-pass rerootingO(n)O(n)

📘 Deep dive

1 House Robber III LC 337 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Houses form a binary tree; robbing two directly-linked (parent–child) houses trips the alarm. Return the maximum money robbable.

💡 Key insight. The adjacency rule means a node's choice depends only on whether its children were robbed, so one post-order pass returns a pair {robThis, skipThis}. robThis = node.val + leftSkip + rightSkip (robbing forces skipping both children); skipThis = max(leftRob, leftSkip) + max(rightRob, rightSkip) (each child independently takes its best). Returning the pair avoids the exponential recomputation of a naïve "rob or not" recursion.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Define dfs(node) returning {maxIfRobbed, maxIfSkipped}; null returns {0, 0}.
  2. Recurse left and right first. Compute robThis = node.val + l[1] + r[1] and skipThis = max(l[0], l[1]) + max(r[0], r[1]).
  3. Return {robThis, skipThis}; the final answer is max(root.rob, root.skip).

⏱️ Time O(n) — each node visited once. 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height.

⚠️ Gotchas. The classic mistake is memoizing on the node alone while recursing into grandchildren (the "rob, then skip children, recurse grandchildren" formulation) — correct but messier; the tuple form is cleaner and avoids a HashMap. In skipThis each child takes its own max independently — don't force both children into the same choice. Remember the answer is the max of the root's two states, not just robThis.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Robbing a node bans robbing its children, so for each node I return two numbers: the best if I rob it (its value plus both children's skip-values) and the best if I skip it (each child's better option). One post-order pass computes this in O(n), O(h) stack. The naïve recursion recomputes overlapping subproblems exponentially; returning the pair collapses that."
📄 HouseRobberIII.java

2 Binary Tree Cameras LC 968 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Place the fewest cameras on tree nodes so that every node is monitored; a camera covers its own node, its parent, and its children.

💡 Key insight. Cameras are most efficient on the parents of leaves, so decide greedily bottom-up with three returned states: 0 = uncovered, 1 = covered but no camera here, 2 = camera here. If either child is uncovered (0), this node MUST hold a camera (count it, return 2); if either child has a camera (2), this node is covered (return 1); if both children are merely covered, this node is uncovered (return 0) and defers upward. Treat null as covered (1) so leaves return uncovered and push a camera up one level.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. dfs(null) returns 1 (null is "already covered" so it never forces a wasted camera on a leaf).
  2. Recurse both children. If l == 0 || r == 0, a child is exposed: increment cameras and return 2.
  3. Else if l == 2 || r == 2, a child's camera covers this node: return 1.
  4. Else both children are covered but nothing watches this node: return 0.
  5. After the traversal, if the root came back 0 (uncovered), add one final camera.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one post-order visit per node. 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height.

⚠️ Gotchas. The base case is the crux: returning 0 for null would wrongly put cameras on leaves; it must be 1. Don't forget the post-root check — if the root ends uncovered, it needs its own camera (the recursion can't push past the root). The state ordering matters: test "child uncovered" before "child has camera". This greedy is provably optimal here, but say so — interviewers probe whether you can justify greediness over a full DP.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I want cameras on parents of leaves, so I go bottom-up with three states per node: uncovered, covered-without-camera, has-camera. If a child is uncovered I'm forced to place a camera here; if a child has a camera I'm covered; otherwise I'm uncovered and let my parent handle me. Null counts as covered so leaves bubble a camera up. One O(n) pass, plus a final check to cover the root if it's still exposed."
📄 BinaryTreeCameras.java

3 Sum of Distances in Tree LC 834 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. For every node in an undirected tree of n nodes, return the sum of distances from that node to all others.

💡 Key insight. Computing each node's answer independently is O(n²); rerooting does it in O(n) with two DFS passes. Pass 1 (post-order from node 0) computes count[v] = subtree size and ans[0] = sum of distances from the root, via ans[0] += ans[child] + count[child] (every subtree node sits one edge deeper). Pass 2 (pre-order) shifts the root from a parent to a child: the count[child] nodes below move one step closer and the remaining n − count[child] nodes move one step farther, so ans[child] = ans[parent] − count[child] + (n − count[child]).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build an undirected adjacency list. Handle n == 1 (answer [0]).
  2. postOrder(0, −1): set count[node] = 1; for each child, recurse, then add count[child] to this count and ans[child] + count[child] to ans[node]. This fills all subtree sizes and the root's answer.
  3. preOrder(0, −1): for each child, set ans[child] = ans[node] − count[child] + (n − count[child]) using the parent's finished answer, then recurse.
  4. Return ans[].

⏱️ Time O(n) — two linear DFS traversals over n−1 edges. 💾 Space O(n) — adjacency lists, count/ans arrays, and recursion stack.

⚠️ Gotchas. Edges are undirected — track the parent to avoid recursing back. The rerooting formula only works because ans[parent] is already final when you compute the child (hence pre-order, parent before child). Deriving that −count[child] + (n − count[child]) transition is the whole problem — practise stating it crisply. For very deep trees, the recursion can stack-overflow; mention an explicit stack as a fallback.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A separate BFS per node is O(n²); rerooting makes it O(n). First a post-order pass gives me every subtree size and the root's total distance, accumulating each child's answer plus its subtree size. Then a pre-order pass slides the root down one edge at a time: nodes in the child's subtree get one closer, everyone else one farther, so ans[child] = ans[parent] − count[child] + (n − count[child]). Two linear passes, O(n) overall."
📄 SumOfDistancesInTree.java

🔁 Practice plan

Order: 337 to lock in "return a tuple of states per node", then 968 for the three-state greedy bottom-up decision (and articulating why it's optimal), then 834 for the full two-pass rerooting transition. From a blank file, re-derive the {rob, skip} recurrence, the camera state machine with its null base case and post-root fix-up, and the rerooting −count + (n − count) formula. Practise saying each transition out loud in one sentence.

  • ✅ I can explain why returning a state tuple beats a single number in tree DP.
  • ✅ I can justify the greedy camera placement and its null = covered base case.
  • ✅ I can derive the rerooting transition and explain why pre-order ordering is required.
  • ✅ Follow-ups to rehearse: Binary Tree Maximum Path Sum (LC 124, return-vs-accumulate), Diameter of Binary Tree (LC 543), and Distribute Coins in Binary Tree (LC 979, flow up the tree).

🌲 Tries

A trie (prefix tree) stores a set of strings as shared root-to-node paths, turning prefix queries into a single walk down character edges. It is high-ROI for Google L6 because the pattern shows up whenever a problem hinges on shared prefixes — autocomplete, dictionary matching, multi-word grid search — and because pairing a trie with a grid DFS is a clean, interview-favorite way to collapse repeated work. The same "store a future gate in a map" discipline also powers the streaming rate-limiter design that frequently rides alongside this topic.

Source: Prep Tracker tab · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The problem talks about prefixes, startsWith, autocomplete, or "does any word begin with…" — the defining trie use case.
  • You must match many words at once against some text or grid; searching each independently re-walks shared prefixes.
  • A 2-D board where you collect all dictionary words formable by adjacent cells → trie + backtracking DFS with prefix pruning.
  • Fixed small alphabet (e.g. 26 lowercase letters) makes a TrieNode[26] array of children natural and cache-friendly.
  • A streaming/timestamped "allow this only once per window" design — a hash-map gate is the trie-chapter cousin of these structural problems.

🧠 Core idea & template

A trie node owns one child slot per alphabet symbol plus a flag (or stored word) marking where an inserted word ends. Insert walks the string, creating missing children, and marks the terminal node. Search/startsWith walk the same path; reaching the end means the prefix exists, and the terminal flag distinguishes a real word from a path that's merely a prefix. The big win in grid problems: the trie's edges tell you exactly which next characters are still worth exploring, so a DFS branch dies the instant it stops being a prefix of any word.

class TrieNode {
    TrieNode[] children = new TrieNode[26];
    boolean isWord;            // (or: String word; at the terminal node)
}

void insert(TrieNode root, String w) {
    TrieNode node = root;
    for (int i = 0; i < w.length(); i++) {
        int c = w.charAt(i) - 'a';
        if (node.children[c] == null) node.children[c] = new TrieNode();
        node = node.children[c];
    }
    node.isWord = true;       // mark the terminal node
}

// Follow the path for s; null if it breaks. search() also checks isWord.
TrieNode walk(TrieNode root, String s) {
    TrieNode node = root;
    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
        int c = s.charAt(i) - 'a';
        if (node.children[c] == null) return null;
        node = node.children[c];
    }
    return node;
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Implement Trie (Prefix Tree) LC 208 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core 26-ary prefix treeO(L) / opO(total chars)
2Logger Rate Limiter LC 359 ↗ Easy ⭐ Google fav Hash map of next-allowed timeO(1) / callO(D)
3Word Search II LC 212 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core Trie + grid DFS backtrackingO(M·N·4·3^(L-1))O(total chars)

📘 Deep dive

1 Implement Trie (Prefix Tree) LC 208 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Implement a trie supporting insert, search (exact word), and startsWith (any word with this prefix).

💡 Key insight. A prefix tree where each node owns 26 child slots, one per lowercase letter, so a word is a root-to-node path. An isWord flag marks where a complete inserted word ends — that flag is exactly what separates a real word from a path that's merely a prefix.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. insert: walk the word, allocating any missing child, then set isWord = true on the terminal node.
  2. search: walk the word; return node != null && node.isWord.
  3. startsWith: walk the prefix; the path simply existing (walk != null) is enough.
  4. A shared private walk helper follows a string and returns the final node or null.

⏱️ Time O(L) per op — one step per character of length L 💾 Space O(total characters inserted) — up to 26 children per node

⚠️ Gotchas. The whole point of isWord: after inserting "apple", search("app") must be false while startsWith("app") is true. Don't conflate the two. Convert chars with ch - 'a' and assume the stated alphabet. For arbitrary/Unicode keys, swap the array for a Map<Character, Node>.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I model each node as 26 child links plus a boolean for whether a word ends here. Insert walks the string creating nodes as needed and flags the last one; search walks and checks that flag; startsWith just checks the path exists. Every operation is linear in the key length, independent of how many words I've stored."
📄 ImplementTrie.java

2 Logger Rate Limiter LC 359 ↗ Easy ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Print each unique message at most once every 10 seconds; shouldPrintMessage(timestamp, message) returns whether it's allowed now.

💡 Key insight. A message is allowed iff at least 10 seconds have passed since it last printed. Instead of storing the last-printed time, store the earliest timestamp it may print again (lastPrinted + 10); then the check is one comparison and the update is one map write. Timestamps arrive non-decreasing, so the stored gate is always a valid future bound.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep Map<String, Integer> nextAllowed.
  2. Look up the message; if present and timestamp < allowedAt, return false (inside cooldown).
  3. Otherwise set nextAllowed[message] = timestamp + 10 and return true.

⏱️ Time O(1) per call — one hash lookup, at most one insert 💾 Space O(D) — one entry per distinct message ever printed

⚠️ Gotchas. The boundary is "exactly 10s later is allowed": store +10 and reject only when timestamp < allowedAt (strict <), so a message gated until 11 prints at 11, not 12. Storing the next-allowed time (vs. last-seen) saves an addition per call and reads more clearly. Space grows unboundedly with distinct messages — for an infinite stream you'd want a sliding window / eviction, a great follow-up to raise.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "For each message I remember the next timestamp it's allowed to print — last time plus ten. A call is allowed if its timestamp has reached that gate, in which case I push the gate forward. It's O(1) per call. The catch is the boundary at exactly ten seconds, and the fact that memory grows with the number of distinct messages, which I'd bound with eviction if the stream were unbounded."
📄 LoggerRateLimiter.java

3 Word Search II LC 212 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a 2-D board of letters and a dictionary, return every word formable by a path of adjacent (up/down/left/right) cells, no cell reused within a word.

💡 Key insight. Searching each word independently re-walks the same grid prefixes over and over. Instead, build ONE trie of all words and DFS the grid a single time: the trie's edges say which characters are still worth pursuing, so a path dies the moment it stops being a prefix of any word. Storing the full word at its terminal node makes collection O(1), and nulling that word after capture deduplicates words reachable by multiple paths.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build a trie from words, storing the full word string at each terminal node.
  2. Start a DFS from every cell, descending the trie by the cell's letter; return immediately if there's no matching child (prefix prune).
  3. When a node carries a word, add it to the result and null it out (dedupe).
  4. Mark the cell '#' before recursing into the 4 neighbours; restore it afterward (backtrack).

⏱️ Time O(M·N·4·3^(L-1)) — each cell seeds a DFS bounded by trie depth L, branching to 3 unused neighbours after the first step 💾 Space O(total characters in words) for the trie, plus O(L) recursion depth

⚠️ Gotchas. The trie prune is what makes this tractable — without it you'd DFS blindly. Mark visited in place ('#') and always restore on the way out, or sibling paths break. Null the captured word so the same word found via two paths isn't reported twice. Storing the whole word at the terminal node avoids reconstructing it from the path. Optional optimization: prune dead trie leaves after capture to speed later cells.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Running Word Search I per word repeats the same prefix walks, so I put all the words in one trie and walk the grid once. At each cell I descend the trie by that letter; if there's no edge, the prefix can't extend any word, so I stop. When I hit a node that ends a word I record it and clear it to avoid duplicates. I mark cells visited in place and restore them on backtrack. The trie turns a pile of independent searches into one pruned DFS."
📄 WordSearchII.java

🔁 Practice plan

Build LC 208 from a blank file first — the insert/walk helper and the isWord-vs-prefix distinction are the foundation everything else reuses. Then do LC 359 as a palate-cleanser on the "store a future gate" idea, being precise about the boundary. Finish with LC 212, re-deriving the trie-backed DFS and articulating exactly where the prefix prune fires. Rehearse these Google-favorite follow-ups out loud: LC 211 (Add and Search Word, with . wildcards forcing a branching DFS through the trie), LC 648 (Replace Words — shortest-root replacement), and the streaming-eviction variant of the rate limiter.

  • ✅ I can explain why search needs the isWord flag but startsWith does not.
  • ✅ I can state each trie operation's cost as O(L) and why it's independent of the number of stored words.
  • ✅ I can justify storing the next-allowed timestamp in the rate limiter and nail the exactly-10s boundary.
  • ✅ I can describe how a trie prunes the Word Search II DFS and where a branch is abandoned.
  • ✅ I can explain the in-place visited marking and why the captured word is nulled to dedupe.
  • ✅ I can adapt the array-of-26 children to a hash-map of children for arbitrary alphabets.

⛰️ Heap / Priority Queue

Heaps shine whenever you need the smallest or largest element of a changing set without fully sorting it — "top-k", "k closest", "running median", "sliding extreme." The Google L6 signal is knowing when a bounded heap (size k) beats a full sort, when twin heaps beat one, and when a monotonic deque, bucket sort, or quickselect is the sharper tool than a literal PriorityQueue.

Source: Prep Tracker, Deep Research Adds & curated Staff gaps · 6 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Top k / k largest / k closest / k most frequent" ⇒ a bounded heap of size k (evict the worst), or quickselect / bucket sort for a flat array.
  • "Running median" or "balance the smaller and larger halves" ⇒ two heaps (max-heap low, min-heap high) kept size-balanced.
  • "Maximum / minimum over every sliding window" ⇒ a monotonic deque, not a heap — O(n) instead of O(n log n).
  • "K-way merge" of sorted streams ⇒ a min-heap seeded with each stream's head.
  • Need order statistics that drop the extremes (trimmed mean) ⇒ ordered multisets / TreeMaps partitioned by rank.
  • Comparisons on distance ⇒ compare squared distance to avoid sqrt and stay integer-exact.

🧠 Core idea & template

The reusable trick for "top k" is a bounded heap of the opposite polarity: to keep the k largest, use a min-heap of size k so the weakest survivor sits on top and is cheapest to evict. For order statistics on a static array, quickselect partitions to the answer's rank in O(n) average without sorting. For a streaming median, two balanced heaps keep both middle elements at the tops.

// ── Bounded heap: keep the k LARGEST with a size-k MIN-heap ──
PriorityQueue<Integer> heap = new PriorityQueue<>();   // min-heap
for (int x : nums) {
    heap.offer(x);
    if (heap.size() > k) heap.poll();   // drop the smallest → top-k survive
}
// heap.peek() is now the k-th largest.

// ── Quickselect: k-th largest = (n-k)-th smallest ──
int target = n - k, lo = 0, hi = n - 1;
while (lo < hi) {
    int p = partition(a, lo, hi);       // pivot lands at its sorted rank
    if (p == target) break;
    else if (p < target) lo = p + 1;
    else hi = p - 1;
}
return a[target];

// ── Two heaps for a streaming median ──
// max-heap `low` (smaller half), min-heap `high` (larger half);
// keep low.size() == high.size() or low.size() == high.size()+1;
// median = low.peek() (odd) or (low.peek()+high.peek())/2.0 (even).

🗺️ Problems at a glance

📘 Deep dive

1 Top K Frequent Elements LC 347 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the k most frequently occurring values in an array.

💡 Key insight. A frequency can never exceed n, so index buckets by frequency: bucket[f] lists every value seen exactly f times. Walking buckets from high frequency down and collecting until we have k values is linear — no heap, no log factor.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Count occurrences into a frequency map.
  2. Scatter each value into bucket[frequency] (array of size n+1).
  3. Walk buckets from n down to 1, appending values until k are collected.

⏱️ Time O(n) — count, scatter, then one descending pass over n+1 buckets. 💾 Space O(n) — the frequency map plus the bucket array.

⚠️ Gotchas. The bucket array must be sized n+1 because a single value repeated n times has frequency n. The heap alternative (size-k min-heap on frequency) is simpler to reason about but O(n log k) — slower than the bucket sort.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Frequencies are bounded by n, so I bucket values by their count and read the buckets top-down until I have k. That's a linear-time bucket sort, beating the O(n log k) heap. I'd reach for the heap only if k were tiny and I wanted the simpler code."
📄 TopKFrequentElements.java

2 K Closest Points to Origin LC 973 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return the k points nearest the origin from a list of 2-D points.

💡 Key insight. Keep a max-heap of the k closest points seen so far, keyed on squared distance (no sqrt — it's monotonic, so comparisons stay exact and integer-cheap). For each new point, if it's nearer than the current farthest (the heap top), swap it in. The heap stays at size k, so we never sort all n points.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Max-heap by squared distance so the farthest of the current best-k sits on top.
  2. Offer each point; if the heap exceeds k, poll (evict the farthest).
  3. Drain the heap into the result.

⏱️ Time O(n log k) — n points, each an O(log k) heap op. 💾 Space O(k) — the heap holds at most k points.

⚠️ Gotchas. Compute squared distance in long to avoid overflow when coordinates are large. Use a max-heap (not min) so the element you evict is the current farthest. Quickselect gives O(n) average but mutates the array and degrades to O(n²) on bad pivots — the heap's O(n log k) is the stable choice.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep a size-k max-heap on squared distance — squaring avoids the sqrt and keeps everything integer. Each point either displaces the current farthest or is dropped, so I only ever hold k points and finish in n log k. Quickselect is the O(n) alternative if mutating the input is fine."
📄 KClosestPointsToOrigin.java

3 Kth Largest Element in an Array LC 215 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return the k-th largest element of an unsorted array (by value, not distinctness).

💡 Key insight. The k-th largest is the (n-k)-th smallest in sorted order. Quickselect Lomuto-partitions around a pivot so everything left of its final index is smaller; that index is the pivot's sorted rank, so we recurse into only the side that can contain the target — average O(n) because the work halves each step. Picking the pivot median-of-three (first/mid/last) deterministically dodges the sorted-input worst case while staying reproducible (no Math.random).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Set target = n - k (the sorted-ascending index of the answer).
  2. Partition [lo, hi]: choose the median-of-three pivot, park it at hi, scan moving smaller elements left, then drop the pivot into its slot.
  3. If the pivot's index equals target stop; else recurse left or right depending on which side holds target.
  4. Return nums[target].

⏱️ Time O(n) average (geometric sum n + n/2 + …); O(n²) worst case on adversarial pivots. 💾 Space O(1) — partitions in place with an iterative loop.

⚠️ Gotchas. The n-k index conversion is the classic off-by-one — "k-th largest" maps to the (n-k)-th smallest. Median-of-three (not a fixed first/last pivot) is what keeps sorted or reverse-sorted inputs from triggering the quadratic case. Duplicates are fine; the partition is by value. If you need streaming or non-mutating behaviour, a size-k min-heap (O(n log k)) is the safer pick.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I reframe k-th largest as the (n-k)-th smallest and quickselect to that rank: each partition fixes one pivot at its true position and I recurse into only the relevant half, so it's linear on average. I pick the pivot median-of-three to avoid the sorted-input blow-up, and it mutates the array — if that's not allowed I'd use a size-k heap instead."
📄 KthLargestElementInAnArray.java

4 Sliding Window Maximum LC 239 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. For every contiguous window of size k, report its maximum.

💡 Key insight. Maintain a deque of indices whose values are strictly decreasing front-to-back; the front is always the current window's maximum. A new element evicts every smaller value from the back (they can never be the max while this larger, newer element is in window), and indices that fall off the left edge are dropped from the front. Each index enters and leaves once, so the whole sweep is linear.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each i, drop the front index if it has slid past i - k.
  2. Pop from the back while nums[back] < nums[i], then push i.
  3. Once i ≥ k-1, record nums[front] as that window's max.

⏱️ Time O(n) — every index is pushed and popped at most once. 💾 Space O(k) — the deque holds at most one window's worth of indices.

⚠️ Gotchas. Store indices, not values, so you can tell when the front has expired off the window. The deque is strictly decreasing — popping on < (keeping equal duplicates) is fine and avoids losing a tie that's still valid. A size-k heap also works but is O(n log k); the deque is the asymptotically optimal answer the interviewer wants.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep a deque of indices with decreasing values so the front is the window max. New elements kick out smaller ones from the back since they're now dominated, and I expire indices off the left. Each index is added and removed once, so it's linear — strictly better than the O(n log n) heap."
📄 SlidingWindowMaximum.java

5 Find Median from Data Stream LC 295 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Support addNum and findMedian over a growing stream of numbers.

💡 Key insight. Split the stream into two halves by value: a max-heap holds the smaller half (its top is the largest low) and a min-heap holds the larger half (its top is the smallest high). Keeping the two tops adjacent in sorted order means the median is the larger heap's top (odd count) or the average of both tops (even count). The size invariant — low.size() equals high.size() or is one larger — makes the median an O(1) read after each O(log n) insert.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Insert into low, then move low's max into high (so high holds genuinely larger values).
  2. If high is now larger than low, move its min back to low to restore balance.
  3. Median: low.peek() if low is bigger, else (low.peek() + high.peek()) / 2.0.

⏱️ Time addNum O(log n) — one or two heap ops; findMedian O(1) — inspect tops. 💾 Space O(n) — every element lives in exactly one heap.

⚠️ Gotchas. The "push to low, hand its top to high, then rebalance" dance guarantees both ordering (every low ≤ every high) and the size invariant in one pass — skipping a step lets the halves drift out of order. Divide by 2.0 (not integer 2) for the even-count average. For a bounded sliding-window median you'd need removable heaps or an ordered multiset instead.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep the lower half in a max-heap and the upper half in a min-heap, balanced so their tops straddle the middle. Each insert is a couple of heap operations to route the value and rebalance, and the median is just a peek — O(log n) to add, O(1) to read."
📄 FindMedianFromDataStream.java

6 Finding MK Average LC 1825 ↗ Hard 🆕 Recent 2025-26

Problem. Over the last m elements of a stream, drop the k smallest and k largest and return the integer mean of the rest; -1 until m elements exist.

💡 Key insight. Partition the window's m values into three ordered multisets by rank: bottom (the k smallest), mid (the middle m-2k), and top (the k largest). Each multiset caches its count and running sum, so the answer is simply mid.sum / mid.count in O(1). The only real work is keeping the partition correct as values stream in and age out — a bounded rebalance shuffles boundary elements across the two cut lines.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. addElement: append to the window, provisionally add to mid; if the window now exceeds m, evict the oldest from whichever tier holds it.
  2. rebalance: Phase 1 fixes sizes (bottom and top must each hold exactly k, pulling from / pushing to mid); Phase 2 fixes order across the bottom|mid and mid|top boundaries by swapping out-of-place extremes.
  3. calculateMKAverage: return -1 if fewer than m elements, else mid.sum / mid.count.

⏱️ Time addElement O(log m) — a few TreeMap ops; calculateMKAverage O(1) — cached sum. 💾 Space O(m) — the stream window plus the three multisets over those m values.

⚠️ Gotchas. Use a TreeMap<value, count> as the multiset so duplicate values (very common in this problem) are counted, not collapsed. Eviction must route the aged-out value to the correct tier by comparing against bottom.max() / top.min(), then rebalance. Maintain the invariant every bottom ≤ every mid ≤ every top, or the trimmed mean reads the wrong band. The cached sums are what make the query O(1) — recomputing would be O(m).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep the window split into three ranked multisets — the bottom k, the middle, and the top k — each caching its sum, so the trimmed mean is an O(1) read. On each insert and eviction I touch one multiset then rebalance a bounded number of boundary elements across the two cuts, which is O(log m). TreeMaps give me the min/max and counting I need with duplicates."
📄 FindingMKAverage.java

🔁 Practice plan

Start from the bounded-heap idiom (973) and immediately contrast it with the two non-heap optimisations the interviewer will probe: bucket sort on Top K Frequent (347) and quickselect on Kth Largest (215) — be able to state when each beats the heap. Then drill the two-heaps median (295) and the monotonic deque (239) until both come out clean from a blank file, since they're the "you should know the optimal, not just the heap" gotchas. Finish with MK Average (1825) as the ordered-multiset capstone. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse aloud: "now make the median support removals / a sliding window", and "now return the top-k in sorted order".

  • ✅ I can explain why keeping the k largest uses a size-k min-heap (and vice versa).
  • ✅ I can write quickselect, convert "k-th largest" to the (n-k)-th smallest, and justify median-of-three.
  • ✅ I can argue the sliding-window maximum deque is O(n) because each index is pushed and popped once.
  • ✅ I can maintain two balanced heaps and prove the median is an O(1) read.
  • ✅ I can explain why I compare squared distance and why I use long for it.
  • ✅ I can describe the three-multiset partition for a trimmed-mean stream and why cached sums keep the query O(1).

🗂️ Ordered-Set / Data-Stream

This family is about maintaining order statistics over a moving window — nearest neighbor by value, or the median — when elements both enter and leave. The tools are a balanced ordered set (TreeSet floor/ceiling), value bucketing, or two heaps with lazy deletion. It's high-ROI for Google L6 because the naive answers are easy but the windowed/streaming versions force genuine data-structure judgment: the median problem in particular is a classic Google on-site that tests whether you can make a heap support an operation it doesn't natively have.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 2 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Within k indices AND within t in value" → a window over indices + a structure that finds the nearest value (bucketing or TreeSet floor/ceiling).
  • "Median / k-th smallest of every window of size k" → two balanced heaps (or a balanced BST / two multisets).
  • Elements leave the window, and your structure can't delete an arbitrary element cheaply (a heap) → reach for lazy deletion.
  • You need nearest value below/above a query, not just min/max → TreeSet.floor / TreeSet.ceiling, or bucket id ± 1.
  • Overflow alarm: averaging two large ints for a median → compute a + (b-a)/2 in long/double.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two reusable idioms. (A) Bucketing for "near in value": map each value to a bucket of width t+1 so two values in the same bucket are automatically within t; only the two adjacent buckets can also be within t, and you check those explicitly. Keep one representative per bucket and evict the one leaving the window. (B) Two heaps + lazy deletion for "median of window": a max-heap holds the smaller half and a min-heap the larger half; since a heap can't remove an arbitrary leaving element, you stash it in a "pending removal" map and only physically discard it when it surfaces to a top, tracking logical sizes for rebalancing.

// (A) Bucketing: same bucket => within t; else check neighbors ±1.
long width = (long) t + 1;
Map<Long, Long> bucket = new HashMap<>();          // bucket id -> a value in it
for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
    long id = Math.floorDiv((long) nums[i], width); // floorDiv so negatives bucket right
    if (bucket.containsKey(id)) return true;
    if (bucket.containsKey(id-1) && nums[i] - bucket.get(id-1) <= t) return true;
    if (bucket.containsKey(id+1) && bucket.get(id+1) - nums[i] <= t) return true;
    bucket.put(id, (long) nums[i]);
    if (i >= k) bucket.remove(Math.floorDiv((long) nums[i-k], width)); // evict out-of-window
}

// (B) Two heaps + lazy deletion: median over a sliding window of size k.
PriorityQueue<Integer> low  = new PriorityQueue<>(Comparator.reverseOrder()); // max-heap
PriorityQueue<Integer> high = new PriorityQueue<>();                          // min-heap
Map<Integer,Integer> pending = new HashMap<>();   // value -> times marked for removal
// insert into the right half; on exit, pending.merge(out,1,sum) + adjust logical sizes;
// rebalance so lowSize == highSize or highSize+1; prune() ghosts off each top before peeking.

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Contains Duplicate III LC 220 ↗ Hard ◻️ Gap-fill Value bucketing (or TreeSet floor/ceiling) over an index window O(n)O(min(n, indexDiff))
2Sliding Window Median LC 480 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged Two heaps + lazy deletion O(n log k)O(k)

📘 Deep dive

1 Contains Duplicate III LC 220 ↗ Hard ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Is there a pair of indices i, j with |i-j| <= indexDiff and |nums[i]-nums[j]| <= valueDiff?

💡 Key insight. Bucket values into ranges of width valueDiff+1 so any two numbers in the same bucket are guaranteed within valueDiff; the only other candidates within valueDiff live in the two adjacent buckets, which you check explicitly. Slide a window of width indexDiff over the indices keeping one representative per bucket — landing in an occupied bucket, or finding an adjacent-bucket neighbor within valueDiff, is an answer. Evicting the bucket of the element that fell out of the window keeps at most indexDiff buckets live, giving O(n).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Reject degenerate inputs: indexDiff <= 0 or valueDiff < 0 → false.
  2. Set width = (long)valueDiff + 1 and a map bucketId -> value.
  3. For each i: compute id = floorDiv(nums[i], width). If id is occupied → true. Else check buckets id-1 and id+1 for an actual gap <= valueDiff.
  4. Store nums[i] in bucket id; once i >= indexDiff, remove the bucket of nums[i-indexDiff] (it just left the window).

⏱️ Time O(n) — each index does O(1) bucket lookups plus one eviction 💾 Space O(min(n, indexDiff)) — buckets retained for the current window

⚠️ Gotchas. Bucket width must be valueDiff+1, not valueDiff — otherwise a same-bucket pair could be exactly valueDiff+1 apart. Use Math.floorDiv, not /: Java truncates toward zero, which would mis-bucket negatives (e.g. −1 and 0 must stay separable). Use long for values and arithmetic — valueDiff up to ~2³¹ and negative nums overflow int on the difference. The TreeSet floor/ceiling alternative is correct but O(n log k); bucketing is the O(n) answer to mention.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force checks all pairs in the window at O(n·k). The trick is bucketing values into ranges of width t+1: any two numbers sharing a bucket differ by at most t for free, and the only other within-t candidates are in the two neighboring buckets, which I check directly. I keep one value per bucket and evict whatever leaves the index window, so I hold O(k) buckets and each step is O(1) — overall O(n). I'd also mention a TreeSet using floor and ceiling as a clean O(n log k) alternative, and call out floorDiv plus long to handle negatives and overflow."
📄 ContainsDuplicateIII.java

2 Sliding Window Median LC 480 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. For every contiguous window of size k, output its median.

💡 Key insight. Maintain the window as two heaps — a max-heap (low, smaller half) and a min-heap (high, larger half) — so the median is low's top (odd k) or the average of both tops (even k). Heaps can't remove an arbitrary leaving element in O(log k), so use lazy deletion: record the outgoing value in a delayed-removal map and only physically discard it once it surfaces to a heap top. Effective sizes (raw size minus pending deletions) drive rebalancing. Average as low + (high-low)/2 in long to dodge int overflow.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Insert nums[i]: if <= low's top push to low (else high), bumping the matching logical size.
  2. Expire when i >= k: pending.merge(out, 1, sum) and decrement the logical size on the side out belongs to (compare it against the current boundary).
  3. Rebalance by logical sizes: if lowSize > highSize+1 move a top from low to high; if lowSize < highSize move the other way.
  4. Prune ghosts off both tops (a poll/insert may expose a marked value) before any meaningful peek, then once i >= k-1 read the median.

⏱️ Time O(n log k) — each slide is O(log k) heap work plus amortized lazy cleanup 💾 Space O(k) — heaps and the pending-deletion map track only window-sized data

⚠️ Gotchas. Track logical sizes (excluding ghosts) for rebalancing — using the raw heap sizes breaks the invariant. Always prune both tops before peeking; a stale top silently corrupts the median. When expiring, decide which heap loses a count by comparing out to the live boundary (low.peek()), not by re-searching. The overflow trap on even windows is real (2147483647 twice): compute a + (b-a)/2.0, never (a+b)/2. The pending map stores counts (a value can be marked multiple times), so decrement, don't just remove.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Re-sorting each window is O(n·k log k). Instead I keep a max-heap for the lower half and a min-heap for the upper half so the median is one or two heap tops in O(1). The catch is that elements leave the window and a heap can't delete an arbitrary element — so I mark the departing value in a pending-removal map and only actually pop it when it reaches a top, while tracking logical sizes for rebalancing. That makes each slide O(log k) and the whole thing O(n log k). I also average with a + (b-a)/2 to avoid int overflow on big values."
📄 SlidingWindowMedian.java

🔁 Practice plan

Warm up with 220's bucketing, then re-solve it with a TreeSet floor/ceiling so you can offer both and compare O(n) vs O(n log k). 480 is the marquee Google on-site here — rebuild the two-heaps-with-lazy-deletion machine from a blank file until the prune/rebalance dance and the logical-size bookkeeping are automatic, since that's where most people's implementations break. Always say the overflow-safe median formula out loud.

  • ✅ I can explain why bucket width valueDiff+1 makes same-bucket pairs automatically valid, and why only ±1 neighbors need checking.
  • ✅ I can justify Math.floorDiv and long for negatives and overflow in 220.
  • ✅ I can implement two heaps with lazy deletion and explain why logical sizes (not raw sizes) drive rebalancing.
  • ✅ I can state both as their respective complexities — 220 in O(n), 480 in O(n log k), O(k) space.
  • ✅ Follow-ups to rehearse: 295 Find Median from Data Stream (the un-windowed two-heaps base), 239 Sliding Window Maximum (monotonic deque), and 346/352 Data Stream problems for the streaming-summary muscle.

📅 Intervals & Line Sweep

Interval problems are about reasoning over ranges on a number line — meetings, bookings, building footprints, free slots. The two universal weapons are sort-then-sweep (sort by start, then make one linear pass where overlap can only happen between neighbours) and the ordered/heap structure (a min-heap of end times or a TreeMap of disjoint ranges) when you need to track concurrency or mutate the timeline online. This family is high-ROI for Google L6 because the same five-line idiom unlocks an entire ladder of questions, from a one-liner overlap check up to the Skyline sweep — and interviewers love to start easy and escalate.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 11 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • Input is a list of [start, end] pairs — meetings, bookings, ranges, segments.
  • Question asks "do any overlap?", "merge overlaps", "how many rooms/resources at once?", or "find the gaps".
  • You need the maximum number of simultaneously-active intervals → think min-heap of end times (or a +1/−1 sweep).
  • Online API: book / addRange / removeRange / query arriving one at a time → ordered map (TreeMap) keyed by start, navigate with floorKey/ceilingKey.
  • "Critical points where something changes" along an axis (skyline, max concurrency, +1/−1 events) → line sweep over sorted edges.
  • Times on a 24-hour clock, angles, or any wrap-around domain → sort + check the wraparound pair too.

🧠 Core idea & template

Almost every interval problem reduces to: impose an order, then exploit locality. Once intervals are sorted by start, anything that overlaps a given interval is contiguous with it, so a single left-to-right sweep that compares each interval against a running frontier (the furthest end seen so far) handles merging, gap-finding, and overlap detection. When you instead need how many overlap at once or an online timeline, swap the running scalar for a min-heap of end times or a TreeMap of disjoint ranges.

// Sort-then-sweep: the reusable skeleton behind merge / free-time / overlap.
Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[0], b[0]));
int end = intervals[0][1];                 // running frontier (furthest end so far)
for (int i = 1; i < intervals.length; i++) {
    if (intervals[i][0] > end) {           // strict gap: no overlap with the frontier
        // ... emit a gap, or start a fresh merged run ...
        end = intervals[i][1];
    } else {
        end = Math.max(end, intervals[i][1]); // overlap/contained: extend the frontier
    }
}

// Max-concurrency variant: min-heap of end times; peak size = rooms needed.
PriorityQueue<Integer> ends = new PriorityQueue<>();
for (int[] m : sortedByStart) {
    if (!ends.isEmpty() && ends.peek() <= m[0]) ends.poll(); // a resource freed
    ends.add(m[1]);
}
int roomsNeeded = ends.size();

// Online timeline: TreeMap start->end of disjoint ranges, navigated by floorKey/ceilingKey.
