📘 Deep dive

1 Meeting Rooms LC 252 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given meeting intervals, decide whether one person can attend all of them — i.e. whether no two overlap.

💡 Key insight. A person can attend everything iff no two meetings overlap. After sorting by start time, an overlap can only occur between adjacent meetings, so a single pass comparing each start against the previous end decides it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort the intervals by start time.
  2. Walk from index 1: if intervals[i][0] < intervals[i-1][1] (this meeting starts before its predecessor ends) return false.
  3. If the loop finishes with no conflict, return true. Touching endpoints (start == prevEnd) are allowed.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — the sort dominates; the scan is O(n) 💾 Space O(1) — in-place, ignoring sort scratch

⚠️ Gotchas. Use strict <, not <= — a meeting ending exactly when the next begins (e.g. [1,5] then [5,10]) does not conflict. An empty list is vacuously true.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force compares all pairs in O(n²). The insight is that after sorting by start, any conflict must be between neighbours, so I sort and do one linear pass checking each start against the previous end — O(n log n), dominated by the sort, O(1) extra space."
📄 MeetingRooms.java

2 Merge Intervals LC 56 ↗ Medium 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Merge all overlapping intervals and return the resulting non-overlapping set.

💡 Key insight. Once sorted by start, overlapping intervals are contiguous. The incoming interval extends the current run iff its start is <= the run's end; if so, stretch the run's end to max(end, incoming end), otherwise start a new run.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort intervals by start.
  2. Seed the output with the first interval as the current run.
  3. For each subsequent interval: if its start <= the last run's end, absorb it via last[1] = Math.max(last[1], cur[1]); else append it as a new run.
  4. Return the built list (trimmed to its true size).

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort dominates; merge sweep is O(n) 💾 Space O(n) — the output list

⚠️ Gotchas. Use <= so touching intervals ([1,4],[4,5] → [1,5]) merge. Always take the max of ends — a fully contained interval like [2,3] inside [1,4] must not shrink the run. Handle the empty input up front.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I sort by start so overlaps become adjacent. Then I sweep, keeping a current merged run; if the next interval starts at or before the run's end I extend the end to the larger of the two, otherwise I close the run and open a new one. Sort is O(n log n), the sweep O(n), output O(n)."
📄 MergeIntervals.java

3 Insert Interval LC 57 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a sorted, non-overlapping interval list and a new interval, insert it and merge as needed.

💡 Key insight. The input is already sorted and disjoint, so the answer splits into three contiguous runs and needs no sort: intervals entirely before the new one (copied), intervals that touch it (collapsed by widening the new interval's bounds), and intervals entirely after (copied).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Phase 1: while intervals[i].end < newStart, copy as-is.
  2. Phase 2: while intervals[i].start <= newEnd, absorb — set start = min(start, cur.start), end = max(end, cur.end); then append the merged interval once.
  3. Phase 3: copy every remaining interval as-is.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one linear scan, no sort 💾 Space O(n) — the output list

⚠️ Gotchas. The phase-1 boundary is strict (end < newStart) while phase-2 is inclusive (start <= newEnd) so touching intervals merge. Don't forget to emit the merged interval exactly once between phases. Empty input or a new interval beyond all others must still work.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because the list is already sorted and disjoint, I don't need a sort — just three passes: copy everything that ends before the new interval, then swallow everything that overlaps by expanding the new interval's min start and max end, then copy the rest. That's a single O(n) sweep."
📄 InsertInterval.java

4 Meeting Rooms II LC 253 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the minimum number of conference rooms required so that no two simultaneous meetings share a room.

💡 Key insight. Process meetings in start order, keeping a min-heap of the end times of meetings currently in progress. Before a meeting starts, free every room whose meeting has already ended (heap.peek() <= start). The heap's peak size over the sweep equals the maximum concurrency — the rooms needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort meetings by start time.
  2. For each meeting: if the heap is non-empty and its smallest end time is <= this start, poll() (reuse a freed room).
  3. Always add this meeting's end time to the heap.
  4. The final heap size = the concurrent peak = answer.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort plus n heap operations 💾 Space O(n) — heap of end times

⚠️ Gotchas. Use <= when freeing so a meeting ending exactly at another's start can hand off the room. You poll at most once per meeting (not in a loop) — that's what keeps the heap size equal to the true concurrent maximum rather than collapsing it.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I sort by start, then walk through meetings keeping a min-heap of end times for in-progress rooms. For each meeting I first reclaim one room if its earliest-ending meeting is already done, then add the new end time. The heap never shrinks below the maximum number of overlapping meetings, so its final size is the answer — O(n log n)."
📄 MeetingRoomsII.java

5 Minimum Time Difference LC 539 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a list of "HH:MM" time points, return the smallest minute-difference between any two, on a 24-hour clock.

💡 Key insight. Convert each time to minutes-since-midnight (0..1439) and sort. The closest pair is either two adjacent sorted values or the wraparound pair (last and first), whose circular distance is 1440 − gap. With more than 1440 entries a collision is forced by pigeonhole, giving answer 0 — and the sort handles that automatically.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Parse each "HH:MM" into h*60 + m minutes.
  2. Sort the minute values.
  3. Scan adjacent pairs tracking the smallest gap.
  4. Also compute the wraparound gap 1440 - last + first and take the overall minimum.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort dominates the parse and scan 💾 Space O(n) — the minutes array

⚠️ Gotchas. The wraparound pair is the classic miss — "23:59" and "00:00" are 1 minute apart, not 1439. Duplicate times give a 0 difference (and they sort adjacently). You could early-return 0 when n > 1440 by pigeonhole, but it isn't required for correctness.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I normalize every time to minutes-after-midnight and sort. The minimum difference is between adjacent sorted times, except I must also consider the clock wrapping from the latest time back to the earliest, which is 1440 minus their direct gap. Sorting makes it O(n log n)."
📄 MinimumTimeDifference.java

6 My Calendar I LC 729 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Implement book(start, end) for half-open events [start, end), returning true and storing the event only if it doesn't double-book.

💡 Key insight. Keep events in a TreeMap keyed by start time. A new [start, end) conflicts iff it overlaps the neighbour immediately before it (floorKey's end > start) or the neighbour immediately after it (ceilingKey's start < end). Only those two neighbours can collide, and each is reachable in O(log n).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. prev = floorKey(start): if it exists and calendar.get(prev) > start, reject.
  2. next = ceilingKey(start): if it exists and next < end, reject.
  3. Otherwise put(start, end) and return true.

⏱️ Time O(log n) per book — two TreeMap navigations 💾 Space O(n) — one entry per event

⚠️ Gotchas. Intervals are half-open, so touching at a boundary is allowed — compare with strict > / <, not >= / <=. Checking only the floor and ceiling neighbours is sufficient because existing events are already non-overlapping; you don't need to scan the whole map.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I store bookings in a TreeMap by start time. To test a new booking I look at just two neighbours — the one starting at or before it and the one starting at or after it — and check for overlap with each. Both lookups are logarithmic, so each booking is O(log n)."
📄 MyCalendarI.java

7 Meeting Rooms III LC 2402 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. With n rooms, assign each meeting (in start order) the lowest-indexed free room, delaying a meeting if all rooms are busy; return the index of the room that hosted the most meetings (lowest index on ties).

💡 Key insight. Two heaps drive the greedy: available holds idle room indices (lowest wins), and busy holds (freeTime, index) ordered by when each room frees. For each meeting, first release every room with freeTime <= start; if a room is free take the lowest index and keep the meeting's real length, otherwise wait for the earliest-freeing room and shift the meeting later so it still runs its full duration.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort meetings by start; seed available with all room indices; keep a count[] per room.
  2. For each meeting, pop from busy into available every room whose freeTime <= start.
  3. If available is non-empty: take the lowest index, count[room]++, push (end, room) to busy.
  4. Else pop the earliest-freeing room, count[room]++, and push (earliestFree + duration, room) — the delayed meeting keeps its full length.
  5. Return the room with the max count, lowest index breaking ties.

⏱️ Time O(n log n + n log rooms) — sort plus heap ops 💾 Space O(n + rooms) — heaps and counts

⚠️ Gotchas. Use long for times — delayed meetings can push free-times past int range. The delayed meeting's new end is roomFreeTime + (end − start), not the original end. The available heap must order by index (not insertion) so the lowest-index tie-break holds; the busy heap breaks ties on index too. Final tie-break also keeps the lower index.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I greedily assign meetings in start order. Two heaps: free rooms by index, and busy rooms by free-time. Before each meeting I reclaim every room that's done. If one's free I take the lowest index; if not, I delay the meeting until the soonest room frees, preserving its duration. I tally per room and return the busiest, lowest index on ties. Heap ops make it O(n log n)."
📄 MeetingRoomsIII.java

8 Employee Free Time LC 759 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given each employee's list of busy intervals, return the finite intervals during which all employees are simultaneously free.

💡 Key insight. Common free time is exactly the gaps left after merging everyone's busy intervals into one timeline. Flatten all intervals into a single list, sort by start, then sweep tracking the furthest busy end so far; whenever the next interval starts after that frontier, the span between them is a gap nobody is busy in.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Flatten every employee's intervals into one list and sort by start.
  2. Initialize end to the first interval's end (the busy frontier).
  3. For each subsequent interval: if cur.start > end, emit the gap [end, cur.start) and reset end = cur.end; else extend end = max(end, cur.end) to swallow overlaps and nested intervals.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort over all n intervals 💾 Space O(n) — the flattened list

⚠️ Gotchas. Use strict > to detect a gap — touching intervals leave no free time. The max on end is essential for intervals fully contained in an earlier one (e.g. [2,3] inside [1,10]), which must not create a phantom gap. The result is the gaps between busy spans; the unbounded time before the first / after the last is excluded.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I ignore which employee owns which interval — free-for-all means I merge every busy interval into one timeline. I flatten and sort by start, then sweep keeping the furthest end reached; each time the next interval starts beyond that frontier I've found a shared free gap. It's the merge-intervals sweep, emitting gaps instead of runs — O(n log n)."
📄 EmployeeFreeTime.java

9 Range Module LC 715 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Support online addRange, removeRange, and queryRange over half-open intervals, keeping the tracked set as disjoint ranges.

💡 Key insight. Maintain disjoint half-open intervals in a TreeMap of start → end, and pivot every operation on floorKey — the interval that could contain or abut a boundary. addRange swallows all touching/overlapping entries into one widened interval; removeRange clips the straddling intervals at the cut boundaries and deletes everything strictly inside; queryRange is covered iff a single interval starting at or before left already reaches >= right.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. addRange(l,r): set start = floorKey(l) if its end reaches l, else l; extend r by floorEntry(r)'s end if larger; clear subMap(start, r); put(start, r).
  2. removeRange(l,r): if the interval straddling r extends past it, re-insert its tail [r, end); if the interval straddling l extends past it, trim it to [start, l); clear subMap(l, r).
  3. queryRange(l,r): start = floorKey(l); return start != null && get(start) >= r.

⏱️ Time O(log n) amortized per op — each interval is inserted/removed O(1) times overall 💾 Space O(n) — one entry per disjoint interval

⚠️ Gotchas. Half-open semantics mean abutting ranges merge in addRange (use floorEntry(right) to catch a range starting exactly at right's left edge). In removeRange, handle the right cut before the left so re-inserting the tail doesn't get clobbered, and use lowerEntry (strictly less) for the straddle checks. queryRange needs full coverage by one interval — partial coverage across two disjoint ranges still returns false.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep the tracked time as disjoint half-open intervals in a TreeMap by start. Every operation anchors on floorKey to find the relevant neighbour. Adding merges all overlapping/abutting ranges into one; removing clips the two boundary ranges and erases what's inside; querying checks that one range starting at or before left already covers through right. Each op is amortized O(log n)."
📄 RangeModule.java

10 The Skyline Problem LC 218 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given buildings as [left, right, height], output the skyline as key points [x, height] where the silhouette's height changes.

💡 Key insight. Sweep a vertical line across all critical x-coordinates (building edges), keeping a multiset of currently-active heights. A left edge inserts a height, a right edge removes one, and a key point is emitted whenever the tallest active height changes after processing an x. Encoding left edges as negative heights and sorting by (x, height) ensures that at a shared x, starts process before ends and taller starts first — which kills spurious points.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Expand each building into two edges: {left, -height} (start, negative) and {right, +height} (end).
  2. Sort edges by x, breaking ties by the signed height.
  3. Maintain a TreeMap height→count multiset, pre-seeded with 0 (ground) so the max is always defined.
  4. For each edge: a start merges its height in; an end decrements/removes one copy. After each edge, if active.lastKey() differs from the previous max, append [x, curMax] and update.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort plus a log-time multiset op per edge 💾 Space O(n) — edge list and active-height multiset

⚠️ Gotchas. The negative-height encoding for starts is the linchpin: at a shared x it forces all starts before ends, and among starts the tallest first — without it you emit duplicate or out-of-order points. The multiset (height→count) is needed, not a plain set, because several buildings can share a height. Seed the map with ground level 0 so a drop to nothing yields a [x, 0] key point. Adjacent equal-height buildings correctly produce no intermediate point.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I turn each building into a start and end event on the x-axis, sort them, and sweep left to right keeping a multiset of active heights with a TreeMap so the current max is the last key. A start adds a height, an end removes one; whenever the max changes I record a key point. The trick is encoding starts as negative heights so ties at the same x resolve starts-before-ends and tallest-first — O(n log n) overall."
📄 TheSkylineProblem.java

11 Non-overlapping Intervals LC 435 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Remove the minimum number of intervals so the remaining ones are pairwise non-overlapping.

💡 Key insight. This is classic activity selection. Sort by end coordinate and greedily keep the interval that finishes earliest, because the earliest finish leaves the most room for later intervals — any optimal schedule can be rewritten to start this way (exchange argument). Drop any interval whose start precedes the last kept end; the answer is total − kept.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort intervals by end. Keep the first as the initial kept interval; set lastEnd = intervals[0][1], kept = 1.
  2. For each later interval: if its start >= lastEnd, keep it (kept++, lastEnd = its end); otherwise it overlaps the earlier-finishing kept interval, so drop it (leave lastEnd unchanged).
  3. Return intervals.length − kept.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — the sort dominates; the sweep is linear 💾 Space O(1) — just the running last-kept end and a counter (besides sort scratch)

⚠️ Gotchas. Sort by end, not start — sorting by start and keeping the earliest-starting interval is the wrong greedy. Use >= when testing the start: a meeting touching at the boundary (start == lastEnd) does not overlap and should be kept. Count what you remove (total − kept), not what you keep. Empty input returns 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's activity selection in disguise — maximise how many intervals I keep, then removals are the rest. I sort by end time and greedily keep each interval that doesn't overlap the last one I kept, since finishing earliest leaves the most room. An exchange argument proves the earliest-end choice is safe. Sort dominates, so O(n log n)."
📄 NonOverlappingIntervals.java

🔁 Practice plan

Climb the ladder in order: start by re-deriving Meeting Rooms and Merge Intervals from a blank file — they encode the sort-then-sweep idiom every later problem reuses. Then do Insert Interval (the no-sort three-phase scan) and Meeting Rooms II (the min-heap concurrency trick). Next master the ordered-map family — My Calendar I → Range Module — practising floorKey/ceilingKey navigation until the neighbour logic is reflexive. Finish with the two true sweeps, Employee Free Time and The Skyline Problem, saying the edge-encoding and tie-break rules out loud. Google-favourite follow-ups worth rehearsing: My Calendar II/III (overlap counting with a difference array / segment tree), Merge Intervals returning total covered length, and Meeting Rooms II via a +1/−1 sweep as an alternative to the heap.

  • ✅ I can explain why sorting by start makes overlaps adjacent, and reproduce the merge sweep blind.
  • ✅ I can argue why a min-heap of end times yields the minimum number of rooms, and code it in under five minutes.
  • ✅ I can navigate a TreeMap with floorKey/ceilingKey/lowerEntry and reason about half-open vs. closed boundary comparisons.
  • ✅ I can derive the Skyline edge encoding (negative starts) and justify each tie-break in the sort.
  • ✅ I remember the wraparound pair in Minimum Time Difference and the long overflow in Meeting Rooms III.

🔙 Backtracking

Backtracking is systematic, DFS-driven exploration of a decision tree: at each step you choose, recurse, then un-choose, pruning whole subtrees the moment they can't lead to a valid answer. It is one of the highest-ROI families for Google L6 because so many onsite problems — combinatorial enumeration, constraint satisfaction, and "generate every valid X" — reduce to the same choose/explore/un-choose skeleton, and interviewers grade you on whether you can prune intelligently and reason about the branching factor.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 11 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The prompt asks for all solutions / subsets / permutations / combinations / valid configurations, not just one or a count you can close-form.
  • Each position has a small, enumerable set of choices, and a solution is a sequence of choices (a root-to-leaf path).
  • There are constraints that can invalidate a partial path early (sum overshoot, attacked square, unbalanced parens) — i.e. pruning is possible.
  • Output size is itself exponential (2n, n!, Catalan) — so an exponential algorithm is expected and acceptable.
  • You need to build a string/board incrementally and undo, rather than recompute state from scratch each call.
  • State can be carried in mutable scratch (a path list, a StringBuilder, used[]/cols[]/diag[] flags) and restored on return.

🧠 Core idea & template

Picture a decision tree. Each node is a partial candidate; each edge is one choice. DFS down: apply a choice (mutate shared state), recurse, then reverse exactly that mutation so siblings start from a clean slate. A complete path (a leaf, or a base case like "remaining == 0") is recorded. Pruning is just an early return the instant the partial candidate can't possibly extend to a valid one. The whole craft is (1) defining the choice set per level, (2) defining "complete", and (3) cutting dead branches as early as possible.

void backtrack(State path, Choices choices, Output out) {
    if (isComplete(path)) {        // leaf / goal -> record a snapshot (deep copy!)
        out.add(copyOf(path));
        return;
    }
    if (isDead(path)) return;      // prune: this partial can never succeed

    for (Choice c : choices(path)) {
        apply(c, path);            // CHOOSE  (mutate shared state)
        backtrack(path, choices, out);   // EXPLORE
        undo(c, path);             // UN-CHOOSE (restore exactly what apply changed)
    }
}

// Two recurring "where does the loop start" idioms:
//   start at i+1  -> each element used at most once  (combinations / subsets-by-loop)
//   start at i    -> element reusable                (CombinationSum)
//   used[] flags  -> order matters, no reuse         (Permutations)

📘 Deep dive

1 Subsets LC 78 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given an array of distinct integers, return the powerset — every possible subset, including the empty set.

💡 Key insight. Each element is an independent binary decision: in or out. That makes the powerset a binary decision tree of height n, with exactly 2n leaves — one per subset.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Recurse on index idx. When idx == nums.length every element has been decided, so the current path is one complete subset — snapshot it.
  2. Branch exclude first: recurse to idx+1 without touching path.
  3. Branch include: path.add(nums[idx]), recurse to idx+1, then path.remove(last) to un-choose.

⏱️ Time O(n·2n) — 2n subsets, O(n) to deep-copy each into the result 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + path buffer; output not counted

⚠️ Gotchas. You must add a deep copy (new ArrayList<>(path)); adding the live path stores a reference that later mutations corrupt. The empty subset falls out naturally from the all-exclude path — don't special-case it. This include/exclude tree differs from the common loop+startIndex subset template but produces the same set (different order).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each number is independently in or out, so I model the powerset as a height-n binary tree and DFS it. At a leaf I've made all n decisions, so I record the path. There are 2n leaves and copying each costs O(n), giving O(n·2n) time and O(n) auxiliary space."
📄 Subsets.java

2 Combination Sum LC 39 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given distinct candidates and a target, return all unique combinations summing to target; each candidate may be reused unlimited times.

💡 Key insight. Allow reuse by recursing on the same index i; forbid permuted duplicates by starting the loop at start so you never revisit an earlier candidate. "Move forward or stay, never back."
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Carry remaining (target minus chosen so far). If remaining == 0, snapshot the path — exact hit.
  2. If remaining < 0, this branch overshot — return to prune.
  3. Loop i from start to end: add cand[i], recurse with start = i (not i+1) to permit reuse, then remove to un-choose.

⏱️ Time O(nT/m) — branching n, depth bounded by target/min-candidate 💾 Space O(T/m) — recursion depth + path

⚠️ Gotchas. Recursing on i+1 instead of i silently turns this into the "use each once" problem. The start index is what kills duplicate orderings like [2,3] vs [3,2] — without it you'd emit permutations. Sorting candidates lets you replace the remaining<0 check with a break for an extra constant-factor prune (the code prunes via the <0 guard).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I DFS over candidates carrying the remaining target. To allow reuse I recurse on the same index; to avoid counting [2,3] and [3,2] separately I never start the loop before the current index. I prune the moment remaining goes negative and record when it hits exactly zero."
📄 CombinationSum.java

3 Letter Combinations of a Phone Number LC 17 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given a digit string (2–9), return all letter combinations the number could spell on a phone keypad.

💡 Key insight. Each digit independently contributes exactly one letter, so a combination is one path through the digit→letters tree — a Cartesian product realized by DFS.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Map each digit to its keypad letters in a fixed array. Return empty immediately if input is empty.
  2. At depth idx, look up the letters for digits[idx] and loop over them: append to a StringBuilder, recurse to idx+1, then delete the last char.
  3. When idx == digits.length() the combination is complete — add path.toString().

⏱️ Time O(4n·n) — up to 4 letters per digit, O(n) to materialize each leaf string 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + StringBuilder

⚠️ Gotchas. Empty input must yield [], not [""] — guard it up front. Digits 7 and 9 have four letters, which is where the 4n bound comes from (2/3/4/5/6/8 have three). Using a StringBuilder with append/deleteCharAt is the clean undo; string concatenation also works but allocates per node.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's a Cartesian product over the per-digit letter sets, which I generate with DFS. At each level I pick one letter for the current digit and recurse; at depth equal to the input length I've chosen a letter for every digit, so I emit the string. With at most four choices per digit it's O(4n·n)."
📄 LetterCombinations.java

4 Generate Parentheses LC 22 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given n, generate all combinations of n pairs of well-formed (balanced) parentheses.

💡 Key insight. Enforce validity by construction with two counters: add '(' only while open < n (budget remains), and add ')' only while close < open (there's an unmatched open to pair). Every leaf is then valid — no post-hoc checking.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Track open and close counts. When path.length() == 2n, the string is fully built and balanced — record it.
  2. If open < n: append '(', recurse with open+1, undo.
  3. If close < open: append ')', recurse with close+1, undo.

⏱️ Time O(4n/√n) — the number of valid strings is the n-th Catalan number 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + path buffer

⚠️ Gotchas. The condition for ')' is close < open, NOT close < n — the latter generates invalid strings like ")(". Because the two pruning rules make every produced string valid, the base case needs no validation. The Catalan growth is why you can't do meaningfully better than enumerating the output.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rather than generate all 2^(2n) bracket strings and filter, I prune at construction: I can open while I have budget, and I can close only against an outstanding open. That guarantees balance, so every leaf of length 2n is a valid answer. The count is the n-th Catalan number, ~4n/(n√n)."
📄 GenerateParentheses.java

5 Permutations LC 46 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given an array of distinct integers, return all of its permutations.

💡 Key insight. A permutation fixes one element per position. A used[] boolean array guarantees each element appears exactly once along any root-to-leaf path; flip it on choose and off on un-choose.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. When path.size() == nums.length every slot is filled — snapshot the permutation.
  2. Loop over all indices; continue on any used[i].
  3. Else mark used[i] = true, add nums[i], recurse; then remove and set used[i] = false to restore.

⏱️ Time O(n·n!) — n! leaves, O(n) to copy each 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + path + used[]

⚠️ Gotchas. Unlike combinations there is no start index — every unused element is a candidate at every level, because order matters. Forgetting to reset used[i] = false on the way up is the classic bug; it strands elements as permanently used. For inputs with duplicates (LC 47) you'd sort and add a "skip equal sibling unless its predecessor was used" guard.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I fill positions left to right. At each position I try every element not yet used, mark it, recurse, then unmark on the way back so siblings can reuse it. There are n! complete orderings and copying each is O(n), so O(n·n!) time with O(n) extra space for the used flags and path."
📄 Permutations.java

6 Confusing Number II LC 1088 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Count integers in [1, n] that become a different valid number when the whole thing is rotated 180° (6↔9, 0/1/8 fixed; 2,3,4,5,7 are invalid).

💡 Key insight. Only numbers built solely from {0,1,6,8,9} can be rotated at all, so generate exactly those by DFS and carry the rotated value alongside the built value. Appending digit d makes value = value*10 + d, but its rotation lands at the front: rotated = rot(d)*place + rotated. Count whenever value != rotated.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. DFS over the five rotatable digits, tracking (value, rotated, place) where place is the positional weight of the next digit.
  2. Skip leading zero (value == 0 && digit == 0); prune any branch whose nextValue > n.
  3. At each node, add 1 to the count when value != 0 and value != rotated (a genuine flip).

⏱️ Time O(5L) — L = digits in n; ≤5 choices per position 💾 Space O(L) — recursion depth

⚠️ Gotchas. The rotated digit must be prepended, not appended — rotating reverses positional order, hence multiplying rot(d) by the running place and adding it on top. Use long for value/rotated/place since n can be 109. A "confusing" number must differ after rotation, so 1, 8, 88, 1001 (palindromic under rotation) do not count. The smart pruning is generating only rotatable digits rather than testing all of [1, n].
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force would test every number to 109 — far too slow. Instead I only build numbers from the five rotatable digits and maintain the rotation incrementally: a new digit appends to the value but its rotated form goes to the front of the rotated value. I count a node when value and rotated disagree, pruning as soon as the value exceeds n, giving O(5L)."
📄 ConfusingNumberII.java

7 N-Queens LC 51 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Place n queens on an n×n board so none attack another; return every distinct board configuration.

💡 Key insight. Place exactly one queen per row, so a solution is just a column choice per row. The two diagonal families have constant invariants: a "\" diagonal shares row - col, a "/" diagonal shares row + col. Encoding column + both diagonals as boolean sets makes each safety test three O(1) lookups.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Recurse by row. When row == n a queen sits safely in every row — build and record the board.
  2. For each col, compute d = row - col + (n-1) and a = row + col; skip if cols[col], diag[d], or anti[a] is occupied.
  3. Otherwise mark all three true and store queenCol[row] = col, recurse to row+1, then clear all three to un-choose.

⏱️ Time O(n!) — ≤n columns in row 0, n−1 in row 1, … with diagonal pruning 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth, three sets, placement array

⚠️ Gotchas. The row - col invariant is negative for cells above the main diagonal, so offset by n-1 to index a 0..2n−2 array. Both diagonal arrays have size 2n-1. Reset all three flags on backtrack — a partial reset leaves phantom attacks. Placing one queen per row implicitly handles the row constraint, so you never check rows.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I place one queen per row and only need to choose its column. A column is safe if its file and both diagonals are free; I track those with three boolean arrays keyed by col, row−col, and row+col, so each check is O(1). I DFS row by row, recording a board when all n rows are placed, with worst case O(n!) before diagonal pruning."
📄 NQueens.java

8 Expression Add Operators LC 282 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a digit string and a target, insert +, -, * (or nothing) between digits so the resulting expression evaluates to target; return all such expressions.

💡 Key insight. The hard part is * precedence without a parser. Carry the value of the last operand (prev): multiplication must undo that operand's prior additive contribution, so cur = cur - prev + prev*operand and the new prev becomes prev*operand. +/- simply fold into cur and reset prev to the signed operand.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. At position idx, grow the next operand digit-by-digit in an inner loop; a multi-digit operand starting with '0' is illegal, so break after the single "0".
  2. The very first operand takes no leading operator. For later operands, try all three: +operand (cur+operand), -operand (cur−operand, prev=−operand), *operand (cur−prev+prev·operand, prev=prev·operand).
  3. At idx == length, if cur == target record the expression string. Truncate the StringBuilder back to its saved length after each branch (un-choose).

⏱️ Time O(4n) — at each of n−1 gaps: split-or-not × three operators 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + expression buffer

⚠️ Gotchas. Use long for cur, prev, and operand — intermediate products overflow int on long inputs even when the final target fits. The leading-zero rule means "05" is never a valid operand but "0" alone is. The cur - prev + prev*operand identity is the whole trick — write it out and sanity-check on "2+3*2" = 8 (prev was 3, so we subtract 3, add 3·2). Truncate, don't rebuild, the StringBuilder.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "At each gap I either extend the current operand or close it and pick an operator, so the branching is roughly four. The subtlety is multiplication binding tighter than the running sum: I keep the last operand's value, subtract its old contribution, and add its product. I guard against leading-zero operands and use longs to survive overflow, exploring O(4n) expressions."
📄 ExpressionAddOperators.java

9 Robot Room Cleaner LC 489 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Control a blind robot (only move/turnRight/turnLeft/clean, no coordinates) to clean every reachable cell of an unknown grid.

💡 Key insight. Impose your own coordinate frame: track (r, c) relative to the start and a dir facing. The trick that makes DFS+backtracking work without absolute positioning is the go-back maneuver: turn 180°, move one cell, turn 180° again — this restores BOTH position and original facing, so the loop can cleanly try the next direction.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Mark (r,c) visited (via an encoded long key) and clean() it.
  2. Probe all four directions in a fixed order. For each, compute the neighbor; if unvisited and move() succeeds, recurse into it, then perform goBack() to return to (r,c) facing the same way.
  3. After handling each direction, turnRight() so the next iteration's direction index lines up with the robot's actual facing.

⏱️ Time O(N) — four probes over N free cells, O(4N), each move O(1) 💾 Space O(N) — visited set + recursion depth bounded by free cells

⚠️ Gotchas. The directions array and the per-iteration turnRight() must stay in lockstep: nd = (dir + turn) % 4 assumes the robot has physically turned right turn times. The go-back must end facing the original direction or the next probe targets the wrong cell — it's two right-turns, a move, two more right-turns. Encode coordinates with an offset (here +100000) so negative relative coords stay distinct and non-negative. You can't randomly teleport, so backtracking must be a physical reversal, not a state pop.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Since the robot is blind, I overlay a relative grid: I track an (r,c) offset from the start and a facing direction. I DFS, cleaning each cell and probing four directions in a fixed rotation. The crux is undoing a move physically — turn around, step back, turn around again — which restores position and heading so the next direction is tried from a known state. It's O(N) over the free cells."
📄 RobotRoomCleaner.java

10 Strobogrammatic Number II LC 247 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return every number of length n that reads the same after a 180° rotation (as strings, no leading zeros except "0" itself).

💡 Key insight. Only five mirror-pairs survive a 180° rotation: (0,0), (1,1), (6,9), (8,8), (9,6). A strobogrammatic number is symmetric — rotating swaps position i with len−1−i and maps each digit to its partner — so build from the outside in, fixing a valid (left,right) pair each step. That enumerates exactly the valid numbers and nothing else. Two guards: the outermost pair skips (0,0) to avoid a leading zero (unless n==1), and an odd centre must be a self-rotatable digit {0,1,8}.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Fill a char[n] buffer with two pointers left/right; recurse moving them toward the middle.
  2. When left > right the number is complete — snapshot the buffer.
  3. When left == right (odd centre), place only a self-rotatable digit (pair[0] == pair[1]: 0, 1, 8) and record.
  4. Otherwise loop the five pairs, skipping (0,0) when left == 0, set buf[left]=pair[0] / buf[right]=pair[1], and recurse inward.

⏱️ Time O(5n/2) — five choices per symmetric pair, ~n/2 pairs 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth plus the char buffer being filled

⚠️ Gotchas. The leading-zero ban applies only to the outermost pair (left == 0) and only for n > 1; for n == 1 the single centre 0 is valid. The odd centre must be self-rotatable — 6 and 9 can never sit in the middle. This is the sibling of Confusing Number II (1088, above) but distinct: here the rotation must equal the number itself, not merely differ.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A strobogrammatic number is symmetric under 180° rotation, and only five digit-pairs survive that rotation, so I build the number from the outside in — fixing a left/right mirror pair at each step and recursing toward the centre. That generates exactly the valid numbers with no filtering. I just guard two cases: the outermost pair can't be (0,0) for n > 1, and an odd-length centre must be a self-rotatable digit. It's O(5^(n/2))."
📄 StrobogrammaticNumberII.java

11 Word Search LC 79 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given an m×n grid of characters and a word, decide whether the word can be spelled along a path of orthogonally adjacent cells, each cell used at most once.

💡 Key insight. DFS from every cell, but fold visited-tracking into the board itself: before recursing, overwrite the current cell with a sentinel '#' so no path steps on it twice, then restore the original character on the way out. This in-place mark/restore makes each backtracking frame O(1) extra space, and bailing the moment word.charAt(index) mismatches prunes the vast majority of dead branches before they expand.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Try each grid cell as a starting root; return true on the first DFS that matches.
  2. In dfs(index, r, c): succeed when index == word.length(); fail on out-of-bounds or when board[r][c] != word.charAt(index).
  3. Save the cell, write '#', recurse into all four neighbours for index+1 (short-circuit on success), then restore the saved char to un-choose.

⏱️ Time O(m·n·4L) — m·n DFS roots, each branching up to 4 ways for L = word length 💾 Space O(L) — recursion depth equals the matched prefix length; no separate visited grid

⚠️ Gotchas. Always restore the cell on backtrack — a missed restore corrupts the board for sibling paths. The mismatch check must precede mutation, and using a sentinel assumes '#' never appears as a real board character. This is the BASE of Word Search II (212), which swaps the single word for a trie to search many words against one board traversal.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I DFS from each cell, matching the word character by character. To enforce 'each cell used once' without a separate visited grid, I temporarily overwrite the cell with a sentinel before recursing and restore it on the way back, which keeps the per-frame space O(1). I prune the instant a character mismatches, so most branches die early. Worst case is O(m·n·4^L) with O(L) recursion depth."
📄 WordSearch.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the ladder easiest→hardest so the skeleton becomes muscle memory before the twists. From a blank file, re-derive in this order: Subsets (the bare choose/explore/un-choose tree) → Combination Sum (start-index vs reuse) → Permutations (used[] flags, no start index) → Letter Combinations & Generate Parentheses (string-building + prune-by-construction) → N-Queens (constraint sets + diagonal invariants) → Expression Add Operators (the cur−prev+prev·operand identity) → Robot Room Cleaner (relative frame + physical go-back). Drill the three loop idioms until you never confuse them. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Subsets II / Combination Sum II (dedup with sorted skip), Permutations II (duplicates), Word Search (grid DFS backtracking), and Sudoku Solver (N-Queens-style constraint tracking).

  • ✅ I can write the generic choose/explore/un-choose template from memory and explain when to deep-copy the path.
  • ✅ I can state which loop idiom (start=i, start=i+1, or used[]) each problem needs and why.
  • ✅ I can explain Generate Parentheses' two pruning conditions and why close < open (not close < n) is correct.
  • ✅ I can derive N-Queens' two diagonal invariants and justify the + (n-1) offset.
  • ✅ I can reproduce the cur - prev + prev*operand multiplication trick and trace it on "2+3*2".
  • ✅ I can explain Confusing Number II's "rotated digit goes to the front" recurrence and the leading-zero / palindrome edge cases.
  • ✅ I can describe the Robot Room Cleaner go-back maneuver and why backtracking must be physical.

🔲 Matrix

Grid problems split into two flavors: geometric index manipulation — traversing or transforming a 2D array in place by reasoning about coordinate mappings and boundaries — and grids as graphs, where cells are nodes and adjacency drives DP or DFS. They're high-ROI for Google L6 because the in-place ones test whether you can nail off-by-one boundary logic under pressure, and the graph ones test whether you spot that a DAG (strictly increasing values) or a unique-anchor trick removes the need for a visited set entirely.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 9 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Spiral", "layer by layer", "peel the border" → four shrinking boundaries (top/bottom/left/right).
  • "Rotate 90°", "transpose", "mirror in place" → decompose into a coordinate-mapping of transpose + row/column reversal.
  • "Set entire row/column to zero" with O(1) space → reuse row 0 and column 0 as marker storage.
  • "Count shapes / connected components" where shapes have a regular form → count a unique anchor cell (e.g. top-left) per shape, no flood fill.
  • "Longest run / line of equal values in any direction" → directional DP reading already-computed neighbours.
  • "Longest strictly increasing path" → the grid is a DAG; DFS + memoization, no visited set.
  • "Minimum-area axis-aligned rectangle from points" → hash points, treat each pair as a diagonal.

🧠 Core idea & template

For in-place geometry, write down the coordinate map (e.g. a 90° clockwise turn sends (r,c) → (c, n-1-r)) and find a sequence of in-place passes that composes to it — transpose then reverse-rows here. For boundary traversal, keep four inward-moving walls and peel one edge per step, guarding the single-row/column case. For grid-as-graph, decide whether adjacency forms a DAG (strict monotonicity ⇒ yes ⇒ memoize, skip visited tracking) or needs DFS/BFS with a visited set; directional run problems become one DP layer per direction.

// (A) Boundary peel — spiral / layer traversal
int top = 0, bottom = m - 1, left = 0, right = n - 1;
while (top <= bottom && left <= right) {
    for (int c = left; c <= right; c++) visit(top, c);     top++;
    for (int r = top;  r <= bottom; r++) visit(r, right);  right--;
    if (top <= bottom) { for (int c = right; c >= left; c--) visit(bottom, c); bottom--; }
    if (left <= right) { for (int r = bottom; r >= top; r--) visit(r, left);   left++;  }
}

// (B) Grid-as-DAG — longest strictly increasing path, memoized DFS
int dfs(int r, int c) {
    if (memo[r][c] != 0) return memo[r][c];
    int best = 1;
    for (int d = 0; d < 4; d++) {
        int nr = r + DR[d], nc = c + DC[d];
        if (inBounds(nr, nc) && grid[nr][nc] > grid[r][c])   // strict => no cycles
            best = Math.max(best, 1 + dfs(nr, nc));
    }
    return memo[r][c] = best;
}

📘 Deep dive

1 Spiral Matrix LC 54 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return all elements of an m×n matrix in spiral (clockwise, starting top-left) order.

💡 Key insight. Maintain four boundaries — top, bottom, left, right — and peel one edge per step, moving that boundary inward afterward. The loop ends when the boundaries cross. The two guards before the bottom row and left column are what prevent re-emitting cells when only a single row or column remains.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Walk the top row left→right, then top++.
  2. Walk the right column top→bottom, then right--.
  3. If top <= bottom still holds, walk the bottom row right→left, then bottom--.
  4. If left <= right still holds, walk the left column bottom→top, then left++. Repeat while the walls haven't crossed.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell is emitted exactly once 💾 Space O(1) — beyond the required output list

⚠️ Gotchas. Without the if (top <= bottom) and if (left <= right) guards, a non-square matrix that collapses to a lone middle row or column will double-emit those cells. Handle the empty matrix up front. The loop condition must be both top <= bottom and left <= right.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep four walls and strip the matrix one border at a time — top row, right column, bottom row, left column — shrinking the corresponding wall after each. The subtlety is the last layer: when only one row or column is left, I have to guard the bottom and left passes so I don't print those cells twice. Every cell is visited once, so O(m·n) time and O(1) extra space."
📄 SpiralMatrix.java

2 Rotate Image LC 48 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Rotate an n×n matrix 90° clockwise in place, without allocating another matrix.

💡 Key insight. A 90° clockwise rotation maps (r, c) → (c, n-1-r). That single mapping factors into two in-place passes: transpose (swap across the main diagonal, (r,c) → (c,r)) followed by reversing each row ((c,r) → (c, n-1-r)). Composing them lands every element at its rotated position using only swaps of existing cells.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Transpose: iterate the upper triangle only (c > r) and swap matrix[r][c] with matrix[c][r].
  2. Restricting to c > r avoids swapping each pair back to where it started.
  3. Reverse each row in place with a two-pointer swap (lo from the left, hi from the right).
  4. The composition equals the clockwise rotation.

⏱️ Time O(n²) — every cell is touched a constant number of times 💾 Space O(1) — all swaps happen in place

⚠️ Gotchas. Transposing over the full grid instead of just the upper triangle swaps every pair twice and yields the original matrix — the c = r + 1 start bound is essential. For counter-clockwise, reverse rows first then transpose (or transpose then reverse columns). This works only for square matrices; a non-square rotation needs a new buffer.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rather than juggling four-way corner cycles, I observe that a clockwise turn is exactly a transpose followed by reversing each row. Transpose mirrors across the main diagonal — and I only walk the upper triangle so I don't undo my own swaps — then reversing each row completes the rotation. Both passes are in place, so O(n²) time and O(1) space."
📄 RotateImage.java

3 Set Matrix Zeroes LC 73 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. If any cell is 0, set its entire row and column to 0 — in place, using O(1) extra space.

💡 Key insight. Instead of O(m+n) side arrays to record which rows/columns to zero, reuse the matrix's own first row and first column as that marker storage: a zero at (r, c) stamps matrix[r][0] and matrix[0][c]. The only conflict is cell (0,0), shared by both markers, so capture whether row 0 and column 0 originally held a zero in two boolean flags before stamping.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Scan row 0 and column 0 once to set firstRowZero / firstColZero flags.
  2. For each interior zero, stamp its row-0 and column-0 markers.
  3. Sweep the interior again, zeroing any cell whose row-marker or column-marker fired.
  4. Finally apply the two saved flags to row 0 and column 0 — done last so their marker values aren't clobbered before being read.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — a constant number of passes over the grid 💾 Space O(1) — markers live inside the matrix; only two boolean flags extra

⚠️ Gotchas. Order matters: if you zero row 0 / column 0 before using them as markers, you destroy the marker information — apply the flags last. The two flags exist precisely because cell (0,0) can't distinguish "row 0 should zero" from "column 0 should zero". Don't zero a row the moment you see a zero, or you'll cascade and wipe the whole matrix.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The naive fix uses two arrays of size m and n. To hit O(1) space I repurpose the first row and first column as those arrays — a zero at (r,c) marks row r and column c there. Because cell (0,0) overlaps both, I first remember in two booleans whether the original first row and first column had any zero, do all the interior marking and zeroing from the markers, and only at the very end zero the first row/column per those booleans. Constant extra space, O(m·n) time."
📄 SetMatrixZeroes.java

4 Battleships in a Board LC 419 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Count the battleships on a board, where each ship is a straight 1×k or k×1 run of 'X' and no two ships are adjacent.

💡 Key insight. Because ships are straight and never touch, each ship has exactly one "top-left" cell: an 'X' whose neighbour directly above and directly to the left are both not 'X' (or off-grid). Count only those cells and you count each ship once — a single pass, no marking of visited cells, board untouched.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Scan every cell; skip anything that isn't 'X'.
  2. If the cell above is 'X', this continues a vertical ship — skip.
  3. If the cell to the left is 'X', this continues a horizontal ship — skip.
  4. Otherwise it's a ship's unique top-left cell — increment the count.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — one scan of the board 💾 Space O(1) — only a counter; the board is not modified

⚠️ Gotchas. This anchor trick relies on the problem's "no two ships are adjacent" guarantee — without it, two touching ships would merge and the count breaks; you'd then need flood-fill / union-find. The bounds checks (r > 0, c > 0) handle ships flush against the top or left edge. The follow-up "do it in one pass without modifying the board" is exactly this solution.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I could flood-fill each ship, but since ships are straight and separated, every ship has a single distinguished cell — its top-left — recognizable because the cells immediately above and to its left aren't part of a ship. I tally only those, which counts each ship exactly once in one pass, O(1) space, and I never touch the board. If ships could be adjacent I'd fall back to connected-components."
📄 Battleships.java

5 Minimum Area Rectangle LC 939 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given points in the plane, find the minimum area of any axis-aligned rectangle formed from four of them, or 0 if none exists.

💡 Key insight. Any two points differing in both x and y can act as a rectangle's diagonal; that fixes the other two corners at (p1.x, p2.y) and (p2.x, p1.y). So hash all points, try every unordered pair as a diagonal, and if both implied corners exist, |dx|·|dy| is a candidate area. Encoding a point as x·40001 + y gives O(1) membership tests.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Insert every point's encoded key into a hash set.
  2. For each unordered pair (i, j), skip if they share an x or a y (that's an edge, not a diagonal).
  3. Check whether both companion corners (x1, y2) and (x2, y1) are present.
  4. If so, update the best with |x1 - x2| · |y1 - y2|; return 0 if no rectangle was ever found.

⏱️ Time O(n²) — every pair of points is examined, each check O(1) 💾 Space O(n) — the hash set of points

⚠️ Gotchas. Only pairs that differ in both coordinates are valid diagonals — skipping same-x or same-y pairs is essential or you'd compute zero-area "rectangles". The radix in the encoding must exceed the coordinate range (coordinates are in [0, 40000], so 40001 is collision-free); a too-small multiplier causes key collisions. An alternative groups points by column and looks for shared y-pairs across columns, also O(n²)-ish.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "An axis-aligned rectangle is pinned down by either diagonal. So I hash every point, then for each pair of points that differ in both x and y — a candidate diagonal — I check if the two complementary corners exist in the set. If they do, I have a rectangle and I track the smallest area. It's O(n²) pairs with O(1) lookups, O(n) space, and I encode points into a single integer to make the set fast."
📄 MinimumAreaRectangle.java

6 Longest Line of Consecutive One in Matrix LC 562 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Find the length of the longest line of consecutive 1s in the matrix, where a line may be horizontal, vertical, or along either diagonal.

💡 Key insight. A maximal line ends at some cell in one of four orientations, and the run ending at (r, c) is just 1 + the run ending at the predecessor in that direction. Four DP layers — each reading one already-computed neighbour (left, up, up-left, up-right) — yield every cell's four run lengths in a single forward sweep; the global max over all of them is the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep four tables: horiz, vert, diag, anti (all 0 where the cell is 0, since a 0 breaks every run).
  2. For a 1, set horiz[r][c] = horiz[r][c-1] + 1 and vert[r][c] = vert[r-1][c] + 1.
  3. Set diag[r][c] = diag[r-1][c-1] + 1 (up-left) and anti[r][c] = anti[r-1][c+1] + 1 (up-right), guarding the borders.
  4. Track the running maximum across all four tables.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — constant work (four transitions) per cell 💾 Space O(m·n) — four DP tables; reducible to O(n) with rolling rows

⚠️ Gotchas. The anti-diagonal reads anti[r-1][c+1], so its column guard is c < n-1, not c > 0 — mixing up which border each diagonal needs is the common bug. A single forward pass suffices only because every transition reads a neighbour already processed earlier in the same scan order. Space drops to O(n) since only the previous row is needed for the vertical and diagonal layers.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "For each of the four directions, the longest run of 1s ending at a cell extends the run ending at the neighbour behind it in that direction by one. So I maintain four DP grids and fill them in one top-to-bottom, left-to-right sweep, each cell reading an already-computed predecessor. The answer is the largest value across all four grids. O(m·n) time, and I can shrink space to one row if asked."
📄 LongestLineOfConsecutiveOne.java

7 Longest Increasing Path in a Matrix LC 329 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Find the length of the longest strictly increasing path in a matrix, moving only up/down/left/right.

💡 Key insight. Because every step must be strictly increasing, values can never repeat along a path, so the reachability graph is a DAG and no visited set is needed. The longest increasing path starting at a cell depends only on that cell, so memoize it: dfs(r,c) = 1 + max over larger neighbours of dfs(neighbour). Each cell is solved once and cached.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate a memo grid (0 = not yet computed) and take the max of dfs over every starting cell.
  2. In dfs(r,c), return the cached value immediately on a hit.
  3. Start longest = 1 (the cell itself); for each of the four neighbours with a strictly greater value, take 1 + dfs(neighbour).
  4. Cache and return the best.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell's value is computed once and reused thereafter 💾 Space O(m·n) — memo table plus recursion depth in the worst case

⚠️ Gotchas. Forgetting the memo turns this into exponential blow-up; with it, each of the m·n cells is expanded once. The strict-increase condition is what guarantees acyclicity — relax it to non-strict and you'd reintroduce cycles and need a visited set. A non-recursive alternative does a topological sort by value (peeling cells with no larger neighbour, like Kahn's algorithm), which avoids deep recursion stacks on huge grids.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I treat the grid as a graph where edges point from smaller to strictly larger neighbours. Strict increase means no cycles — it's a DAG — so I don't even need a visited set. The longest path from a cell only depends on that cell, so I memoize: a cell's answer is one plus the best answer among its larger neighbours. Every cell is computed once, giving O(m·n) time and O(m·n) for the memo. For very large grids I'd mention the topological-peel variant to avoid stack overflow."
📄 LongestIncreasingPath.java

8 Best Meeting Point LC 296 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a grid where each 1 marks a person's home, find the minimum total Manhattan travel distance for everyone to meet at a single cell.

💡 Key insight. Manhattan distance |r1-r2| + |c1-c2| separates into an independent row-cost and column-cost, so the optimal meeting row and column can be chosen separately. For one axis the task is "pick p minimizing Σ|x_i - p|", whose minimizer is the median, not the mean: stepping p toward the side holding more points shortens more distances than it lengthens, so the sum drops until equally many points sit on each side. No sort is needed — scanning rows outermost yields row indices already ascending, and scanning columns outermost yields column indices already ascending.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Collect all 1-cells' row indices by looping rows outer, columns inner — they come out ascending.
  2. Collect their column indices by looping columns outer, rows inner — also ascending.
  3. For each sorted axis, sum absolute deviations from the median with a two-pointer inward walk: hi - lo is each pair's full span, exactly their combined distance to any point between them.
  4. Return rowCost + colCost.

⏱️ Time O(rows·cols) — two passes over the grid to gather coordinates 💾 Space O(#1s) — the two coordinate lists

⚠️ Gotchas. The median, not the mean, is the minimizer — the mean minimizes squared deviations, a different objective. The two-pointer trick (Σ sorted[hi] - sorted[lo]) sidesteps even locating the median explicitly, and handles an even count of points (any value in the middle gap is optimal). Empty grids (no people) return 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Manhattan distance splits into x and y independently, so I solve each axis on its own. On one axis I'm minimizing the sum of absolute deviations, which is the median — moving toward the denser side always helps until the sides balance. I gather coordinates in sorted order for free by choosing the scan direction, then pair smallest with largest to sum the spans, giving O(rows·cols)."
📄 BestMeetingPoint.java

9 Maximal Rectangle LC 85 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a binary matrix of '0'/'1', find the area of the largest rectangle containing only 1s.

💡 Key insight. Every maximal all-1s rectangle has some row as its base, so sweep rows top→bottom maintaining a column histogram where heights[c] is the run of consecutive 1s ending at the current row in column c (reset to 0 the instant a 0 breaks the run). The answer is the max over all rows of "largest rectangle in this row's histogram" — that sub-problem is Largest Rectangle in Histogram (LC 84), solved with a monotonic increasing stack: when a shorter bar pops a taller one, the popped bar learns exactly how far left and right it can extend while remaining the shortest, and height × width is the best rectangle capped by it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a heights[cols] array; for each row set heights[c] = row[c] == '1' ? heights[c] + 1 : 0.
  2. After updating the histogram for a row, run the LC 84 routine and track the global best.
  3. In the histogram routine, push bar indices on a stack in strictly increasing height; when the current bar is ≤ the stack top, pop and measure height × width where width spans from the bar now below top (exclusive) to i (exclusive).
  4. A sentinel at i == cols (height 0) flushes every remaining bar.

⏱️ Time O(rows·cols) — each row builds its histogram in O(cols) and each bar is pushed/popped at most once 💾 Space O(cols) — the heights array and the index stack

⚠️ Gotchas. This composes two classics: it reuses Largest Rectangle in Histogram (84, stack) per row, and is the rectangle cousin of Maximal Square (221, which uses a simpler min-of-three-neighbours DP because squares need only the limiting side). Resetting heights[c] to 0 on a '0' is essential — a wall breaks the column's run. The width formula differs when the stack empties (i) versus not (i - stack[top] - 1); the sentinel pass is what guarantees no tall bar is left unmeasured.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I reduce a 2-D problem to a stack of 1-D ones: every all-1s rectangle bottoms out on some row, so I carry a column histogram of consecutive-1 heights and, per row, solve Largest Rectangle in Histogram. The histogram routine uses a monotonic stack so each bar, when popped by a shorter one, discovers its maximal width as the shortest bar. That's O(rows·cols) overall — it directly composes LC 84, and contrasts with Maximal Square's simpler DP since rectangles aren't constrained to equal sides."
📄 MaximalRectangle.java

🔁 Practice plan

Start with the three in-place geometry problems (54, 48, 73) to drill boundary and coordinate-mapping precision, then move to the graph/DP trio (419 anchor-counting, 562 directional DP, 329 memoized DAG). From a blank file, re-derive: the spiral's two collapse guards (54), the transpose-then-reverse decomposition and why transpose stays in the upper triangle (48), and the "apply first-row/col flags last" ordering in Set Matrix Zeroes (73). Rehearse these Google-favourite follow-ups out loud: "rotate 90° counter-clockwise instead" (48), "set zeroes in true O(1) space" (73, this marker trick), and "longest increasing path without recursion" (329, topological peel). The single highest-value insight to internalize is that strict monotonicity makes a grid a DAG, killing the visited set.

  • ✅ I can explain why the spiral needs guards before the bottom row and left column.
  • ✅ I can derive a 90° rotation as transpose + row-reverse and say why transpose only swaps the upper triangle.
  • ✅ I can explain why row 0 and column 0 can serve as markers and why their flags are applied last.
  • ✅ I can justify the top-left anchor count and name what breaks it (adjacent ships).
  • ✅ I can set up four-direction DP and state which border each diagonal layer must guard.
  • ✅ I can explain why strict increase makes the grid a DAG so memoized DFS needs no visited set.

🕸️ Graphs — BFS/DFS, Topological Sort, Union-Find & Grids

Graphs are the connective tissue of the L6 interview: islands, course prerequisites, account merges and rolling-ball mazes are all the same handful of traversals in disguise — flood-fill DFS, multi-source/level BFS, Kahn's topological sort, union-find, and weighted shortest paths (Dijkstra / Bellman-Ford). Because almost every "real system" modelling question (dependency resolution, network reachability, grouping) reduces to one of these, fluency here is the single highest-ROI pattern family to over-prepare. The skill the interviewer is really probing is modelling — turning a word problem into nodes and edges, then picking the cheapest traversal that fits the structure.

Source: Prep Tracker, Deep Research Adds & curated Staff gaps · 20 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • A 2-D grid where cells connect to 4 (or 8) neighbours — almost always an implicit graph; "count regions / islands" ⇒ flood-fill DFS.
  • "Shortest number of steps / minimum minutes / fewest edits" on an unweighted graph ⇒ BFS by levels.
  • Spread/infection from many starting points at once ("rotting", "gates", "fire") ⇒ multi-source BFS seeding the whole frontier at level 0.
  • "Order tasks given prerequisites", "is there a valid sequence", "detect a cycle in a directed graph" ⇒ topological sort (Kahn's indegree BFS).
  • "Are these connected / how many groups / merge accounts / earliest moment all joined" ⇒ union-find with union-by-rank + path compression.
  • Edge weights with non-negative cost, or a minimax/bottleneck ("smallest maximum elevation along a path") ⇒ Dijkstra with a min-heap.
  • "At most K stops / K edges" with weights ⇒ Bellman-Ford bounded to K+1 rounds.
  • "Use every edge exactly once" (tickets, itinerary) ⇒ Eulerian path via Hierholzer.

🧠 Core idea & template

A few engines cover the whole chapter. Flood-fill DFS sinks a connected region in place so it is never recounted. Level BFS peels the graph one ring at a time, so the layer index is the distance — seed multiple sources to model simultaneous spread. Union-find answers connectivity by merging sets in near-constant amortised time; the component count drops by one on every successful union. Kahn's toposort turns "dependencies + cycle detection" into indegree bookkeeping.

// ── Engine 1: flood-fill DFS on a grid (sink-in-place) ──
void dfs(int[][] g, int r, int c) {
    if (r < 0 || c < 0 || r >= g.length || c >= g[0].length || g[r][c] != LAND) return;
    g[r][c] = WATER;                 // mark visited by mutating
    dfs(g, r+1, c); dfs(g, r-1, c); dfs(g, r, c+1); dfs(g, r, c-1);
}

// ── Engine 2: level BFS (multi-source friendly) ──
Queue<int[]> q = new ArrayDeque<>();
for (every source) q.offer(source);        // all sources at distance 0
int dist = 0;
while (!q.isEmpty()) {
    for (int sz = q.size(); sz > 0; sz--) { // one full ring per dist
        int[] cur = q.poll();
        for (int[] nb : neighbours(cur))
            if (unvisited(nb)) { mark(nb); q.offer(nb); }
    }
    dist++;
}

// ── Engine 3: union-find (rank + path compression) ──
int find(int[] p, int x) { while (p[x] != x) { p[x] = p[p[x]]; x = p[x]; } return x; }
boolean union(int[] p, int[] rk, int a, int b) {
    int ra = find(p,a), rb = find(p,b);
    if (ra == rb) return false;            // already merged → no component change
    if (rk[ra] < rk[rb]) { int t = ra; ra = rb; rb = t; }
    p[rb] = ra;
    if (rk[ra] == rk[rb]) rk[ra]++;
    return true;
}

// ── Engine 4: Kahn's topological sort ──
// build indegree[]; queue all indegree==0; pop, append, decrement neighbours;
// if fewer than V nodes emerge ⇒ a cycle exists.
