📘 Deep dive

1 Flood Fill LC 733 ↗ Easy 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Recolour the 4-connected region of pixels that share the start pixel's original colour with a new colour.

💡 Key insight. This is the paradigm flood-fill: DFS out from the seed, recolouring every cell that still holds the original colour. The one trap is the no-op repaint — if the new colour equals the original, the "already coloured?" check never fails and the recursion never terminates, so guard against it up front.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Read original = image[sr][sc].
  2. If original == color, return immediately — repainting changes nothing.
  3. Otherwise DFS: if in-bounds and the cell equals original, set it to color and recurse into the four neighbours.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell of the target region is visited once. 💾 Space O(m·n) — recursion depth when the whole image is one colour.

⚠️ Gotchas. Forgetting the original == color guard causes infinite recursion. The comparison must be against the original colour captured before any writes, not against the new colour.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I capture the start colour, then DFS recolouring every connected cell that matches it. The subtle bug is when the new colour equals the old one — then my visited test never trips and I loop forever, so I short-circuit that case. Linear in the region size."
📄 FloodFill.java

2 Number of Islands LC 200 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Count the maximal connected blobs of '1' (land) in a grid of '1'/'0'.

💡 Key insight. The first time the scan hits an unvisited land cell, that is a brand-new island. Increment the count, then flood-fill (sink) the whole blob to '0' so its other cells are never counted again. Each cell is touched a constant number of times.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Scan every cell row by row.
  2. On a '1': count++ and call sink.
  3. sink recursively flips the connected land to '0', marking it visited in place.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell is examined once and sunk at most once. 💾 Space O(m·n) — recursion depth when the grid is all land.

⚠️ Gotchas. Mutating the grid is the cheapest visited-marker but destroys the input; if the caller needs it intact, use a separate boolean[][]. On giant all-land grids the recursion can stack-overflow — an explicit stack or BFS is safer.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Every island is a connected component of land. I sweep the grid; the first unvisited land cell I see means a new island, so I bump the counter and drown the entire component so I don't double count. It's a single linear pass."
📄 NumberOfIslands.java

3 Number of Closed Islands LC 1254 ↗ Medium 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Count islands of land (0) fully surrounded by water (1) — i.e. those that never touch the grid border.

💡 Key insight. Any island reachable from the border is not closed. So first flood every border-connected land cell into water; whatever land survives is guaranteed interior, and a standard island count over the remainder is the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Flood-fill from every cell on the four borders, sinking border-connected land (0→1).
  2. Scan the interior; each remaining 0 blob is a closed island — count it and flood it.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — border flooding plus the interior scan each touch a cell O(1) times. 💾 Space O(m·n) — DFS recursion depth.

⚠️ Gotchas. Note the inverted colour convention versus LC 200: here land is 0 and water is 1. Off-by-one on which rows/columns count as the border loses islands hugging the edge.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A closed island just means it doesn't leak to the border. So I pre-flood everything connected to the edges, which removes all the disqualified islands, then I run the ordinary island count on what's left. Two linear passes."
📄 NumberOfClosedIslands.java

4 Clone Graph LC 133 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return a deep copy of a connected, possibly cyclic, undirected graph given a reference node.

💡 Key insight. A single original → clone map is the source of truth. Create a clone the first time a node is seen and register it before recursing into neighbours; re-encountering a node (including via a back edge in a cycle) returns the existing clone, which both terminates the recursion and preserves shared structure.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. DFS from the start node with a Map<Node, Node>.
  2. If the node is already in the map, return its clone.
  3. Otherwise create the clone, put it in the map, then recurse to clone and wire each neighbour.

⏱️ Time O(V+E) — every node cloned once, every edge wired once. 💾 Space O(V) — the map plus recursion stack.

⚠️ Gotchas. Registering the clone in the map after the neighbour loop instead of before reopens infinite recursion on cycles. Handle the null input. The map key must be the original node identity, not its value (values can repeat).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep a map from each original node to its copy. DFS: if I've already cloned a node I return it, which breaks cycles; otherwise I make the copy, record it before recursing, and deep-copy its neighbours. Linear in nodes plus edges."
📄 CloneGraph.java

5 Number of Connected Components in an Undirected Graph LC 323 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given n nodes and an edge list, count the connected components.

💡 Key insight. Start with n isolated components and let each edge potentially merge two of them. An edge only reduces the count when its endpoints were in different sets — union-find reports that in near-constant time — so decrement on every successful union.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Initialise parent[i] = i, components = n.
  2. For each edge, attempt a union; if it merged two distinct sets, components--.
  3. Return components.

⏱️ Time O(n + E·α(n)) — linear init, each edge a near-constant union. 💾 Space O(n) — parent and rank arrays.

⚠️ Gotchas. Without path compression and union-by-rank the amortised bound degrades. A DFS/BFS alternative is equally O(V+E) but needs adjacency lists and explicit visited tracking.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Everyone starts in their own group, so n components. Each edge that joins two previously separate groups removes one component. Union-find merges in near-constant time, and I just count the successful merges."
📄 NumberOfConnectedComponents.java

6 Graph Valid Tree LC 261 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Decide whether n nodes and the given edges form a tree (connected and acyclic).

💡 Key insight. A tree on n nodes has exactly n-1 edges. Reject any other count up front (too few can't connect; too many forces a cycle). Given exactly n-1 edges, "acyclic" and "connected" are equivalent, so union-find alone suffices: if any edge joins two already-connected nodes, there's a cycle.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If edges.length != n-1, return false.
  2. Union each edge; if a union fails (endpoints already share a root), return false — a cycle.
  3. All unions succeeded ⇒ connected and acyclic ⇒ true.

⏱️ Time O(n·α(n)) — near-linear via rank + path compression. 💾 Space O(n) — parent and rank arrays.

⚠️ Gotchas. The n-1 edge-count check is what lets you skip a separate connectivity scan — drop it and a forest containing a cycle could slip through. A single isolated node (n=1, no edges) is a valid tree.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A tree is a connected acyclic graph, and that's exactly n-1 edges. I check the edge count first, which collapses the problem to just cycle detection — union the edges, and if two endpoints are already in the same set I've found a cycle."
📄 GraphValidTree.java

7 Course Schedule LC 207 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given prerequisite pairs, decide whether all courses can be completed (no circular dependency).

💡 Key insight. A prerequisite [a, b] is a directed edge b → a; the schedule is feasible iff the graph is a DAG. Kahn's algorithm repeatedly removes indegree-0 nodes (courses with all prerequisites taken). If a cycle exists, those nodes never reach indegree 0, so fewer than numCourses get processed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build adjacency lists and an indegree array from the pairs.
  2. Queue every course with indegree 0.
  3. Pop a course, increment taken, and decrement each neighbour's indegree, enqueueing any that hit 0.
  4. Return taken == numCourses.

⏱️ Time O(V+E) — each node and edge processed once. 💾 Space O(V+E) — adjacency lists, indegree array, queue.

⚠️ Gotchas. Get the edge direction right — prereq [a,b] means b before a, so indegree[a]++. A DFS three-colour cycle detection is an equally valid alternative.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I model prerequisites as a directed graph and ask whether it's a DAG. Kahn's: start from courses with no prerequisites, take them, and unlock dependents as their last prereq clears. If I can't take all of them, there's a cycle."
📄 CourseSchedule.java

8 Alien Dictionary LC 269 ↗ Hard 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Given words sorted in an unknown alphabet's lexical order, recover any valid letter ordering (or "" if impossible).

💡 Key insight. Each adjacent pair of words yields exactly one constraint: at their first differing position, the earlier word's letter precedes the later word's. Those constraints form a precedence graph over the distinct letters; a valid alphabet is any topological order. Kahn's BFS produces one and flags a cycle (contradiction) when fewer than all letters emerge.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed every distinct character into the graph (so isolated letters still appear).
  2. For each adjacent word pair, find the first differing char and add an edge; if no diff but the first word is longer (prefix violation), return "".
  3. Run Kahn's toposort; if the output length equals the letter count, return it, else "".

⏱️ Time O(C) — C is the total length of all words; edge-building and toposort are linear in C. 💾 Space O(1) — at most 26 nodes and 26² edges, i.e. constant.

⚠️ Gotchas. The prefix case ("abc" before "ab") is invalid and easy to miss. Seed all letters, not just those appearing in an edge, or single-letter alphabets vanish. Only the first differing position gives a constraint — later positions tell you nothing.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Sorted order means each neighbouring pair reveals one 'this letter before that letter' rule from their first mismatch. I build a precedence DAG over the letters and topologically sort it. A cycle means the input is inconsistent, and a longer word before its own prefix is an immediate contradiction."
📄 AlienDictionary.java

9 Pacific Atlantic Water Flow LC 417 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Find every cell from which water can reach both the Pacific (top/left borders) and the Atlantic (bottom/right borders), flowing only to equal-or-lower neighbours.

💡 Key insight. Forward "which cells reach the ocean" is awkward; reverse it. Start at the ocean's border cells and climb to equal-or-higher neighbours, marking everything reachable. Run the reverse flood from the Pacific borders and the Atlantic borders separately, then intersect the two masks.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Two boolean masks, pacific and atlantic.
  2. DFS-climb inward from the top row + left column into pacific; from the bottom row + right column into atlantic — moving only to neighbours of height ≥ current.
  3. Collect cells set in both masks.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell visited at most once per ocean's DFS. 💾 Space O(m·n) — two masks plus recursion depth.

⚠️ Gotchas. The flow condition reverses in the reverse traversal: climb to ≥, not descend to ≤. Forgetting one of the two border seedings drops a whole edge of valid cells.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of asking which cells drain to each ocean, I flood backwards from each ocean's shoreline, climbing uphill. A cell that both floods reach can drain to both oceans. Two DFS passes and an intersection, linear in the grid."
📄 PacificAtlanticWaterFlow.java

10 Rotting Oranges LC 994 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Each minute, rot spreads from every rotten orange (2) to its 4-adjacent fresh oranges (1). Return the minutes until none are fresh, or -1 if some can never rot.

💡 Key insight. Simultaneous spread from many sources is exactly multi-source BFS: every initially rotten orange is a level-0 source. The number of BFS layers to drain the queue equals the minutes until the last fresh orange rots. Counting fresh oranges up front distinguishes "all rotted" from "some unreachable".
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Enqueue all rotten cells; count fresh cells. If zero fresh, return 0.
  2. BFS by layers, incrementing minutes per layer; each newly rotted cell decrements fresh.
  3. Return minutes if fresh == 0, else -1.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell enqueued and processed at most once. 💾 Space O(m·n) — the BFS queue when everything starts rotten.

⚠️ Gotchas. The all-fresh-already-rotten board returns 0, not the post-increment of an empty BFS. Count fresh oranges before BFS so isolated fresh cells correctly yield -1. Use the level-batched for (sz = q.size()...) loop so one ring equals one minute.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rot spreads to all neighbours at once, which is multi-source BFS with every rotten orange at level 0. Each BFS layer is one minute. I track the fresh count so that if any fresh orange is unreachable I return -1 instead of a bogus time."
📄 RottingOranges.java

11 The Maze LC 490 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. A ball rolls in a chosen direction until a wall stops it. Decide whether it can come to rest on the destination starting from the start.

💡 Key insight. The ball does not stop on every cell — it rolls until a wall or border halts it. So the graph's nodes are only the stopping positions, and each edge is a full roll in one of four directions. BFS over those stop points; the destination must itself be a reachable stop.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. BFS from the start, marking visited stop cells.
  2. For each of the four directions, roll while the next cell is open; the cell where it halts is a neighbour.
  3. Enqueue each genuinely new stop; return true if any equals the destination.

⏱️ Time O(m·n·(m+n)) — up to m·n stop states, each rolling O(m+n) to the next wall. 💾 Space O(m·n) — the visited grid and BFS queue.

⚠️ Gotchas. The destination only counts if the ball stops there — rolling through it does not. Mark visited at stop points, not at every traversed cell, or you'll wrongly prune valid rolls. LC 505 (shortest distance) is the weighted Dijkstra sibling.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because the ball rolls until it hits something, the real graph nodes are the resting spots, and an edge is one full roll. I BFS over resting spots, generating neighbours by rolling each direction to a wall, and I only succeed if the destination is itself a resting spot."
📄 TheMaze.java

12 Accounts Merge LC 721 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Merge accounts that share any email into one, each emitting the owner's name followed by all their emails sorted.

💡 Key insight. Two accounts belong to the same person iff they share an email, and "same person" is the transitive closure — connected components. Union accounts by index: map each email to the first account that owns it; when an email recurs, union the current account with that owner.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Union-find over account indices. For each email, if unseen record its owner, else union this account with the recorded owner.
  2. Regroup every email under its component's root into a TreeSet (sorted, deduped).
  3. Prefix each group with the representative account's name.

⏱️ Time O(N log N) — N total emails; the per-component sort dominates the near-constant unions. 💾 Space O(N) — union-find arrays plus the email-to-owner and component maps.

⚠️ Gotchas. Index 0 of each account is the name, not an email — skip it. The name must come from the component representative (all accounts in a group share the same name anyway). A TreeSet handles both dedup and sort in one structure.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Shared emails link accounts transitively, so it's a connected-components problem. I union accounts whenever they share an email, then collect each component's emails into a sorted set and tag on the name. The sort is the dominant cost."
📄 AccountsMerge.java

13 The Earliest Moment When Everyone Become Friends LC 1101 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given timestamped friendship logs, return the earliest time at which all people belong to one friend group, or -1.

💡 Key insight. Friendship is the transitive closure of the introductions, so the group is fully acquainted exactly when it forms a single connected component. Process logs in increasing timestamp order, unioning each pair; the moment the component count first drops to 1 is the earliest such time.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort logs by timestamp ascending.
  2. Initialise components = n. For each log, union the pair; on a successful merge decrement, and if it reaches 1 return that timestamp.
  3. If the logs run out still split, return -1.

⏱️ Time O(L log L + L·α(n)) — sorting dominates; each union is near-constant. 💾 Space O(n) — parent and rank arrays.

⚠️ Gotchas. Only count a merge when it actually joins two distinct sets — redundant introductions inside an existing group don't advance the answer. Sort first; processing logs out of order gives a wrong "earliest".
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Everyone connected means one component. I replay introductions oldest first, union each pair, and the first time the component count hits 1 is my answer. Sorting the logs is the dominant cost; the unions are essentially constant."
📄 EarliestMomentEveryoneFriends.java

14 Reconstruct Itinerary LC 332 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given airline tickets (directed edges), reconstruct the single itinerary starting at "JFK" that uses every ticket exactly once and is lexicographically smallest.

💡 Key insight. Using every ticket exactly once is an Eulerian path over the directed multigraph, and Hierholzer's algorithm builds one in linear time. Storing each airport's destinations in a min-heap makes the walk always take the smallest available next airport, giving the lexicographically smallest route for free. The trick is appending each airport post-order: a stuck vertex can only be the end of the remaining tour, so dead-ends are added first and the final list is reversed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build Map<String, PriorityQueue<String>> so each airport's outgoing edges pop in lexical order.
  2. DFS from "JFK": while the current airport has unused edges, recurse into poll() (consuming the smallest ticket).
  3. When it dead-ends, append the airport to the route (post-order).
  4. Reverse the route at the end.

⏱️ Time O(E log E) — each of E tickets is pushed/popped once across the airports' heaps. 💾 Space O(E) — adjacency heaps plus recursion stack and result list.

⚠️ Gotchas. The post-order-then-reverse is the crux — appending in pre-order produces a wrong path because you can paint yourself into a dead end while edges remain elsewhere. Consume each edge as you traverse (poll), never revisit it. A node can be visited multiple times; it's edges, not nodes, that are used once.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Every ticket used once is an Eulerian path, so I run Hierholzer. Min-heaps on each airport's destinations make the greedy walk lexicographically smallest. The subtlety is that I record an airport only when it dead-ends — those are tails of the tour — and reverse at the end, which splices the sub-tours into the right order."
📄 ReconstructItinerary.java

15 Word Ladder LC 127 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the length of the shortest transformation sequence from beginWord to endWord, changing one letter at a time through words in the dictionary.

💡 Key insight. Words are nodes; an edge joins two words differing in exactly one letter, so the shortest ladder is an unweighted shortest path (BFS). The performance trick is cheap neighbour generation: bucket each word under L wildcard patterns like h*t; words sharing a pattern are one edit apart, making lookup O(L) instead of O(N·L).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If endWord is absent from the dictionary, return 0.
  2. Index every word under each single-wildcard pattern.
  3. BFS by levels from beginWord; for each word, generate its patterns and enqueue all unvisited bucket-mates. Return the level on reaching endWord.

⏱️ Time O(N·L²) — N words, each forming L patterns of length L. 💾 Space O(N·L²) — the pattern-to-words index.

⚠️ Gotchas. The length counts words (nodes), so the start is level 1, not 0. Mark words visited as you enqueue (not as you dequeue) to avoid re-adding within the same level. Bidirectional BFS roughly square-roots the frontier for very large dictionaries.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "One-letter edits are graph edges, so shortest ladder is BFS. Comparing every pair is too slow, so I map each word to wildcard patterns and bucket by them — same bucket means one edit apart. BFS over those buckets gives the shortest path; I count words, so I start the level at 1."
📄 WordLadder.java

16 Shortest Distance from All Buildings LC 317 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Find the empty lot minimising the total travel distance to every building, where distance is grid-walk distance avoiding obstacles. Return -1 if no such lot exists.

💡 Key insight. Each shortest distance on an unweighted grid is a BFS layer. Rather than BFS from each candidate lot (many), BFS once per building (few) and accumulate, into each empty cell, the running distance sum plus a count of how many buildings reached it. The answer is the minimum accumulated distance among cells reached by all buildings.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each building, BFS over empty land with a fresh visited grid; for each reached empty cell add the layer distance to dist[][] and bump reach[][].
  2. Count total buildings.
  3. Scan empty cells with reach == totalBuildings and return the minimum dist, or -1 if none.

⏱️ Time O(B·m·n) — one grid-wide BFS per building B. 💾 Space O(m·n) — the distance-sum and reach-count grids plus the queue.

⚠️ Gotchas. The reach == totalBuildings filter is essential — a lot blocked from even one building is invalid however close it is to the rest. Use a fresh visited per building so each contributes its distance at most once. BFS from buildings, not empty cells, to keep it tractable.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I want the empty lot with the smallest summed distance to all buildings. I BFS from each building, layering distances into shared accumulator grids and counting how many buildings reach each cell. Then I pick the minimum total among cells reachable by every building. BFS from the few buildings, not the many lots."
📄 ShortestDistanceFromAllBuildings.java

17 Swim in Rising Water LC 778 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Water level rises over time; at time t any cell with elevation ≤ t is swimmable. Return the least time to travel from the top-left to the bottom-right corner.

💡 Key insight. A path is usable at time t iff its single highest cell is ≤ t, so we want the path whose maximum elevation is smallest — a minimax (bottleneck) shortest path. Dijkstra works if a path's cost is redefined as the max elevation along it: always expand the reachable cell with the smallest "max-so-far", and the first pop of the bottom-right corner is the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Min-heap keyed on the worst (highest) elevation needed to reach a cell, seeded with the start.
  2. Pop the cell with the smallest max-so-far; if it's the corner, return that value.
  3. For each unvisited neighbour, push max(currentMax, neighbourElevation).

⏱️ Time O(n² log n) — each of n² cells settled once via a heap of size O(n²). 💾 Space O(n²) — the visited grid and priority queue.

⚠️ Gotchas. The edge "cost" is a max, not a sum — using additive Dijkstra gives the wrong answer. Mark a cell visited when pushed (it's settled by minimax order) to avoid re-expansion. Union-find over sorted cells, or binary-search-on-time-plus-BFS, are alternative formulations.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "At time t I can cross any cell at or below height t, so the time to traverse a path is its highest cell — I want to minimise that maximum. That's a bottleneck shortest path: Dijkstra where a path's cost is the max elevation, and the first time I settle the corner is the minimum time."
📄 SwimInRisingWater.java

18 Cheapest Flights Within K Stops LC 787 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Find the cheapest fare from src to dst using at most K intermediate stops, or -1.

💡 Key insight. "At most K stops" means a path of at most K+1 edges, and Bellman-Ford's i-th relaxation round establishes the cheapest cost reachable using at most i edges. Run exactly K+1 rounds. The subtlety: each round must relax off the previous round's costs, or a flight relaxed earlier in the same round could chain into another and sneak in extra stops — so clone the cost array per round.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Init dist[src] = 0, everything else ∞.
  2. For each of K+1 rounds, clone dist into next, relax every flight off the old dist into next, then swap in next.
  3. Return dist[dst] or -1 if still ∞.

⏱️ Time O(K·E) — K+1 rounds, each scanning every flight once. 💾 Space O(n) — two cost arrays of size n, no adjacency structure needed.

⚠️ Gotchas. Without the per-round clone you allow multi-edge chains within one round, over-counting reachability and violating the stop bound. Plain Dijkstra is incorrect here because the cheapest path may use more stops than allowed — the hop constraint breaks its greedy optimality.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "K stops is K+1 edges, which is precisely what bounded Bellman-Ford counts. I run K+1 relaxation rounds, and critically each round relaxes from a frozen copy of the previous round so I never chain two flights in one round and exceed the stop limit."
📄 CheapestFlightsWithinKStops.java

19 Race Car LC 818 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. A car at position 0, speed +1, obeys 'A' (move by speed, then double speed) and 'R' (reset speed to ±1 facing the other way). Return the fewest instructions to reach target.

💡 Key insight. The full state is (position, speed); 'A' goes to (pos+speed, speed*2) and 'R' resets speed to ±1. Each instruction is one unweighted edge, so the fewest instructions is a shortest path found by BFS over states. Bounding positions to [-target, 2·target] keeps the state space finite — any optimal solution stays in that band.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. BFS from state (0, +1) with a visited set on (pos, speed).
  2. From each state generate the 'A' successor (if |pos| stays within 2·target) and the 'R' successor.
  3. Return the BFS level when pos == target.

⏱️ Time O(target log target) — reachable states bounded by the band and doubling speeds. 💾 Space O(target log target) — the visited set and BFS queue over those states.

⚠️ Gotchas. The visited key must include speed, not just position — the same cell at different speeds is a different state. Without the 2·target band the search runs forever. The DP alternative (dp[target]: accelerate just past then reverse, or stop short, reverse, recurse) also runs in O(target log target).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I treat (position, speed) as the node and each instruction as an edge, so the minimum instruction count is BFS. I bound positions to roughly twice the target since no optimal path overshoots further, which keeps the state graph finite and the search terminating."
📄 RaceCar.java

20 01 Matrix LC 542 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. For each cell of a binary matrix, return its distance to the nearest 0, moving only between 4-adjacent cells.

💡 Key insight. This is shortest-distance-to-nearest-source, so seed the BFS queue with all zeros at distance 0 at once rather than running a separate BFS from each one. The wavefront expands one ring per round, so the first time it touches a 1 it has arrived from the closest zero — that arrival distance is the answer. Per-1 BFS would recompute the same wavefront over and over; one multi-source sweep does it in a single pass.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Scan the grid: enqueue every 0 with distance 0, and mark every 1 as unvisited (here dist = -1).
  2. BFS: pop a cell, and for each in-bounds neighbour still at -1, set its distance to dist[cur] + 1 and enqueue it.
  3. Marking on enqueue guarantees each cell is processed exactly once; return the filled dist grid.

⏱️ Time O(rows·cols) — each cell is enqueued and dequeued at most once 💾 Space O(rows·cols) — the queue can hold a whole ring of the grid

⚠️ Gotchas. Seeding from the zeros (not the ones) is the crux — a BFS rooted at each 1 blows up to O((rows·cols)²). Mark a cell visited the instant it's enqueued, not when dequeued, or neighbours get re-added with stale distances. Using -1 as the unvisited sentinel doubles as the "not yet reached" test. A DP alternative (two sweeps: top-left then bottom-right, taking min of neighbours + 1) is also O(rows·cols) and O(1) extra.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Every cell wants its nearest zero, so instead of searching outward from each one, I push all the zeros into the BFS queue together as level-0 sources. The wave spreads a ring at a time, and the first wave to reach a one carries its shortest distance. Marking cells as I enqueue keeps it a single linear pass over the grid. I could also do it as two DP sweeps if asked for O(1) space."
📄 ZeroOneMatrix.java

Build the engines from a blank file in order — flood-fill (733/200/1254), then level BFS (994/127), then union-find (323/261/721/1101) — until each comes out without reference. Then layer the modelling twists: toposort (207/269), reverse-flow (417), and the weighted / state-space variants (787/778/818). The Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse aloud: Eulerian-path post-order ordering on Reconstruct Itinerary (332), BFS-from-buildings accumulation on Shortest Distance from All Buildings (317), and the minimax-Dijkstra reframing on Swim in Rising Water (778).

  • ✅ I can explain why a sunk-in-place flood-fill counts every island exactly once.
  • ✅ I can write multi-source level BFS and justify why one layer equals one unit of time/distance.
  • ✅ I can implement union-find with rank + path compression and state the α(n) amortised bound.
  • ✅ I can run Kahn's toposort and explain how a shortfall in processed nodes proves a cycle.
  • ✅ I can reframe "smallest maximum along a path" as minimax Dijkstra and contrast it with additive Dijkstra.
  • ✅ I can explain why "at most K stops" needs Bellman-Ford with a per-round frozen copy, and why plain Dijkstra fails.
  • ✅ I can derive Hierholzer's post-order-then-reverse and say why pre-order ordering breaks.

🪢 Topological Sort

Topological sort linearises a directed acyclic graph so every edge points forward — the canonical answer whenever a problem is really "order things subject to X must come before Y." It's high-ROI for Google L6 because the pattern hides behind build systems, course/task schedulers, dependency resolution, and "is this even possible?" cycle checks, and the senior signal is recognising it under disguise and reaching for Kahn's indegree-BFS without hesitation. Two more topo-sort problems live in the Graphs chapter — Course Schedule (LC 207, pure cycle detection) and Alien Dictionary (LC 269, deriving the order from constraints) — so treat this chapter together with those.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "A must be done before B" / prerequisites / dependencies → directed edge, then topo order.
  • "Return any valid order" or "is a valid order possible?" → topo sort, where impossibility means a cycle.
  • Build order, task scheduling, compile order, package/version resolution → DAG linearisation.
  • "Minimise tree height by choosing a root" / "find the centroid(s)" → the leaf-trimming variant of topo sort.
  • Nested ordering — items grouped, and groups themselves ordered → two layered topological sorts.
  • You need to detect a cycle in a directed graph → Kahn's: if the emitted order is shorter than the node count, a cycle exists.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two equivalent engines. Kahn's BFS tracks each node's indegree (number of unmet prerequisites); seed a queue with the indegree-0 nodes, and each time you pop a node, decrement its neighbours' indegrees, enqueuing any that hit 0. DFS post-order instead recurses fully into a node's descendants and pushes the node only on the way out, then reverses the post-order. Both are O(V+E); Kahn's is usually the cleaner interview default because it gives cycle detection for free and avoids recursion limits.

Cycle detection. In Kahn's, nodes inside a cycle never reach indegree 0, so if the produced order contains fewer than V nodes, the graph has a cycle (no valid topological order). In DFS you'd instead colour nodes white/grey/black and flag a back-edge to a grey node.

// Kahn's BFS topological sort with built-in cycle detection.
int[] topoSort(int n, int[][] edges) {          // edge [u, v] means u must precede v
    List<List<Integer>> adj = new ArrayList<>();
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) adj.add(new ArrayList<>());
    int[] indeg = new int[n];
    for (int[] e : edges) { adj.get(e[0]).add(e[1]); indeg[e[1]]++; }

    Deque<Integer> q = new ArrayDeque<>();
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) if (indeg[i] == 0) q.offer(i);  // no prerequisites

    int[] order = new int[n];
    int k = 0;
    while (!q.isEmpty()) {
        int u = q.poll();
        order[k++] = u;                                        // safe to place now
        for (int v : adj.get(u))
            if (--indeg[v] == 0) q.offer(v);                   // unlocked its last prereq
    }
    return k == n ? order : new int[0];                        // shortfall == cycle
}

// Leaf-trimming variant (undirected): peel degree-1 nodes layer by layer until <= 2 remain.
Deque<Integer> leaves = new ArrayDeque<>();
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) if (degree[i] == 1) leaves.offer(i);
int remaining = n;
while (remaining > 2) {
    int sz = leaves.size(); remaining -= sz;                   // trim a whole layer
    for (int i = 0; i < sz; i++)
        for (int nb : adj.get(leaves.poll()))
            if (--degree[nb] == 1) leaves.offer(nb);
}
// survivors == the 1 or 2 centroids

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace

3Sort Items by Group LC 1203 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature Two-level (groups + items) Kahn'sO(n + m + E)O(n + m + E)

📘 Deep dive

1 Course Schedule II LC 210 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given numCourses and prerequisite pairs, return any valid order to take all courses, or an empty array if no order exists.

💡 Key insight. A prerequisite [a, b] is a directed edge b → a ("take b before a"), and any valid study plan is exactly a topological order of that graph. Kahn's BFS emits a course the moment its last remaining prerequisite is satisfied (its indegree hits 0); a cycle leaves some courses stuck above indegree 0 forever, which surfaces as a too-short order.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build adjacency lists and an indegree array; for each pair [a, b] add edge b → a and increment indegree[a].
  2. Seed a queue with every course whose indegree is 0 (no prerequisites).
  3. Pop a course, append it to the order, and decrement each successor's indegree; enqueue any successor that reaches 0.
  4. If the order ends up with all numCourses entries, return it; otherwise a cycle blocked some courses, so return an empty array.

⏱️ Time O(V + E) — each course and each prerequisite edge touched once 💾 Space O(V + E) — adjacency lists plus indegree array, queue, and output

⚠️ Gotchas. Edge direction is the classic trap: [a, b] means b → a, not a → b — reverse it and you topo-sort the wrong graph. The cycle signal is the length check (idx == numCourses), not a separate traversal. Don't forget isolated courses with no prerequisites — they start at indegree 0 and must be seeded. Course Schedule I (LC 207) is the same machinery but only asks the yes/no feasibility, so reuse this and just return the boolean.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each prerequisite is a directed edge, and I need a linear order respecting all of them — that's a topological sort. I'll use Kahn's algorithm: count incoming prerequisites per course, start with the zero-prerequisite courses, and repeatedly take a course and 'release' its dependents by dropping their indegree, enqueuing any that hit zero. If I manage to emit all courses I have a valid plan; if I get stuck short, there's a cycle and it's impossible. It's O(V+E) because every node and edge is processed once."
📄 CourseScheduleII.java

2 Minimum Height Trees LC 310 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given an undirected tree on n nodes, return all roots that minimise the resulting tree's height (there are 1 or 2 such centroids).

💡 Key insight. Rooting at a graph centroid minimises height, and a tree has at most two centroids. Find them by topological leaf-trimming: a degree-1 node (leaf) can only make the tree taller, so peel off all current leaves layer by layer like an onion; whatever survives when ≤ 2 nodes remain are exactly the centroids.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Handle n == 1 (return [0]) and n == 2 (return [0, 1]) directly.
  2. Build undirected adjacency lists and a degree array.
  3. Seed a queue with all current leaves (degree 1).
  4. While more than 2 nodes remain, trim a whole layer: record the layer size, subtract it from remaining, and for each leaf decrement its neighbours' degrees, enqueuing any neighbour that drops to degree 1.
  5. The 1 or 2 nodes left in the queue are the centroids.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each node and undirected edge is removed once 💾 Space O(n) — adjacency lists, degree array, leaf frontier

⚠️ Gotchas. You must trim layer by layer (snapshot leaves.size() before the inner loop) — peeling one node at a time and re-checking miscounts the rounds and can return the wrong centroids. Don't forget the n == 1 / n == 2 base cases; with n == 2 both endpoints have degree 1 and the loop would over-trim. The stop condition is remaining > 2 (not > 1) because there can legitimately be two centroids. This is undirected "indegree" = degree, a leaf-pruning cousin of Kahn's.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Trying every root is O(n²); instead I use the fact that the best roots are the tree's centroids, of which there are at most two. Leaves are never centroids, so I repeatedly strip the outer layer of leaves, exactly like topological pruning on an undirected graph using degree instead of indegree. When two or fewer nodes are left, those are the centroids. Each node and edge is removed once, so it's linear."
📄 MinimumHeightTrees.java

3 Sort Items by Group LC 1203 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Order n items so that (a) all dependencies before → item are respected and (b) items in the same group are contiguous; return any valid order or empty if impossible.

💡 Key insight. The "same group must be contiguous" constraint decomposes the problem into two nested topological sorts. Each dependency becomes an edge between the two items' groups when they differ (ordering the groups) or an edge between the two items when they share a group (ordering within the group). Sort the groups, sort the items inside each group, then emit items group-by-group in group order.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Make grouping total: give each ungrouped item (-1) its own fresh unique group id, so "every item belongs to exactly one group" holds.
  2. For each dependency before → i: if group[before] != group[i] add a group-graph edge and bump that group's indegree; otherwise add an item-graph edge and bump that item's indegree.
  3. Run Kahn's topo sort on the group graph and on the item graph; if either returns null (cycle), the whole thing is impossible → empty array.
  4. Bucket the globally item-sorted list by group (preserving intra-group order), then concatenate buckets in group-topo order to build the result.

⏱️ Time O(n + m + E) — n items, m groups, E dependency edges, each processed once 💾 Space O(n + m + E) — two sets of adjacency lists, indegree arrays, per-group buckets

⚠️ Gotchas. The make-grouping-total step is the unlock — without giving -1 items unique groups, you can't treat grouping uniformly and contiguity breaks. A cycle can hide at either level (between groups or within a group), so check both topo sorts. When bucketing, iterate over the item-topo order so each bucket keeps valid intra-group ordering, then walk groups in group-topo order — mixing these two orderings up is the subtle bug. Edge direction follows beforeItems.get(i): those items come before i, i.e. before → i.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Contiguity-by-group plus per-item dependencies is really a two-layer ordering problem. First I give each ungrouped item its own singleton group so every item has exactly one group. Then each dependency either constrains the order of two groups, if the items are in different groups, or the order of two items inside one group. I topologically sort the groups and, separately, the items; a cycle in either makes it impossible. Finally I emit items by walking the groups in their topo order and, within each group, the items in their topo order. Everything is touched once, so O(n + m + E)."
📄 SortItemsByGroups.java

🔁 Practice plan

Lock in Kahn's algorithm on Course Schedule II first until building the indegree array, seeding the queue, and reading the length-shortfall cycle signal are automatic — that template is 80% of every topo problem. Then internalise the leaf-trimming reframing with Minimum Height Trees (degree-as-indegree, peel by layers). Finish with Sort Items by Group to practise composing two topo sorts and the make-grouping-total trick. Re-derive each from a blank file and say the cycle-detection argument out loud every time.

  • ✅ I can write Kahn's BFS from scratch and explain why a short output means a cycle.
  • ✅ I can state the difference between Kahn's BFS and DFS post-order topo sort and when I'd pick each.
  • ✅ I can get the prerequisite edge direction right without second-guessing.
  • ✅ I can explain why leaf-trimming finds the 1–2 centroids and why I trim a whole layer at a time.
  • ✅ I can decompose Sort Items into a group-level and an item-level topo sort and justify the unique-group trick.
  • ✅ Follow-ups rehearsed: Course Schedule (207, feasibility), Alien Dictionary (269, derive order) — both in the Graphs chapter — and Parallel Courses (1136, count topo layers).

🚀 Advanced Graphs (L6 Differentiators)

This is the family that separates a strong L5 from an L6: weighted shortest paths (Dijkstra), bridge/articulation detection (Tarjan), connectivity tallies via union-find, and full-game solving by retrograde DP. Each problem is really a clean classic dressed in a story — the differentiator is recognizing the classic fast and narrating why the algorithm is correct, not just coding it.

Source: Prep Tracker tab · 6 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Time for a signal/fluid/cost to reach every node" with non-negative weights → Dijkstra; the answer is the max over all shortest arrivals.
  • Grid where entry into a cell is gated by a time/value threshold, plus you can "wait" → Dijkstra by arrival time with a parity adjustment.
  • "Edge whose removal disconnects the graph" / "critical connection" / "find all bridges" → Tarjan low-link DFS.
  • "Count paths/pairs where the maximum (or minimum) along the path equals the endpoints" → sort by value + union-find, add nodes in order.
  • Asymmetric trigger relation ("a reaches b but not necessarily b reaches a") → directed graph + reachability from each source.
  • Two-player turn-based game with cycles and possible draws → retrograde analysis (BFS backward from terminal states), not naive forward minimax.

🧠 Core idea & template

Three idioms cover this chapter. (1) Dijkstra: a min-heap keyed on tentative distance always settles the closest unsettled node next; skip stale heap entries with a d > dist[node] guard. (2) Tarjan bridges: one DFS assigns each node a discovery time and a low-link (the smallest discovery time reachable via tree edges plus at most one back edge); edge (u,v) is a bridge iff low[v] > disc[u]. (3) Union-find by value: process nodes in sorted order so each new component contains exactly the nodes reachable through smaller-or-equal values. Here is the Dijkstra skeleton that powers two of these problems:

// Dijkstra: shortest arrival time from a source over non-negative weights.
int[] dist = new int[n];
Arrays.fill(dist, Integer.MAX_VALUE);
dist[src] = 0;
PriorityQueue<int[]> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[1], b[1]));
pq.offer(new int[]{src, 0});               // {node, dist}
while (!pq.isEmpty()) {
    int[] cur = pq.poll();
    int node = cur[0], d = cur[1];
    if (d > dist[node]) continue;          // stale entry — already settled shorter
    for (int[] edge : adj[node]) {          // edge = {neighbor, weight}
        int next = edge[0], nd = d + edge[1];
        if (nd < dist[next]) {
            dist[next] = nd;
            pq.offer(new int[]{next, nd});
        }
    }
}

📘 Deep dive

1 Network Delay Time LC 743 ↗ Medium 🟪 L6 differentiator

Problem. Given directed edges (u, v, w) with travel time w, a signal starts at node k; return the time for all n nodes to receive it, or -1 if some node never does.

💡 Key insight. A node's arrival time is its shortest-path distance from k under non-negative weights, so Dijkstra computes every node's arrival simultaneously. The whole network is covered exactly when the slowest shortest arrival lands — so the answer is max(dist[i]). Any unreached node (dist == ∞) means the signal never fully propagates → -1.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build 1-indexed adjacency lists of {neighbor, weight}.
  2. Run Dijkstra from k: min-heap keyed on tentative distance, relaxing each edge and skipping stale pops with d > dist[node].
  3. Scan dist[1..n]: if any is Integer.MAX_VALUE return -1, else return the maximum.

⏱️ Time O(E log V) — each edge relaxes at most once; each heap op is O(log V) 💾 Space O(V + E) — adjacency lists, dist array, and heap

⚠️ Gotchas. Nodes are 1-indexed — size arrays at n + 1 and leave index 0 unused. Without the stale-entry guard you waste work re-relaxing settled nodes. Edge weights are non-negative, which is exactly the precondition that makes Dijkstra (not Bellman-Ford) correct.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force would re-run a BFS/relaxation from every node, but I notice the time to reach a node is just its shortest path from the source, so this is single-source shortest path with non-negative weights — textbook Dijkstra. I settle nodes closest-first from a min-heap; the moment the farthest node settles, the network is fully covered, so I return the max distance, or -1 if anything stayed infinite. That's O(E log V) because each edge relaxes once and every heap operation is logarithmic."
📄 NetworkDelayTime.java

2 Detonate the Maximum Bombs LC 2101 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Each bomb has a center and blast radius; detonating one triggers every bomb inside its radius, recursively. Return the maximum number of bombs you can detonate by manually setting off exactly one.

💡 Key insight. Triggering is directed and asymmetric — a big bomb can reach a small one without the reverse. So add edge a → b when b lies inside a's radius; the bombs set off by manually detonating a are exactly the set reachable from a. Try every starting bomb and take the largest reachable count.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each ordered pair (i, j), add i → j if dx² + dy² ≤ r_i², computing in long to dodge overflow.
  2. From each bomb, run a DFS/BFS counting reachable nodes (mark on push so none is double-counted).
  3. Return the best count over all start nodes.

⏱️ Time O(n³) — O(n²) edges to build, plus an O(n²) traversal from each of n bombs 💾 Space O(n²) — dense adjacency lists in the worst case

⚠️ Gotchas. Do not build an undirected graph — the relation isn't mutual, and treating it as symmetric over-counts. Coordinates and radius can be up to 1e5, so dx*dx + dy*dy overflows int; cast to long and compare squared distance to squared radius (never use floating-point sqrt). Bombs exactly on the boundary count (≤, not <).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The trap is assuming the trigger relation is symmetric — it isn't, a larger bomb reaching a smaller one doesn't mean the reverse. So I model it as a directed graph, edge a→b when b is within a's radius, and the answer for starting at a is just the count of nodes reachable from a. With only ~100 bombs I can afford O(n²) edges and an O(n²) traversal from each start, so O(n³) is fine. I compare squared distances in long to avoid both overflow and floating-point error."
📄 DetonateTheMaximumBombs.java

3 Minimum Time to Visit a Cell in a Grid LC 2577 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Start at (0,0) at time 0; each move to an adjacent cell costs 1 second, but you may only enter cell (r,c) once the clock is ≥ grid[r][c]. Return the minimum time to reach the bottom-right, or -1.

💡 Key insight. Minimizing arrival time over unit-cost moves with entry thresholds is Dijkstra keyed on time. The twist is waiting: you burn time by stepping to an adjacent cell and back (+2 per bounce). Because each step flips the parity of both (r+c) and the clock, the earliest legal entry into a future cell is grid[nr][nc], bumped by 1 when its parity disagrees with (nr+nc) — that +1 is one extra bounce.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If both cells adjacent to the start have value > 1, you can never take a first step → return -1 up front.
  2. Dijkstra by time from (0,0); first settle of the target is the optimum.
  3. For each neighbor: arrive = t + 1; if that's still earlier than grid[nr][nc], jump to grid[nr][nc] and add 1 if (arrive − (nr+nc)) is odd.
  4. Relax and push if it improves dist[nr][nc].

⏱️ Time O(mn log(mn)) — each cell settles once; heap ops are logarithmic 💾 Space O(mn) — the distance grid and heap

⚠️ Gotchas. The only unreachable case is the blocked start (both first moves gated above time 1) — once you've moved, the wait-by-bouncing trick guarantees you can always reach any cell, so the loop's trailing -1 is effectively just the start guard. The parity correction is the subtle part: forgetting it lets you "arrive" at a time you physically can't stand there. (x & 1) == 1 tests oddness.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's shortest-time on a grid with non-negative effective costs, so Dijkstra by arrival time. The wrinkle is that I can wait by pacing back and forth, two seconds a round trip. Each step changes the parity of row+col and of the clock together, so to enter a cell whose value is in the future I arrive at exactly its value, plus one if the parity is wrong — that one represents a single extra bounce. The only way to be stuck is if both cells next to the start are gated past time 1, which I check first; otherwise the bottom-right is always reachable."
📄 MinimumTimeToVisitCell.java

4 Number of Good Paths LC 2421 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. In a tree with node values vals, a good path has equal endpoint values and no intermediate node exceeding that value. Count all good paths (a single node counts).

💡 Key insight. A good path's endpoints share the maximum value on the path. Process nodes in increasing value and grow components with union-find, joining each new node only to already-added neighbors (value ≤ it). Then the component formed at value v contains exactly the nodes reachable through values ≤ v — and any two value-v nodes in it form a good path. A component holding c nodes of value v contributes c·(c−1)/2.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed good = N (every single node is trivially a good path).
  2. Bucket nodes by value; iterate values ascending.
  3. For each value-v node, union it with neighbors whose value ≤ v.
  4. Group those value-v nodes by DSU root; for each root holding c of them, add c·(c−1)/2.

⏱️ Time O(N log N + E·α) — sorting values dominates; unions are near-constant amortized 💾 Space O(N + E) — adjacency, DSU arrays, value buckets

⚠️ Gotchas. You must add nodes strictly in ascending value order so that when you tally at value v, the component already contains every relevant smaller-valued connector. Only union toward neighbors with value ≤ v (a strictly larger neighbor isn't processed yet). The c == 1 case correctly adds 0, which is why seeding good = N up front cleanly handles single-node paths. Path-halving in find keeps the DSU near-flat.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The defining fact is that the endpoints of a good path are the maximum on that path, so I sweep values from low to high and use union-find to maintain 'components reachable through values seen so far.' When I activate all nodes of value v and union them with their already-active neighbors, every pair of value-v nodes that lands in the same component is a good path — that's c-choose-2 per component. I seed the count with N for the trivial single-node paths. Sorting is the bottleneck at N log N; the unions are effectively linear."
📄 NumberOfGoodPaths.java

5 Critical Connections in a Network LC 1192 ↗ Hard 🟪 L6 differentiator

Problem. Given an undirected connected network of n servers and bidirectional connections, return every connection whose removal would disconnect some pair of servers (the bridges).

💡 Key insight. A critical connection is a bridge. Tarjan's DFS gives each node a discovery time disc[u] and a low-link low[u] — the smallest discovery time reachable from u's subtree using tree edges plus at most one back edge. Edge (u, v) with child v is a bridge iff low[v] > disc[u]: v's subtree has no back edge climbing to u or above, so that edge is the subtree's only link out.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build undirected adjacency lists; set disc[] = -1 as the unvisited marker.
  2. DFS from each unvisited node. On entry: disc[u] = low[u] = timer++.
  3. For a tree edge to unvisited v: recurse, then low[u] = min(low[u], low[v]); if low[v] > disc[u], record (u, v) as a bridge.
  4. For a back edge to a visited non-parent v: low[u] = min(low[u], disc[v]).

⏱️ Time O(V + E) — one DFS touching every node and edge once 💾 Space O(V + E) — adjacency lists, disc/low arrays, recursion stack

⚠️ Gotchas. Skip the edge back to the parent — but only once: a second u–parent edge is a genuine parallel/back edge and means the connection isn't a bridge, so the code uses a skippedParent flag rather than a blanket v == parent skip. Use disc[v] (not low[v]) when relaxing a back edge. The strict inequality low[v] > disc[u] is what distinguishes a bridge from a cycle edge (low[v] == disc[u] means v can climb back to u).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Removing an edge disconnects the graph exactly when that edge is a bridge — it lies on no cycle. I find all bridges in a single DFS using Tarjan's low-link: disc is when I first see a node, and low is the earliest node its subtree can reach via one back edge. A tree edge u→v is a bridge precisely when low[v] is strictly greater than disc[u], meaning v's whole subtree has no alternate route back up to u. One subtlety: I only ignore the parent edge once, so parallel edges are correctly treated as back edges. It's linear, O(V+E)."
📄 CriticalConnections.java

6 Cat and Mouse LC 913 ↗ Hard 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Mouse (starts at node 1) and Cat (starts at node 2) alternate moves on an undirected graph; mouse aims for the hole (node 0), cat aims to catch the mouse, cat may never enter the hole. With optimal play, return 1 (mouse wins), 2 (cat wins), or 0 (draw).

💡 Key insight. Forward minimax stalls on cyclic draws, so solve by retrograde analysis: seed the known terminal states (mouse at hole → mouse wins; mouse meets cat → cat wins) and propagate verdicts backward. A state resolves the instant we can prove it — if the mover has any move into a state they win, they win; if every move leads to an opponent win, they lose. The "all moves lose" case is caught with a degree counter decremented as each successor resolves against the predecessor; reaching 0 means forced loss. States never reached stay draws.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. State = (mouse, cat, turn); result and degree tables sized n × n × 2.
  2. Precompute each state's branching factor, subtracting the illegal cat-into-hole move.
  3. Seed terminals: result[0][c][*] = MOUSE_WIN, result[x][x][*] = CAT_WIN; enqueue them.
  4. BFS backward: for each resolved state, visit predecessors. If the successor is a win for the predecessor's mover, mark and enqueue; else decrement degree, and on hitting 0 mark a forced loss and enqueue.
  5. Answer is result[1][2][MOUSE_TURN] (0 if it never resolved → draw).

⏱️ Time O(V³) — O(V²) turn-states, each examining O(V) predecessor moves once 💾 Space O(V²) — result and degree tables over (mouse, cat, turn)

⚠️ Gotchas. The hole (node 0) is special: exclude it from the cat's legal moves both when computing degrees and when enumerating predecessors. Predecessors are reverse moves — if it's the mouse's turn now, the cat just moved, so vary the cat's previous position (and vice versa). The degree counter is what makes "all children lose ⇒ I lose" efficient; you can't just check children on demand because they resolve incrementally. Unreached states defaulting to 0 (DRAW) is intentional, not a bug.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A naive minimax loops forever because the game can cycle into a draw, so I work backward from the outcomes I already know — mouse on the hole is a mouse win, mouse and cat colliding is a cat win — and propagate. A state is a win for whoever moves if they have any edge into a winning state; it's a loss only if every one of their moves leads to an opponent win, which I detect by counting down that state's unresolved moves. Whatever the backward BFS never colors is a draw. There are V² position-pairs times 2 turns, each touched by O(V) predecessors, so O(V³). The one trick to remember is the cat can never step onto the hole, so I strip that move everywhere."
📄 CatAndMouse.java

🔁 Practice plan

Re-derive in this order — each builds the muscle for the next. (1) Network Delay Time from a blank file: get the heap comparator, the stale-entry guard, and the "max over all dist" finish without peeking. (2) Detonate: reach for a directed graph reflexively and remember the long squared-distance compare. (3) Minimum Time to Visit a Cell: reproduce the parity bump argument out loud. (4) Critical Connections: write Tarjan's DFS cold — disc/low init, the bridge test low[v] > disc[u], and the single-parent-skip — this is the highest-value L6 muscle here. (5) Number of Good Paths: the "process by ascending value + union-find" pattern. (6) Cat and Mouse: rebuild the retrograde BFS with the degree counter. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Cheapest Flights Within K Stops (787) as a Dijkstra/Bellman-Ford variant, Path With Minimum Effort (1631) and Swim in Rising Water (778) as Dijkstra-on-grid cousins of 2577, and Number of Provinces (547) / Redundant Connection (684) for union-find warmups.

  • ✅ I can explain why Dijkstra (not BFS or Bellman-Ford) is correct here, and what the non-negative-weight precondition buys me.
  • ✅ I can state the stale-entry guard and why it preserves correctness while bounding heap work.
  • ✅ I can derive the bridge condition low[v] > disc[u] from the meaning of disc and low — and handle parallel edges.
  • ✅ I can articulate the "endpoints are the path max ⇒ sweep by value + union-find" idea and the c·(c−1)/2 tally.
  • ✅ I can explain why forward minimax fails on Cat and Mouse and how retrograde analysis with a degree counter fixes it.
  • ✅ I can justify the parity +1 in the grid wait, and why the only unreachable case is the blocked start.

🌉 Minimum Spanning Tree

A Minimum Spanning Tree (MST) connects every node of a weighted, undirected graph with the cheapest possible set of edges and no cycles. It is high-ROI for Google L6 because "wire up all the cities / points / servers for the least cost" is a recurring system-design-flavoured prompt, and a fluent Kruskal-vs-Prim answer signals you can pick the right algorithm for the graph's density.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 2 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Connect all of {cities, points, devices, nodes} at minimum total cost" with an undirected, weighted graph.
  • You only care about the total weight of the connecting edges, never about shortest paths between specific pairs (that would be Dijkstra, not MST).
  • Edges are explicitly listed and sparse → reach for Kruskal (sort + union-find).
  • The graph is implicit and dense — every pair connectable via some distance (Manhattan, Euclidean) so E ≈ n² → reach for Prim with an O(n²) array.
  • A correctness requirement to detect "impossible to connect everything" (return -1) maps cleanly onto "fewer than n−1 edges accepted".

🧠 Core idea & template

Both algorithms exploit the cut property: for any partition of the vertices into two sides, the cheapest edge crossing the cut is safe to include in some MST. They differ only in how they pick safe edges. Kruskal sorts ALL edges globally and adds the next-cheapest one whenever it joins two different components (union-find rejects edges that would form a cycle); it grows a forest that merges into one tree. Prim instead grows a single tree outward from a start node, repeatedly attaching the cheapest edge that reaches an unattached vertex. Rule of thumb: sparse, edge-list graph → Kruskal; dense, implicit graph → Prim with a flat array. Both accept exactly n−1 edges; if you cannot, the graph is disconnected.

// ---- KRUSKAL: sort edges, union-find, take edge if it joins two components ----
Arrays.sort(edges, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[2], b[2])); // cheapest first
UnionFind uf = new UnionFind(n);
int total = 0, accepted = 0;
for (int[] e : edges) {
    if (uf.union(e[0], e[1])) {        // different components -> no cycle, keep it
        total += e[2];
        if (++accepted == n - 1) break; // a spanning tree has exactly n-1 edges
    }
}
// accepted < n - 1  =>  graph was disconnected (no MST)

// ---- PRIM (dense / implicit graph, O(n^2)): grow one tree from node 0 ----
int[] minDist = new int[n];            // cheapest known edge from i to the tree
boolean[] inTree = new boolean[n];
Arrays.fill(minDist, Integer.MAX_VALUE);
minDist[0] = 0;
int total2 = 0;
for (int iter = 0; iter < n; iter++) {
    int u = -1;                         // extract-min by linear scan (no heap needed)
    for (int j = 0; j < n; j++)
        if (!inTree[j] && (u == -1 || minDist[j] < minDist[u])) u = j;
    inTree[u] = true; total2 += minDist[u];
    for (int v = 0; v < n; v++)         // relax every remaining vertex against u
        if (!inTree[v]) minDist[v] = Math.min(minDist[v], dist(u, v));
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Connecting Cities With Minimum Cost LC 1135 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Kruskal + union-findO(E log E)O(n)
2Min Cost to Connect All Points LC 1584 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature Prim, dense O(n²) arrayO(n²)O(n)

📘 Deep dive

1 Connecting Cities With Minimum Cost LC 1135 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given n cities (1-indexed) and a list of weighted connections, return the minimum cost to connect all cities, or −1 if that is impossible.

💡 Key insight. This is a textbook MST over an explicitly-listed, sparse edge set — exactly Kruskal's territory. Sort connections by cost ascending and greedily accept an edge only when its endpoints lie in different components; the cut property guarantees each such edge is safe, and union-find both rejects cycle-forming edges and tells us in near-constant time whether two cities are already linked.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort connections by weight c[2] ascending — cheapest edges first.
  2. Build a union-find over n + 1 slots (slot 0 unused because cities are 1-indexed).
  3. Walk the sorted edges; for each, call union(c[0], c[1]). If it returns true (the two endpoints were in different sets, now merged), add c[2] to the running total.
  4. Track how many edges were accepted; the instant it hits n − 1, a spanning tree exists — return the total.
  5. If the loop ends having accepted fewer than n − 1 edges, some city was unreachable — return −1.

⏱️ Time O(E log E) — sorting the connections dominates; each union/find is effectively O(α(n)) ≈ constant. 💾 Space O(n) — the parent and rank arrays of the union-find.

⚠️ Gotchas. Cities are 1-indexed, so size the union-find at n + 1 or you index out of bounds. Use union-by-rank and path compression — without both, deep chains make find linear and the "near-constant" claim breaks. Remember the disconnected case returns −1, not 0; an empty/insufficient edge list is the common test that catches a missing check.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Connecting every city for the least money is a minimum spanning tree, and since the edges are given explicitly I'll run Kruskal: sort by cost, then sweep edges adding each one whose endpoints aren't already connected — union-find detects that. I accept exactly n−1 edges for a tree; if I can't, the graph is disconnected and I return −1. Sorting is the cost, O(E log E), with O(n) space for the disjoint-set."
📄 ConnectingCitiesWithMinimumCost.java

2 Min Cost to Connect All Points LC 1584 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given n points on a plane, return the minimum total Manhattan-distance cost to connect all of them so every pair is reachable.

💡 Key insight. The graph is the COMPLETE graph on the points (every pair connectable, weight = Manhattan distance), so E = n². On a dense graph, Prim with a flat "minimum-distance-to-tree" array beats a binary heap: each round does an O(n) linear extract-min and an O(n) relaxation, giving O(n²) with zero heap overhead — strictly better than the O(n² log n) you'd pay materialising all n² edges for a heap-Prim or Kruskal.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep minDist[j] = the cheapest single edge linking point j to the growing tree, and a boolean inTree[]. Seed minDist[0] = 0 to start the tree at point 0.
  2. Repeat n times: linear-scan for the unattached point u with the smallest minDist, mark it in-tree, and add minDist[u] to the total.
  3. Relax: for every remaining point v, compute the Manhattan distance from the newcomer u and lower minDist[v] if it's closer.
  4. After n rounds the tree spans all points; return the accumulated total.

⏱️ Time O(n²) — n rounds, each a linear extract-min plus a linear relaxation pass. 💾 Space O(n) — the minDist array and the inTree marker.

⚠️ Gotchas. Don't reflexively reach for a heap — for this dense implicit graph the heap makes it slower (O(n² log n)). Manhattan distance is |dx| + |dy|, not Euclidean. Handle n ≤ 1 (cost 0, nothing to connect). Distances can be large; the sum stays within int for LeetCode's bounds, but be ready to justify long if pressed.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Every pair of points can be wired, so this is an MST on a complete graph with n² edges. Because it's dense, I use Prim with a plain minDist array instead of a heap: each of n rounds I pick the closest unattached point in O(n) and relax the rest in O(n), so O(n²) total and O(n) space. A heap-based Prim or Kruskal over all n² edges would be O(n² log n) — worse — so the array form is the right call here."
📄 MinCostToConnectAllPoints.java

🔁 Practice plan

Do 1135 first to nail the Kruskal + union-find idiom (including a from-scratch disjoint-set with path compression and union-by-rank), then 1584 to internalise when Prim's dense O(n²) form is the deliberate, faster choice. From a blank file, re-derive: the union-find find/union, the "accept n−1 edges or return −1" loop, and the Prim minDist/relax double loop. Be able to state the density decision rule in one sentence.

  • ✅ I can explain why an MST accepts exactly n−1 edges and how union-find rejects cycles.
  • ✅ I can decide Kruskal vs Prim from the graph's density and defend it.
  • ✅ I can argue why heap-Prim is slower than array-Prim on a complete graph.
  • ✅ Follow-ups to rehearse: Number of Operations to Make Network Connected (LC 1319), Optimize Water Distribution in a Village (LC 1168, virtual-node trick), and Min Cost to Connect All Points re-solved with Kruskal to compare.

🤑 Greedy

Greedy algorithms commit to the locally-best choice at each step and never look back — the hard part is proving that local optimality rolls up to a global optimum (usually via an exchange argument or a conservation/invariant argument). Google L6 loves these because the code is tiny but the justification separates engineers who memorize from those who can reason; "why is greedy correct here?" is the real question.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 4 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Find the minimum number of …" or "is it feasible to …" where a single forward sweep with a running total seems to suffice.
  • A conservation law is lurking (total gas vs total cost) so feasibility reduces to a global sum check.
  • The most frequent / most constrained element dictates the structure (scheduling, intervals).
  • "Repeatedly take the two smallest / cheapest and combine" → min-heap driven greedy (Huffman-style).
  • You can argue: if an optimal solution disagrees with the greedy choice, you can swap to match greedy without making it worse (exchange argument).

🧠 Core idea & template

Greedy has two recurring shapes. (1) Single-sweep with an invariant: maintain a running quantity, reset or commit at the moment it violates a constraint, and rely on a conservation argument to prove the answer is unique/correct. (2) Heap-driven combination: repeatedly pop the extremal element(s), merge, push the result, and let an exchange argument prove the rarest/cheapest items belong deepest. Always be ready to state the proof — the skeletons are trivial.

// Shape 1 — single sweep with a resettable running total (feasibility + answer)
int total = 0, running = 0, start = 0;
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
    int diff = gain(i) - cost(i);
    total += diff;
    running += diff;
    if (running < 0) { start = i + 1; running = 0; } // commit: restart candidate here
}
return total >= 0 ? start : -1;                       // conservation decides feasibility

// Shape 2 — repeatedly combine the two cheapest via a min-heap (Huffman)
PriorityQueue<Node> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a.weight - b.weight);
// ... seed pq with leaves ...
while (pq.size() > 1) {
    Node a = pq.poll(), b = pq.poll();
    pq.add(new Node(a.weight + b.weight, a, b));      // merge the two minima
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Gas Station LC 134 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Conservation + one-pass restartO(n)O(1)
2Task Scheduler LC 621 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Frame the busiest task + backfillO(t)O(1)
3Huffman Coding — Medium 📌 Reported L6 Min-heap merge (prefix code)O(k log k)O(k)
