📘 Deep dive

1 Gas Station LC 134 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Around a circular route, station i gives gas[i] and the drive to i+1 costs cost[i]; return a starting index from which you can complete the loop, or -1.

💡 Key insight. A solution exists iff total gas ≥ total cost (conservation of fuel). And if the running tank dips below zero at station i, no start in [start..i] can work — each such start reaches i with even less fuel — so the only viable next candidate jumps to i+1. One pass yields both the feasibility check and the unique answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Maintain total (global surplus) and tank (fuel since the current candidate start), plus start = 0.
  2. For each i: let diff = gas[i] - cost[i]; add it to both total and tank.
  3. If tank < 0: you can't reach i+1 from the current start, so set start = i + 1 and reset tank = 0.
  4. After the sweep, return start if total >= 0, else -1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single sweep over the stations 💾 Space O(1) — two running totals

⚠️ Gotchas. Don't restart the candidate at i — it must be i+1, because i itself is part of the failing stretch. The answer is unique when one exists (a known property), so you never need to try multiple candidates. The total >= 0 guard is what proves the final start actually completes the loop.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force tries every start in O(n²). But fuel is conserved: if the grand total of gas minus cost is non-negative, some start works, and it's unique. As I sweep, the moment my tank goes negative I know nothing from my current start through here can succeed, so I leapfrog the start to the next station. One O(n) pass, O(1) space."
📄 GasStation.java

2 Task Scheduler LC 621 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given task labels and a cooldown n (identical tasks must be ≥ n apart), return the minimum number of time units (including idles) to run all tasks.

💡 Key insight. The busiest task dictates the skeleton. Place its maxFreq copies n+1 apart, forming (maxFreq-1) full frames of width (n+1) plus a final partial frame holding the countOfMax tasks tied for the maximum: (maxFreq-1)*(n+1) + countOfMax slots. When there are enough distinct tasks to backfill every gap, no idling is needed and the answer is just the task count — hence the max with the array length.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Tally frequencies into a 26-bucket array.
  2. Find maxFreq, then countOfMax = how many tasks share that peak.
  3. Compute framed = (maxFreq - 1) * (n + 1) + countOfMax.
  4. Return max(framed, tasks.length) — a dense input may leave no idle gaps.

⏱️ Time O(t) — one pass to tally (fixed 26-letter alphabet) 💾 Space O(1) — a 26-bucket frequency array

⚠️ Gotchas. The max(framed, tasks.length) is essential: with many distinct tasks the frames overflow with real work and the formula would undercount idle-free schedules. Multiple tasks tied at the max all sit in the last frame (that's countOfMax, not 1). This closed form sidesteps a heap simulation, which also works but is O(t log 26).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The most frequent task forces the worst-case spacing, so I lay it out first: maxFreq-1 frames of width n+1, then a tail with every task tied for the max. Other tasks slot into the gaps. If there are so many tasks that the gaps overflow, there's no idling and the answer is simply the number of tasks — that's the max() guard."
📄 TaskScheduler.java

3 Huffman Coding Medium 📌 Reported L6

Problem. Build an optimal prefix-free binary code for a set of symbols with given frequencies, minimizing total weighted code length (and support encode/decode round-trips).

💡 Key insight. An optimal prefix code is built bottom-up by greedily merging the two least-frequent nodes into a parent until one tree remains. The exchange argument guarantees the two rarest symbols sit deepest as siblings, so repeatedly combining the minima (via a min-heap keyed on frequency) minimizes total weighted length. Labeling left edges 0 and right edges 1 yields a prefix-free code — no codeword is an ancestor of another — so the bitstream decodes unambiguously.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Count symbol frequencies; push one leaf node per symbol into a min-heap ordered by frequency.
  2. While the heap has > 1 node: poll the two smallest a, b, push a parent with weight a.freq + b.freq and children a, b.
  3. Walk the final tree, appending 0 on left edges and 1 on right edges, recording each leaf's path as its code.
  4. Edge case: a single distinct symbol is a lone leaf → assign it code "0".
  5. Encode by concatenating codes; decode by scanning bits until the accumulated prefix matches a symbol (safe because the code is prefix-free).

⏱️ Time O(k log k) — k distinct symbols, logarithmic heap ops 💾 Space O(k) — heap, tree, and code table

⚠️ Gotchas. The single-symbol case has tree height 0, so naive traversal yields the empty string — special-case it to "0". Decoding relies on the prefix property: the first matching prefix is always the intended symbol, so greedily cut there. Tie-breaking among equal frequencies doesn't affect optimality (total length is the same), only the specific codes. Off-LeetCode, but a reported L6 systems-flavored question.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I want to give rare symbols long codes and common ones short codes. Greedy: always merge the two least-frequent nodes, which pushes the rarest symbols deepest — an exchange argument shows any optimal tree can be rearranged to match this without increasing cost. A min-heap makes each merge O(log k). Left=0, right=1 gives a prefix-free code, so decoding is an unambiguous walk down the tree."
📄 HuffmanCoding.java

4 Minimum Domino Rotations for Equal Row LC 1007 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given dominoes as parallel tops/bottoms arrays, return the fewest top↔bottom swaps to make one whole row (all tops or all bottoms) hold a single value, or -1 if impossible.

💡 Key insight. If some value X can fill an entire row, X must appear on every domino — and therefore on the very first domino. So the only candidates worth testing are tops[0] and bottoms[0]; check just those two with linear rotation counts. A candidate fails the instant some domino shows it on neither face.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For a candidate target, scan all dominoes counting rotTop (swaps to bring target to the top) and rotBottom (to the bottom); if any domino has target on neither face, return a sentinel n+1 (impossible).
  2. The cost for that target is min(rotTop, rotBottom).
  3. Take best = min(rotationsFor(tops[0]), rotationsFor(bottoms[0])).
  4. Return best > n ? -1 : best.

⏱️ Time O(n) — at most two linear passes (a small constant per candidate) 💾 Space O(1) — only a few counters

⚠️ Gotchas. You must test both tops[0] and bottoms[0] — the fill value may be on either face of the first domino. Use a sentinel (n+1) for "impossible" so the final > n check cleanly distinguishes it from a real answer of 0. Don't loop over all six possible values; only the first domino's two faces can ever work, which is the whole point of the O(n) bound.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Any value that fills a whole row has to be on every domino, so it has to be on the first one — that leaves only two candidates, tops[0] and bottoms[0]. For each I count, in one pass, the rotations to push it all to the top versus all to the bottom, bailing out if a domino can't supply it. The smaller count across both candidates is the answer; if neither works, -1. Linear time, constant space."
📄 MinimumDominoRotations.java

🔁 Practice plan

Do Gas Station (134) first and practice saying the conservation proof — that's the whole question. Then Task Scheduler (621): derive the framing formula on paper and explain the max(framed, length) guard with a dense example. Finish with Huffman (—), which is the canonical "prove greedy is optimal via exchange argument" exercise; rebuild the tree from a blank file and walk the encode/decode round-trip. Google follow-ups to rehearse: Jump Game II (45) and Jump Game (55) as single-sweep greedy; Reorganize String (767) as the heap cousin of Task Scheduler; and "what if the alphabet were arbitrary / Unicode?" for the scheduler.

  • ✅ I can state the conservation argument for why Gas Station's greedy is correct and unique.
  • ✅ I can derive the Task Scheduler framing formula and explain when idling drops to zero.
  • ✅ I can give the exchange-argument proof that Huffman's merge is optimal.
  • ✅ I can handle the single-symbol Huffman edge case and explain prefix-free decoding.
  • ✅ I can recognize when a problem is greedy vs. when it secretly needs DP.

📐 Dynamic Programming

Dynamic programming is the art of recognising that a hard problem is just a small recurrence over overlapping subproblems, then storing each subproblem's answer exactly once. It is the single highest-ROI family for a Google L6 loop: interviewers use it to test whether you can name the state, write the transition, justify the base case, and then casually drop the table to O(1) rolling variables — all while talking. Master the handful of idioms here (linear scan, unbounded knapsack, grid walk, two-string edit, digit DP, and state-machine counting) and most "medium-hard" DP questions collapse into a known shape.

Source: Prep Tracker, Deep Research Adds & curated Staff gaps · 19 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Count the number of ways", "min/max cost", "is it possible" + choices made step by step → almost always DP.
  • Optimal substructure ("best answer uses the best answer to a smaller piece") AND overlapping subproblems (the same piece recurs) → memoize or tabulate.
  • A 1-D sequence where each position depends on the previous one or two → linear DP, usually droppable to rolling scalars.
  • "Coins / items reusable, hit an exact target" → unbounded knapsack on the amount axis (and watch combinations-vs-permutations loop order).
  • A grid with right/down moves, or two strings compared char-by-char → 2-D table (paths, LCS, edit distance, maximal square).
  • "Count valid strings of length n under local constraints" → state-machine DP; "count numbers ≤ N built from a digit set" → digit DP keeping a tight-prefix flag.
  • Tiny answer hidden behind a huge input (pigs, information bounds) → it's counting/information theory, not a table — recognise the imposter.

🧠 Core idea & template

Every DP answer is four lines you say out loud: (1) State — what does dp[...] mean, in words? (2) Transition — how does a state combine smaller states? (3) Base case — the smallest states' answers. (4) Order & answer — fill so dependencies precede, then read the target cell. The chapter's recurring shapes:

  • Linear / rolling (Climbing Stairs, House Robber, Decode Ways). State: dp[i] over prefix ending at i. Transition: f(dp[i-1], dp[i-2]). Collapse the array to two scalars.
  • Unbounded knapsack (Coin Change I/II). State: dp[a] over amount a. Transition: relax with each coin; sweep amounts ascending to allow reuse. Coins-outer counts combinations; amount-outer counts permutations.
  • Grid (Unique Paths, Maximal Square). State: dp[i][j] at a cell. Transition: combine top / left / diagonal neighbours. Often rollable to one row.
  • Two-string (LCS, Edit Distance). State: dp[i][j] over two prefixes. Transition: match → diagonal; mismatch → best of the three neighbours.
  • State machine / digit DP (Attendance II, Numbers ≤ N). State: a small tuple (counts, trailing run, tight flag). Transition: one symbol/digit appended, drop illegal states.
// 1-D rolling skeleton (e.g. Climbing Stairs / House Robber).
int prev2 = base0, prev1 = base1;
for (int i = 2; i <= n; i++) {
    int cur = combine(prev1, prev2, input[i]);   // the TRANSITION
    prev2 = prev1; prev1 = cur;
}
return prev1;

// 2-D table skeleton (e.g. LCS / Edit Distance).
int[][] dp = new int[m + 1][n + 1];
// base: dp[0][*], dp[*][0] set by the problem
for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++)
    for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++)
        dp[i][j] = (match(i, j))
            ? dp[i - 1][j - 1] + matchBonus               // diagonal
            : combine(dp[i - 1][j], dp[i][j - 1], dp[i - 1][j - 1]);
return dp[m][n];

📘 Deep dive

1 Climbing Stairs LC 70 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. You climb 1 or 2 steps at a time; count the distinct ways to reach the top of an n-step staircase.

💡 Key insight. Reaching step i is the Fibonacci recurrence: you arrive either from step i-1 (a 1-step) or step i-2 (a 2-step). State dp[i] = ways to reach step i; transition dp[i] = dp[i-1] + dp[i-2]; base dp[0] = dp[1] = 1. Only the last two values matter, so carry two scalars.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Initialise prev2 = 1, prev1 = 1 (ways to reach step 0 and 1).
  2. For i from 2 to n: cur = prev1 + prev2; shift prev2 = prev1, prev1 = cur.
  3. Return prev1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass advancing the pair 💾 Space O(1) — two scalars regardless of n

⚠️ Gotchas. Base cases: both ways(0) and ways(1) are 1, so the loop only starts at 2. The answer grows exponentially — at n = 45 it's still within int (1836311903) but larger n would overflow; mention widening to long. Don't write the naive O(2ⁿ) recursion without memoization.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The last move to step n is either a single or a double, so the count is ways(n-1) + ways(n-2) — Fibonacci. I keep only the two most recent values and roll them forward, giving linear time and constant space."
📄 ClimbingStairs.java

2 House Robber LC 198 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Maximise loot from a row of houses, where robbing two adjacent houses trips the alarm.

💡 Key insight. State best[i] = max loot considering houses [0..i]. Transition: at house i you either skip it (keep best[i-1]) or rob it (best[i-2] + nums[i], since adjacency forbids i-1): best[i] = max(best[i-1], best[i-2] + nums[i]). Base both rolling values start at 0. Only the previous two results matter, so two scalars replace the array.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. prev2 = 0, prev1 = 0 (best loot ending two / one houses back).
  2. For each value v: cur = max(prev1, prev2 + v); shift prev2 = prev1, prev1 = cur.
  3. Return prev1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass 💾 Space O(1) — two scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. The "rob" branch adds to prev2 (two back), never prev1 — mixing them up double-counts adjacent houses. Starting both at 0 cleanly handles the empty array and the first house. This is the parent pattern for many "no two adjacent" variants (trees, circles).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "At each house I make a binary choice: skip it and inherit the best so far, or rob it and add to the best from two houses back. The max of those is the new best; I carry just the last two totals, so it's linear time, constant space."
📄 HouseRobber.java

3 House Robber II LC 213 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Same as House Robber, but the houses are arranged in a circle, so the first and last are adjacent.

💡 Key insight. The circular adjacency means the first and last cannot both be robbed. That single constraint splits into two independent linear problems: rob [0..n-2] (allow first, forbid last) or [1..n-1] (forbid first, allow last); the answer is the max of the two. Each range uses the standard House Robber recurrence best = max(skip, rob + loot[i-2]).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If n == 1, return nums[0] (no wrap-around).
  2. Return max(robLine(nums, 0, n-2), robLine(nums, 1, n-1)).
  3. robLine runs the linear House Robber over the inclusive index range with rolling scalars.

⏱️ Time O(n) — two linear scans 💾 Space O(1) — rolling variables in the helper

⚠️ Gotchas. The single-house case must be special-cased, or [0..n-2] becomes an empty range. You only need two runs, not three — excluding either endpoint already breaks the cycle. Don't try to patch the circular constraint inside one pass; the clean reduction to two linear problems is the expected answer.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The only new wrinkle is that house 0 and house n-1 touch. So I solve two ordinary House Robber problems — one that forbids the last house, one that forbids the first — and take the better. Each is linear, so the whole thing stays O(n)."
📄 HouseRobberII.java

4 Jump Game LC 55 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Each entry is a max jump length from that index; determine whether you can reach the last index starting from index 0.

💡 Key insight. State reach[i] = farthest index reachable having processed indices 0..i; transition reach = max(reach, i + nums[i]) for every index you can still stand on. Collapse the table to one scalar farthest: scan left to right, and the moment an index i exceeds the current farthest you're stuck. You succeed iff the last index ends up reachable.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. farthest = 0. For i from 0: if i > farthest, return false (an unbridgeable gap).
  2. Relax farthest = max(farthest, i + nums[i]).
  3. If farthest >= n-1, return true early. After the loop, return true.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one linear pass, each index relaxes the reach once 💾 Space O(1) — only the running farthest-reachable index

⚠️ Gotchas. The stuck check (i > farthest) must come before relaxing, or a 0 at a just-reached cell slips through. A single-element array is trivially true. The greedy reach is the O(1)-space collapse of a boolean reachability DP — say so to show you see the connection.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I track the farthest index reachable so far. Walking left to right, if I ever reach an index beyond that frontier, there was a gap I couldn't cross — return false. Otherwise I keep extending the frontier and succeed once it covers the last index."
📄 JumpGame.java

5 Jump Game II LC 45 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Same jump rules, but the last index is always reachable; return the minimum number of jumps to get there.

💡 Key insight. Reframe min-jumps as a BFS over "levels" of reach, done greedily in O(1) space. All indices reachable within j jumps form a contiguous prefix [0, curEnd]; while scanning that level, track farthest — the boundary of the NEXT level. When the scan index hits curEnd, the current level is exhausted, so spend one jump and advance curEnd to farthest. The number of boundary crossings to cover the last index is the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. jumps = 0, curEnd = 0, farthest = 0.
  2. For i from 0 to n-2: relax farthest = max(farthest, i + nums[i]).
  3. When i == curEnd: increment jumps and set curEnd = farthest (commit to the next level).
  4. Return jumps.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass; each index updates farthest once 💾 Space O(1) — three scalar pointers, no queue

⚠️ Gotchas. Loop to n-2, not n-1: reaching the last index inside the current level needs no extra jump, and looping to n-1 would over-count by one. This is implicit BFS — naming it as level-by-level breadth-first reach is the cleanest justification of why greedy is optimal here.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Think of it as BFS layers: with j jumps I can reach some contiguous range. I scan the current range, noting the farthest the next jump could take me; when I exhaust the range I 'spend' a jump and extend the boundary. Counting those extensions gives the minimum, in linear time without a queue."
📄 JumpGameII.java

6 Coin Change LC 322 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given coin denominations and a target amount, return the fewest coins that sum to exactly that amount, or -1 if impossible.

💡 Key insight. Unbounded knapsack on the amount axis. State dp[a] = fewest coins summing to exactly a. Transition: try each coin c as the LAST coin used, leaving subproblem a-c: dp[a] = min over c of dp[a-c] + 1. Coins are reusable, so sweeping amounts ascending reads dp[a-c] of the same row, already accounting for using c any number of times. Base dp[0] = 0; unreachable amounts stay at a sentinel "infinity".
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Fill dp[0..amount] with sentinel amount+1 (no real solution can exceed amount coins); set dp[0] = 0.
  2. For each amount a from 1, and each coin c <= a: dp[a] = min(dp[a], dp[a-c] + 1).
  3. Return dp[amount] > amount ? -1 : dp[amount].

⏱️ Time O(amount · coins) — each amount tries every coin once 💾 Space O(amount) — one dp row indexed by amount

⚠️ Gotchas. Use a sentinel that can't be reached legitimately (amount+1) so the final "unreachable" check is unambiguous — and so dp[a-c] + 1 never silently wraps. Greedy "largest coin first" is wrong for arbitrary denominations (e.g. coins {1,3,4}, amount 6); you must DP. dp[0] = 0 is the load-bearing base case.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I define dp[a] as the min coins to make amount a. For each amount I consider every coin as the final one used and take dp[a-coin]+1. Because coins repeat, I sweep amounts upward so reuse is free. Anything still at the infinity sentinel is unreachable, so I return -1 there."
📄 CoinChange.java

7 Coin Change II LC 518 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Count the number of distinct combinations of coins (order-insensitive) that sum to a target amount.

💡 Key insight. State dp[a] = number of distinct combinations summing to a using the coins processed so far. Transition while folding in coin c: dp[a] += dp[a-c] for a from c upward. The crucial detail is loop ORDER: coins OUTER, amount INNER introduces each coin once, so combinations are counted without regard to order ({1,2} = {2,1}). Swapping the loops would count permutations. Base dp[0] = 1 (the empty combination).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. dp[0] = 1, rest 0.
  2. For each coin c (outer): for a from c to amount (inner): dp[a] += dp[a-c].
  3. Return dp[amount].

⏱️ Time O(amount · coins) — each (coin, amount) pair relaxed once 💾 Space O(amount) — single rolling row reused across coins

⚠️ Gotchas. The single most-tested point: coins outer, amount inner for combinations; reversing the loops counts ordered sequences (a different, usually wrong, answer). dp[0] = 1 seeds "take nothing." Watch amount = 0: the answer is 1 (the empty combination), which the base case already gives.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "dp[a] counts ways to form a. I introduce one coin at a time in the outer loop and add dp[a-coin] inward — this guarantees each combination is counted once regardless of order. If I instead looped amounts outside, I'd count permutations. The empty set seeds dp[0]=1."
📄 CoinChangeII.java

8 Word Break LC 139 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Decide whether a string can be segmented into a space-separated sequence of dictionary words.

💡 Key insight. State dp[i] = can the prefix s[0..i) be fully segmented. Transition: dp[i] is true iff there's a split point j < i with dp[j] true AND s[j..i) a dictionary word — a valid segmentation of the first j chars followed by one word ending at i. Base dp[0] = true (empty prefix). The answer is dp[n].
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Put the dictionary in a HashSet for O(1) lookups.
  2. dp[0] = true. For each end i from 1 to n, scan starts j from 0 to i-1.
  3. If dp[j] and dict.contains(s.substring(j, i)), set dp[i] = true and break.
  4. Return dp[n].

⏱️ Time O(n²·L) — for each end scan all starts; substring/lookup costs L 💾 Space O(n) — the dp array (plus the word set)

⚠️ Gotchas. Break as soon as one valid split is found — you only need existence, not all of them. The plain backtracking version is exponential on adversarial inputs (e.g. "aaaa...ab"); the dp memoizes prefixes and tames it. The substring cost L is real — for very long words consider a trie or rolling hash.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "dp[i] asks whether the first i characters split cleanly. It's true if some earlier cut point j is itself splittable and the chunk between j and i is a dictionary word. I build dp left to right; dp[n] is the answer. Memoizing prefixes turns the exponential brute force into n-squared."
📄 WordBreak.java

9 Decode Ways LC 91 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. A→1 … Z→26; count the ways a digit string can be decoded back into letters.

💡 Key insight. State dp[i] = number of decodings of the prefix s[0..i). Transition: two feeds — take s[i-1] as a ONE-digit letter (valid for '1'..'9') contributing dp[i-1], and take s[i-2..i) as a TWO-digit letter (valid for "10".."26") contributing dp[i-2]. A leading '0' decodes nothing alone, surviving only as the second digit of 10 or 20. Base dp[0] = 1 (empty). Carry the last two counts.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If s[0] == '0', return 0 (no valid first letter).
  2. prev2 = 1, prev1 = 1. For i from 2 to len: cur = 0.
  3. If s[i-1] != '0', add prev1 (single 1..9). If 10 <= two <= 26 where two = 10·s[i-2] + s[i-1], add prev2.
  4. Shift the pair; return prev1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass over the string 💾 Space O(1) — two rolling counters

⚠️ Gotchas. Zeros are the whole difficulty: '0' is only legal as the tail of "10"/"20"; "06" and any leading zero decode to 0. The two-digit window is valid only in [10, 26] — "27" or "01" don't qualify. A single invalid zero collapses the count to 0 (e.g. "2101" → 1), so don't forget to reset cur each step.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's Fibonacci-shaped with validity gates. The count for the prefix ending at i is the count ending at i-1 (if the last digit is a standalone letter) plus the count ending at i-2 (if the last two digits form 10–26). Zeros knock out one or both branches, and a stranded zero zeroes the whole thing."
📄 DecodeWays.java

10 Longest Increasing Subsequence LC 300 ↗ Medium 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Return the length of the longest strictly increasing subsequence of an integer array.

💡 Key insight. Patience sorting beats the obvious O(n²) DP. Keep tails, where tails[k] is the smallest possible tail of any increasing subsequence of length k+1. For each x, binary search the first tail >= x and overwrite it (x extends a shorter run more cheaply); if none is >= x, append (x extends the longest run). tails stays sorted, so the search is valid, and its length is the LIS length.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. size = 0. For each x: lower-bound binary search in tails[0..size) for the first element >= x.
  2. Write tails[lo] = x.
  3. If lo == size, increment size (x started a strictly longer subsequence).
  4. Return size.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — one binary search per element 💾 Space O(n) — the tails array

⚠️ Gotchas. tails is NOT an actual LIS — it only tracks minimal tails, so don't try to read the subsequence out of it directly (reconstruction needs extra parent pointers). For "strictly" increasing use a lower-bound (>= x) search; for "non-decreasing" you'd switch to upper-bound. Know the O(n²) DP (dp[i] = 1 + max dp[j] over j<i with nums[j]<nums[i]) as the simpler fallback to mention first.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The quadratic DP extends each element from the best smaller-ending run. To get n log n, I maintain the smallest tail for every achievable length; each new value either replaces the first tail that's at least as big (keeping options open) or extends the array. The length of that tails array is the LIS length."
📄 LongestIncreasingSubsequence.java

11 Unique Paths LC 62 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Count paths from the top-left to the bottom-right of an m × n grid moving only right or down.

💡 Key insight. State dp[i][j] = number of paths to cell (i,j). Transition: each cell is reached from above or from the left, so dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j] + dp[i][j-1], with the top row and left column all 1. Processing row by row, dp[j] already holds the value from the row above; adding dp[j-1] (just-updated left cell) collapses the grid to one rolling row. (Closed form: C(m+n-2, m-1).)
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Initialise a length-n row to all 1s (top row: one path each).
  2. For each subsequent row, for j from 1: dp[j] += dp[j-1] (from-above + from-left).
  3. Return dp[n-1].

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — visit every cell once 💾 Space O(n) — one row of width n

⚠️ Gotchas. When rolling to one row, dp[j] on the right side is the old (above) value and dp[j-1] is the new (left) value — that's exactly what you want; reversing the iteration would break it. The first column stays 1 implicitly (untouched). For an L6 flourish, mention the combinatorial closed form and the overflow risk for large grids.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Every cell's path count is the sum of the cell above and the cell to the left, with the borders all 1. I sweep row by row reusing a single array: before I update a cell it holds the row-above value, and the neighbour to its left is already the current row — so dp[j] += dp[j-1] in place."
📄 UniquePaths.java

12 Unique Paths II LC 63 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Same grid path count, but some cells are obstacles that no path may pass through.

💡 Key insight. State dp[j] = ways to reach cell (i,j), rolled over rows. Transition for a free cell is dp[j] += dp[j-1] (above already in dp[j], left in the updated dp[j-1]); an OBSTACLE forces dp[j] = 0 since no path may terminate on it. Base dp[0] = 1 for the start cell unless it is blocked.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. dp[0] = grid[0][0] == 1 ? 0 : 1 (a blocked start ⇒ zero paths overall).
  2. For each cell: if it's an obstacle, dp[j] = 0; else if j > 0, dp[j] += dp[j-1].
  3. A free cell at j == 0 keeps its carried-over from-above value untouched.
  4. Return dp[n-1].

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — every cell relaxed once 💾 Space O(n) — a single rolling row

⚠️ Gotchas. If the start (or end) cell is itself an obstacle, the answer is 0 — handle the blocked-start seed explicitly. The first-column free cell must NOT add dp[j-1] (there's no left neighbour); guard with j > 0. A full obstacle row cuts the grid, correctly driving the count to 0 — verify your zeroing propagates.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's the same right/down path count, but an obstacle cell contributes zero ways and resets its dp entry. I roll one row across the grid; free cells add the left neighbour, blocked cells become 0, and a blocked start zeroes everything from the seed."
📄 UniquePathsII.java

13 Maximal Square LC 221 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. In a binary matrix, find the area of the largest square containing only 1s.

💡 Key insight. State dp[i][j] = side length of the largest all-ones square whose BOTTOM-RIGHT corner is (i,j). Transition: a square of side k ending here needs three squares of side k-1 ending just above, just left, and up-left to coexist, so when the cell is '1', dp[i][j] = 1 + min(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1], dp[i-1][j-1]); a '0' forces 0. The answer is the max side seen, squared. A zero border row/column removes edge special-cases.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate dp[m+1][n+1] (row/col 0 are the zero border); track maxSide.
  2. For each (i,j) with matrix[i-1][j-1] == '1': dp[i][j] = 1 + min(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1], dp[i-1][j-1]); update maxSide.
  3. Return maxSide * maxSide (area, not side).

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell computed in O(1) from three neighbours 💾 Space O(m·n) — the dp table (reducible to one row)

⚠️ Gotchas. It's the min of three neighbours, not the sum or max — a square is limited by its smallest adjoining square. Return the area (side²), not the side. The padding border lets edge cells use dp[i-1][*]/dp[*][j-1] without bounds checks. Easy to reduce to O(n) space with a saved diagonal, worth mentioning.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I anchor each square at its bottom-right corner. To grow a square of side k there, I need squares of side k-1 above, to the left, and diagonally — so the side is one plus the minimum of those three. The largest side squared is the answer; a zero-padded border keeps the edges clean."
📄 MaximalSquare.java

14 Longest Common Subsequence LC 1143 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the length of the longest subsequence common to two strings (characters in order, not necessarily contiguous).

💡 Key insight. State dp[i][j] = LCS length of the first i chars of a and first j of b. Transition: if a[i-1] == b[j-1] the two chars pair up, dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1] + 1; otherwise drop one end, dp[i][j] = max(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1]). Base row/column 0 are empty-prefix cases of 0.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate dp[m+1][n+1] (zeros default to the empty-prefix base).
  2. For i,j from 1: on a char match, dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1] + 1; else max(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1]).
  3. Return dp[m][n].

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — fill every cell once 💾 Space O(m·n) — the full dp grid (reducible to two rows)

⚠️ Gotchas. LCS ≠ longest common substring — subsequences may have gaps, so the mismatch branch takes a max rather than resetting to 0. Two rows (or one with a saved diagonal) suffice for O(min) space; the full table is kept here for easy backtracking of the actual subsequence. This is the template under edit distance and diff tools.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I compare prefixes. When the current characters match, they extend the diagonal subproblem by one. When they don't, I drop a character from one string and take the better of the two resulting subproblems. dp[m][n] is the answer, in O(mn)."
📄 LongestCommonSubsequence.java

15 Edit Distance LC 72 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Find the minimum number of single-character inserts, deletes, or replacements to turn word1 into word2.

💡 Key insight. State dp[i][j] = min edits to turn the first i chars of word1 into the first j of word2. Transition: if the current chars match, inherit dp[i-1][j-1] (free); else 1 + min of delete dp[i-1][j], insert dp[i][j-1], replace dp[i-1][j-1]. Base dp[i][0] = i (delete all) and dp[0][j] = j (insert all).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed the first row/column with dp[i][0] = i, dp[0][j] = j.
  2. For i,j from 1: on a match, dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1]; else 1 + min(replace, delete, insert).
  3. Return dp[m][n].

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell from three neighbours in O(1) 💾 Space O(m·n) — full table (reducible to two rows)

⚠️ Gotchas. Map each neighbour to the right operation: above = delete from word1, left = insert into word1, diagonal = replace. The base row/column encode building a string from empty by pure inserts/deletes — getting them wrong silently shifts every answer. On a match the move is free (no +1); a common slip is always adding 1.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "This is Levenshtein. dp[i][j] is the cost to transform the first i of word1 into the first j of word2. Equal characters cost nothing and carry the diagonal; otherwise I pay one edit and take the cheapest of insert, delete, or replace, which map to the left, above, and diagonal cells."
📄 EditDistance.java

16 Numbers At Most N Given Digit Set LC 902 ↗ Hard ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given a sorted set of single digits (1–9), count how many positive integers ≤ N can be written using only those digits (repeats allowed).

💡 Key insight. Digit DP split by length, with a "tight prefix" notion. (1) Any number with FEWER digits than N is automatically smaller; with d allowed digits there are d^len of length len (leading digit is free since all digits are 1..9) — sum over len = 1..L-1. (2) For EXACTLY L digits, walk N position by position keeping the prefix tight: at position i, each allowed digit strictly LESS than N's digit frees the remaining L-i-1 slots, contributing (#smaller digits)·d^(L-i-1); if N's digit is allowed, stay tight and continue, else stop. (3) Finally +1 if N itself is fully constructible (the tight walk reached the end).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Precompute powers[k] = d^k (free numbers filling k slots).
  2. Add powers[len] for every shorter length 1..L-1.
  3. Walk N's digits: for each, count allowed digits < num[i] times powers[L-i-1]; note whether num[i] itself is allowed (can stay tight). If not, return the running count.
  4. If the tight walk completes, return count + 1 (N itself counts).

⏱️ Time O(L·d) — L digits of N, each scanning the d allowed digits 💾 Space O(L) — the powers-of-d table

⚠️ Gotchas. The "tight prefix" flag is the soul of digit DP: once N's current digit isn't in the set you can never match the remaining prefix, so you stop — forgetting this over-counts. The "+1 for N itself" is easy to miss and only applies when every digit of N is constructible. Leading digits are unconstrained here precisely because the digit set excludes 0; with 0 in the set the shorter-length counting changes.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I count by length. All shorter lengths are unconditionally below N, giving d^len each. For equal length I march down N's digits keeping a tight prefix: any allowed digit below the current one lets the rest run free, while matching it keeps me tight. If I stay tight to the end, N itself is constructible, so I add one."
📄 NumbersAtMostNGivenDigitSet.java

17 Student Attendance Record II LC 552 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Count length-n records over {A, L, P} that are "rewardable": at most one A total, and never 3 or more L's in a row (answer mod 1e9+7).

💡 Key insight. A state machine over (a, l) where a = total A's so far (0 or 1) and l = number of L's at the very END (0, 1, or 2) — six states. Transition by appended char: 'P' resets trailing L's to 0 ((a,*) → (a,0)); 'A' is legal only from a=0 → (1,0) (also resetting L's); 'L' is legal only from l<2 → (a,l+1). Base dp[0][0] = 1 (empty record). Roll n times and sum all six buckets.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep dp[2][3] counting records of the current length per state; seed dp[0][0] = 1.
  2. For each of n steps, build next: from each populated (a,l), add to next[a][0] (P), next[1][0] if a==0 (A), next[a][l+1] if l<2 (L) — all mod 1e9+7.
  3. After n rolls, sum all six states.

⏱️ Time O(n) — constant work (six states) per appended character 💾 Space O(1) — a fixed 2×3 state table

⚠️ Gotchas. An 'A' breaks any L-run, so it must reset l to 0 (not just bump a) — a frequent bug. Apply the modulus on every addition to avoid long overflow at large n. The empty record (n=0) counts as 1. The six-state machine generalises cleanly to other "at most k of X, no run of Y" counting problems.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I track just enough memory to enforce both rules: how many A's I've used and how many L's are currently trailing — six states. Appending P, A, or L deterministically moves between states, dropping anything illegal. I roll the counts n times and sum the survivors, all mod 1e9+7, in linear time and constant space."
📄 StudentAttendanceRecordII.java

18 Poor Pigs LC 458 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. One of buckets is poisoned; with a given test duration and time-to-die, find the minimum number of pigs that can identify the poisoned bucket.

💡 Key insight. This is information-theoretic counting, NOT search — recognising the imposter is the whole point. With t = floor(minutesToTest / minutesToDie) rounds, a single pig's outcome is "died in round 1..t, or never" = t+1 distinguishable states. Pigs act independently, so p pigs encode (t+1)^p joint states, and you need at least one per bucket: (t+1)^p >= buckets. The minimum p = ceil(log_{t+1}(buckets)), computed by multiplying (t+1) until it covers all buckets (integer-only, dodging float rounding).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. statesPerPig = minutesToTest / minutesToDie + 1.
  2. pigs = 0, reachable = 1; while reachable < buckets: reachable *= statesPerPig; pigs++.
  3. Return pigs.

⏱️ Time O(log_{t+1}(buckets)) — one multiply per pig added 💾 Space O(1) — a running product and counter

⚠️ Gotchas. Don't model this as a DP table or a search — it's a base-(t+1) encoding argument. Compute the count by repeated multiplication, not Math.log, to avoid float rounding (log can return 6.9999 instead of 7). Edge case: a single bucket needs 0 pigs. Each pig encodes one digit in base t+1 — that mental model is what interviewers want to hear.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each pig is one base-(t+1) digit: across t rounds it can die in any round or survive, giving t+1 outcomes. p pigs distinguish (t+1)^p cases, so I need the smallest p with (t+1)^p ≥ buckets. I find it by multiplying t+1 until it covers the buckets — integer math, no log rounding."
📄 PoorPigs.java

19 Maximum Profit in Job Scheduling LC 1235 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given jobs as (start, end, profit), pick a subset of non-overlapping jobs that maximises total profit.

💡 Key insight. This is weighted interval scheduling. Sort jobs by end time so that "best profit using only jobs that finish early enough" becomes a prefix you can binary-search into. State dp[i] = best total profit using jobs[0..i] (by end), which is monotonic non-decreasing. Transition: either skip job i (dp[i-1]) or take it — add its profit to dp[idx], where idx is the latest job whose end <= this job's start, found by binary search: dp[i] = max(skip, take).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build job triples and sort by end time; record a parallel ends[] array.
  2. For each i: binary-search ends[0..i-1] for the largest index whose end <= jobs[i].start (latest non-conflicting job).
  3. take = jobs[i].profit + (idx == -1 ? 0 : dp[idx]); skip = (i == 0 ? 0 : dp[i-1]); dp[i] = max(skip, take).
  4. Return dp[n-1] (0 for no jobs).

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — the sort dominates; each job does one O(log n) binary search 💾 Space O(n) — the jobs array plus parallel dp/ends arrays

⚠️ Gotchas. Sort by end, not start — that's what makes the compatible-prefix binary search valid, since dp is monotonic in end-sorted order. Compare ends with <= the next start so jobs that abut at a boundary count as non-overlapping. The "take" branch reads dp[idx] (the best compatible prefix), not dp[i-1] — mixing them up double-books overlapping jobs.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's weighted interval scheduling: I can't just take the most jobs, I want the most profit. I sort by end time so 'best profit among jobs ending before I start' is a prefix I binary-search into. For each job dp is the max of skipping it or taking it plus the best compatible earlier prefix. Sort dominates, so O(n log n)."
📄 MaximumProfitInJobScheduling.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the list in order — it climbs 1-D → grid → string → math/state by design. First internalise the say-it-in-four-lines habit (state, transition, base, order) on Climbing Stairs and House Robber, then re-derive each from a blank file: the rolling pair, the circular-split, the unbounded-knapsack loop order (the combinations-vs-permutations distinction in Coin Change I vs II is a classic trap), the grid roll, and the two-string table. Save the three hard ones for last and rehearse them out loud: digit DP's tight-prefix flag (902), the six-state attendance machine (552), and the base-(t+1) encoding for Poor Pigs (458). Google-favourite follow-ups to have ready: Coin Change (322) reconstructing the actual coins, Maximal Square (221) reduced to O(n) space, and LIS (300) reconstructing the subsequence with parent pointers.

  • ✅ I can state the four ingredients — state, transition, base, fill order — for any DP before coding.
  • ✅ I can collapse Climbing Stairs / House Robber / Decode Ways to two rolling scalars.
  • ✅ I can explain why House Robber II reduces to two linear runs, and why one pass can't do it.
  • ✅ I can articulate the coins-outer (combinations) vs amount-outer (permutations) loop-order rule.
  • ✅ I can write the LCS and Edit Distance tables and map each neighbour to its operation.
  • ✅ I can derive Maximal Square's 1 + min(3 neighbours) and return area, not side.
  • ✅ I can solve LIS in O(n log n) with patience sorting and explain why tails isn't a real LIS.
  • ✅ I can explain digit DP's tight-prefix flag (902) and the six-state attendance machine (552).
  • ✅ I can recognise Poor Pigs as base-(t+1) encoding and compute it without floating-point logs.

🎒 0/1 Knapsack & Subset-Sum DP

The 0/1 knapsack pattern answers "which items, each used at most once, hit a target capacity?" — and a huge family of problems is really subset-sum in disguise once you do the right algebra. The signature implementation is a 1-D dp over capacity swept high-to-low so each item is counted once. It is high-ROI for Google L6 because the recurring win is the reduction: spotting that "equal partition" or "assign ± signs to reach a target" collapses into one clean subset-sum recurrence.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 2 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • Each item is taken or not (used at most once), and you care whether/how-many-ways a target capacity is reachable.
  • "Partition into two equal halves", "subset summing to X", "assign +/− to reach a target" — all reduce to subset-sum.
  • Bounded integer weights/values so capacity is a small dimension (O(n · sum) is acceptable).
  • Boolean reachability (can we?) → OR DP; counting (how many ways?) → additive DP.
  • You catch yourself writing a 2-D dp[i][cap] but only ever read row i-1 ⇒ collapse to 1-D with a high-to-low sweep.

🧠 Core idea & template

The 2-D state is dp[i][s] = "using the first i items, can we / in how many ways can we make sum s?" Since item i only reads row i-1, collapse to a 1-D array. The critical detail: sweep capacity from high to low so dp[s - v] still refers to the previous item's state — a low-to-high sweep would let the same item be used multiple times (that's the unbounded knapsack variant).

// STATE:      dp[s] over the first i items considered so far
//             reachability: dp[s] = can we make sum s?   (boolean, OR)
//             counting:     dp[s] = #subsets making s?   (int, +=)
// TRANSITION (per item v): dp[s] |= dp[s - v]   OR   dp[s] += dp[s - v]
// SWEEP:      s from HIGH down to v  -> each item used at most once (0/1)
// GOAL:       dp[target]

dp[0] = base;                        // empty subset: reachable / one way
for (int v : items) {
    for (int s = target; s >= v; s--) {   // HIGH -> LOW for 0/1 (single use)
        dp[s] = combine(dp[s], dp[s - v]);  // ||  for reachable, +=  for count
    }
}
return dp[target];

// Reachable-vs-count is the only line that changes.
// Low->high sweep instead = UNBOUNDED knapsack (each item reusable).

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Partition Equal Subset Sum LC 416 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav Boolean subset-sum to total/2, 1-D high→lowO(n·sum)O(sum)
2Target Sum LC 494 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged Algebra → count subsets summing to (total+target)/2O(n·S)O(S)

📘 Deep dive

1 Partition Equal Subset Sum LC 416 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Decide whether nums can be split into two subsets with equal sum.

💡 Key insight. Two equal halves exist iff some subset sums to total / 2 — a boolean 0/1 subset-sum. dp[s] = "can we hit exactly s with the items seen so far"; per item v, dp[s] |= dp[s - v]. If total is odd, it's impossible immediately.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sum the array; if odd, return false. Let half = total / 2.
  2. dp = boolean[half+1], dp[0] = true (empty subset sums to 0).
  3. For each v, sweep s from half down to v: dp[s] = dp[s] || dp[s - v].
  4. Short-circuit: return true as soon as dp[half] becomes true.

⏱️ Time O(n·sum) — each item relaxes every capacity up to total/2 once 💾 Space O(sum) — a single boolean row indexed by subset sum

⚠️ Gotchas. The high-to-low sweep is mandatory: a low-to-high sweep lets one item contribute multiple times, silently solving the wrong (unbounded) problem. The odd-total early-out avoids an off-by-one on a half that isn't an integer. dp[0] = true is the base case — forgetting it makes everything unreachable. This is pseudo-polynomial: it's O(n·sum), not polynomial in input bits — fine here because sums are bounded.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Equal partition is equivalent to finding a subset that sums to half the total, which is classic 0/1 subset-sum. I keep a boolean array indexed by achievable sum, start with dp[0]=true, and for each number OR in dp[s-v]. Sweeping capacities high-to-low guarantees each number is used at most once. If the total is odd I bail immediately. It's O(n·sum) time, O(sum) space."
📄 PartitionEqualSubsetSum.java

2 Target Sum LC 494 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Assign a + or - to every number in nums; count the assignments whose signed sum equals target.

💡 Key insight. Let P be the positive set and N the negative set. Then sum(P) - sum(N) = target and sum(P) + sum(N) = total, so sum(P) = (total + target) / 2. Counting sign assignments collapses into counting subsets that sum to that fixed value — a 0/1 subset-sum count DP. The only change from problem 1 is += instead of ||.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Compute total. If |target| > total or (total + target) is odd, return 0 (no valid P).
  2. Let S = (total + target) / 2. Use dp = int[S+1], dp[0] = 1 (one way to make 0: pick nothing).
  3. For each v, sweep c from S down to v: dp[c] += dp[c - v].
  4. Return dp[S].

⏱️ Time O(n·S) — S = (total+target)/2, each item relaxes capacities once 💾 Space O(S) — a single count row indexed by subset sum

⚠️ Gotchas. Guard both impossibilities: |target| > total and the parity check (total + target) odd — otherwise you index a negative/fractional S. dp[0] = 1 (not 0) is the counting base case. Still sweep high-to-low so each item is counted at most once. Watch sign/overflow: target can be negative, and the count can be large (fits in int for the given constraints, but be deliberate). Zeros in nums are handled naturally here because each contributes a dp[c] += dp[c-0]… (note: this implementation uses the algebra form, so a 0 simply doubles reachable counts via S unaffected) — mention you'd verify zero handling.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I turn the sign assignment into algebra: the plus-group minus the minus-group equals target, and the two groups sum to the total, so the plus-group must sum to (total+target)/2. That makes it 'count the subsets summing to a fixed S' — the same 0/1 subset-sum as equal-partition but counting instead of boolean, so the line becomes dp[c] += dp[c-v]. I reject impossible cases when (total+target) is odd or target exceeds total. O(n·S) time, O(S) space."
📄 TargetSum.java

🔁 Practice plan

Do 416 first to lock in the boolean 1-D subset-sum and the high-to-low sweep, then 494 to practise the algebraic reduction and the switch from || to +=. From a blank file, write the 1-D knapsack loop and explain in one sentence why the sweep direction controls single-vs-multiple use. Rehearse the follow-ups below — they all reuse this exact skeleton.

  • ✅ I can explain why the capacity sweep must go high-to-low for 0/1, and what low-to-high would compute (unbounded).
  • ✅ I can derive sum(P) = (total + target)/2 for Target Sum and state both rejection conditions.
  • ✅ I can switch between boolean-reachable and integer-count DP by changing a single line.
  • ✅ Follow-up: Coin Change II (LC 518) — count combinations with unbounded coins; same array, low-to-high sweep.
  • ✅ Follow-up: Ones and Zeroes (LC 474) and Last Stone Weight II (LC 1049) — multi-dimensional / minimisation knapsack reductions.

🎈 Interval DP

Interval DP solves problems on a line where the answer for a range [i, j] is built from answers on its sub-ranges, by fixing one element as the "last operation" or "split point". The state is a 2-D table over endpoints filled by increasing length; the recurring trick is choosing what to fix (last balloon, first cut, which pile to take) so the two halves become independent. It is high-ROI for Google L6 because the modelling — especially "iterate over the last action, not the first" — is a clean, reusable insight that interviewers love to probe.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The answer for a segment depends on choosing a split/pivot inside it, and sub-segments are solved the same way: [i, j] → [i, k] + [k, j].
  • Operations on a sequence where removing/cutting an element changes the neighbours of what remains (balloons, stones, sticks).
  • Two-player optimal-play / game theory on a row where each turn takes from an end.
  • n up to a few hundred to ~1000 (so O(n²) states and an O(n²) or O(n³) fill are acceptable).
  • You're tempted to fix the first move but the halves stay coupled — try fixing the last move (or the boundary length) so they decouple.

🧠 Core idea & template

Define dp[i][j] over a range delimited by indices i and j (often padded with sentinels). Fill by increasing length so every sub-range is ready before the range that uses it. For each range, scan a pivot k with i < k < j that splits the work into two independent sub-ranges plus a local cost; the pivot is whichever choice makes the halves stop interacting — typically the last thing removed or the first cut.

// STATE:      dp[i][j] = best answer for the range bounded by i and j
// TRANSITION: dp[i][j] = OPT over i < k < j of
//                          dp[i][k] + localCost(i, k, j) + dp[k][j]
// FILL ORDER: by increasing length so sub-ranges are ready first
// GOAL:       dp[0][n+1]  (with sentinel padding) or dp[0][n-1]

for (int len = 2; len <= N; len++) {          // length of the span
    for (int i = 0; i + len <= N; i++) {      // left boundary
        int j = i + len;                      // right boundary
        for (int k = i + 1; k < j; k++) {     // pivot = last op / first cut
            int val = dp[i][k] + localCost(i, k, j) + dp[k][j];
            dp[i][j] = OPT(dp[i][j], val);    // max or min as the problem asks
        }
    }
}
return dp[0][N - 1];

// Game variant (take from an end): dp[i][j] = score difference for mover,
//   dp[i][j] = max(a[i] - dp[i+1][j], a[j] - dp[i][j-1]); base dp[i][i] = a[i].

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Stone Game LC 877 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Game DP on score difference, take from an endO(n²)O(n²)
2Burst Balloons LC 312 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav Interval DP fixing the LAST balloon burstO(n³)O(n²)
3Minimum Cost to Cut a Stick LC 1547 ↗ Hard ◻️ Gap-fill Interval DP over sorted cuts, first cut splitsO(m³)O(m²)

📘 Deep dive

1 Stone Game LC 877 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Two players alternately take a pile from either end of piles; both play optimally. Return whether Alice (first) ends with more stones than Bob.

💡 Key insight. Track the score difference (current player minus opponent) rather than two separate totals. dp[i][j] = the best difference the mover can force on piles[i..j]; taking a pile and then subtracting the subgame value flips the perspective to the opponent. Alice wins iff dp[0][n-1] > 0.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Base case: dp[i][i] = piles[i] (one pile, mover just takes it).
  2. Fill by increasing length: dp[i][j] = max(piles[i] - dp[i+1][j], piles[j] - dp[i][j-1]).
  3. The subtraction encodes "whatever margin the opponent forces on the remainder counts against me."
  4. Return dp[0][n-1] > 0.

⏱️ Time O(n²) — fill each cell of the upper triangle once 💾 Space O(n²) — the dp table (reducible to O(n) with a rolling row)

⚠️ Gotchas. The minus sign is the whole trick — adding the subgame value computes the wrong thing (it assumes both players cooperate). There's a famous O(1) shortcut: with an even number of piles and a nonzero total, the first player can always grab all odd-indexed or all even-indexed piles, so Alice always wins — but the general DP is what generalises to the variants interviewers ask. Fill strictly by increasing length so dp[i+1][j] and dp[i][j-1] already exist.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of tracking both scores, I track the difference for whoever's about to move. On range [i,j] I either take the left pile and inherit the negation of the opponent's best on [i+1,j], or take the right and inherit the negation of their best on [i,j-1]; I keep the max. Base case is a single pile. Alice wins iff dp[0][n-1] is positive — O(n²). For this exact constraint there's even an O(1) parity argument, but the DP is the reusable answer."
📄 StoneGame.java

2 Burst Balloons LC 312 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Bursting balloon i earns nums[i-1]*nums[i]*nums[i+1] (out-of-range neighbours count as 1). Burst all balloons to maximise total coins.

💡 Key insight. If you fix the first balloon to burst, the two sides remain coupled (the burst changes both neighbours). Instead fix the last balloon k to burst in an open interval (i, j): by the time k pops, everything else in (i, j) is gone, so its surviving neighbours are exactly the boundaries i and j, contributing vals[i]*vals[k]*vals[j] — and now the two sub-intervals are independent.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Pad the array: vals = [1, nums…, 1] so edge bursts see a 1 on the missing side.
  2. dp[i][j] = max coins from bursting all balloons strictly between boundaries i and j (exclusive).
  3. Fill by increasing span; for each interval scan k in (i, j) as the last burst: dp[i][j] = max(dp[i][k] + vals[i]*vals[k]*vals[j] + dp[k][j]).
  4. Answer is dp[0][n+1] (the whole padded range).

⏱️ Time O(n³) — O(n²) intervals, each scanning O(n) split points 💾 Space O(n²) — the dp table

⚠️ Gotchas. Choosing the first burst instead of the last is the classic trap — it doesn't decouple the halves. The boundaries i and j are exclusive (never burst inside this subproblem), which is why k's neighbours are always vals[i] and vals[j]. The sentinel padding shifts indices by one — index carefully. The interval endpoints are positions, not values; don't multiply by the wrong cells.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The hard part is that bursting changes neighbours, so the order matters and naive recursion couples both sides. The trick is to think about which balloon I burst last in a range: at that moment everything between the two boundaries is already gone, so the balloon only touches the boundaries, and the left and right sub-ranges became independent subproblems. I pad with 1s, define dp over exclusive boundaries, and fill by length — O(n³)."
📄 BurstBalloons.java

3 Minimum Cost to Cut a Stick LC 1547 ↗ Hard ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. A stick of length n must be cut at the given positions cuts. Each cut costs the current length of the piece being cut. Find the minimum total cost over all cut orders.

💡 Key insight. Add sentinels 0 and n to cuts and sort. For the piece spanning boundaries cuts[i]…cuts[j], whichever cut k you make first costs the full length cuts[j] - cuts[i] (independent of which cut it is), and then the two resulting pieces are cut independently. So dp[i][j] = (cuts[j]-cuts[i]) + min over k of dp[i][k] + dp[k][j].
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Copy cuts, append 0 and n, and sort so boundaries are ordered.
  2. dp[i][j] = min cost to make every cut strictly between cuts[i] and cuts[j]; zero when no cut lies between them.
  3. Fill by increasing length (span ≥ 2 boundaries ⇒ ≥1 cut inside). For each interval pick the first cut k: best = min(dp[i][k] + dp[k][j]).
  4. dp[i][j] = (c[j]-c[i]) + best. Answer is dp[0][m+1].

⏱️ Time O(m³) — m sorted boundaries, O(m²) intervals each scanning O(m) splits 💾 Space O(m²) — the dp table

⚠️ Gotchas. You must sort and add the 0/n sentinels — cutting in input order is not optimal and the cost is defined by the enclosing piece's length. The piece length c[j]-c[i] is added once per interval regardless of which cut is chosen first; don't fold it into the inner loop incorrectly. Initialise best to a large value, and note adjacent boundaries (no cut between) correctly contribute 0. Structurally identical to Burst Balloons — recognise the family.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Greedy on cut order fails because each cut's price is the length of the piece it's in. I sort the cut positions with 0 and n as sentinels, then for any piece between two boundaries I reason about the first cut: it always costs that piece's full length, and afterwards the two sub-pieces are independent. So dp[i][j] = length of the piece + the best split into dp[i][k] and dp[k][j]. Fill by increasing span — O(m³). It's the same skeleton as Burst Balloons."
📄 MinimumCostToCutAStick.java

🔁 Practice plan

Start with 877 to get comfortable with the 2-D fill-by-length skeleton and the score-difference idea, then do 312 (the canonical "fix the last operation" problem), then 1547 to see the same structure with sorted cut boundaries and "fix the first cut". From a blank file, re-derive the increasing-length triple loop and articulate why you fix the last burst vs the first cut. Rehearse the follow-ups below.

  • ✅ I can explain why Burst Balloons fixes the last balloon, not the first, and how that decouples the halves.
  • ✅ I can derive the score-difference recurrence for Stone Game and state the O(1) parity shortcut.
  • ✅ I can recognise that Cut-a-Stick and Burst Balloons share one interval-DP skeleton.
  • ✅ Follow-up: Predict the Winner (LC 486) — Stone Game without the even/equal-sum guarantees; same difference DP.
  • ✅ Follow-up: Remove Boxes (LC 546) and Strange Printer (LC 664) — interval DP needing an extra state dimension (run length / colour).

🎭 Bitmask / Subset DP

When the state space is "which subset of a small universe have I covered/visited so far?", an integer bitmask is the DP key: each of the up-to-~20 elements becomes one bit, and a single int enumerates all 2^n subsets. This family is high-ROI for Google L6 because the hard part is the modelling leap — recognising that "set of skills", "set of visited nodes", or "buckets filled" collapses into one machine word — and that reframing instantly unlocks otherwise-exponential problems.

Source: High-ROI Staff gap (curated 2026) · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • Small universe: n ≤ 16–20 elements, and the answer depends on which subset you've handled, not just how many.
  • Phrases like "cover all skills", "visit every node", "use each item at most once" combined with a tiny element count.
  • You want to memoise over a set, and the set is too unstructured to summarise with a single number (so a bitmask becomes the key).
  • Shortest-path / reachability where the node alone isn't a sufficient state — you must remember what you've already seen (node, visitedMask).
  • "Partition into k groups" with small n: either backtrack over assignment, or DP over a subset-of-used-elements mask.

🧠 Core idea & template

Let one bit of an integer represent "element i is included / covered / visited". The DP state is that mask (optionally paired with a position), and each transition ORs in more bits — masks only grow, so the state graph is a DAG (or, for unweighted shortest path, BFS layers). Iterate masks in increasing order for a forward DP, or BFS over (node, mask) when you need fewest edges. The full mask (1<<n)-1 is the goal.

// STATE:      dp[mask] = best answer for "covered set == mask"
// TRANSITION: for each reachable mask, try adding element p:
//             next = mask | bitOf[p];  dp[next] = combine(dp[next], dp[mask] + cost[p])
// GOAL:       dp[(1<<n) - 1]

int FULL = (1 << n) - 1;
dp[0] = identity;                    // empty set is the base case
for (int mask = 0; mask <= FULL; mask++) {
    if (!reachable(dp[mask])) continue;
    for (int p = 0; p < choices; p++) {
        int next = mask | bitOf[p];  // OR in p's contribution; masks only grow
        if (next == mask) continue;  // p adds nothing here
        dp[next] = better(dp[next], extend(dp[mask], p));
    }
}
return dp[FULL];

// Unweighted shortest path variant -- BFS over (node, mask), multi-source seed:
//   state = (node, 1<<node) for every node; first state with mask == FULL = answer.

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Partition to K Equal Sum Subsets LC 698 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature Backtracking, fill one bucket at a time, pruneO(k·2ⁿ)O(n)
2Smallest Sufficient Team LC 1125 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature Set-cover DP over skill-coverage maskO(2ᵐ·P)O(2ᵐ)
3Shortest Path Visiting All Nodes LC 847 ↗ Hard 🟥 Staff-tagged BFS over (node, visitedMask), multi-sourceO(2ⁿ·n²)O(2ⁿ·n)

📘 Deep dive

1 Partition to K Equal Sum Subsets LC 698 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given nums and k, decide whether the array can be split into k non-empty subsets all having the same sum.

💡 Key insight. Every subset must sum to target = total / k, so instead of juggling all k buckets at once, fill one bucket at a time: keep adding numbers until it hits target, then "close" it and start the next. The last bucket completes automatically once the first k-1 are exact.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If total % k != 0, return false. Sort ascending; if the largest element > target, return false.
  2. Backtrack with a used[] flag array, a running cur sum, and bucketsLeft. Scan candidates from high index downward.
  3. When cur == target, recurse for a fresh bucket (reset cur=0, scan from the top, bucketsLeft-1).
  4. Two prunes: skip any candidate that overflows target; and if a number can't even start an empty bucket (cur == 0 after a failed attempt), no empty bucket can hold it — break immediately.

⏱️ Time O(k·2ⁿ) — n placement decisions across k buckets, heavily pruned in practice 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth plus the used[] flags

⚠️ Gotchas. Sorting descending (placing large, hard-to-fit numbers first) makes prunes fire near the root — placing small numbers first explores far more dead ends. Forgetting the cur == 0 → break prune is the difference between passing and TLE. Don't reset the scan start to 0 mid-bucket; keep going downward to avoid re-trying symmetric placements within the same bucket.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force tries every assignment of n items to k buckets — k^n. The key realisation is that buckets are interchangeable and each must hit total/k, so I greedily fill one bucket to exactly target before opening the next, which prunes the symmetry. Sorting descending and adding the 'can't start an empty bucket ⇒ abandon' prune keeps the worst case at O(k·2ⁿ) but makes it fast on real inputs."
📄 PartitionToKEqualSumSubsets.java

2 Smallest Sufficient Team LC 1125 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a list of required skills (≤16) and people each owning a subset of skills, return the indices of a smallest team whose combined skills cover every required skill.

💡 Key insight. This is minimum set cover, which is NP-hard in general — but with ≤16 skills the covered-skills set fits in a 16-bit mask, so we DP over coverage states (2¹⁶) instead of over people. dp[mask] = the smallest team covering exactly the skills in mask, stored itself as a long person-bitmask so we can compare sizes and reconstruct with no parent array.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Map each required skill to a bit index; precompute each person's skill mask skillOf[p] (intersected with required skills).
  2. dp[0] = 0L (cover nothing with nobody). Iterate masks 0 … full; skip unreachable ones.
  3. From a reachable mask, for each person p compute next = mask | skillOf[p]; the candidate team is dp[mask] | (1L<<p). Keep it if next was unseen or the candidate has fewer set bits (Long.bitCount).
  4. Reconstruct by reading the set bits of dp[full].

⏱️ Time O(2ᵐ·P) — 2ᵐ coverage states, each tried against P people 💾 Space O(2ᵐ) — one team bitmask per coverage state

⚠️ Gotchas. Store the team (person-bitmask) in dp, not just a count — otherwise you can't reconstruct. People can be up to 60, so the team mask must be a long (1L not 1). Skip next == mask (the person adds no new skill) to avoid pointlessly inflating the team. Compare with Long.bitCount, not raw long value — a larger bit pattern can still have fewer bits.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's set cover, NP-hard in general, so I exploit the small skill count: at most 16 skills means coverage fits in a bitmask and there are only 2¹⁶ states. I DP forward over coverage, OR-ing in each person's skills and keeping whichever team has fewer members for each reached coverage. Storing the team as a long person-mask lets me both compare sizes and reconstruct directly. That's 2¹⁶·P ≈ a few million ops."
📄 SmallestSufficientTeam.java

3 Shortest Path Visiting All Nodes LC 847 ↗ Hard 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Given an undirected connected graph (n ≤ 12), return the length of the shortest walk that visits every node; you may start and end anywhere and revisit nodes/edges freely.

💡 Key insight. A node alone is not a sufficient state because revisiting is allowed; the real state is (node, visitedMask). Every edge only ever adds bits to the mask, so although the plain-node graph has cycles, the state graph never regresses. Edges are unweighted ⇒ BFS over states gives fewest steps, and seeding the queue with all nodes at once handles "start anywhere".
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If n == 1, return 0. Compute fullMask = (1<<n)-1.
  2. Multi-source seed: enqueue (node, 1<<node) for every node and mark visited[node][1<<node].
  3. BFS level by level (each level = one step). Pop (node, mask); if mask == fullMask, return the current step count.
  4. For each neighbour, compute nextMask = mask | (1<<next); if that state is unvisited, mark and enqueue.

⏱️ Time O(2ⁿ·n²) — 2ⁿ·n states, each expanding up to n neighbours 💾 Space O(2ⁿ·n) — visited set and queue over all states

⚠️ Gotchas. The visited set must key on (node, mask), not just node — keying on node alone would forbid the legal revisits and break correctness. Return at dequeue time on the first full mask (BFS guarantees it's shortest); checking only the start states misses the answer. The n == 1 base case (0 edges) is easy to forget. Single-source BFS gives a path from one fixed start, not the global optimum — the multi-source seed is essential.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because we can revisit, the state isn't the node — it's the node plus the set of nodes seen, which I pack into a bitmask. Edges add bits and never remove them, so over states there are no useless cycles. Unweighted edges mean BFS finds the minimum number of steps, and since we can start anywhere I seed the queue with all n start states. The first time I pop a state whose mask is full, that step count is the answer — O(2ⁿ·n²) overall, fine for n up to 12."
📄 ShortestPathVisitingAllNodes.java

🔁 Practice plan

Do them in glance order: 698 to internalise "fill one bucket at a time + symmetry prunes", then 1125 to drill the forward coverage-mask DP, then 847 to fuse bitmask state with BFS. From a blank file, re-derive the generic dp[mask] → dp[mask | bitOf[p]] loop and the multi-source BFS seed without looking. Rehearse the Google-favourite follow-ups below.

  • ✅ I can explain why the state must be (node, mask) in 847, and why single-source BFS is wrong.
  • ✅ I can justify the two prunes in 698 and why sorting descending makes them fire early.
  • ✅ I can store and reconstruct the chosen set from a long bitmask in 1125 without a parent array.
  • ✅ Follow-up: Travelling Salesman (Held–Karp) — dp[mask][i] = min cost ending at i having visited mask; same family with a position dimension.
  • ✅ Follow-up: Maximum Students Taking Exam (LC 1349) and Number of Ways to Wear Hats (LC 1434) — row-by-row / element-by-element bitmask DP with adjacency constraints.

🔢 Bit Manipulation & Math / Sampling

A grab-bag of "do you actually understand the primitive" problems: two's-complement bit tricks, number theory (gcd, primes), fast exponentiation, the bitwise trie, and randomized sampling under streaming or weighted constraints. Individually short, but Google loves them as warm-ups and as the hidden subroutine inside a bigger system-design or data-structures question — and the sampling ones are really about proving uniformity, not writing code. High signal-per-minute, so know the reservoir-sampling and weighted-pick idioms cold at L6.

Source: Prep Tracker, Deep Research Adds & curated Staff gaps · 9 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Without using +/-", "in O(log n)", or "count set bits" → you're being asked for a bit-level identity, not a library call.
  • A function repeated over 0..n where each answer reuses a smaller one's bits → bottom-up DP on the binary structure (drop the low bit, halve the index).
  • "gcd", "repeating pattern / period", "tile that builds the whole" → Euclid's algorithm; for strings, the concatenation-commutes trick.
  • "How many primes below n" → Sieve of Eratosthenes, cross from i*i.
  • "Maximize XOR over a pair", "two numbers in an array" → build a binary trie and walk the opposite bit greedily.
  • "Pick uniformly from a stream / unknown length" → reservoir sampling; "pick proportional to weight" → prefix sums + binary search.
  • Anything with Integer.MIN_VALUE, negation, or i*i overflow → widen to long before the arithmetic.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two reusable engines dominate this chapter. First, the bit identities: addition = XOR (sum without carry) plus (a&b)<<1 (the carry), and popcount(i) = popcount(i>>1) + (i&1). Second, greedy walks: binary exponentiation multiplies in x^(2^k) only where the exponent's bit is set, and the binary trie descends toward the opposite bit to force each XOR bit to 1. For sampling, memorize the two idioms below — they come up verbatim and the interviewer wants the probability argument.

// Reservoir sampling, size 1: uniform pick from an unknown-length stream.
T chosen = null; int k = 0;
for (T item : stream) {
    k++;
    if (rng.nextInt(k) == 0) chosen = item;   // keep the k-th element w.p. 1/k
}
// Invariant: after k items every seen item is held with probability 1/k.

// Weighted pick: prefix sums + lower-bound binary search.
int[] prefix; int total;            // prefix[i] = w[0]+...+w[i], total = prefix[last]
int pick() {
    int target = rng.nextInt(total) + 1;          // uniform in [1, total]
    int lo = 0, hi = prefix.length - 1;
    while (lo < hi) {                             // smallest index with prefix >= target
        int mid = (lo + hi) >>> 1;
        if (prefix[mid] < target) lo = mid + 1; else hi = mid;
    }
    return lo;
}

// Binary exponentiation: x^n in O(log n).
double pow(double x, long n) {
    double r = 1.0;
    while (n > 0) { if ((n & 1) == 1) r *= x; x *= x; n >>= 1; }
    return r;
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance
