📘 Deep dive

1 Counting Bits LC 338 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return an array ans where ans[i] is the number of set bits (popcount) of every integer from 0 to n.

💡 Key insight. i shares all but its lowest bit with i>>1 (i with its last bit dropped), so popcount(i) = popcount(i>>1) + (i&1). Every value becomes an O(1) lookup of an already-computed smaller index — pure bottom-up DP, no per-number bit loop.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate dp[n+1]; dp[0] = 0 by default.
  2. For i from 1 to n: dp[i] = dp[i >> 1] + (i & 1) — reuse the count for i with its last bit removed, then add 1 if i is odd.
  3. Return dp.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one constant-work transition per index 💾 Space O(n) — the answer array (O(1) auxiliary beyond output)

⚠️ Gotchas. The naive answer calls Integer.bitCount per value (O(n log n)) — fine, but interviewers want the O(n) recurrence. An equivalent transition is dp[i] = dp[i & (i-1)] + 1 (drop the lowest set bit); know at least one. Watch the n=0 case: the array is just [0].
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force is popcount each number independently. The insight is that shifting i right by one drops exactly its lowest bit, whose value I can recover with i&1, so every popcount is one already-solved subproblem plus a constant — linear time, linear output."
📄 CountingBits.java

2 Greatest Common Divisor of Strings LC 1071 ↗ Easy ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Return the largest string x such that x repeated some number of times equals str1 AND x repeated some number equals str2 (or "" if none).

💡 Key insight. A common base string exists iff str1+str2 == str2+str1 — concatenation commutes only when both strings are powers of one shared period. Given that, the divisor's length is exactly gcd(len1, len2) (the largest tile length that evenly partitions both), so the answer is the prefix of that length.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If (str1 + str2).equals(str2 + str1) is false, no common period exists → return "".
  2. Compute g = gcd(str1.length(), str2.length()) via Euclid's algorithm (gcd(a,b) = gcd(b, a mod b)).
  3. Return str1.substring(0, g) — the tile that repeats to form both strings.

⏱️ Time O(m+n) — the two concatenation-equality checks dominate; gcd is O(log), prefix copy O(g) 💾 Space O(m+n) — the concatenations built for the commutativity test

⚠️ Gotchas. The commutativity check is the whole trick — without it you might wrongly return a prefix when the strings have no shared period (e.g. "LEET"/"CODE"). Don't compute gcd of the strings recursively character-by-character; gcd is over the lengths. The prefix can come from either string — they agree on it once the check passes.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "If a single tile builds both, then gluing them in either order gives the same string, so I test str1+str2 == str2+str1 first. Once that holds, the period length must divide both lengths, and the biggest such divisor is their gcd — so I return that-length prefix."
📄 GreatestCommonDivisorOfStrings.java

3 Count Primes LC 204 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Count the prime numbers strictly less than a non-negative integer n.

💡 Key insight. Sieve of Eratosthenes: assume every number is prime, then for each prime i strike out its multiples. Crossing can start at i*i rather than 2*i because any smaller multiple k*i with k<i was already crossed when the smaller factor k was processed — this is exactly what brings the work down to near-linear.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If n < 3 return 0 (no primes below 2).
  2. Make boolean[] composite of size n; iterate i from 2 to n-1.
  3. If composite[i], skip. Otherwise it's prime: increment count.
  4. Guard (long) i*i >= n (overflow-safe) and otherwise mark composite[j] = true for j = i*i; j < n; j += i.
  5. Return count.

⏱️ Time O(n log log n) — the harmonic-over-primes sum of multiples crossed 💾 Space O(n) — one boolean per candidate below n

⚠️ Gotchas. "Strictly less than" — primes counted are < n, so countPrimes(2) == 0. The start index i*i overflows int for large i; cast to long in the guard. Trial-dividing each number up to sqrt is O(n√n) and too slow for the large n these tests use — reach straight for the sieve.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of testing each number for primality, I invert it: I mark composites. Starting each prime's crossing at its square avoids redundant work, because smaller multiples already got crossed by smaller primes, giving the classic n log log n bound."
📄 CountPrimes.java

4 Sum of Two Integers LC 371 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Compute a + b without using the + or - operators.

💡 Key insight. Addition splits into two independent bitwise parts: a ^ b is the sum ignoring carries, and (a & b) << 1 is exactly the carry bits shifted into their next column. Feeding the carry back as the new b and repeating until no carry remains reproduces a ripple-carry adder. Java's two's-complement ints make this work for negatives unchanged.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. While b != 0: compute carry = (a & b) << 1 (bits where both are 1 carry left).
  2. Set a = a ^ b (the columns summed without carrying).
  3. Set b = carry (re-add the carry next pass).
  4. When b becomes 0, return a.

⏱️ Time O(1) — at most 32 iterations, one per bit width 💾 Space O(1) — only scalar registers

⚠️ Gotchas. The loop must terminate — it does because each pass pushes carries strictly leftward, and after 32 shifts the carry falls off the top. Negatives "just work" thanks to two's complement; don't special-case them. This is a no-op trick in Java but a genuine question in languages with overflow checks — mention that you rely on wraparound semantics.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I'm rebuilding a hardware adder. XOR gives the carry-less sum; AND-then-shift-left gives the carries that need to ripple into the next column. I loop, folding carries back in, until there are none — at most 32 rounds because carries only move left."
📄 SumOfTwoIntegers.java

5 Pow(x, n) LC 50 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Implement x^n (x a double, n a signed int) in better than linear time.

💡 Key insight. x^n is the product of x^(2^k) over the set bits of n (binary exponentiation): square the base each step and multiply it into the result only when the current low bit is 1 — O(log n) multiplications instead of n. Negative exponents become 1/x^|n|; widening n to long before negating dodges the Integer.MIN_VALUE overflow (-(-2^31) is not a valid int).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Widen long exp = n first; if exp < 0 set x = 1/x and exp = -exp (safe now).
  2. result = 1.0; while exp > 0: if (exp & 1) == 1 do result *= x (this power-of-two factor is present).
  3. Square the base x *= x and shift exp >>= 1.
  4. Return result.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — one squaring per bit of the exponent 💾 Space O(1) — a handful of scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. The classic trap is n = Integer.MIN_VALUE: -n overflows in int, so widen to long before negating. Decide on iterative vs recursive — iterative avoids the stack and the n/2-with-odd-remainder bookkeeping. For very negative n the true result underflows to 0.0, which is expected, not a bug.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Linear multiplication is O(n); instead I read the exponent in binary. Each bit corresponds to a repeatedly-squared power of x, and I only fold in the ones whose bit is set — that's log n multiplies. Negative exponents I handle by inverting x and using long to avoid the MIN_VALUE negation overflow."
📄 PowXN.java

6 Maximum XOR of Two Numbers in an Array LC 421 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given an integer array, find the maximum value of nums[i] ^ nums[j] over all pairs.

💡 Key insight. Insert every number's 32-bit binary, MSB-first, into a binary trie. To maximize a ^ x for a fixed a, be greedy bit-by-bit from the top: at each level prefer the child holding the OPPOSITE bit (which sets that XOR bit to 1) and fall back to the same bit only when the opposite branch is absent. Walking one number against the whole trie finds its best partner; doing this for all numbers yields the global maximum.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build a trie: for each number, descend bits 31→0, creating child[bit] nodes as needed.
  2. For each number, query: at each bit set want = bit ^ 1; if child[want] exists, OR (1 << bit) into the running XOR and descend it, else descend child[bit] (it must exist).
  3. Track the max XOR over all queries; return it.

⏱️ Time O(32n) — each number costs one 32-step insert and one 32-step query 💾 Space O(32n) — the trie holds up to 32 nodes per inserted number

⚠️ Gotchas. Iterate bits MSB-first — greedily maximizing high bits is what makes the greed correct (a 1 in a higher bit always beats any combination of lower bits). The fallback branch is guaranteed to exist because the number itself was inserted. There's an equivalent prefix-hashset method (fix the answer's bits top-down and check whether two stored prefixes XOR to the target prefix, also O(32n)) — mention it as the alternative if asked to avoid the trie.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force is O(n²) over pairs. Instead I store all numbers in a bitwise trie and, for each number, walk down always taking the opposite bit when it's available, because making a high XOR bit a 1 dominates everything below it. Each insert and query is 32 steps, so it's linear in n."
📄 MaximumXorOfTwoNumbers.java

7 Linked List Random Node LC 382 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given a singly linked list, return a uniformly random node's value — ideally without knowing the length up front or copying the list.

💡 Key insight. Reservoir sampling of size 1: walk the list keeping one chosen value, and at the k-th node (1-indexed) replace the choice with probability 1/k. By induction every node ends with probability 1/n, so you never need to know n in advance or store the list — the canonical answer when the length is unknown or the list is huge / streaming.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Store the head in the constructor (O(1), no buffering).
  2. getRandom: set chosen = head.val, k = 1.
  3. For each subsequent node, increment k and, if rng.nextInt(k) == 0 (probability 1/k), set chosen = node.val.
  4. Return chosen after the pass.

⏱️ Time getRandom O(n) — one pass per call; constructor O(1) 💾 Space O(1) — no buffering of the list regardless of length

⚠️ Gotchas. The probability at node k must be exactly 1/k — using nextInt(k) == 0 is the clean way; an off-by-one (e.g. 1/(k+1)) skews the distribution. The trivial alternative — dump to an array and index — is O(n) space and disqualifies you when the interviewer says "the list might not fit in memory." Be ready to prove uniformity by induction.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "If I knew the length I'd pick a random index, but the point is I don't. Reservoir sampling keeps a single candidate and swaps it in at node k with probability 1/k; the telescoping product of keep-probabilities leaves every node at exactly 1/n, in one pass and O(1) memory."
📄 LinkedListRandomNode.java

8 Random Pick with Weight LC 528 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given positive weights w, implement pickIndex() returning index i with probability proportional to w[i].

💡 Key insight. Laying the weights end-to-end on a number line, index i owns the interval (prefix[i-1], prefix[i]]. A uniform integer target in [1, total] therefore lands in index i with probability proportional to w[i]; the first prefix sum >= target — found by a lower-bound binary search — is that index. Prefix sums make each pick O(log n).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Constructor: build prefix[i] = w[0] + ... + w[i]; remember total = prefix[last].
  2. pickIndex: draw target = rng.nextInt(total) + 1 (uniform in [1, total]).
  3. Lower-bound binary search for the smallest index whose prefix sum is >= target: while lo < hi, mid = (lo+hi) >>> 1, prefix[mid] < target ? lo = mid+1 : hi = mid.
  4. Return lo.

⏱️ Time constructor O(n); pickIndex O(log n) per call via binary search 💾 Space O(n) — the prefix-sum array

⚠️ Gotchas. The +1 on the target and the half-open interval convention must agree, or boundary indices get the wrong probability — sample in [1, total] and search for >= target. Use a lower-bound search (hi = mid, not mid-1), since you want the first qualifying index. Prefix sums can overflow int if weights are large — widen if the constraints warrant.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I turn weights into cumulative ranges: index i occupies a slice of the line whose length is its weight. I roll a uniform point in [1, total] and binary-search the first prefix sum that reaches it — that's the index, in log n per query after an O(n) build."
📄 RandomPickWithWeight.java

9 Add Binary LC 67 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given two binary strings a and b, return their sum as a binary string.

💡 Key insight. Schoolbook base-2 addition: walk both strings from the right, and at each column add the two bits plus the incoming carry. The result bit is sum % 2 and the carry out is sum / 2. The only state crossing columns is that single carry, so one right-to-left pass suffices — append the low bit first and reverse once at the end.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Indices i = |a| - 1, j = |b| - 1, carry = 0; loop while i >= 0 || j >= 0 || carry != 0.
  2. sum = carry, then add a.charAt(i--) - '0' and b.charAt(j--) - '0' when each index is still in range.
  3. Append '0' + (sum & 1) as the emitted bit; set carry = sum >> 1. Reverse the buffer at the end since it was built low-to-high.

⏱️ Time O(max(|a|,|b|)) — one pass over the longer input 💾 Space O(max(|a|,|b|)) — the result buffer, one bit per column plus a possible carry

⚠️ Gotchas. Keeping carry != 0 in the loop condition is what emits the final overflow bit (e.g. "1" + "111" → "1000"). Convert characters with - '0', never parse the whole string to an integer — long inputs overflow even long, which is the whole reason it's a string problem. Don't forget the final reverse(); the buffer is built least-significant-first.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I add the two binary strings exactly like long addition on paper: from the rightmost column leftward, summing the two bits and a carry, writing sum % 2 and carrying sum / 2. The single carry is the only state I track. Keeping the carry in the loop condition handles unequal lengths and a leftover carry in one shot, and I reverse at the end because I built the answer low bit first."
📄 AddBinary.java

🔁 Practice plan

Warm up easiest→hardest: Counting Bits and GCD of Strings to lock in the "reuse a smaller answer" and "Euclid" reflexes, then Count Primes (sieve), Sum of Two Integers and Pow(x,n) (bit identities + binary exponentiation), then the two heavyweights — Maximum XOR (trie) and the sampling pair. From a blank file, re-derive: the popcount recurrence, the carry/XOR adder loop, the binary-exponentiation skeleton, the reservoir-sampling invariant, and the prefix-sum + lower-bound search. Rehearse the Google favourites — reservoir sampling (382) and weighted pick (528) — until you can write and justify the probability in under five minutes each, plus the trie walk for 421.

  • ✅ I can explain why popcount(i) = popcount(i>>1) + (i&1) and write Counting Bits in one line.
  • ✅ I can prove str1+str2 == str2+str1 is necessary and sufficient for a common string period.
  • ✅ I can write the Sieve of Eratosthenes from scratch and explain why crossing starts at i*i.
  • ✅ I can implement addition with only XOR/AND/shift and explain why it terminates in ≤32 rounds.
  • ✅ I can code binary exponentiation and handle Integer.MIN_VALUE by widening to long.
  • ✅ I can build a bitwise trie and argue why taking the opposite bit MSB-first maximizes XOR.
  • ✅ I can prove reservoir sampling gives each of n nodes probability exactly 1/n in O(1) space.
  • ✅ I can derive the prefix-sum + lower-bound-binary-search weighted pick and get the boundary convention right.

🌴 Segment Tree / Fenwick (BIT)

When an array must answer range queries and absorb point updates, neither a plain array (O(n) query) nor a prefix-sum array (O(n) update) is enough — you need a balanced tree of partial aggregates. Fenwick (Binary Indexed) trees give O(log n) prefix sums and updates in a few lines using the low-bit trick; the divide-and-conquer mindset also powers "count pairs with a range constraint" problems via merge sort. These show up at Google L6 as the upgrade path the moment an interviewer says "now support updates" or "count how many subarray sums fall in a range."

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 2 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Range sum/min/max query" interleaved with point or range updates → Fenwick tree or segment tree.
  • A prefix-sum solution is the obvious first idea but updates would be O(n) each → upgrade to a BIT.
  • "Count subarrays whose sum lies in [lower, upper]" or "count smaller/greater elements to the right" → prefix sums + (BIT or merge-sort counting).
  • You need ordered-statistic counting ("how many earlier prefixes fall in a window") while keeping data sorted → merge sort with a counting step.
  • Constraints up to ~10^5 with many mixed operations, so each op must be O(log n).

🧠 Core idea & template

A Fenwick tree stores partial sums so that node i covers the i & -i elements ending at i — the lowest set bit isolates the block it owns. Walking down by i -= i & -i accumulates a prefix sum; walking up by i += i & -i propagates a point update; each touches O(log n) nodes, and a range sum is a difference of two prefixes. The merge-sort counting idiom is the cousin: while merging two already-sorted halves, slide a window to count cross-pairs satisfying a constraint in linear time per level.

// Fenwick (Binary Indexed Tree), 1-indexed
int[] tree = new int[n + 1];
void update(int i, int delta) {                 // i is 1-indexed
    for (; i <= n; i += i & -i) tree[i] += delta;   // climb to each owning node
}
int prefix(int i) {                             // sum of first i elements
    int s = 0;
    for (; i > 0; i -= i & -i) s += tree[i];        // drop lowest set bit each step
    return s;
}
int rangeSum(int l, int r) { return prefix(r) - prefix(l - 1); }

// Merge-sort counting: count cross-pairs in [lower, upper] over sorted halves
int start = mid + 1, end = mid + 1;
for (int i = lo; i <= mid; i++) {
    while (start <= hi && p[start] - p[i] <  lower) start++;
    while (end   <= hi && p[end]   - p[i] <= upper) end++;
    count += end - start;                       // both bounds advance monotonically -> O(n)
}

📘 Deep dive

1 Range Sum Query - Mutable LC 307 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Support update(index, val) and sumRange(left, right) on an integer array, both efficiently and interleaved.

💡 Key insight. A Fenwick (Binary Indexed) tree stores partial sums indexed so that node i covers the i & -i elements ending at i — that low bit isolates the block this node owns. Walking down by i -= i & -i accumulates a prefix sum; walking up by i += i & -i propagates a point update; each touches only O(log n) nodes. A range sum is the difference of two prefix sums.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a 1-indexed tree[n+1] and a copy of the current nums (to compute deltas).
  2. Build by calling update(i, nums[i]) for each element.
  3. update(index, val): compute delta = val - nums[index], store the new value, then for i = index+1; i <= n; i += i & -i add delta to tree[i].
  4. sumRange(left, right) = prefix(right+1) - prefix(left), where prefix(i) loops i -= i & -i summing tree[i].

⏱️ Time O(log n) per update and per sumRange; O(n log n) build 💾 Space O(n) — the BIT plus the values copy for deltas

⚠️ Gotchas. The tree is 1-indexed; converting a 0-indexed array position means index+1, and sumRange uses prefix(right+1) - prefix(left) (not right). You must store the old value to compute the delta — a Fenwick tree adds deltas, it can't overwrite a position directly. i & -i relies on two's-complement; it isolates the lowest set bit. A build via n updates is O(n log n) — fine here, but mention the O(n) linear build if pressed.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A prefix-sum array gives O(1) queries but O(n) updates; a plain array is the reverse. A Fenwick tree balances both at O(log n) by storing sums of binary-aligned blocks. The low-bit i & -i tells me the size of the block a node owns, so I climb by adding it on updates and descend by subtracting it on prefix queries. Range sum is just prefix(r) minus prefix(l-1)."
📄 RangeSumQueryMutable.java

2 Count of Range Sum LC 327 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Count the number of contiguous subarrays whose sum lies in [lower, upper] inclusive.

💡 Key insight. The sum of subarray (i, j) equals prefix[j+1] - prefix[i], so a range sum in [lower, upper] becomes: for each right prefix P, count left prefixes L with P - upper <= L <= P - lower. Embedding this count inside merge sort exploits that each half is already sorted — a two-pointer window over the right half tallies the valid left prefixes in linear time per merge, while the merge itself restores sorted order.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build prefix[0..n] as a long[] (sums can overflow int).
  2. Run merge sort on the prefix array. At each node, recurse left and right and accumulate their counts.
  3. With both halves sorted, slide start/end over the right half: for each left index i, advance start past prefixes where p[start] - p[i] < lower and end past where p[end] - p[i] <= upper; add end - start. Both pointers only move forward → O(n) per level.
  4. Merge the two halves back into sorted order and return the total count.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — merge sort with O(n) counting per level 💾 Space O(n) — prefix array plus the merge scratch buffer

⚠️ Gotchas. Use long for prefixes — adversarial inputs like [Integer.MIN_VALUE, Integer.MAX_VALUE] overflow int. The two windows are asymmetric: < lower (strict) for start vs <= upper (inclusive) for end, so the count is the half-open [start, end) width. The counting must happen before the merge, while both halves are still independently sorted. An alternative is a BIT/segment tree over coordinate-compressed prefix values — same O(n log n), but the merge-sort version is cleaner here.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force is O(n²) over all subarrays. I convert to prefix sums so a subarray sum is a difference of two prefixes; then counting subarrays in [lower, upper] becomes, for each right prefix, counting left prefixes in a value window. I piggyback that count on merge sort: when both halves are sorted I can sweep the window with two monotonic pointers in O(n), and the merge keeps everything sorted for the parent. Overall O(n log n), with long to avoid overflow."
📄 CountOfRangeSum.java

🔁 Practice plan

Memorize the four-line Fenwick tree cold by re-deriving Range Sum Query - Mutable (307) from a blank file until the i & -i climb/descend is automatic and you can explain the 1-indexing. Then tackle Count of Range Sum (327), which fuses prefix sums with merge-sort counting — write it twice, once getting the strict-vs-inclusive window bounds right, and rehearse the overflow argument. Google follow-ups to rehearse: Count of Smaller Numbers After Self (315) and Reverse Pairs (493) as the same merge-sort/BIT counting family; Range Sum Query 2D - Mutable (308) for a 2D Fenwick; and "support range updates" (lazy segment tree / difference-BIT) as the next escalation.

  • ✅ I can write a Fenwick tree from scratch and explain why i & -i isolates a node's block.
  • ✅ I can justify the 1-indexing and the delta-based update.
  • ✅ I can reduce subarray-sum-in-range to counting prefix differences in a value window.
  • ✅ I can embed that count in merge sort and explain the O(n) two-pointer step and the long guard.
  • ✅ I can name when to reach for a BIT vs. a segment tree vs. merge-sort counting.

🏗️ Design

"Design a data structure" problems test whether you can pick the right backing containers and keep every operation cheap as state mutates. The Google L6 signal here is API discipline: name your invariants, justify O(1)/O(log n) per method, and reason about correctness across pushes, evictions, out-of-order updates, and snapshots — exactly the muscles you use when designing a service's in-memory layer.

Source: Prep Tracker, Deep Research Adds & curated Staff gaps · 9 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Support these methods in O(1)/O(log n)" ⇒ pick containers per-operation; pair a hash map with an array or linked list to get both random access and cheap mutation.
  • Need a running min/max that survives pops or corrections ⇒ an auxiliary stack of per-level minima, or a TreeMap multiset of live values.
  • Out-of-order or correctable timestamped updates ⇒ keep a source-of-truth map plus a mirrored ordered multiset so you can retire stale values.
  • "Value at-or-before a query time" / versioned reads ⇒ append-only per-key timelines + binary search.
  • "Snapshot then query history" ⇒ record only on change with a monotonic snap id; binary-search the timeline.
  • getRandom() in O(1) ⇒ a dense array for uniform indexing + a value→index map for O(1) deletion via swap-with-last.
  • Eviction by recency (LRU) or frequency (LFU) ⇒ hash map of nodes + doubly-linked list(s); for LFU, bucket lists per frequency with a minFreq pointer.
  • "Most recent across many sources" (feed) ⇒ k-way merge with a max-heap seeded by each source's newest item.

🧠 Core idea & template

The recurring move is combine two containers so each covers the other's weakness. A hash map gives O(1) lookup but no order; an array gives O(1) indexed/random access but O(n) middle-deletion; a doubly-linked list gives O(1) splice but no lookup; a TreeMap gives O(log n) ordered min/max. Most problems below are one of these pairings plus an invariant you maintain on every mutation.

// ── Map + array: O(1) insert / delete / getRandom (LC 380) ──
List<Integer> vals; Map<Integer,Integer> idx;   // value → slot in vals
boolean remove(int v) {
    Integer i = idx.get(v);
    if (i == null) return false;
    int last = vals.size() - 1, lastVal = vals.get(last);
    vals.set(i, lastVal); idx.put(lastVal, i);    // move tail into the hole
    vals.remove(last); idx.remove(v);             // pop tail → O(1), no shifting
    return true;
}

// ── Append-only timeline + binary search (versioned reads) ──
// per key: parallel lists of (timestamp/snapId, value), strictly increasing.
// query(t): rightmost record with key <= t  →  binary search.

// ── Map + doubly-linked list: O(1) recency / eviction (LRU/LFU) ──
// node = {key,val[,freq]} in a hash map; splice nodes between a sentinel
// head (most-recent) and tail (evict target) in O(1) on every touch.

📘 Deep dive

1 Min Stack LC 155 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. A stack supporting push, pop, top, and getMin, all in O(1).

💡 Key insight. The minimum can only change at push/pop boundaries, so alongside each value we remember the minimum of the stack at the moment it was pushed. The top of this auxiliary min-stack is therefore always the current minimum, and popping the main stack pops the matching min in lockstep — no rescanning.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep two stacks: values and mins.
  2. On push, push the value and push min(value, current min) onto mins.
  3. On pop, pop both. getMin reads mins.peek().

⏱️ Time O(1) — push, pop, top, getMin are each single stack operations. 💾 Space O(n) — the auxiliary min-stack mirrors the value stack's depth.

⚠️ Gotchas. Push the carried-forward minimum even when the new value is larger, so the two stacks stay the same height and pop in lockstep. Duplicate minima must each get their own mins entry, or popping one drops the min prematurely. A value+count encoding can save space but the paired stack is the clean, correct default.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I shadow the value stack with a min stack that records the running minimum at each level. The current min is always the top of that second stack, so getMin is O(1), and pops stay consistent because both stacks move together."
📄 MinStack.java

2 Stock Price Fluctuation LC 2034 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Stream of (timestamp, price) records, possibly out of order and correctable. Support current (latest), maximum, and minimum over the corrected prices.

💡 Key insight. Because any past timestamp can be corrected, a running min/max won't do. Keep a source-of-truth timeToPrice map and mirror it as a TreeMap multiset priceCounts (price → how many timestamps hold it) for O(log n) min/max. On a correction, decrement the OLD price's count before adding the new one, so the multiset always reflects current prices only.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. update: if the timestamp already exists, decrement its old price's multiset count (removing the key at zero); store the new price and increment its count; track latestTime.
  2. current: return timeToPrice.get(latestTime).
  3. maximum/minimum: return priceCounts.lastKey() / firstKey().

⏱️ Time update / minimum / maximum O(log n) — TreeMap ops; current O(1) — a map lookup. 💾 Space O(n) — one entry per distinct timestamp.

⚠️ Gotchas. On a correction you must retire the stale price from the multiset, or maximum/minimum report a value that no longer exists. Remove a multiset key when its count hits zero, otherwise firstKey/lastKey return a phantom price. latestTime tracks the largest timestamp seen, which may differ from the most recently updated one.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Corrections mean I can't keep a simple running extreme, so I hold the authoritative timestamp→price map and mirror live prices in a TreeMap multiset. On an update I subtract the old price and add the new, so min and max are just the TreeMap's ends in log n, and current is an O(1) lookup of the latest timestamp."
📄 StockPriceFluctuation.java

3 Detect Squares LC 2013 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Add points to a stream; for a query point, count axis-aligned squares with positive area formed using three added points.

💡 Key insight. An axis-aligned square is fixed once you pick the query point and a second point on the same vertical line (same x); the side length is the y-gap, which forces the two remaining corners. So you only iterate candidates sharing the query's x. Counting duplicate points with a multiset lets a query multiply the three other corners' counts — that product is exactly how many distinct squares those repeated points form.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. add: bump a per-point multiset (encode (x,y) as a long key) and a per-column map x → (y → count).
  2. count: scan the query column's y-values; for each y ≠ qy, the signed side is y - qy.
  3. Multiply the counts of the two far corners on each side (left at qx - side, right at qx + side) and accumulate cntVertical * (left + right).

⏱️ Time add O(1); count O(K) where K = points sharing the query's x-coordinate. 💾 Space O(N) — one multiset entry per distinct point added.

⚠️ Gotchas. Skip y == qy — a zero-height "side" is not a square. Multiply the corner counts (duplicates create multiple squares); a boolean "exists" check undercounts. Check both the left (qx - side) and right (qx + side) squares for each vertical pair. The long key must pack coordinates without collision within the value range.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I index points by exact coordinate and by column. A square is pinned by the query point plus another point in the same column — that gives the side length and forces the other two corners. I sum, over each such vertical partner, the product of the far-corner counts on both sides, so duplicate points correctly multiply the square count."
📄 DetectSquares.java

4 Design Hit Counter LC 362 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Record hits by timestamp and report how many occurred in the past 300 seconds.

💡 Key insight. A fixed circular buffer of 300 one-second buckets caps memory regardless of traffic. Bucket i serves every timestamp t with t % 300 == i, storing the LAST timestamp that wrote it plus a hit count. When a hit or query lands on a bucket whose stored timestamp is more than 300s stale, that bucket belongs to an earlier window and is lazily reset before use — so expiry costs nothing until touched.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Two parallel arrays of size 300: times[] (last timestamp per bucket) and hits[].
  2. hit(t): at index t % 300, if the stored timestamp differs, reset the bucket to count 1; else increment.
  3. getHits(t): sum hits[i] for every bucket whose t - times[i] < 300.

⏱️ Time hit O(1); getHits O(300) = O(1) — a fixed scan over the ring. 💾 Space O(300) = O(1) — two fixed arrays, independent of traffic.

⚠️ Gotchas. The boundary is exclusive — a hit exactly 300s old has dropped out (t - times[i] < 300, not ≤). Lazily resetting a stale bucket on write is what keeps memory bounded versus an unbounded timestamp queue (fine for sparse traffic, dangerous for bursts). Same-second hits accumulate into one bucket. To scale the window to a much larger range, switch to a timestamp queue or a binary-indexed structure.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I use 300 second-buckets in a ring, each remembering the last second it counted. A hit either freshens a stale bucket to one or increments the current second, and a query sums only buckets within the last 300 seconds. Memory is hard-capped at O(300) no matter how many hits arrive — the win over a raw timestamp queue."
📄 DesignHitCounter.java

5 Insert Delete GetRandom O(1) LC 380 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. A set supporting insert, remove, and getRandom (uniform over current elements), all in O(1) average.

💡 Key insight. getRandom needs a contiguous, gap-free array to index into, but deleting a middle element of an array is O(n) due to shifting. Since order doesn't matter, to remove a value we look up its slot via a hash map, overwrite that slot with the LAST element (patching the moved element's index in the map), and pop the tail — a true O(1) deletion that keeps the array dense.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Maintain values (a list) and indexOf (value → slot).
  2. insert: if absent, append and record its index.
  3. remove: move the tail value into the removed slot, fix its index, pop the tail, erase the value.
  4. getRandom: index values with a uniform random position.

⏱️ Time insert / remove / getRandom all O(1) average — array tail ops plus map lookups. 💾 Space O(n) — the value list plus a parallel value→index map.

⚠️ Gotchas. When swapping the tail into the hole, update the moved element's recorded index before popping, or the map points at a removed slot. Removing the final element is itself a no-shift pop. Edge case: removing the last remaining element (hole equals tail) must still erase cleanly. Hash collisions make it amortised, not strictly worst-case, O(1).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Random access wants a dense array, but array deletes are O(n). The trick is order is irrelevant, so I delete by swapping the target with the last element and popping the tail, fixing the swapped element's index in my value→index map. All three operations are O(1) on average."
📄 InsertDeleteGetRandomO1.java

6 Time Based Key-Value Store LC 981 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Support set(key, value, timestamp) and get(key, timestamp) returning the value stored at the largest timestamp ≤ the query (or "").

💡 Key insight. The problem guarantees set is called with strictly increasing timestamps per key, so each key's history is already sorted just by appending. That turns get — "the value at the largest timestamp ≤ t" — into a plain binary search over that key's timestamp list, with no sorting at query time.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Per key keep parallel lists: stamps (timestamps, ascending) and vals (index-aligned values).
  2. set: append the timestamp and value.
  3. get: binary-search for the rightmost timestamp ≤ query; return its value or "" if none.

⏱️ Time set O(1) amortized (append); get O(log n) — binary search within one key. 💾 Space O(n) — every set keeps one (timestamp, value) record.

⚠️ Gotchas. The binary search is the "floor" variant (largest ≤ t), not exact-match — track the best index as you go right. Return "" for both an unknown key and a query earlier than all stored timestamps. The append-only sortedness relies on the strictly-increasing-timestamp guarantee; without it you'd need an ordered map and insertion, not a plain list.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Since timestamps per key only ever increase, appending keeps each key's history sorted for free. A get is then a floor binary search — the rightmost record at or before the query time — so writes are O(1) and reads O(log n)."
📄 TimeBasedKeyValueStore.java

7 Snapshot Array LC 1146 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. An array of length slots supporting set(index, val), snap() (returns a snapshot id), and get(index, snapId) reading the value at that snapshot.

💡 Key insight. Snapshotting the whole array on every snap() would be O(length) per snap and explode memory. Instead each index keeps its OWN timeline — a list of (snapId, value) records appended only when that index actually CHANGES. The records are naturally sorted by snap id, so get is a binary search for the last record whose id ≤ the queried snapshot. snap() just hands back the current id and bumps it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed each index's timeline with (0, 0) so early gets resolve to the default.
  2. set: if the last record's id equals the current snapId, overwrite it (collapse same-snapshot writes); else append a new record.
  3. snap: return snapId++.
  4. get: binary-search the index's timeline for the rightmost record with id ≤ snapId; return its value.

⏱️ Time set O(log k) / get O(log k) where k = snapshots that changed the index; snap O(1). 💾 Space O(total writes) — one record per actual value change, not per snap.

⚠️ Gotchas. Record on change, never per snapshot — that's the whole point versus the naive copy. Collapse multiple sets within the same snapshot onto one record (overwrite if the last id matches), or the timeline bloats and duplicates a snap id. Seed (0,0) so a get before any write returns the default. The binary search is again the floor variant.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Copying the array per snapshot is wasteful, so each index keeps its own change-log of (snapId, value). A snap is just an O(1) id bump; a set appends or overwrites within the current snapshot; and a get binary-searches the index's log for the last value at or before the queried snapshot. Memory scales with writes, not snapshots."
📄 SnapshotArray.java

8 Design Twitter LC 355 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Support postTweet, follow, unfollow, and getNewsFeed returning the 10 most recent tweets from a user and those they follow.

💡 Key insight. A global, ever-increasing timestamp stamped on every tweet makes "most recent" a total order across all users. Each user keeps their own tweet list (newest last) and a followee set. A news feed is then a k-way merge of the relevant users' lists: seed a max-heap with each list's newest tweet and pop 10 times, pushing the predecessor after each pop — never scanning whole histories.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. postTweet: append (tweetId, clock++) to the user's list.
  2. getNewsFeed: collect the user plus followees; push each source's newest tweet (as [time, owner, index]) into a max-heap.
  3. Pop up to 10 times, adding the id and pushing that source's next-older tweet.
  4. follow/unfollow: edit the followee set (a user never follows themselves).

⏱️ Time postTweet O(1); follow/unfollow O(1); getNewsFeed O(F + 10 log F) for F followed users. 💾 Space O(T + F) — all tweets plus follow relationships.

⚠️ Gotchas. The heap entry must remember its position in the owner's list so you can step to the next-older tweet after a pop. A user must see their own tweets — add self to the source set. Cap the feed at 10 and stop early. Handle users with no tweets (skip empty lists when seeding the heap).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A global clock totally orders tweets by recency. The feed is a k-way merge: I seed a max-heap with the newest tweet from the user and each followee, pop ten, and after each pop push that author's next-older tweet. I only ever touch as far back as the ten freshest require, so it's roughly F plus ten heap pops."
📄 DesignTwitter.java

9 LFU Cache LC 460 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. A fixed-capacity cache evicting the least-frequently-used key (ties broken by least-recently-used), with O(1) get and put.

💡 Key insight. Bucket keys by frequency. Each frequency owns a doubly-linked list of exactly the keys at that count, ordered by recency (most-recent at head, least-recent at tail). Touching a key splices its node from bucket f into bucket f+1 in O(1), and the LRU tie-break within a frequency is free because the list is already recency-ordered. A minFreq pointer names the bucket to evict from — its tail is globally the least-frequent, least-recent key.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Maintain nodes (key → node) and freqBuckets (frequency → DLL), plus minFreq.
  2. get: look up the node, touch it (promote frequency), return its value.
  3. put: update + touch if present; else if full, evict minFreq bucket's tail; insert the new node at frequency 1 and set minFreq = 1.
  4. touch: remove from bucket f; if that emptied the minFreq bucket, increment minFreq; re-insert at the head of bucket f+1.

⏱️ Time O(1) for get and put — all splices and lookups are constant work. 💾 Space O(capacity) — node map plus frequency buckets holding live keys.

⚠️ Gotchas. minFreq only resets to 1 on insert and increments when the old min bucket empties after a promotion — get this wrong and eviction targets the wrong key. New keys always start at frequency 1, so a fresh insert makes minFreq = 1. Handle capacity == 0 (store nothing). Use sentinel head/tail nodes so splices never null-check. Within a frequency, head is MRU and tail is the LRU eviction target.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I bucket keys by access frequency, each bucket a recency-ordered doubly-linked list. A touch is an O(1) splice from frequency f to f+1, and eviction pops the tail of the minFreq bucket — least frequent, and least recent among those. The minFreq pointer stays correct because it only resets to 1 on insert or bumps when the min bucket drains."
📄 LFUCache.java

🔁 Practice plan

Group these by their backing-container trick and re-derive each from a blank file. The map+array pairing: Insert/Delete/GetRandom (380) — be able to explain the swap-with-last deletion cold. The map+TreeMap-multiset pairing for correctable streams: Stock Price (2034), and the lazy-reset ring of Hit Counter (362). The append-only timeline + floor binary search: Time Based KV Store (981) and Snapshot Array (1146) — note how the per-index change-log avoids per-snapshot copies. The auxiliary-structure invariants: Min Stack (155). The heap k-way merge: Design Twitter (355). The map+DLL eviction capstone: LFU (460), the hardest to get O(1) right. For every one, state your invariants and the per-operation complexity out loud before coding. Google-favourite follow-ups: "now make it thread-safe", "now bound memory / add TTL eviction", and "now support get-at-or-before efficiently".

  • ✅ I can pick backing containers per operation and justify each method's complexity.
  • ✅ I can explain the swap-with-last O(1) array deletion and why the index map must be patched first.
  • ✅ I can mirror a correctable map in a TreeMap multiset and retire stale values on update.
  • ✅ I can write a floor binary search over an append-only timeline (981 / 1146) and seed defaults correctly.
  • ✅ I can describe the per-index change-log that makes Snapshot Array scale with writes, not snapshots.
  • ✅ I can implement LFU with per-frequency DLLs and explain exactly when minFreq moves.
  • ✅ I can build a news feed as a max-heap k-way merge and bound it to the 10 freshest tweets.
